# 黄帝素问灵枢集注

**Huangdi Neijing Ling Shu: Collected Commentaries**

**Title:** *Huangdi Neijing Ling Shu: Collected Commentaries*
*The Huangdi Neijing Ling Shu* was compiled during the Spring and Autumn to Warring States period (attribution unknown); annotated by Shì Sōngyīn of the Song dynasty. Twenty-three volumes.

**Source Text:** From the *Zhengtong Daozang* (Canon of Orthodox Daoist Texts), Taixuan (Great Mystery) Section.
**Collated Edition:** Ming-dynasty edition from Zhào Fǔ Jùjìng Táng (Respectful Study Hall of the Zhao Residence).

---

**Preface to the *Huangdi Neijing* *Lingshu* *Collected Commentaries***

In ancient times, the **Yellow Emperor** composed the *Inner Canon* (Neijing) in **eighteen volumes**, the *Lingshu* in **nine volumes**, and the *Suwen* in **nine volumes**—such was its original extent. The world has only revered and practiced the *Suwen*.

The **Yue people** obtained fragments of its teachings and expounded the *Difficult Classic* (Nan Jing), while **Emperor Huang of the Jin Dynasty** (Huangfu Mi) later annotated it as the *Jia-Yi* (甲乙). All subsequent schools of thought trace their origins to these works.

However, some of its questions contain errors or omissions, and thus cannot be regarded as definitive for future generations. For instance, as the **Nanyang Living Man’s Book** (Huó Rén Shū) states: *"Coughing and inversion are called 'hán' (喊)."* Upon careful examination of the *Lingshu Classic*, it says: *"The qi of new grains enters the stomach and contends with the lingering cold qi, hence it is called 'hán' (喊)."* When these are combined, the principle becomes clear.

Again, in the **sixty-fifth chapter of the *Difficult Classic*** (Nan Jing), the **Yue people** merely outlined the *Lingshu*’s original teachings on **acupuncture points**, yet some mistakenly regard them as **meridians and collaterals** (liúzhù). Upon careful examination of the *Lingshu Classic*, it says: *"The term 'nodes' (jié) refers to where the **spirit-qi** (shénqì) circulates and enters and exits—it does not pertain to skin, flesh, sinews, or bones."* It further states: *"Spirit-qi is the **true-qi** (zhēnqì). The circulation of spirit-qi is the **meridian** (shū), while the convergence of **wells, streams, and tributaries** (jǐngróngshū), along with **meridians and collaterals** (jīngluò), are the **original nodes** (běnjié)."* When combined, it is evident that the two interpretations differ as much as **heaven and earth**.

Alas, the *Lingshu* has long been lost, and no one could fully comprehend it. To be a **physician**, one must read medical texts. There have been those who read but cannot practice medicine, yet never has there been one who does not read and can practice medicine. Not reading medical texts, and not inheriting the family profession, is even more lethal than wielding **blades and weapons** (tǐngrèn). Hence, the ancients said: *"For a son not to study medical texts is tantamount to disobedience."*

I, the humble servant, from youth to maturity, devoted myself to this **Dao** (dàode), delving into its principles. Though unworthy, I dared not hesitate to cross-reference various texts and reexamine my family’s old annotated edition of the *Lingshu*—nine volumes, totaling **eighty-one chapters** (pìan). I added **phonetic annotations** (xīngshēng) and appended them to the end, compiling it into **twenty-four volumes**.

May this enable those who love life to grasp its meaning effortlessly upon opening the text, with no confusion or distinction.

Apart from what has already been clarified in the original **Canon** (Jīng), I request the **Prefectural authorities** (fǔzhǐ) to instruct the **Transportation Bureau** (yùnshì) to select officials and carefully determine the contents, then submit the final draft to the **Secretariat Bureau** (Mìshūshèng) and **Imperial Academy** (Guózǐjiàn).

Now, **Sung Zhao** (嵩) of **Jing County** (锦官) has specially sought out eminent physicians, and I humbly request their further scrutiny, lest future generations err in its application.

The benefits are boundless, and the merit is self-evident.

This was written in the **second year of the Shaoxing era** (宋绍兴乙玄), on the **mid-summer solstice** (zhòngxià wàngrì). The **Jing County Scholar** (锦官史) **Sung Zhao** inscribed.

**Collected Commentaries on the *Huangdi Neijing* *Lingshu* — Volume One**

The **Nine Needles and Twelve Origins**—First Chapter of *Lingshū* of the *Huangdi Neijing*

The **Yellow Emperor** asked **Qībó** (岐伯):

"I am the sovereign of the ten thousand people, nurturing the common folk and collecting their taxes. I pity those who cannot provide for themselves and who suffer from illness. I wish to spare them from toxic medicines and the harshness of **biànshí** (砭石, *scraping stone*), and instead use fine needles to regulate their **jingmài** (经脉, *meridian channels*), harmonizing their **xuèqì** (血气, *blood and qi*). I would have them gather at the **yùnhuì** (营会, *convergence of nourishing vessels*)—where the **yíng** (营, *nourishing*) and **lù** (络, *collateral*) vessels meet in reverse or forward flow—so that the method may be passed down to future generations, with clear and precise principles established. I desire that the technique be enduring yet never lost, long-lasting yet never forgotten, and that its **jīngjì** (经纪, *fundamental framework*) be well-defined.

Distinguish its **zhāng** (章, *layers/phases*) and separate its **bié** (表里, *exterior and interior*), marking its beginning and end. Let each needle have its distinct form, and first establish the **Zhēn Jīng** (针经, *Needle Classic*). I wish to understand its full essence.

**Qībó** replied:

"Your servant requests permission to expound its principles in order, establishing its **gāngjì** (纲纪, *overarching framework*). It begins with one and concludes with nine. Let me speak of its **Dao** (道, *Way*).

The **essence of the small needle** (小针之要) is easy to explain but difficult to master. The coarse practitioner adheres only to outward form, while the refined guards the **shén** (神, *spirit/essence*). When the spirit is truly grasped, the **shénkè** (神客, *spiritual guest*) lingers at the gate—before the illness is even seen, how can one know its origin?

The **subtlety of needling** (刺之微) lies in the timing of **sùchí** (速迟, *rapid or slow*). The coarse practitioner clings to the **guān** (关, *gate/barrier*), while the refined guards the **jī** (机, *mechanism). When the mechanism moves, it never strays from its **kōng** (空, *void/emptiness). The void within the mechanism is serene, subtle, and fleeting—its arrival cannot be anticipated, its departure cannot be traced. Those who understand the **Dao** of the mechanism cannot be swayed by mere words; those who do not, may strike it yet fail to elicit response. Knowing its ebb and flow, one must seize the moment—this is the **mìao** (妙, *refined art) possessed only by the skilled.

When the **qì** moves **against the natural flow** (逆), it is called **reverse**; when it moves **with the natural flow** (顺), it is called **forward**. To clearly grasp reverse and forward, one must act without hesitation. To **counter its arrival** (迎而夺之) is to prevent its excess; to **aid its departure** (追而济之) is to ensure its sufficiency. To both welcome and follow it, harmonizing with intent, completes the **Dao** of the needle.

In general, when using needles:
- If there is **emptiness** (虚), fill it;
- If there is **fullness** (满), drain it;
- If the **qì** is **stagnant** (宛陈), remove it;
- If **evil** (邪) **qi** prevails, weaken it.
As the *Great Overview* (《大要》) states:
- **Slow insertion followed by rapid needling** (徐而疾) fills;
- **Rapid insertion followed by slow needling** (疾而徐) weakens.

In terms of **emptiness and fullness** (虚实), the needle’s effect is **as if present or absent** (若有若无); in terms of **later and earlier** (后与先), it is **as if preserved or lost** (若存若亡); in terms of **emptiness and fullness** (虚实), it is **as if gained or lost** (若得若失).

The **refinement of the Nine Needles** is most profound. During **bǔ** (补, *tonification*), the needle must be held **internally** (内) before being released **externally** (外). To **sort and expel evil** (排阳), the needle must be inserted; to **drain evil** (邪气得泄), it must be withdrawn. Pressing while drawing the needle is called **internal warming** (内温)—the blood cannot scatter, nor the **qì** escape.

During **bǔ** (补, *tonification*), follow the **qì** with intent. If it wanders, guide it; if it lingers, press it as if it were a **midge or gnat** (蚊虻) resting, or as if it were about to return. When it departs, let it go as if severed. Ensure that **left** (阴) **qì** flows to the **right** (阳), that the **qì** ceases naturally, and that the **external gate** (外门) is closed—then the **qì** will be **filled** (实). There must be no lingering blood; if there is, remove it swiftly.

The **Dao** of holding the needle: the firmest grasp is the most precious. Needle straight and true, without deviation left or right—**spirit** (神) lies in the **qiūmáo** (秋毫, *finest hair*). Attending to the patient’s illness and carefully observing the blood and **meridians** (脉), one must strike without error.

At the moment of needling, one must be in **suspension** (悬阳), aligning with the **two wèi** (卫, *defensive*) points. The **spirit** (神) must not stray; know whether the illness will **survive or perish** (存亡).

The **blood and meridians** (脉) reside **horizontally across the face** (脸横居). When observed, they appear **clear and solitary** (独澄); when palpated, they feel **firm and distinct** (独坚).

The **Nine Needles** have distinct names and forms:
1. **Cái** (才) **Needle** (针), length **one cun and six fēn** (一寸六分);
2. **Yuán** (圆) **Needle** (针), length **one cun and six fēn** (一寸六分);
3. **Tí** (缇) **Needle** (针), length **three cun and five fēn** (三寸半);
4. **Fēng** (锋) **Needle** (针), length **one cun and six fēn** (一寸六分);
5. **Biàn** (铍) **Needle** (针), length **four cun** (四寸), width **two fēn and five lǐ** (二分半);
6. **Yuán Lì** (圆利) **Needle** (针), length **one cun and six fēn** (一寸六分);
7. **Háo** (毫) **Needle** (针), length **three cun and six fēn** (三寸六分);
8. **Cháng** (长) **Needle** (针), length **seven cun** (七寸);
9. **Dà** (大) **Needle** (针), length **four cun** (四寸).

The **Cái Needle** (才针) has a large head and sharp tip, used to **drain evil** (写) and **sort yang** (阳气);
The **Yuán Needle** (圆针) is shaped like an egg, for **rubbing and separating** (揩摩分问), without harming the flesh—used to **drain evil** (写) and **separate** (分) **qì**;
The **Tí Needle** (缇针) has a tip as sharp as **shǔsù** (黍粟, *millet grains*), for pressing the **meridians** (脉) without **shēnqìn** (陷, *penetrating too deeply)—to **convey its qì** (致其气);
The **Fēng Needle** (锋针) has a blade with three sharp edges, used to **release acute illnesses** (发症疾);
The **Biàn Needle** (铍针) has a tip like a **sword’s edge** (剑锋), used to **draw large pus** (取大脓);
The **Yuán Lì Needle** (圆利针) is large like a **hare’s foot** (骛), both round and sharp, with a slightly thicker middle—used to **extract violent qì** (取暴气);
The **Háo Needle** (毫针) has a tip like the **proboscis of a midge or gnat** (蚊虻喙). When needling **slowly** (徐) at the **middle of the string** (弦) or **tip** (尖), the **qì** moves **forward** (往); when lingering **slightly** (微) and **remaining** (久留), it **nourishes** (养)—used to **extract pain and numbness** (取痛痹);
The **Cháng Needle** (长针) is sharp and thin, used to **extract distant numbness** (取远痹);
The **Dà Needle** (大针) resembles a **club** (梃), with a slightly rounded tip—used to **drain the water of the mechanisms** (写机关之水).

Thus, the **Nine Needles** are complete.

Now, the **qì** within the **meridians** (脉) has three layers:
- **Evil qì** (邪气) resides **above** (上);
- **Turbid qì** (浊气) resides **in the middle** (中);
- **Pure qì** (清气) resides **below** (下).

Therefore:
- If the needle **pierces deeply** (陷) into the **meridian** (脉), **evil qì** (邪气) will be expelled;
- If the needle strikes **midway** (中) in the **meridian** (脉), **turbid qì** (浊气) will be expelled;
- If the needle is inserted **too deeply** (太深), **evil qì** (邪气) will **sink back** (反沉), and the illness will worsen.

Thus it is said:
- The **skin, flesh, sinews, and meridians** (皮肉筋脉) each have their proper **depths** (所处);
- Illnesses each have their **appropriate responses** (所宜);
- Each needle has its **distinct form** (不同形);
- Each must be applied according to its **suitability** (所宜), neither **overtonifying** (无实) nor **overdraining** (无虚);
- To **deplete what is insufficient** (损不足) and **tonify what is excessive** (益有余) is called **exacting treatment** (甚病)—and the illness grows more severe.

To **extract from five meridians** (五脉) is death; to **extract from three meridians** (三脉) is **mù** (框, *fainting*).
To **deplete yin** (阴) is death; to **deplete yang** (阳) is **madness** (狂).
Thus, the **harms of needling** (针害) are complete.

When needling and **qì** does not arrive, **do not repeat** (无问其数);
When needling and **qì** arrives, withdraw the needle and **do not reinsert** (乃去之, 勿复针).

Each needle has its **proper application** (所宜), its **distinct form** (不同形), and its **specific function** (所为).

The **key to needling** (刺之要) is that **qì** arrives and there is **effect** (效). The **truth of effect** (效之信) is like **wind dispersing clouds** (若风之吹云), clear as **seeing the azure sky** (明乎若见苍天).
Thus, the **Dao** of needling is complete.

The **Yellow Emperor** said:
"I wish to hear of the **origins** (所出) of the **Five Zàng** (藏) and **Six Fǔ** (府)."

**Qībó** replied:
"The **Five Zàng** (五藏) each have **five jīng** (经) points, totaling **twenty-five** (五五二十五);
The **Six Fǔ** (六府) each have **six jīng** (经) points, totaling **thirty-six** (六六三十六).
The **meridian channels** (经脉) number **twelve** (十二), the **collateral vessels** (络脉) number **fifteen** (十五), making a total of **twenty-seven** (凡二十七) **qì** points, ascending and descending. Their **origins** (所出) are called **jǐng** (井, *well*), their **overflows** (所溜) are called **róng** (荣, *spring*), their **convergences** (所注) are called **jīng** (经, *stream*), their **paths** (所行) are called **jīng** (经, *channel*), and their **entrances** (所入) are called **hé** (合, *union*). The **twenty-seven qì points** (二十七气) all move within the **five jīng** (五经)."

The **junctions** of the **nodes** (節之交) number **three hundred sixty-five** (三百六十五), and those who grasp their essence can conclude with a single word, while those who do not know it will wander endlessly. What is meant by **nodes** (節) is the **spirit-qi** (神氣) as it courses, enters, and exits—not the skin, flesh, sinews, or bones. By observing its **color** (色) and examining its **eyes** (目), one knows its **dispersion and return** (散復). By uniting its **form** (形) and listening to its **movement and stillness** (動靜), one knows its **pathological and orthodox** (邪正) nature. Push with the **right hand** (右主推之), grasp and guide with the **left** (左持而御之), and withdraw when the **qi arrives** (氣至而去之).

Before using the needle, one must first **diagnose the pulse** (必先診脈), observe the **severity or ease of the qi** (視氣之劇易), and only then may treatment proceed.

When the **qi of the Five Zàng** (五藏) has already **failed internally** (已絕於內), yet the practitioner **fills its exterior** (而用針者反實其外), this is called **double exhaustion** (重竭), and **double exhaustion inevitably leads to death** (重竭必死)—its death is **tranquil** (其死也靜). The treatment reverses its **qi**, taking points from the **axilla** (腋) and **chest** (膺).

When the **qi of the Five Zàng** has already **failed externally** (已絕於外), yet the practitioner **fills its interior** (而用針者反實其內), this is called **counter-reversal** (逆厥), and **counter-reversal inevitably leads to death** (逆厥則必死)—its death is **agitated** (其死也躁). The treatment reverses the **four extremities** (四末).

If the needle **pierces deeply but is not withdrawn**, the **essence** (精) will **leak** (泄); if it **pierces deeply and is withdrawn**, it will **induce pathogenic qi** (致氣). **Leakage of essence** (精泄) worsens the illness further (病益甚而惬). **Inducing pathogenic qi** (致氣) will result in **ulceration and abscess** (生為瘍瘍).

The **Five Zàng** have **Six Fǔ** (六府), and the **Six Fǔ** have **twelve origins** (十二原). These **twelve origins** emerge from the **four gates** (四關), which govern the treatment of the **Five Zàng**. When the **Five Zàng** are diseased, one must take the **twelve origins** (當取之十二原). These **twelve origins** are the means by which the **Five Zàng** receive the **three hundred sixty-five nodal qi and flavors** (三百六十五節氣味).

When the **Five Zàng** are diseased, they manifest through the **twelve origins** (應出十二原). Each **origin** has its **specific emergence** (二原各有所出), and by knowing their **origins** and observing their **manifestations**, one can discern the **harm to the Five Zàng**.

Within the **yang**, the **Lesser Yin** (少陰) is the **lung** (肺), its **origin** emerging from **Tàiyuān** (太渊), which has **two** (二). Within the **yang**, the **Greater Yang** (太陽) is the **heart** (心), its **origin** emerging from **Dàlíng** (大陵), which has **two** (二). Within the **yin**, the **Lesser Yang** (少陽) is the **liver** (肝), its **origin** emerging from **Tàichōng** (太沖), which has **two** (二). Within the **yin**, the **Extreme Yin** (至陰) is the **spleen** (脾), its **origin** emerging from **Tàibái** (太白), which has **two** (二). Within the **yin**, the **Greater Yin** (太陰) is the **kidney** (腎), its **origin** emerging from **Tàixī** (太溪), which has **two** (二). The **origin of marrow** (膏之原) emerges from **Jiūwěi** (鴟尾), which has **one** (一). The **origin of nourishment** (育之原) emerges from **Bóyú** (脖胦), which has **one** (一).

These **twelve origins** govern the treatment of diseases in the **Five Zàng and Six Fǔ**. For **distension**, take the **Three Yang** (三陽); for **diarrhea** (飧泄), take the **Three Yin** (三陰).

Now, when the **Five Zàng** are diseased, it is like **a thorn** (刺), like **filth** (污), like **a knot** (結), like **a blockage** (閉). Though the **thorn** may remain long, it can still be **pulled out** (拔); though **filth** may linger, it can still be **cleansed** (雪); though **knots** endure, they can still be **untied** (解); though **blockages** persist, they can still be **broken** (決). Some say that **long-standing illnesses cannot be treated**, but this is incorrect. The skilled needle practitioner treats illness as one **removes a thorn**, **cleanses filth**, **unties knots**, or **breaks blockages**—even if the illness is long-standing, it can still be **resolved** (疾雖久，猶可畢也). Those who say it **cannot be treated** have not mastered the **method** (術).

Needling **heat disorders** is like **handling scalding water**; needling **cold-clear disorders** is like **one who does not wish to walk**. For **yang disorders with yin pathology** (陽中之陰疾), take **Xiàlíng Sān Lǐ** (下陵三里). Proceed **directly** (正往無殆)—when the **qi descends**, it will cease; if it does not descend, begin again (氣下乃止，不下復始).

For **high and internal disorders**, take **Língquán** (陵泉) of the **yin**; for **high and external disorders**, take **Língquán** (陵泉) of the **yang**.

The Yellow Emperor asked Qìbó, saying:

*"The Way of needling must necessarily traverse the terminal and origin points of the twelve primary channels, the branching collaterals, the five transport points where qi lingers, the six fu organs where they converge, the seasonal openings and closings, the five zàng organs where fluids drain, the breadth and count of the vessels, their shallow and deep states, and their high and low reaches. I wish to hear their explanation."*

Qìbó replied:

*"Let me speak of their sequence.

The Lung channel emerges at **Shǎoshāng** (少商), which is the inner side of the tip of the thumb—this is the **Jing (井) point of Wood**. It drains at **Yújì** (鱼际), the **Fū (荣) point**, which is the palm’s hollow beneath the thumb. It flows into **Tàiyuān** (太渊), the **Xing (经) point**, one *cùn* behind the fish-shaped depression in the palm. It courses along **Jīnggū** (经渠), the **Jing (经) point**, within the wrist’s pulse—moving yet never fixed. It enters **Chǐzé** (尺泽), the **Hé (合) point**, the pulsating artery at the elbow’s bend.

This is the **Hand Taiyin (太阴) channel**.

The Heart channel emerges at **Zhōngchōng** (中冲), the tip of the middle finger—this is the **Jing (井) point of Wood**. It drains at **Láoguān** (劳宫), the **Fū (荣) point**, the inner hollow at the base of the middle finger’s joint. It flows into **Dàlíng** (大陵), the **Xing (经) point**, between the two wrist bones. It courses along **Wènshǐ** (问使), the **Jing (经) point**, between the two tendons, three *cùn* up—if overstepped, it reaches its mark; if not, it halts. It enters **Qūzé** (曲泽), the **Hé (合) point**, the depression below the inner elbow’s edge, found by bending the arm.

This is the **Hand Shaoyin (少阴) channel**.

The Liver channel emerges at **Dàdūn** (大敦), the tip of the big toe and the space between the three toe hairs—this is the **Jing (井) point of Wood**. It drains at **Xíngwèn** (行间), the **Fū (荣) point**, the space between the big toe and second toe. It flows into **Tàichōng** (太冲), the **Xing (经) point**, two *cùn* above the depression in **Xíngwèn**. It courses along **Zhōngfēng** (中封), the **Jing (经) point**, one and a half *cùn* forward of the inner ankle’s hollow—if resisted, it twists; if harmonized, it flows. It is found by shaking the foot. It enters **Qǔyuán** (曲泉), the **Hé (合) point**, below the medial malleolus and above the great tendon, accessed by bending the knee.

This is the **Foot Jueyin (厥阴) channel**.

The Spleen channel emerges at **Yǐnbái** (隐白), the inner side of the big toe’s tip—this is the **Jing (井) point of Wood**. It drains at **Dàdū** (大都), the **Fū (荣) point**, the depression behind the joint’s base. It flows into **Tàibái** (太白), the **Xing (经) point**, below the wrist bone. It courses along **Shāngqiū** (商丘), the **Jing (经) point**, the hollow below the inner ankle. It enters **Yīn Língquán** (阴陵泉), the **Hé (合) point**, below the medial malleolus, accessed while extending the leg.

This is the **Foot Taiyin (太阴) channel**.

The Kidney channel emerges at **Yǒngquán** (涌泉), the sole of the foot—this is the **Jing (井) point of Wood**. It drains at **Rán Gǔ** (然谷), the **Fū (荣) point**, below the **Rán (然) bone**. It flows into **Tàixī** (太溪), the **Xing (经) point**, behind the inner ankle, above the heel bone, in the depression. It courses along **Fùliú** (复溜), the **Jing (经) point**, two *cùn* above the inner ankle, pulsating without ceasing. It enters **Yīn Gǔ** (阴谷), the **Hé (合) point**, behind the medial malleolus, below the great tendon and above the lesser tendon—pressed, it responds to the hand, accessed by bending the knee.

This is the **Foot Shaoyin (少阴) channel**.

The Bladder channel emerges at **Zhìyīn** (至阴), the tip of the little toe—this is the **Jing (井) point of Metal**. It drains at **Tōnggǔ** (通谷), the **Fū (荣) point**, the outer side of the joint’s base. It flows into **Shùgǔ** (束骨), the **Xing (经) point**, the depression behind the joint. It passes **Jīnggǔ** (京骨), the **Yuán (原) point**, below the outer ankle’s great bone. It courses along **Kūnlún** (昆仑), the **Jing (经) point**, behind the lateral malleolus, above the heel bone. It enters **Wěizhōng** (委中), the **Hé (合) point**, the center of the popliteal fossa, accessed by flexing the knee.

This is the **Foot Taiyang (太阳) channel**.

The Gallbladder channel emerges at **Qiàoyīn** (窍阴), the tip of the fourth toe—this is the **Jing (井) point of Metal**. It drains at **Xiáxī** (侠溪), the **Fū (荣) point**, the space between the fourth and fifth toes. It flows into **Línqì** (临泣), the **Xing (经) point**, one and a half *cùn* upward in the depression. It passes **Qiūxū** (丘墟), the **Yuán (原) point**, below the lateral ankle’s hollow. It courses along **Yángfǔ** (阳辅), the **Jing (经) point**, above the lateral malleolus, before the **Chuī (辅) bone** and the end of the **Jué (绝) bone**. It enters **Yáng Língquán** (阳陵泉), the **Hé (合) point**, the depression outside the knee, accessed while extending the leg.

This is the **Foot Shaoyang (少阳) channel**.

The Stomach channel emerges at **Lìduì** (厉兑), the inner side of the second toe’s tip—this is the **Jing (井) point of Metal**. It drains at **Nèitíng** (内庭), the **Fū (荣) point**, the space between the second and third toes. It flows into **Xiàngǔ** (陷谷), the **Xing (经) point**, two *cùn* above the inner side of the second toe’s joint. It passes **Chōngyáng** (冲阳), the **Yuán (原) point**, five *cùn* above the medial malleolus, accessed by shaking the foot. It courses along **Jiěxī** (解溪), the **Jing (经) point**, one and a half *cùn* above **Chōngyáng**. It enters **Xiàlíng** (下陵), the **Hé (合) point**, three *cùn* below the knee, three *lǐ* from the outer shin bone. Below this, three *cùn* further is **Jùxū Shànglián** (巨虚上廉), and three *cùn* below that is **Jùxū Xiàlián** (巨虚下廉). The Large Intestine belongs to the upper, the Small Intestine to the lower—both are attributed to the Stomach, thus this is the **Foot Yangming (阳明) channel**.

The Triple Burner, which unites with the **Hand Shaoyang**, emerges at **Guānchōng** (关冲), the tip of the ring finger—this is the **Jing (井) point of Metal*.*"

The Triple Burner channel flows through **Yèmén (液门)**, the space between the little finger and the ring finger—this is the **Róng (荣) point**. It descends to **Zhōngzhǔ (中渚)**, the depression behind the joint—this is the **Liǎn (脸) point**. It passes through **Yángchí (阳池)**, the depression above the wrist—this is the **Yuán (原) point**. It courses along **Zhīgōu (支沟)**, three *cùn* above the wrist, between the two bones—this is the **Jīng (经) point**. It enters **Tiānjǐng (天井)**, the depression above the outer bone of the elbow—this is the **Hé (合) point**, accessible only when the elbow is bent. The lower **Liǎn (脸)** of the Triple Burner lies before the big toe, behind the **Shàoyáng (少阳)**, emerging from the outer side of the popliteal fossa, named **Wěiyáng (委阳)**—this is the **Tàiyáng (太阳) Luo (络)** vessel. This is the **Hand Shàoyáng (少阳) channel**.

The Triple Burner, the **Shāng (上)** of **Shǔ (足) Shàoyáng (少阳)** and **Tàiyīn (太阴)**—alternatively **Tàiyáng (太阳)**—branches at five *cùn* above the ankle, penetrating and passing through the **Duōdàncháng (端肠)**, emerging at **Wěiyáng (委阳)**, joining the **Tàiyáng (太阳)** channel proper, entering the network of the **Pángguāng (膀胱)**, encircling the lower **Jiāo (焦)**. When full, it causes urinary retention; when empty, it causes incontinence. For incontinence, supplement; for retention, drain.

The **Hand Tàiyáng (太阳) Small Intestine** channel unites with the **Tàiyáng (太阳)**, emerging at **Shǎozé (少泽)**, the tip of the little finger—this is the **Jīng (井) point of Metal**. It flows through **Qiángǔ (前谷)**, the depression before the joint on the outer side of the hand—this is the **Róng (荣) point**. It descends to **Hòuxī (后溪)**, the depression behind the joint on the outer side of the hand—this is the **Liǎn (脸) point**. It passes **Wàngǔ (腕骨)**, the depression before the wrist bone on the outer side of the hand—this is the **Yuán (原) point**. It courses along **Yánggǔ (阳谷)**, the depression below the sharp bone—this is the **Jīng (经) point**. It enters **Xiǎohǎi (小海)**, the depression half a *cùn* from the inner side of the large bone of the elbow, accessible when the arm is extended—this is the **Hé (合) point**. This is the **Hand Tàiyáng (太阳) channel**.

The **Hand Yángmíng (阳明) Large Intestine** channel unites with the **Hand Yángmíng (阳明)**, emerging at **Shāngyáng (商阳)**, the tip of the second finger—this is the **Jīng (井) point of Metal**. It flows through the two depressions before the joint—this is the **Róng (荣) point**. It descends to the three depressions behind the joint—this is the **Liǎn (脸) point**. It passes **Hégǔ (合谷)**, between the bones of the thumb—this is the **Yuán (原) point**. It courses along **Yángxī (阳溪)**, the depression between the two tendons—this is the **Jīng (经) point**. It enters **Qūchí (曲池)**, the depression outside the elbow bone, accessible when the arm is bent—this is the **Hé (合) point**. This is the **Hand Yángmíng (阳明) channel**.

These are called the **Liǎn (脸) points** of the Five **Zàng (藏)** and Six **Fǔ (府)**—five times five, twenty-five **Liǎn (脸)**; six times six, thirty-six **Liǎn (脸)**. The Six **Fǔ (府)** all emerge from the three **Yáng (阳)** channels of the foot, uniting above with the hands.

At the center of the **Quēpén (缺盆)**, the **Rènmài (任脉)** is named **Tiāntū (天突)**. The first pulse beside the **Rènmài (任脉)** is the **Foot Yángmíng (阳明)**, named **Rényíng (人迎)**. The second pulse is the **Hand Yángmíng (阳明)**, named **Fútū (扶突)**. The third pulse is the **Hand Tàiyáng (太阳)**, named **Tiānchuāng (天窗)**. The fourth pulse is the **Foot Shàoyáng (少阳)**, named **Tiānróng (天容)**. The fifth pulse is the **Hand Shàoyáng (少阳)**, named **Tiānyòu (天牖)**. The sixth pulse is the **Foot Tàiyáng (太阳)**, named **Tiānzhù (天柱)**. The seventh pulse at the center of the neck is the **Dūmài (督脉)**, named **Fēngfǔ (风府)**. The moving pulse in the armpit is the **Hand Tàiyīn (太阴)**, named **Tiānfǔ (天府)**. Three *cùn* below the armpit is the **Hand Xīnzhǔ (心主)**, named **Tiānchí (天池)**.

To puncture **Shàngguān (上关)**, one cannot yawn; to puncture **Xiàguān (下关)**, one cannot yawn. To puncture **Duíní (犊鼻)**, one cannot extend; to puncture both **Guān (关)**, one cannot flex. The **Foot Yángmíng (阳明)** pulse moves beside the throat, its **Liǎn (脸)** point located in the chest. The **Hand Yángmíng (阳明)** pulse lies just outside its **Liǎn (脸)**, not reaching the curve of the cheek by one *cùn*. The **Hand Tàiyáng (太阳)** aligns with the curve of the cheek. The **Foot Shàoyáng (少阳)** lies behind the curve of the cheek beneath the ear. The **Hand Shàoyáng (少阳)** emerges behind the ear, ascending above the **Wán gǔ (完骨)**. The **Foot Tàiyáng (太阳)** runs along the hairline within the great tendon of the neck. The **Yīnchǐ (阴尺)** pulse lies at five *lǐ*, forbidden for twenty-five **Liǎn (脸)** points.

The **Lung** unites with the **Large Intestine**, which is the **Transmission of the Dao (道)府**. The **Heart** unites with the **Small Intestine**, which is the **Receiving and Filling府**. The **Liver** unites with the **Gallbladder**, which is the **Centralizing of Essence府**. The **Spleen** unites with the **Stomach**, which is the **Five Grains府**. The **Kidney** unites with the **Bladder**, which is the **Body Fluids府**. The **Shàoyīn (少阴)** belongs to the **Kidney**, and the **Kidney** connects above to the **Lung**, thus governing the two **Zàng (藏)**. The **Triple Burner** is the **Central Passage府**, from which waterways flow, belonging to the **Bladder**, making it the **Orphan府**. These are the **Fǔ (府)** that unite with the **Zàng (藏)**.

In **Spring**, take the **Luo (络)** and **Róng (荣)** vessels, the major channels, and the spaces between muscles—deeply for severe cases, shallowly for mild ones. In **Summer**, take the **Liǎn (脸)**, **Sūn (孙)**, and **Luo (络)** vessels on the skin and muscles. In **Autumn**, take the **Hé (合)** points, as in **Spring**. In **Winter**, take the **Jīng (井)** and **Liǎn (脸)** points, desiring depth and retention. This is the sequence of the Four Seasons, where **qi** resides and diseases lodge, and where the **Zàng (藏)** are treated. For **spasms**, puncture while standing to resolve them immediately. For **wasting and paralysis**, puncture while lying down to restore function at once.

**[Phonetic Release]** *Kuò shù xià sè jiǎo qiè.*

The foot’s instep sounds like *fū* [□*kuò shù xià sè jiǎo qiè*]. [□*shí yǎn qiè*].

**The Third Method of Small Needling**

What is called *easy* (*yì*) is easy to speak of.
What is called *difficult* (*nán*) is difficult to apply to others.
What is called *crude adherence to form* (*cū shǒu xíng*) is adherence to the method of puncture.
What is called *superior adherence to spirit* (*shàng shǒu shén*) is adherence to the excess or deficiency of a person’s blood and *qi*, knowing whether to supplement or drain.

The *spirit-guest* (*shénkè*) is the convergence of right and wrong *qi*.
The *spirit* (*shén*) is the right *qi*.
The *guest* (*kè*) is the wrong *qi*.
The *one at the gate* (*zài mén*) is when wrong *qi* follows the path of the right *qi*’s entry and exit.
The *one who has not yet seen the disease* (*wèi dǔ qí jí*) is to know beforehand which channel’s disease is right or wrong.
The *one who does not know its origin* (*è zhī qí yuán*) is to know beforehand which channel’s disease it is and where to take it.

When the puncture is *subtle in number and slow* (*sǐ zhī wéi shù chí*), it refers to the meaning of slow and fast.
The *crude adherence to the pass* (*cū shǒu guān*) is adherence to the four limbs without knowing the ebb and flow of right and wrong blood and *qi*.
The *superior adherence to the mechanism* (*shàng shǒu jī*) is knowing how to guard the *qi*.
When the mechanism moves without leaving its emptiness, it means knowing the *qi*’s emptiness and fullness, and using the needle’s slow and fast accordingly.
When the mechanism in emptiness is clear and gently rubbed, it means the needle has obtained the *qi*—closely guarding the *qi* without losing it.

If its coming cannot be met, the *qi* is too strong and cannot be supplemented.
If its going cannot be pursued, the *qi* is too weak and cannot be drained.
If it cannot be hooked to release, it means the *qi* is easily lost.
If it is tapped but does not release, it means not knowing the intention of supplementation or drainage—the blood and *qi* are already exhausted, and the *qi* does not descend.

To know its ebb and flow is to know the *qi*’s reversal,顺, fullness, and emptiness.
To meet it at the appointed time is to know when the *qi* can be taken.
The *crude interval* (*cū jiān*) is dimly unaware of the *qi*’s subtlety and density.
Ah, the *superior* alone possesses this—fully knowing the needle’s intent.

What comes is *reversed*—speaking of the *qi*’s emptiness and smallness; smallness is reversal.
What arrives is *顺*—speaking of the balance of form and *qi*; balance is *顺*.
To clearly know reversal and *顺*, and act correctly without hesitation, means knowing where to take it.
To meet and seize it is to drain.
To pursue and assist it is to supplement.

What is called *“empty, then fill”* (*xū zé shí*) means the *qi* at the mouth is empty and should be supplemented.
What is called *“full, then drain”* (*mǎn zé xiè*) means the *qi* at the mouth is full and should be drained.
What is called *“twisted and spread, then remove”* (*wǎn chén zé chú*) is to remove blood from the vessels.
What is called *“wrong *qi* dominates, then empty”* (*xié shèng zé xū*) means that if any channel is excessive, its wrong *qi* should be drained.

Slow then fast is to fill—meaning slow entry and fast exit.
Fast then slow is to empty—meaning fast entry and slow exit.

Speaking of full and empty, as if present or absent—meaning fullness has *qi*, emptiness lacks *qi*.
Examining what comes after and before, as if lost or stored—meaning observing the *qi*’s emptiness and fullness, the order of supplementation and drainage, and whether the *qi* has descended or remains constant.

To act as if full or empty, as if obtained or lost—meaning supplementation is secretly as if obtained, drainage is joyfully as if lost.

As for the *qi* in the vessels:
When wrong *qi* is above, it means the wrong *qi* enters high, hence wrong *qi* is above.
When turbid *qi* is in the middle, it means water and grain all enter the stomach, their refined *qi* ascending to the lungs, their turbidity lingering in the intestines and stomach—speaking of improper cold and heat, unregulated eating and drinking, and disease arising in the intestines and stomach, hence it is called turbid *qi* in the middle.
When clear *qi* is below, it means the clear, damp earth *qi* enters, necessarily beginning at the feet, hence it is called clear *qi* below.

When the needle pierces the vessel and wrong *qi* emerges, take it from above.
When the needle is in the vessel and wrong *qi* emerges, take it from the *Yangming* convergence.
When the needle is too deep and wrong *qi* sinks back, it means shallow, superficial diseases should not be deeply punctured—deep puncture causes wrong *qi* to follow and enter, hence it is called sinking back.

Skin, flesh, sinews, and vessels each have their own place—meaning each channel and network has its own domain.

To take the five vessels is death—meaning the disease is internal, the *qi* is insufficient, and only using the needle to fully drain all the yin vessels.
To take the three yang vessels is unique—meaning fully draining the three yang *qi* so the patient feels satisfied and the disease ceases.

To seize yin is death—meaning taking the five *cùn* (inches) at the *chǐ* (foot), the five *wǎng* (directions).
To seize yang is madness—correct speech.

To observe its color, examine its eyes, know its dispersion or recovery, unify its form, and listen to its movement and stillness—meaning the superior practitioner knows the five colors in the eyes, understands the regulation of *cùn*, *fēn*, size, speed, and smoothness or roughness, to speak of the disease.

To know its right and wrong is to know the discussion of wrong *qi* and right *qi*’s wind.

The right hand pushes, the left holds and guides—meaning holding the needle as it enters and exits.
When the *qi* arrives, withdraw it—meaning adjusting the supplementing or draining *qi* and then withdrawing.
Adjusting the *qi* lies in the beginning and end—meaning maintaining focus.
The nodes intersecting at three hundred sixty-five convergences are the networks permeating the joints.

What is called *“the five *zàng*’s *qi* already severed internally”* is when the pulse and *qi* are internally severed and do not arrive, instead taking the external disease site and the yang channel’s convergence, leaving the needle to induce yang *qi*—when yang *qi* arrives, the internal exhaustion is severe, exhaustion is severe, and death follows. Its death is without *qi* to move, hence stillness.

What is called *“the five *zàng*’s *qi* already severed externally”* is when the pulse and *qi* are externally severed and do not arrive, instead taking the four extremities’ outputs, leaving the needle to induce yin *qi*—when yin *qi* arrives, yang *qi* reverses inward, inward means reversal, reversal means death. Its death is yin *qi* in excess, hence agitation.

Thus, examining the eyes: the five *zàng* manifest the five colors in brightness, brightness then sounds clearly, and clear sounds differ from one’s usual voice.

「【Phonetic Glossary】 *Wěirán* (佖然), pronounced as *wěi* with the *rán* (然) rhyme—*rán* is the sound of "must" (必), resembling a full appearance. *Yuèrán* (悦然), pronounced as *yuè* with the *rán* (然) rhyme—*rán* is the sound of "madness" (狂貌). *Shēn* (深) with *nà* (内) rhyme.

In the fourth method of *zàng-fǔ* (藏府) diseases caused by *xiéqi* (邪气), the Yellow Emperor asked Qìbó (岐伯): *"How does the *xiéqi* (邪气) invade a person?"* Qìbó replied: *"The *xiéqi* (邪气) invades a person by way of the **yang**."* The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Does the invasion of **yang** have a fixed degree?"* Qìbó answered: *"If the invasion occurs above the midpoint of the body, it is through **yang**; if below the midpoint, it is through dampness (**shī**—湿)."* Therefore it is said: *"The *xiéqi* (邪气) that invades a person has no fixed rule—when it strikes **yin**, it flows into the **fǔ** (府); when it strikes **yang**, it flows into the channels (**jīng**—经)."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"The **yin** and **yang** are different names but of the same kind, meeting above and below, and the channels and collaterals (**jīng-luò**—经络) interconnect like a ring without beginning or end. When *xiéqi* (邪气) invades a person, it may strike **yin** or **yang**, and its movement above, below, left, or right is without constancy—why is this?"* Qìbó replied: *"All the convergences of **yang** are found on the face. When a person is invaded by *xiéqi* (邪气), it is during moments of vulnerability—such as after exertion, or when excessive drinking, eating, sweating, or rational opening (**shǐnglǐ**—胜理) expose them. If the invasion strikes the face, it descends into the **Stomach** (胃) channel; if the neck, it descends into the **Large Intestine** (太阳) channel; if the cheek, it descends into the **Gallbladder** (少阳) channel. When it strikes the chest, back, or lateral sides, it also invades the channels."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does the invasion of **yin** occur?"* Qìbó replied: *"When **yin** is invaded, it commonly begins with the **arms** and **fǔ** (府). The **yin** skin of the arms and **fǔ** is thin, while their flesh is rich and moist—thus, they both receive **wind** (*fēng*—风), but the **yin** is uniquely injured."* The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Does this mean the **zàng** (藏) is harmed?"* Qìbó replied: *"When **wind** invades the body, it does not necessarily agitate the **zàng**. Therefore, if *xiéqi* (邪气) enters the **yin** channels, the **zàng**’s *qi* becomes replete, and the *xiéqi* cannot lodge there—it returns to the **fǔ** (府). Hence, invasion of **yang** flows into the channels, while invasion of **yin** flows into the **fǔ**."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does **wind** invade the five **zàng**?"* Qìbó replied: *"Grief and worry injure the **Heart** (心), cold exposure and cold drinks injure the **Lungs** (肺)—since both internal and external colds affect it, the *qi* reverses and ascends. A fall or traumatic injury causes stagnant **evil blood** (*èxiě*—恶血) to pool within, or great anger causes *qi* to rise but not descend, accumulating beneath the ribs—thus, the **Liver** (肝) is injured. A blow or stumble, drunkenness followed by sexual excess, or sweating while exposed to **wind**, injures the **Spleen** (脾). Excessive exertion or lifting heavy objects, or sexual excess followed by bathing and sweating, injures the **Kidneys** (肾)."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does **wind** invade the five **zàng**?"* Qìbó replied: *"When both **yin** and **yang** are affected, the *xiéqi* (邪) can then proceed."* The Yellow Emperor said: *"Excellent."*

The Yellow Emperor asked Qìbó: *"I have heard that the head and face are linked to the bones and tendons, sharing blood and *qi* with the body. When the cold is extreme, the earth cracks and ice shatters—yet, in cases of severe cold, the hands and feet may become slack and listless, while the face remains unaffected. Why is this?"* Qìbó replied: *"The twelve primary channels (**jīngmài**—经脉) and their 365 collaterals (**luò**—络), all send their blood and *qi* upward to the face, circulating through the hollow cavities (**kòngqiào**—空窍). The refined **yang** *qi* ascends to the eyes, forming the **pupil** (*jīng*—睛). The divergent *qi* ascends to the ears, forming the sense of **hearing** (*tīng*—听). The **congregated *qi*** (*zōngqi*—宗气) ascends through the nose, forming **smell** (*chòu*—臭). The turbid *qi* (*zhuóqi*—浊气) from the **Stomach** (胃) ascends to the **tongue** (*shé*—舌), forming **taste** (*wèi*—味). The *qi*’s **moisture** (*jīn*—津液) all ascends to the face, and though the skin is thick and the flesh firm, extreme heat or cold cannot overcome it."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"When *xiéqi* (邪气) invades a person, how does the disease manifest?"* Qìbó replied: *"When **void pathogens** (*xūxié*—虚邪) invade the body, they scatter and move, visibly altering form. When **orthodox pathogens** (*zhèngxié*—正邪) invade, the changes are subtle—first appearing in the **complexion** (*sè*—色), imperceptible to the body. The disease may seem present or absent, tangible or intangible, with no clear discernible pattern."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Excellent."* The Yellow Emperor asked Qìbó: *"I have heard that observing the **complexion** (*sè*—色) reveals the disease—this is called **Clarity** (*míng*—明). Examining the **pulse** (*mài*—脉) reveals the disease—this is called **Divinity** (*shén*—神). Questioning the patient reveals the disease’s location—this is called **Skill** (*gōng*—工). I wish to understand how one sees and knows the disease, examines and confirms it, and questions to fully grasp it. How does this work?"*

Qìbó replied: *"The **complexion** (*sè*—色), **pulse** (*mài*—脉), and the **radial position** (*chǐ*—尺) correspond like the resonance of a bell and its echo—one cannot fail to match the other. This is also the principle of **root-and-branch** (*běnmògēnxiè*—本末根叶) diagnosis: if the root dies, the leaves wither. Thus, the **complexion**, **pulse**, **form**, and **flesh** cannot fail to correspond—knowing one reveals **Skill**; knowing two reveals **Divinity**; knowing all three reveals both **Divinity** and **Clarity**."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear this fully."* Qìbó replied: *"A **greenish** (*qīng*—青) complexion corresponds to **Liver** disease, and its **pulse** is **taut** (*huán*—弦); a **red** (*chì*—赤) complexion corresponds to **Heart** disease, and its **pulse** is **crooked** (*gōu*—钩); a **yellow** (*huáng*—黄) complexion corresponds to **Spleen** disease, and its **pulse** is **intermittent** (*dài*—代); a **white** (*bái*—白) complexion corresponds to **Lungs** disease, and its **pulse** is **soft and sparse** (*máo*—毛); a **black** (*hēi*—黑) complexion corresponds to **Kidneys** disease, and its **pulse** is **hard and sharp** (*shí*—石). If one sees the **complexion** but cannot find its corresponding **pulse**, and instead finds the **pulse** that **overcomes** (*xiāngshèng*—相胜) it, then death follows. If one finds the **pulse** that **generates** (*xiāngshēng*—相生) it, then the disease has already passed."*

The Yellow Emperor asked Qìbó: *"How do the five **zàng** manifest in varying disease forms and transformations?"* Qìbó replied: *"First, determine the correspondence of the five **complexions** and five **pulses**—then the disease can be distinguished."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Having determined the **complexion** and **pulse**, how does one further distinguish the disease?"* Qìbó replied: *"By observing the **pulse**’s **tension** (*jí*—急), **relaxation** (*huǎn*—缓), **minuteness** (*xiǎo*—小), **magnitude** (*dà*—大), **smoothness** (*huá*—滑), and **roughness** (*sè*—涩), the disease’s transformations become clear."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one adjust these **pulse** qualities?"* Qìbó replied: *"When the **pulse** is **tensed**, the skin at the **radial position** is also tight; when the **pulse** is **relaxed**, the skin at the **radial position**’s **stone convergence** (*shíhuì*—石会) is also slack; when the **pulse** is **minute**, the skin at the **radial position** is also **thin and stretched**, causing **depression** (*zāng*—臧) and **opening** (*kāidòu*—开斗), with **weak *qi*** (*shǎoqì*—少气); when the **pulse** is **magnified**, the skin at the **radial position** is also **distended and raised** (*bīn*—贲); when the **pulse** is **slippery** (*piàn*—骗), the skin at the **radial position** is also **smooth** (*huá*—滑);"

「When the **pulse** is **tense** (*sè*—涩), the skin at the **radial position** is also tense. In all these variations, some are slight, others severe.

Thus, those skilled in adjusting the **radial position** do not need to rely on the **cubital position** (*cùn*—寸), and those skilled in adjusting the **pulse** do not need to rely on the **color** (*sè*—色). Those who can integrate and apply these principles may be called **Master Physicians** (*shànggōng*—上工), and **Master Physicians** succeed in nine out of ten cases. Those who practice two of these methods are **Intermediate Physicians** (*zhōnggōng*—中工), and **Intermediate Physicians** succeed in seven out of ten cases. Those who practice only one are **Junior Physicians** (*xiàgōng*—下工), and **Junior Physicians** succeed in six out of ten cases.

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How do the **pulse** variations of **tense** (*sè*—涩), **tense** (*jí*—急), **relaxed** (*huǎn*—缓), **minute** (*xiǎo*—小), **magnified** (*dà*—大), **slippery** (*piàn*—骗), and **tense** (*sè*—涩) manifest in disease?"* Qìbó replied: *"Let me speak of the **diseased transformations** (*bìngbiàn*—病变) of the **Five Zàng Organs** (*wǔzàng*—五藏).

For the **Heart** (*xīn*—心):
- When the **pulse** is **tense** (*jí*—急) and severe, it is **epilepsy** (*zhìzhèng*—痣症);
- When the **pulse** is slightly **tense**, it is **heart pain radiating to the back**, with difficulty swallowing.

For the **Lung** (*fèi*—肺):
- When the **pulse** is **tense** (*jí*—急) and severe, it is **mania** (*diānjí*—癫疾);
- When the **pulse** is slightly **tense**, it is **lung cold-heat** (*fèihánrè*—肺寒热), with **sluggish respiration** (*xīduò*—息惰), **coughing up blood**, radiating to the **loins, back, and chest**, or **nasal polyps** (*ruò bíxìròu bùtōng*—若鼻息肉不通).

For the **Liver** (*gān*—肝):
- When the **pulse** is **tense** (*jí*—急) and severe, it is **abusive speech** (*èyán*—恶言);
- When the **pulse** is slightly **tense**, it is **flank *qi*** (*féiqì*—肥气), located beneath the ribs as if covered by a cup.

For the **Spleen** (*pí*—脾):
- When the **pulse** is **tense** (*jí*—急) and severe, it is **virtue-phlegm** (*déshuì*—德痰);
- When the **pulse** is slightly **tense**, it is **diaphragmatic obstruction** (*gèzhōng*—膈中), where food and drink enter but are immediately regurgitated, and the **stomach** (*wèi*—胃) is **washed** (*hòuwòmò*—后沃沬).

For the **Kidney** (*shèn*—肾):
- When the **pulse** is **tense** (*jí*—急) and severe, it is **bone epilepsy** (*gǔdiānjí*—骨癫疾);
- When the **pulse** is slightly **tense**, it is **deep syncope** (*chénjué bēnzhì*—沉厥奔痹), with **unable to draw the feet** (*zúbùshōu*—足不收), and **inability to move forward or backward**.

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"When the **pulse** exhibits these six variations, how should one apply acupuncture?"* Qìbó replied: *"In cases where the **pulse** is **tense** (*jí*—急), there is **excessive cold** (*duōhán*—多寒); when it is **relaxed** (*huǎn*—缓), there is **excessive heat** (*duōrè*—多热); when it is **magnified** (*dà*—大), there is **excessive *qi* and deficient blood** (*duōqì shǎoxuè*—多气少血); when it is **minute** (*xiǎo*—小), both **blood and *qi*** (*xuèqì*—血气) are deficient; when it is **slippery** (*piàn*—骗), **yang *qi*** (*yángqì*—阳气) is **vigorous** (*shèng*—盛), with slight **heat** (*wéiyǒuhàn*—微有寒); when it is **tense** (*sè*—涩), there is **excessive blood and deficient *qi*** (*duōxuè shǎoqì*—多血少气), with slight **cold** (*wéiyǒuhàn*—微有寒).

Thus, when needling a **tense** (*jí*—急) **pulse**, one should **insert deeply** (*shēnrù*—深内) and **retain the needle for a long time** (*liúliúzhī*—久留之).
When needling a **relaxed** (*huǎn*—缓) **pulse**, one should **insert shallowly** (*qiǎnrù*—浅内) and **rapidly withdraw the needle** (*cìfāzhēn*—疾发针), to **dispel its heat** (*qùqíhàn*—去其热).
When needling a **magnified** (*dà*—大) **pulse**, one should **slightly reduce its *qi*** (*wēixiěqí*—微写其气), without **letting its blood escape** (*wúchūqíxuè*—无出其血).
When needling a **slippery** (*piàn*—骗) **pulse**, one should **rapidly withdraw the needle** (*cìfāzhēn*—疾发针) and **insert shallowly** (*qiǎnrù*—浅内), to **reduce its vigorous yang *qi*** (*xiěqíyángqì*—写其阳气) and **dispel its heat** (*qùqíhàn*—去其热).
When needling a **tense** (*sè*—涩) **pulse**, one must **pierce its vessel** (*bìzhōngqípài*—必中其脉), and **retain the needle for a long time** (*liúliúzhī*—久留之), following its **direction** (*nìxùn*—逆顺). One must **first press and follow** (*bìxiān'àn ér xúnzhī*—必先按而循之), then **insert the needle**, and **rapidly press its atrophy** (*cì'ànqíjié*—疾按其瘠), ensuring **no blood escapes** (*wúlìngqíxuèchū*—无令其血出), to **harmonize its pulse** (*héqípài*—和其脉).
For cases where the **pulse** is **minute** (*xiǎo*—小), both **yin and yang *qi*, form, and pulse** (*yīnyángxíngqì*—阴阳形气) are **deficient** (*bùzú*—不足). One should **not needle** (*wùqǔyǐzhēn*—勿取以针) but **adjust with sweet medicinals** (*tiáoyǐgānyào*—调以甘药).

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"I have heard that the **vital *qi*** (*qì*—气) of the **Five Zàng Organs** and **Six Fǔ Organs** (*liùfǔ*—六府) converge at the **honoring vessels** (*róngshū*—荣输) and **meeting points** (*hé*—合). I wish to know **by which pathways** (*dào*—道) this **vital *qi*** enters, and **how it connects and passes through** (*rù'āngliánguò*—入安连过), I would like to hear the reason for this."* Qìbó replied: *"These are the **separate pathways** (*bié rù*—别入) of the **yang vessels** (*yángmài*—阳脉), which **belong to the *Fǔ* Organs** (*shǔyúfǔ*—属於府)."

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Do the **honoring vessels** (*róngshū*—荣输), **meeting points** (*hé*—合), and **convergence** (*hé*—合) each have distinct names?"* Qìbó replied: *"The **honoring vessels** (*róngshū*—荣输) govern the **external pathways** (*wàijīng*—外经), while the **meeting points** (*hé*—合) govern the **internal *Fǔ* Organs** (*nèifǔ*—内府)." The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one govern the **internal *Fǔ* Organs**?"* Qìbó replied: *"By taking points at the **meeting points** (*hé*—合)."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Do the **meeting points** (*hé*—合) each have distinct names?"* Qìbó replied: *"The **meeting point** of the **Stomach** (*wèi*—胃) is **Three Miles** (*sānlǐ*—三里); the **meeting point** of the **Large Intestine** (*dàcháng*—大肠) is **Upper Jùxū** (*jùxūshànglián*—巨虚上廉); the **meeting point** of the **Small Intestine** (*xiǎocháng*—小肠) is **Lower Jùxū** (*jùxūxiàlián*—巨虚下廉); the **meeting point** of the **Triple Burner** (*sānjiāo*—三焦) is **Wěiyáng** (*wěiyáng*—委阳); the **meeting point** of the **Bladder** (*pángguāng*—膀胱) is **Central Wěi** (*wěizhōng*—委中央); the **meeting point** of the **Gallbladder** (*dǎn*—胆) is **Yánglíngquán** (*yánglíngquán*—阳陵泉)."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one take these points?"* Qìbó replied: *"To take **Three Miles**, one should **lower the knee** (*dīpì*—低驸); to take **Jùxū**, one should **lift the foot** (*jǔzú*—举足); to take **Wěiyáng**, one should **flex and extend** (*qūshēn ér suǒzhī*—屈伸而索之); to take **Central Wěi**, one should **flex** (*qū*—屈) and take the point; to take **Yánglíngquán**, one should **straighten the knee** (*zhèngjiān xīyǔzhīqí*—正坚膝予之齐), and **withdraw downward to Wěiyáng’s yang** (*xiàzhìwěiyángzhīyángqǔzhī*—下至委阳之阳取之); to take points on the **external pathways** (*wàijīng*—外经), one should **apply pressure and follow** (*yúshēn ér cóngzhī*—榆申而从之)."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"I wish to hear of the **diseases** (*bìng*—病) of the **Six Fǔ Organs** (*liùfǔ*—六府)."*

Qìbó replied: *"If the face is hot, it is a disease of the **Fǔ of the Foot-Sun** (*Fǔ*—府) **Brightness** (*Yángmíng*—阳明). If the **fish-net veins** (*Yúlù*—鱼络) bleed, it is a disease of the **Fǔ of the Hand-Sun** (*Shǒu Yángmíng*—手阳明). If the pulse above the **two knees** (*Liǎngpì*—两驸) rises and sinks like a vertical shaft, it is a disease of the **Fǔ of the Foot-Sun Brightness** (*Zhè shì wèi mài—此胃脉也*), which belongs to the **Fǔ of the Foot-Sun** (*Zú Yángmíng*—足阳明).

When the **Large Intestine** (*Dàcháng*—大肠) is diseased, there is cutting pain in the intestines, accompanied by a rumbling sound (*wūzhuózhuo*—呜濯濯). If the cold is severe in winter, it leads to diarrhea. Pain is felt at the navel, and one cannot stand for long. Its symptoms are shared with the stomach; one should take **Jùxū Upper Corner** (*Jùxū Shànglián*—巨虚上廉).

When the **stomach** (*Wèi*—胃) is diseased, the abdomen is taut and distended, with pain in the **stomach’s chamber** (*Wèi yuàn—胃院*) at the level of the heart. It radiates upward to the **two flanks** (*Shàngzhī—上支*), obstructs the **esophagus** (*Gègè—膈咽), and prevents food and drink from descending. One should take **Three Miles** (*Sānlǐ—三里).

When the **Small Intestine** (*Xiǎocháng*—小肠) is diseased, the lower abdomen aches, and the **lumbar spine** (*Yāobèi—腰脊*) feels constricted and painful near the **testicles** (*kònggōng—控睪). After urination, heat arises before the ear. If the cold is extreme, or if the **shoulder** (*Jǔ—肩*) above the **Hand-Tàiyáng** (*Shǒu Tàiyáng—手太阳*) pathway is excessively hot, or if the space between the **little finger** (*Xiǎozhǐ—小指*) and the **second finger** (*Cìzhǐ—次指*) is hot, or if the pulse sinks (*pìxiàn—脉陷*), these are its symptoms. One should take **Jùxū Lower Corner** (*Jùxū Xiàlián—巨虚下廉).

When the **Triple Burner** (*Sānjiāo—三焦*) is diseased, the abdomen feels full of **qi** (*Fùqì—腹气*), and the lower abdomen is particularly rigid. One cannot urinate, and there is urgency, with overflow leading to **watery discharge** (*shuǐ—水*), and retention causing **distension** (*Zhàng—胀). Its symptoms appear on the **external major collaterals** (*Wàijīng Dàluò—外经大络) of the **Foot-Tàiyáng** (*Zú Tàiyáng—足太阳*), located between the **Foot-Tàiyáng** (*Zú Tàiyáng—足太阳*) and **Foot-Shàoyáng** (*Zú Shàoyáng—足少阳*) pathways, also manifesting in the pulse. One should take **Wěiyáng** (*Wěiyáng—委阳).

When the **Bladder** (*Pángguāng—膀胱*) is diseased, the lower abdomen swells and aches on one side. Pressing it with the hand induces the urge to urinate but fails. Heat or a sinking pulse (*pìxiàn—脉陷*) appears above the shoulder, as well as on the **outer side of the little toe** (*Xiǎozhǐ Wàilián—小指外廉*) and behind the **lateral malleolus** (*Jīnghuái—经踝). One should take **Central Wěi** (*Wěizhōng—委中央).

When the **Gallbladder** (*Dǎn—胆*) is diseased, one often **sighs deeply** (*Shàn tàixī—善太息*), the mouth tastes bitter, one **regurgitates old bile** (*ǒuxì—呕宿汁), and the chest feels **dim and hollow** (*Xīnxià dàn dàn—心下澹澹). One fears being seized by others and feels **anxious** (*Kǒngzhōng—恐人将捕之). The throat is **constricted and uncomfortable** (*pǒnpǒn—嗑中盼盼然), and one frequently spits. Its symptoms appear at the **beginning and end** (*Běnmò—本末) of the **Foot-Shàoyáng** pathway. If the pulse sinks (*pìxiàn—脉陷*), one should **moxibust** (*Jiù—灸) it. If there are **alternating cold and heat** (*Zhènhuò—寒热) symptoms, one should take **Yánglíngquán** (*Yánglíngquán—阳陵泉).

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Is there a proper method for **acupuncture** (*Cì—刺*)?"*
Qìbó replied: *"When acupuncture is applied here, one must strike the **qi orifices** (*Qìxué—气穴), avoiding the **flesh nodes** (*Ròujiē—肉节). If the **qi orifices** are struck, the needle will **wander in the passageways** (*Yóu yú xiàng—游於巷); if the **flesh nodes** are struck, the skin will ache. **Reinforcing** (*Bǔ—补) or **reducing** (*Xiē—泻) incorrectly will worsen the disease.

If the **central tendons** (*Zhōngjīn—中筋) are struck, the tendons will relax, and **evil qi** (*Xiéqì—邪气) will not exit. It will **clash with the true essence** (*Zhēn—真) and become chaotic, neither dispersing nor returning inward to lodge again. If the needle is not used properly, what should be **harmonious** (*Shùn—顺) is treated as **contrary** (*Nì—逆).

**Audio Annotation:**
*‘Zhōng yú yīngbèi’ (中於膺背) is also written as ‘Zhōng yú jiānbèi’ (中於肩背).*
*‘Yì zhōng qí jīng’ (亦中其经) is also written as ‘Xià qí jīng’ (下其经).*
*‘Fǔhù dāng qiè’ (腑户当切).*
*‘Tánzé shàng núlǎo jiào qiè’ (潭泽上奴教切), and ‘xià jiē tóng jiāyǐ jīng’ (下皆同《甲乙经》).*
*‘Tán’ (潭) is pronounced with a **muddy** (*Zhuó—浊) tone, ‘Zé’ (泽) with a **clear** (*Yì—液) tone.*
*‘Rù ér bùkè’ (入而不客) is also written as ‘róng’ (容).*
*‘Zhì zhèng shàng zhì xià zòng’ (痣症上治下纵).*
*‘Tīng yīn jiè’ (听音戒).*
*‘Hǎn yǐ liè qiè’ (喊乙劣切).*

「To **expel** (*Xī—息*) the **stagnant qi** (*Bēn—奔*)—lower pronunciation.

**Sour** (*Suān—酸*) pronounced **sour**.
**Mark** (*Hén—痕*) pronounced **Gia** (*Jiā—贾*).
**Treat** (*Liáo—疗*)—*Túhuí* (*徒回*) pronunciation.
**Fall** (*Pū—仆*) pronounced **Fù** (*付*).

**Bivalve** (*Hé—蛤*)—upper pronunciation *Húhuí* (*胡恢*), meaning **a long worm within the abdomen** (*Chángchóng—长虫*).
Lower pronunciation *Húgě* (*胡葛*), meaning **borer worm** (*Dùchóng—蠹虫*).

**Drowsy** (*Shuì—睡*)—*Zhúchuí* (*竹垂*) pronunciation.
**Emaciated** (*Jí—瘠*) pronounced *Róngměi* (*荣美*).
**Elm** (*Yóu—榆*) pronounced *Chóngmù* (*舂木*).

**Testicle** (*Gào—睪*) pronounced **high** (*Gāo—高*), meaning **the scrotal testicle** (*Yīnwán—阴丸*).

**The [main] channels** (*Wéijié—维厥*)—**Note:** This passage refers to the **main channels** (*Wéijié—维厥*), which include the **Yang Main Channel** (*Yángwéi—阳维*) and **Yin Main Channel** (*Yīnwéi—阴维*), hence the term **main channels** (*Wéijié—维厥*).»

---
**Huángdì Nèijīng Língshū Jízhù Juàn zhī sān Gēnjié Dìwǔ Fǎ yīn**

**Qībó** said:
The **Dao/Way** of Heaven and Earth—how they resonate in harmony, their **qi** of cold and warmth flowing in exchange—who prevails, the Yin or the Yang? The Yin **Dao/Way** manifests in pairs, the Yang in singularity. When **diseased-qi** arises in spring and summer, Yin **qi** is scant while Yang **qi** dominates; if Yin and Yang are unbalanced, what **supplementation** or **drainage** can be applied?

When **diseased-qi** arises in autumn and winter, Yang **qi** is scant while Yin **qi** prevails, its vigor overwhelming as Yang **qi** wanes. Thus, the **stem and leaves** wither and decay, and the **moist rains descend**, returning to their source. As Yin and Yang shift in exchange, what **drainage** or **supplementation** can be applied?

The **strange pathogens** that stray from the **main channels** are countless; if one does not grasp their **roots and nodes**, the **Five Zàng** (organs) and **Six Fǔ** (entrails) will falter, their **joints and pivots** (关枢) defeated. The **portals** open and the **pathogens flee**, causing a great imbalance of Yin and Yang—irreparable once lost.

The **mysteries of the Nine Needles** lie in their **commencement and conclusion**; thus, one who knows these can **diagnose and treat in a single utterance**. If one does not grasp their **commencement and conclusion**, the **Dao/Way of acupuncture** will utterly perish.

The **Taiyang** **main channel** roots in **Zhìyīn** (至阴), its **node** at the **Mìngmén** (命门). The **Mìngmén** is the **eye**. The **Yángmíng** **main channel** roots at **Lìduì** (厉兑), its **node** at **Kēdà** (颗大). The **Kēdà** is the **ear-pincher** (钳耳). The **Shàoyáng** **main channel** roots at **Qiàoyīn** (窍阴), its **node** at **Chuānglóng** (窗笼). The **Chuānglóng** is the **ear canal**.

The **Taiyang** governs **opening** (开), the **Yángmíng** governs **closing** (阖), and the **Shàoyáng** governs the **pivot** (枢). Thus, if **opening** is impaired, the **flesh-joints** (肉节) are drenched and **sudden diseases** arise. Therefore, those with **sudden diseases** are treated at the **Taiyang**; one examines whether the **qi** is in **excess** or **deficiency**, for those with **drenching** (渎) will see their **skin and flesh** soften, forming pus and weakness.

If **closing** is impaired, the **qi** has nowhere to rest, and **flaccid ailments** (痿疾) arise. Therefore, those with **flaccid ailments** are treated at the **Yángmíng**; one examines whether the **qi** is in **excess** or **deficiency**, for those with **nowhere to rest** will have their **true qi** stagnate while **pathogenic qi** occupies its place.

If the **pivot** is impaired, the **bones** will **shake and tremble** (县), unable to settle upon the earth. Therefore, those with **shaking bones** are treated at the **Shàoyáng**; one examines whether the **qi** is in **excess** or **deficiency**, for those with **shaking bones** will have **loose joints** (节缓) that fail to contract. This **shaking** is what is meant by **shaking bones**; one must **exhaustively probe its source**.

The **Tàiyīn** **main channel** roots at **Yǐnbái** (隐白), its **node** at **Tàicāng** (太仓). The **Shàoyīn** **main channel** roots at **Yǒngquán** (涌泉), its **node** at **Liánquán** (廉泉). The **Juéyīn** **main channel** roots at **Dàdūn** (大敦), its **node** at **Yùyīng** (玉英), its **collateral** at **Shànzhōng** (擅中). The **Tàiyīn** governs **opening**, the **Juéyīn** governs **closing**, and the **Shàoyīn** governs the **pivot**. Thus, if **opening** is impaired, the **granary** (仓) will **lack output**, causing **diaphragmatic rupture** (膈洞). Those with **diaphragmatic rupture** are treated at the **Tàiyīn**; one examines whether the **qi** is in **excess** or **deficiency**, for those with **opening** impaired will suffer from **insufficient qi** and disease.

If **closing** is impaired, the **qi** will **sever**, causing **joy and sorrow** (喜悲) to alternate. Those with **sorrow** are treated at the **Juéyīn**; one examines whether the **qi** is in **excess** or **deficiency**.

If the **pivot** is impaired, the **pulse** will have **blockages** (结) and fail to flow. Those with **blocked pulses** are treated at the **Shàoyīn**; one examines whether the **qi** is in **excess** or **deficiency**, for all cases of **blockage** stem from **deficiency**.

The **foot Taiyang** **main channel** roots at **Zhìyīn**, **trickles** (溜) at **Jīnggǔ** (京骨), **flows into** (注) **Kūnlún** (昆仑), and **enters** (入) **Tiānjì** (天柱) and **Fēiyáng** (飞扬).
The **foot Shàoyáng** **main channel** roots at **Qiàoyīn**, **trickles** at **Qiūxū** (丘墟), **flows into** **Yángfǔ** (阳辅), and **enters** **Tiānróng** (天容) and **Guāngmíng** (光明).
The **foot Yángmíng** **main channel** roots at **Lìduì**, **trickles** at **Chōngyáng** (冲阳), **flows into** **Xiàlíng** (下陵), and **enters** **Rényíng** (人迎) and **Fēnglóng** (丰隆).
The **hand Taiyang** **main channel** roots at **Shàozé** (少泽), **trickles** at **Yánggǔ** (阳谷), **flows into** **Shàohǎi** (少海), and **enters** **Tiānchuāng** (天窗) and **Zhīzhèng** (支正).
The **hand Shàoyáng** **main channel** roots at **Guānchōng** (关冲), **trickles** at **Yángchí** (阳池), **flows into** **Zhīgōu** (支沟), and **enters** **Tiānyòu** (天牖) and **Wàiguān** (外关).
The **hand Yángmíng** **main channel** roots at **Shāngyáng** (商阳), **trickles** at **Hégǔ** (合谷), **flows into** **Yángxī** (阳溪), and **enters** **Fútū** (扶突) and **Piānlì** (偏历).

These are the **Twelve Main Channels** (十二经); their **collaterals** and **networks** (络) must all be treated accordingly.

The **body’s fifty circuits** (营) complete one cycle in a day and five in a night, **distributing** the **essence** of the **Five Zàng** (organs). If the pulse does not conform to this count, it is called **maddened pulse** (狂生). The **fifty circuits** refer to the **Five Zàng** all receiving **qi**; one holds their **pulse gates** (脉口) and counts its arrivals. If **fifty beats** occur without a single pause, all **Five Zàng** receive **qi**; if **forty beats** occur with one pause, one **Zàng** lacks **qi**; if **thirty beats** occur with one pause, two **Zàng** lack **qi**; if **twenty beats** occur with one pause, three **Zàng** lack **qi**; if **ten beats** occur with one pause, four **Zàng** lack **qi**; if fewer than **ten beats** occur with one pause, all five **Zàng** lack **qi**. This is what is called **shortened pulse**; its key lies in **commencement and conclusion**.

By **fifty beats without pause**, one establishes the norm. To **know the fate** of the **Five Zàng** and **shorten the pulse**, one must **count swiftly, then pause**. The **Yellow Emperor** said:
*"To invert or conform to the five corporeal forms*—that is, the **size of one’s bones and joints**, the **firmness or fragility of one’s flesh**, the **thickness or thinness of one’s skin**, the **clarity or turbidity of one’s blood**, the **smoothness or roughness of one’s qi**, the **length or brevity of one’s pulse**, the **abundance or scarcity of one’s blood**, and the **number of one’s channels and collaterals**—these I already know. Such knowledge belongs even to the **common folk** (布衣匹夫之士).

But what of **noble lords and great officials** (王公大人), those who **consume grain and blood** (血食之君)? Their **bodies are soft and fragile**, their **flesh and muscles weak**, their **blood and qi** **turbulent and swift** (栗悍滑利). Can their **needling**—whether **slow or fast**, **shallow or deep**—be the same as that of the common folk?"

**Qībó** replied:
*"The **flavor of rich foods** (膏粱菽蕾) renders such equality impossible! If the **qi** is **smooth**, the pulse will **emerge swiftly**; if the **qi** is **rough**, the pulse will **emerge late**. If the **qi** is **turbulent**, one must use a **small needle** and **insert shallowly**; if the **qi** is **rough**, one must use a **large needle** and **insert deeply**. Deep insertion requires **dwelling**, shallow insertion requires **speed**. Thus, when treating the **common folk**, one **inserts deeply** and **dwells**; when treating the **noble**, one **needles lightly** and **proceeds slowly**—all this is due to their **turbulent, swift qi**."

The **Yellow Emperor** asked:
*"How does one address the **inversion or conformity** of **form and qi**?"*

**Qībó** replied:
*"If **form and qi** are **deficient** while **disease-qi** is in **excess**, the **pathogen prevails**; one must **urgently drain** it. If **form and qi** are in **excess** while **disease-qi** is **deficient**, one must **urgently supplement** it. If **form and qi** are both **deficient** and **disease-qi** is also **deficient**, this is a case where **Yin and Yang qi are both deficient**; one must **not needle** it, for needling will **aggravate the deficiency**. If needling aggravates the deficiency, **Yin and Yang will both be exhausted**, the **blood and qi depleted**, the **Five Zàng** **hollow and void**, the **sinews, bones, and marrow** **withered and dry**. The **aged** will **perish**, the **strong** will **never recover**.

If **form and qi** are both in **excess** and **disease-qi** is also in **excess**, this is a case where **Yin and Yang are both in excess**; one must **urgently drain the pathogen** and **balance the excess and deficiency**."*

Thus it is said: **"Excess must be drained; deficiency must be supplemented.** This is the principle.

Therefore, it is said: **Needling without discerning excess and deficiency causes true and pathogenic *qi* to clash.** If one supplements when the body is already **replete**, then **Yin and Yang will overflow**, the **intestines and stomach will distend**, the **lungs and liver will rupture internally**, and **Yin and Yang will be disordered**. If one drains when the body is **hollow and void**, then the **meridians and vessels will be emptied**, the **blood and *qi* will be exhausted and dried**, the **intestines and stomach will be struck in terror**, the **skin will become thin and clinging**, the **hair will wither and fall prematurely**, and **life’s end will be hastened**.

Therefore, it is said: **The essential skill in needling lies in knowing how to balance Yin and Yang**; when **Yin and Yang are harmonized**, the **vital *qi* will shine forth**, and when **form and *qi* are united**, the **spirit will be securely housed**.

Therefore, it is said: **The superior practitioner harmonizes the *qi*, the mediocre practitioner disrupts the pulse, and the inferior practitioner exhausts the *qi*, endangering life.** Therefore, it is said: **The inferior practitioner must not be taken lightly.**

One must **thoroughly examine the diseases of the Five Zàng**, their **manifestations in the Five Mai**, the **fullness and hollowness of the meridians and collaterals**, and the **softness or coarseness of the skin** before proceeding to treatment.

---
**Annotations (as per request, but not part of the translation):**
- *qi* (氣) – Vital energy, as in Traditional Chinese Medicine
- **Yin and Yang** – Fundamental concepts in Daoist and TCM cosmology
- **true and pathogenic *qi*** – *正邪* (zhēng-xié): True *qi* (healthy) vs. pathogenic *qi* (disease)
- **Five Zàng** (五藏) – The five vital organs (heart, liver, spleen, lungs, kidneys)
- **Five Mai** (五脉) – The five major pulses (radial, median, lateral, etc.)
- **spirit** (神) – *Shén*, the refined essence or consciousness
- **superior/mediocre/inferior practitioner** – *上工/中工/下工* (shàng-gōng/zhōng-gōng/xià-gōng): Skill levels in TCM
- **予#3** – Likely a typo or placeholder; assumed to mean "the patient" (予, *yú*, often used for "I" or "the patient" in medical texts)
- **骨县音摇** – *"Gǔ xiàn yīn yáo"* (Bone County pronunciation: "yao"): A phonetic note for *"郭"* (guō, "distend") as *"郭"* (yáo, "wither") in some dialects. *(Not included in translation per guidelines.)*

「Lì hàn shàng bǐ zhāo qiè, xià xiá àn qiè—its meaning is *courageous and robust* (a description of vigor).

The *Daoist* pronunciation of "奇" (*qí*) is "jī" (as in *箕*).

**Longevity and Premature Death: The Sixth Law of Strength and Weakness**

The Yellow Emperor asked Master Shào: *"I have heard that among humans, there are those with strength and those with weakness, those who are frail and those who are strong, those with short lives and those with long, those with excess yin and those with excess yang. I wish to hear the principles behind these."* Master Shào replied: *"Within yin lies yin, and within yang lies yang. If one understands yin and yang thoroughly, then acupuncture has a method. When the disease’s origin is known, acupuncture has its logic. Carefully measure the disease’s onset and align with the season—internally harmonizing with the Five Zàng and Six Fǔ, externally harmonizing with sinews, bones, skin, and flesh. Thus, within the body, the Five Zàng are yin, and the Six Fǔ are yang; externally, sinews and bones are yin, and skin and flesh are yang.

Therefore, when a disease occurs in the yin of yin, one must puncture the *honor* and *shu* pulses of yin;
When a disease occurs in the yang of yang, one must puncture the *he* pulses of yang;
When a disease occurs in the yin of yang, one must puncture the *jing* channels of yin;
When a disease occurs in the yang of yin, one must puncture the *luò* vessels.

Hence, when a disease is in yang, it is called *fēng* (wind);
When a disease is in yin, it is called *bì* (obstruction);
When both yin and yang are diseased, it is called *fēng-bì* (wind-obstruction).

A disease with form but no pain belongs to the yang category;
A disease without form but with pain belongs to the yin category.

When there is no form but pain exists, it is because yang is intact while yin is injured—urgently treat the yin, do not attack the yang;
When there is form but no pain, it is because yin is intact while yang is injured—urgently treat the yang, do not attack the yin.

When both yin and yang are active, the disease alternately has form and lacks form, and is accompanied by agitation—this is called *yin overcoming yang*, meaning it is neither superficial nor deep; its form will not persist long.*

The Yellow Emperor asked Master Bó Gāo: *"I have heard of the sequence of disease in form and qi, and their external and internal responses. How does this work?"* Master Bó Gāo replied: *"Wind and cold injure the form; grief, fear, anger, and resentment injure the qi. When qi is injured, the Zàng are diseased; when the form is injured by cold, the form itself responds. When sinews and vessels are injured by wind, the sinews and vessels respond. This is how the form and qi correspond externally and internally."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one apply acupuncture in such cases?"* Master Bó Gāo replied: *"For a disease lasting nine days, three punctures suffice; for a disease lasting one month, ten punctures suffice. Adjust the number based on the severity and proximity of the disease. For lingering obstruction that does not leave the body, examine the blood vessels and exhaust their blood."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one determine the difficulty of treating external and internal diseases?"* Master Bó Gāo replied: *"If the form is first diseased but has not yet entered the Zàng, halve the number of days; if the Zàng are first diseased and the form responds afterward, double the number of days. This is the principle of external and internal difficulty."*

The Yellow Emperor asked Master Bó Gāo: *"I have heard that form has degrees of slackness and tension, qi has fluctuations of abundance and decline, bones vary in size, flesh in firmness and fragility, and skin in thickness and thinness. How does one establish longevity or premature death based on these?"* Master Bó Gāo replied: *"When form and qi support each other, longevity follows; when they do not, death is premature. When skin and flesh mutually reinforce each other, longevity follows; when they do not, death is premature. When blood, qi, channels, and vessels surpass the form in strength, longevity follows; when they cannot, death is premature."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"What is meant by the form’s slackness and tension?"* Master Bó Gāo replied: *"When the form is full and the skin slack, longevity follows; when the form is full but the skin taut, death is premature. When the form is full and the pulse firm and large, it is harmonious; when the form is full but the pulse small and weak, the qi is declining—decline brings peril. If the form is full but suddenly fails to rise, the bones are small; small bones mean premature death. When the form is full and the flesh is large yet firm with clear definition, the flesh is firm—firm flesh brings longevity. When the form is full but the flesh is large without clear definition and lacks firmness, the flesh is fragile—fragile flesh brings premature death. This is the Heavenly law governing life and death, determining the form and observing qi to establish longevity or premature death. One must grasp this principle, establish the form and observe the qi, and then treat the patient accordingly, deciding life or death."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have heard that longevity and premature death cannot be measured."* Master Bó Gāo replied: *"If a person’s foundation is low and their height does not reach their base, they will not live to thirty; if they have additional causes of illness, they may not even reach twenty."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does the interplay of form and qi determine longevity or premature death?"* Master Bó Gāo replied: *"In a healthy person, if qi surpasses the form, longevity follows; in a diseased person, if flesh and form are depleted but qi surpasses the form, death follows; if the form overpowers the qi, danger ensues."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have heard that acupuncture has three variations. What are these three variations?"* Master Bó Gāo replied: *"There is puncturing the *yíng* (nutritive), puncturing the *wèi* (defensive), and puncturing lingering cold-obstruction in the *jing* (channel) vessels."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one apply acupuncture to these three variations?"* Master Bó Gāo replied: *"Puncturing the *yíng* causes blood to flow; puncturing the *wèi* releases qi; puncturing cold-obstruction generates internal heat."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How do *yíng*, *wèi*, and cold-obstruction manifest as diseases?"* Master Bó Gāo replied: *"When *yíng* generates disease, it manifests as chills and fever, shortness of breath, and blood flowing erratically above and below. When *wèi* generates disease, it manifests as pain in the qi that comes and goes, agitation, and wind-cold lodged in the intestines and stomach. When cold-obstruction generates disease, it lingers without leaving, causing intermittent pain and numbness in the skin."*
