The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one puncture cold-obstruction to generate internal heat?"* Master Bó Gāo replied: *"For a commoner, use fire to cauterize; for a noble, apply moxibustion with medicine."* The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one apply medicinal moxibustion?"* Master Bó Gāo replied: *"Take twenty *shēng* (升) of pure wine, one *shēng* of Sichuan pepper, one *jīn* (斤) of dried ginger, and one *jīn* of cassia bark—four ingredients total. All are to be ground into a powder, soaked in wine, and mixed with one *jīn* of cotton and four *zhǎng* (丈) of fine white cloth. Combine these with the wine, place in a horse-dung furnace, seal with mud, and prevent leaks. After five days and five nights, remove the cloth and cotton, dry them in the sun, then re-soak them in wine until all the liquid is exhausted."*

Each time the cloth is soaked, mark the day and then dry it. Once dry, combine the residue with the cotton and cloth, folding them into six or seven bandages, each six or seven *chǐ* (尺) in length. Use these to warm fresh mulberry charcoal, then apply the heated bandages to the cold-obstruction sites where acupuncture was performed, allowing the heat to penetrate to the afflicted area. When the cold returns, reheat the bandages and apply them again—thirty times in total. Once perspiration emerges, wipe the body with the bandages, also thirty times, before rising. Avoid exposure to wind afterward. Each acupuncture session must be followed by moxibustion; thus, the ailment shall be cured. This is what is meant by *internal heat*.

---
**Phonetic Glosses:**
- *颇* (pō) – rhymes with *权* (quán).
- *腘* (wěi) – pronounced *腘坚上渠永切* (wěi jiān shàng qú yǒng qiè), meaning the fatty layer in the *knee crease* (腹中腘脂).
- *佛忾* (fú xì) – pronounced *上扶勿切* (shàng fú wù qiè), meaning *suppressed* (郁), to *relax the mind* (为意舒).
- *下许气切* (xià xǔ qì qiè) – unclear in context.
- *吹咀* (chuī zhǔ) – pronounced *上音甫* (shàng yīn fǔ), *下才与切* (xià cái yú qiè), meaning *to grind into powder*.
- *媪* (yù) – pronounced *於文切* (yú wén qiè), referring to *marital relations* (姻媪气).
- *眸其日* (móu qí rì) – pronounced *上音醉* (shàng yīn zuì), meaning *same as* (同).

---
**The Seventh Method of Official Needles (官针第七法星):**
The essence of acupuncture lies in the mastery of official needles. Each of the nine needles has its proper use—long or short, large or small—each applied according to its purpose. If misapplied, the disease cannot be cured.

- If the ailment is shallow but the needle is deep, it will injure the good flesh, causing *flaccidity* (瘫) of the skin.
- If the ailment is deep but the needle is shallow, the pathogenic *qi* will not be expelled, leading to *large abscesses* (大脓).
- If the ailment is minor but the needle is large, the expulsion of *qi* will be excessive, harming the patient.
- If the ailment is severe but the needle is small, the pathogenic *qi* will not be fully released, resulting in failure.

When the needle’s use is lost, the greater harm is done. Now, let us discuss its proper applications:

- If the disease is in the skin at an irregular location, use a *cai* needle (才针) at the affected site, avoiding white-skinned areas.
- If the disease is in the *flesh* (分肉), use a *yuán* needle (圆针) at the affected site.
- If the disease is in the *meridians and collaterals* (经络疯痹), use a *fēng* needle (锋针).
- If the disease is in the *pulse* (脉) and *qi* is deficient, requiring supplementation, use a *tí* needle (缇针) at the *jing-well*, *ying-spring*, or *shu-stream* points.
- If the disease results in a *large abscess* (大脓), use a *bì* needle (铍针).
- If the disease is a *sudden outbreak of stagnant qi* (痹气暴发), use a sharp *yuán* needle (圆利针).
- If the disease is *stagnant qi causing persistent pain* (痹气痛而不去), use a *háo* needle (毫针).
- If the disease is *internal* (在中), use a *long needle* (长针).
- If the disease is *water retention causing inability to move joints* (水肿不能通关节), use a *large needle* (大针).
- If the disease is *fixed in the five viscera* (五藏固居), use a *fēng* needle (锋针) at the *jing-well*, *ying-spring*, or *shu-stream* points, applying the method of the four seasons.

There are nine methods of acupuncture, corresponding to nine variations:

1. **Shū Sà (输刺):** Acupuncture at the *confluences*, *collaterals*, *viscera*, and *facets* (输藏脸) of the meridians.
2. **Yuǎn Dào Sà (远道刺):** Distant-point acupuncture—if the disease is above, treat below, targeting the *facets* (府脸).
3. **Jīng Sà (经刺):** Acupuncture at the *major meridians*, their *networks*, and *branches* (结络经分).
4. **Luò Sà (络刺):** Acupuncture at the *minor collaterals* and *blood vessels* (小络之血脉).
5. **Fèn Sà (分刺):** Acupuncture between the *flesh layers* (分肉之间).
6. **Dà Xiě Sà (大写刺):** Acupuncture for *large abscesses* using a *bì* needle (铍针).
7. **Máo Sà (毛刺):** Acupuncture for *superficial stagnation* in the skin (浮痹皮肤).
8. **Jù Sà (巨刺):** Contralateral acupuncture—left side treated for right side ailments, and vice versa.
9. **Cuì Sà (悴刺):** Cauterization acupuncture—using a *burning needle* (燔针) to treat stagnation (痹).

There are twelve acupuncture techniques corresponding to the twelve meridians:

1. **Ǎi Sà (偶刺):** *Paired acupuncture*—with the hand aligned to the heart or back, one needle is inserted forward, another backward, treating *heart stagnation* (心痹). This is also called *傍针* (pang-zhen, oblique needling).
2. **Bào Sà (报刺):** *Responsive acupuncture*—needling where pain is irregular. If moving upward or downward, insert directly inward without withdrawing the needle; use the left hand to press the affected area before reinserting.
3. **Huī Sà (恢刺):** *Expansive acupuncture*—insert directly and obliquely, lifting forward and backward to relax *tight tendons*, treating *tendon stagnation* (筋痹).
4. **Qí Sà (齐刺):** *Uniform acupuncture*—insert one needle straight, two obliquely, treating *slightly deep cold* (寒气小深). Alternatively, *three-needle technique* (三刺) for *deeper stagnant qi* (痹气小深).
5. **Yáng Sà (扬刺):** *Elevated acupuncture*—insert one needle straight, four obliquely, then float it, treating *widespread cold* (寒气之博大).
6. **Zhí Zhēn Sà (直针刺):** *Direct-needle acupuncture*—pull the skin taut before inserting, treating *superficial cold* (寒气之浅).
7. **Shū Sà (输刺):** *Confluence acupuncture*—insert and withdraw directly, using sparse shallow insertions to reach depth, treating *excessive heat* (气盛而热).
8. **Duǎn Sà (短刺):** *Short acupuncture*—needle *bone stagnation* (骨痹), shaking slightly to reach depth, rubbing the needle against the bone.
9. **Fú Sà (浮刺):** *Floating acupuncture*—insert obliquely and float the needle, treating *muscle tension and cold* (肌急而寒).
10. **Yīn Sà (阴刺):** *Yin acupuncture*—needle left and right alternately, treating *cold contraction* (寒厥) and *midline cold contraction* (中寒厥), targeting the *minor yin* behind the ankle (足踝后少阴).
11. **Páng Zhēn Sà (傍针刺):** *Oblique-needle acupuncture*—insert one needle straight, one obliquely, treating *persistent stagnation* (留痹久居).
12. **Zàn Sà (赞刺):** *Assisting acupuncture*—insert and withdraw repeatedly with shallow needling to induce bleeding, treating *flaccidity and swelling* (瘫肿).

If the pulse resides deep and is unseen, needle it *slightly inward* and leave the needle for a long time to reach the *empty pulse qi* (空脉气). For shallow pulses, do not needle directly; press until the pulse is cut off before inserting, ensuring no *essence* (精) is lost—only the *pathogenic qi* (邪气) should be expelled.

The so-called *three-needle technique* (三刺) where *stomach qi* (谷气) emerges:
- First, needle shallowly to cut the skin, expelling *yang pathogenic factors* (阳邪).
- Second, needle slightly deeper to reach the muscles, cutting the skin but not entering the *flesh layers* (分肉问), expelling *yin pathogenic factors* (阴邪).
- Once the needle enters the *flesh layers*, *stomach qi* emerges.

Thus, the *Needling Method* (刺法) states:
*"Begin with shallow needling to drive out pathogenic qi and summon blood qi; later, needle deeply to reach yin pathogenic factors."*

In the final stage, needle to the utmost depth to expel *stomach qi* (谷气).
This is what is meant.

Thus, those who wield the needle, if they fail to account for the years that have passed upon a person, the waxing and waning of their *qi* (气), or the rise and fall of their *empty* and *full* states (虚实), cannot be considered skilled practitioners.

In general, there are five needling techniques, corresponding to the five *zàng* organs (五藏):
1. **Half-needling (半刺)**—Half-needling is shallow insertion with swift withdrawal, ensuring no injury to the flesh, resembling plucking a hair; it targets *skin qi* (皮气), which pertains to the *lung* (肺).
2. **Leopard-spot needling (豹文刺)**—Leopard-spot needling involves piercing left, right, front, and back around the central pulse, to draw *channel-network blood* (经络之血); this pertains to the *heart* (心).
3. **Barrier needling (关刺)**—Barrier needling is a straight insertion left and right, reaching the tendons' uppermost layer, to treat *tendon stagnation* (筋痹); care must be taken not to cause bleeding. This pertains to the *liver* (肝), or it is also called **Deep-needling (渊刺)** or **Gorge needling (岂刺)**.
4. **Converging-valley needling (合谷刺)**—Converging-valley needling involves piercing the *blended flesh layers* (鹦足) on either side, targeting *muscle stagnation* (肌痹); this pertains to the *spleen* (脾).
5. **Empty-needling (输刺)**—Empty-needling is a straight insertion and withdrawal, penetrating to the bone, to treat *bone stagnation* (骨痹); this pertains to the *kidney* (肾).

---
**Phonetic Notes (音释):**
- *燔* (fán) above "针" (zhēn) is pronounced as *fán* (or *fèn*).
- *恢* (huī) in "恢刺" (huī cì) is pronounced *kuí* (苦回切), meaning "great" (大). One variant reads *guài* (怪).

**Chapter 8: The Dao of Spirit (本神第八)**

The Yellow Emperor asked Qibo:
*"In the art of needling, one must first establish harmony with the spirit (神).
Blood, pulse, nourishment (营), qi, and spirit—these are what the five viscera store.
When these overflow and stray from their proper abode, the essence (精) is lost, the soul (魂) and vital breath (魄) scatter, and will, intent, and cognition wander from the body. What causes this? Is it the fault of Heaven, or human error?
What are *virtue (德)*, *qi*, *life (生)*, *essence (精)*, *spirit (神)*, *soul (魂)*, *vital breath (魄)*, *heart (心)*, *intent (意)*, *will (志)*, *thought (思)*, *wisdom (智)*, and *deliberation (虑)*?
I beg you to explain their nature."*

Qibo replied:
*"Heaven’s influence within us is *virtue (德)*, Earth’s influence is *qi (气)*.
When *virtue* flows and *qi* is nurtured, life emerges—this is called *essence (精)*.
When two essences converge, it is called *spirit (神)*.
That which follows the spirit in its movements is called *soul (魂)*.
That which enters and exits alongside the essence is called *vital breath (魄)*.
That which governs the body is called *heart (心)*.
When the heart recalls, it is called *intent (意)*.
Where intent lingers, it is called *will (志)*.
When will adapts to change, it is called *thought (思)*.
When thought reaches beyond, it is called *deliberation (虑)*.
When deliberation guides action, it is called *wisdom (智)*.
Thus, the wise in nurturing life must align with the four seasons, balance joy and anger, and dwell in harmony.
Regulate yin and yang, temper firmness and softness—then perverse influences will not take root, and longevity will follow.

Therefore:
- Excessive vigilance and anxious deliberation injure the *spirit (神)*; when the spirit is injured, fear and dread become uncontrollable.
- Grief and sorrow, when excessive, exhaust and sever life.
- Excessive joy scatters the spirit, preventing its return.
- Sorrow and worry obstruct *qi*, halting its flow.
- Rage confuses and disrupts.
- Fear disperses the spirit, leaving it ungathered.

When the heart is restless with anxious thought, the *spirit (神)* is injured; the spirit injured, fear and dread overwhelm, flesh wastes, hair withers, complexion darkens—death comes in winter.
When the *spleen (脾)* is burdened by unresolved sorrow, *intent (意)* is injured; intent injured, confusion reigns, limbs fail to move, hair withers, complexion darkens—death comes in spring.
When the *liver (肝)* is moved by excessive grief, the *soul (魂)* is injured; the soul injured, madness and forgetfulness ensue, limbs contract, sides stiffen—hair withers, complexion darkens—death comes in autumn.
When the *lung (肺)* indulges boundless joy, the *vital breath (魄)* is injured; the vital breath injured, madness follows—mind departs the body, skin dries, hair withers, complexion darkens—death comes in summer.
When the *kidney (肾)* is consumed by unchecked rage, *will (志)* is injured; will injured, one forgets past words, the waist and spine lose mobility—hair withers, complexion darkens—death comes in late summer.
When fear persists unresolved, *essence (精)* is injured; essence injured, bones ache, atrophy sets in, and essence drains downward.

Thus, the five viscera store *essence*—they must not be harmed.
If harmed, they lose their guard, yin becomes deficient, and without *qi*, death follows.
Therefore, the practitioner of needling must observe the patient’s demeanor to discern the fate of *spirit, soul, vital breath, intent, and will*.
If any of these five are injured, needling cannot restore them.

The *liver (肝)* stores blood; blood houses the *soul (魂)*.
When liver *qi* is deficient, fear arises; when excessive, anger follows.
The *spleen (脾)* stores *nourishment (营)*; *nourishment* houses *intent (意)*.
When spleen *qi* is deficient, the limbs grow weak, the viscera unsettled; when excessive, the abdomen distends, the bowels stagnate.
The *heart (心)* stores *pulse (脉)*; *pulse* houses *spirit (神)*.
When heart *qi* is deficient, sorrow takes hold; when excessive, laughter is unceasing.
The *lung (肺)* stores *qi*; *qi* houses *vital breath (魄)*.
When lung *qi* is deficient, the nose is congested, breath is shallow; when excessive, wheezing and chest fullness force upward breathing.
The *kidney (肾)* stores *essence*; *essence* houses *will (志)*.
When kidney *qi* is deficient, syncope occurs; when excessive, distension arises, and the viscera grow unsettled.

One must carefully examine the diseased forms of the five viscera to discern their *qi* as deficient or replete, then adjust accordingly.

---
**Chapter 9: The Dao of Terminus and Commencement (终始第九)**

*"The art of needling is complete in its terminus and commencement.
To master terminus and commencement, the five viscera are the key—then yin and yang are defined.
Yin governs storage, yang governs transport.
Yang receives *qi* from the extremities, yin receives *qi* from the viscera.

Thus, in *draining (泻)*, one follows the *qi*’s arrival; in *tonifying (补)*, one aligns with its flow.
To know arrival and flow is to harmonize *qi*.

The method of harmonizing *qi* must unite yin and yang—the five viscera as yin, the six bowels as yang.
This tradition is passed down through bloodlines, sworn as an oath:
Respect it, and prosperity follows; neglect it, and ruin comes.
Do not act without principle, for such will bring untimely doom.

Reverently uphold the Dao of Heaven—now speak of terminus and commencement.

*Terminus and commencement* are rooted in the channels and vessels.
Hold to the *pulse (脉口)* and *Renying (人迎)*, to know yin and yang’s excess or deficiency, their balance or imbalance—thus Heaven’s Dao is complete.

The *balanced person* is without disease.
The disease-free have *pulse (脉口)* and *Renying* in harmony with the four seasons, their rise and fall in unison, their six channels unobstructed, their extremities’ cold and warmth regulated, their form, flesh, blood, and *qi* in perfect accord—this is the *balanced person*.

The *qi*-deficient have *pulse (脉口)* and *Renying* diminished, failing to meet the standard of the *cùn* and *shǐ* measurements."*

Thus, when both yin and yang are deficient, supplementing the yang will exhaust the yin, and draining the yin will deplete the yang. In such cases, one may administer mild medicinals but must avoid excessive doses.

Do not apply moxibustion here—if the condition persists, draining will only ruin the *zàng* organs’ *qi*.

When *Renying* is excessively strong once, the disease lies in the *zu* (足) *shǎo yáng* (少陽). If it is both strong and restless, the disease lies in the *shǒu* (手) *shǎo yáng*.
When *Renying* is excessively strong twice, the disease lies in the *zu* *tài yáng* (太陽). If it is both strong and restless, the disease lies in the *shǒu* *tài yáng*.
When *Renying* is excessively strong thrice, the disease lies in the *zu* *yáng míng* (陽明). If it is both strong and restless, the disease lies in the *shǒu* *yáng míng*.
When *Renying* is excessively strong four times—great and rapid—it is called *overflowing yang* (*yì yáng*), and *overflowing yang* is an *external blockage* (*wài gé*).

When the *pulse (pulse mouth, 脉口)* is excessively strong once, the disease lies in the *zu* *jué yīn* (厥陰). If it is both strong and restless, it lies in the *shǒu* *xīn zhǔ* (心主).
When the *pulse* is excessively strong twice, the disease lies in the *zu* *shǎo yīn* (少陰). If it is both strong and restless, it lies in the *shǒu* *shǎo yīn*.
When the *pulse* is excessively strong thrice, if it is both strong and restless, it lies in the *shǒu* *tài yīn* (太陰). If the pulse is both great and rapid, it is called *overflowing yin* (*yì yīn*), and *overflowing yin* with *internal blockage* (*nèi guān*) is untreatable and fatal.

When *Renying* and the *tài yīn* pulse are both excessively strong—four times or more—it is called *blockage at the pass* (*guān gé*), and such cases are given a short prognosis.

When *Renying* is excessively strong once, drain the *zu* *shǎo yáng* and supplement the *zu* *jué yīn*—two drains for every one supplement—once daily, verifying by palpation. If the *qi* is harmonized before completion, cease.

When *Renying* is excessively strong twice, drain the *zu* *tài yáng* and supplement the *zu* *shǎo yīn*—two drains for every one supplement—every other day, verifying by palpation. If the *qi* is harmonized before completion, cease.

When *Renying* is excessively strong thrice, drain the *zu* *yáng míng* and supplement the *zu* *tài yīn*—two drains for every one supplement—twice daily, verifying by palpation. If the *qi* is harmonized before completion, cease.

When the *pulse* is excessively strong once, drain the *zu* *jué yīn* and supplement the *zu* *shǎo yáng*—two supplements for every one drain—once daily, verifying by palpation. If the *qi* is harmonized before completion, cease.

When the *pulse* is excessively strong twice, drain the *zu* *shǎo yīn* and supplement the *zu* *tài yáng*—two supplements for every one drain—every other day, verifying by palpation. If the *qi* is harmonized before completion, cease.

When the *pulse* is excessively strong thrice, drain the *zu* *tài yīn* and supplement the *zu* *yáng míng*—two supplements for every one drain—twice daily, verifying by palpation. If the *qi* is harmonized before completion, cease. This is why it is taken twice daily: the *tài yáng* governs the stomach, which is rich in *gǔ qì* (谷气), hence it may be taken twice daily.

When *Renying* and the *pulse* are both excessively strong—three times or more—it is called *both yin and yang overflowing*. In such cases, if not treated, the blood vessels will become obstructed, *qi* will have nowhere to flow, and it will stagnate internally, injuring the *zàng* organs.

If moxibustion is applied here, the condition will transform into another disease.

In acupuncture, when the *qi* is balanced, cease. Supplementing yin and draining yang clarifies the *qi*—hearing and vision sharpen. If this is reversed, blood and *qi* will not circulate.

What is meant by *qi* arriving and taking effect: draining will worsen deficiency. Deficiency is when the pulse is large as before but not firm—if it remains as before but lacks firmness, though it may seem unchanged, the disease persists.

Supplementing will strengthen solidity. Solidity is when the pulse is large as before but more firm—if it remains as before but is not firm, though it may seem resolved, the disease persists.

Thus, supplementing strengthens, draining weakens. Though pain may not follow the needle’s withdrawal, the disease will inevitably decline.

One must first master the diseases arising from the twelve channels before attaining proficiency in *terminus and commencement*.

Thus, yin and yang must not be displaced, and deficiency and excess must not be inverted—treat according to the channel.

In acupuncture, three insertions reach the *gǔ qì*. If the pathogenic *qi* is erratic and improperly combined, yin and yang will shift positions, and the sequence of treatment will be reversed. If the needle is applied too deeply or shallowly, or if the timing is incorrect, the *qi* will stagnate and overflow, requiring the needle to remove it. Hence, the first insertion expels yang pathogens, the second expels yin pathogens, and the third reaches the *gǔ qì*—when *gǔ qì* arrives, cease.

When *gǔ qì* arrives, it is known that supplementation has solidified and draining has induced deficiency. If only the pathogenic *qi* departs while yin and yang remain unbalanced, the disease may seem to have resolved.

Thus, supplementing strengthens, draining weakens. Though pain may not follow the needle’s withdrawal, the disease will inevitably decline.

When yin is excessive and yang is deficient, first supplement the yang, then drain the yin to harmonize them.
When yin is deficient and yang is excessive, first supplement the yin, then drain the yang to harmonize them.

If the three pulses move between the *zu* *dà zhǐ* (大指), examine their deficiency or excess. If deficient, drain them—this is called *compounding deficiency*, which will worsen the disease.

In treating these cases, press with the fingers: if the pulse is rapid and firm, drain it quickly. If it is slow and weak, supplement it. If this is reversed, the disease will worsen.

Their movement: *yáng míng* is above, *jué yīn* is in the middle, and *shǎo yīn* is below.
If the chest is hollow and the back is hollow, the shoulders are deficient—take from above.

For *double tongue* (重舌), pierce the *tongue pillar* (舌柱) with a *bì* needle (铍针).

If the hand cannot extend but only flexes, the disease lies in the *sinews*.
If the hand cannot flex but only extends, the disease lies in the *bones*.
For bone disorders, treat the bones; for sinew disorders, treat the sinews.

When supplementing, take from a solid region deeply, pressing the wound lightly to fully expel the pathogenic *qi*.
When treating a deficient region, insert shallowly to nourish the pulse, pressing firmly to prevent pathogenic *qi* from entering.

When pathogenic *qi* arrives, it is tense and rapid; when *gǔ qì* arrives, it is slow and harmonious.

For a solid pulse, insert deeply to release the *qi*.
For a deficient pulse, insert shallowly to prevent essence-*qi* from escaping, expelling only the pathogenic *qi*.

When needling any painful region, the pulse will always be solid.
Thus it is said: For ailments arising above the waist, both the **Hand Taiyin** and **Hand Yangming** channels govern them; for ailments below the waist, both the **Foot Taiyin** and **Foot Yangming** channels govern them.

When disease originates above, treat below; when disease originates below, treat above. For headaches, treat the feet; for waist pain, treat the buttocks.

Diseases arising in the head cause heaviness in the head; those arising in the hands cause heaviness in the arms; those arising in the feet cause heaviness in the feet.
In treating disease, first needle the region where the ailment first manifested.

Spring *qi* resides in the hair, summer *qi* in the skin, autumn *qi* in the muscles, and winter *qi* in the sinews and bones. When treating these diseases, adjust the needle depth according to the season.
Thus, for obese patients, use the depth of **autumn-winter**; for emaciated patients, use the depth of **spring-summer**.

Pain is *yin*; when pressing a painful area with the hand and it cannot be pressed, it is *yin*—needle deeply.
Diseases above are *yang*; diseases below are *yin*. Itching is *yang*—needle shallowly.

If disease first arises in *yin*, treat the *yin* first, then the *yang*; if it first arises in *yang*, treat the *yang* first, then the *yin*.

When needling **hot reversal** (*re* syndrome), retaining the needle will instead induce cold; when needling **cold reversal**, retaining the needle will instead induce heat.
For hot reversal, use **two *yin* and one *yang*** [needle]; for cold reversal, use **two *yang* and one *yin*** [needle].
By "two *yin*," it means two needles in *yin* points; by "one *yang*," it means one needle in a *yang* point.

For chronic illnesses, pathogenic *qi* penetrates deeply. When treating such diseases, insert deeply and retain the needle for a long time, repeating daily until the patient’s left and right sides are balanced, the blood vessels are cleared, and the needling is complete.

In all needling, one must first observe the patient’s **form and *qi***.
If the flesh is not yet wasted, the breath is weak, and the pulse is restless—this is **turbulent reversal**—needling must be precise: scattered *qi* can be gathered, and concentrated *qi* can be dispersed.

Reside in quiet seclusion, focus on the movement of the spirit, close doors and windows so the soul and spirit do not scatter, concentrate on a single thought, and let the **refined *qi*** circulate. Avoid human voices to preserve the essence; unify the spirit, directing the will toward the needle. Needle shallowly and retain, lightly and superficially, to shift the spirit. Only rest when *qi* arrives.

For males, needle inward; for females, needle outward. Firmly resist without withdrawing, carefully guard without inserting—this is called **obtaining the *qi***.

The prohibitions of needling are as follows:
Do not needle after recent intercourse, nor have intercourse after needling;
Do not needle when drunk, nor drink after needling;
Do not needle when angry, nor become angry after needling;
Do not needle after exertion, nor exert oneself after needling;
Do not needle after eating, nor eat after needling;
Do not needle when hungry, nor go hungry after needling;
Do not needle when thirsty, nor drink after needling;
After great shock or fear, first stabilize the *qi*, then needle.

For those arriving by carriage, let them rest outside before needling, as if after a meal.
For those arriving on foot, let them sit and rest, as if after walking ten *li*.

These twelve prohibitions: if violated, the pulse becomes disordered, *qi* scatters, the **camp and defense** are disrupted, and the **channel *qi*** flows irregularly. Needling under these conditions will cause *yang* diseases to enter *yin*, and *yin* diseases to manifest as *yang*, allowing pathogenic *qi* to re-emerge. A careless practitioner fails to observe these, thus **harming the body**, causing excessive dissipation of the **essence and fluids**, and failing to harmonize the **Five Flavors**—this is called **losing the *qi***.

The **Taiyang** channel’s terminal signs: the eyes are covered, the gaze reverses, moles or freckles appear, the skin turns white, sweating ceases, and when sweating ceases, death follows.

The **Shaoyang** channel’s terminal signs: deafness, all joints relax excessively, the **eye system** is severed. When the eye system is severed, death follows within one and a half days. At death, the complexion turns **green-white**.

The **Yangming** channel’s terminal signs: the mouth and eyes move uncontrollably, the patient is prone to startling, speaks incoherently, and turns yellow. When the **upper and lower channels** are blocked and *qi* cannot flow, death follows.

The **Shaoyin** channel’s terminal signs: the face darkens, teeth grow long and become filthy, the abdomen swells and becomes obstructed, and both upward and downward passage ceases before death.

The **Jueyin** channel’s terminal signs: internal heat, dry throat, frequent urination, restlessness, and in severe cases, the tongue curls upward like a coiled egg before death.

The **Taiyin** channel’s terminal signs: abdominal distension and obstruction, inability to breathe, frequent belching and vomiting. If vomiting occurs, the face turns red; if not, the upward and downward passage is blocked, the face turns black, the skin and hair wither, and death follows.

Leigong asked the Yellow Emperor, saying: *"The teachings on the **prohibited pulses**, the principles of acupuncture—beginning with the **channel pathways**, tracing their courses, measuring their extent—how they connect internally to the five **viscera** and externally to the six **bowels**, I wish to hear the full **Dao** of this.*

The Yellow Emperor replied: *When a person first takes form, **essence** is formed first. From **essence**, the brain and marrow arise; bones form the **framework**, **channels** circulate the **vital substances**, tendons provide **strength**, flesh forms the **barrier**, and skin and hair harden and lengthen. When grain enters the stomach, the **channel pathways** open, and **blood-qi** flows.*

Leigong said: *"I wish to hear fully of the **channels’** origins."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"The **channels** are what enable life and death, govern a hundred ailments, and balance excess and deficiency—they must not be obstructed.

**The Lung (Hand-Taiyin) Channel** arises from the **middle burner**, descends to network with the **large intestine**, ascends along the stomach’s orifice, passes the diaphragm to belong to the lungs, then from the lungs’ root emerges beneath the armpit, descends along the **inner arm**, traversing before the **Heart (Hand-Shaoyin) Channel’s** pathway, enters the **cubital fossa**, follows the **ulnar side of the forearm**, passes beneath the **radial bone**, enters the **wrist’s radial pulse position**, ascends to the **fish** (intersection), follows the **fish’s crease**, and exits at the **tip of the thumb**. Its branch: from behind the wrist, it strays outward along the **second finger’s inner edge**, exiting at its tip.

When this channel is disturbed, the **lung** becomes distended and full, with a **swollen, labored breathing and cough**, pain in the **suprasternal fossa**, and in severe cases, the hands clasp as if in **convulsion**—this is **arm tetany**. It governs diseases of the **lung**: **cough**, upward breathlessness, thirst, **chest fullness**, **pain along the inner arm**, **convulsive coldness in the hand**, and **palm heat**.

When **qi** is excessive, there is **shoulder and back pain**, **wind-cold**, **sweating**, **frequent urination**, and **yawns**. When **qi** is deficient, there is **shoulder and back coldness**, **shortness of breath**, and **urine color changes**. For these ailments: if excessive, **bleed it**; if deficient, **supplement it**; if hot, **rapidly**; if cold, **retain the needle**; if **sunken and weak**, **moxibustion**; if neither excessive nor deficient, **follow the channel’s pathway**. When excessive, the **radial pulse** is three times larger than the **carotid**; when deficient, the **radial pulse** is smaller than the **carotid**.

**The Large Intestine (Hand-Yangming) Channel** arises at the **second finger’s tip**, follows its **outer edge**, enters between the **two tendons** at the wrist, ascends along the **outer forearm**, enters the **outer elbow**, follows the **outer upper arm**, ascends to the **shoulder**, emerges before the **acromion**, ascends to the **cervical spine’s junction**, descends into the **suprasternal fossa** to network with the **lungs**, passes the diaphragm to belong to the **large intestine**. Its branch: from the **suprasternal fossa**, it ascends the neck, pierces the **cheek**, enters the **lower teeth**, re-emerges beside the mouth, crosses the **philtrum** (left to right, right to left), and ascends beside the **nostrils**.

When this channel is disturbed, there is **toothache and neck swelling**. It governs diseases of the **body fluids**: **jaundiced eyes**, **dry mouth**, **lockjaw**, **hoarseness**, **pain at the outer shoulder**, and **second finger dysfunction**.

When **qi** is excessive, the regions it passes become **hot and swollen**; when deficient, there is **chill and numbness**. For these ailments: if excessive, **bleed it**; if deficient, **supplement it**; if hot, **rapidly**; if cold, **retain the needle**; if **sunken and weak**, **moxibustion**; if neither excessive nor deficient, **follow the channel’s pathway**. When excessive, the **carotid pulse** is three times larger than the **radial**; when deficient, the **carotid** is smaller than the **radial**.

**The Stomach (Foot-Yangming) Channel** arises at the **nasal philtrum’s junction**, descends along the **outer nose**, enters the **upper teeth**, re-emerges beside the mouth, encircles the **lips**, descends to meet **Chengjiang**, then follows the **posterior jaw edge**, exits at **Daying**, ascends along the **cheekbone**, passes before the **ear**, follows the **hairline**, reaching the **forehead and crown**. Its branch: from **Daying**, it descends to **Renying**, follows the **throat**, enters the **suprasternal fossa**, descends the diaphragm to belong to the **stomach** and network with the **spleen**. Its direct course: from the **suprasternal fossa**, it descends along the **inner breast**, passes beside the **umbilicus**, enters **Qijie**. Its branch: arising from the **stomach’s orifice**, it descends along the **abdomen**, reaching **Qijie** to converge, then descends to **Hegu**, reaches **Futu**, descends to the **knee crease**, follows the **outer leg**, enters the **dorsum of the foot**, and penetrates between the **second and third toes**. Its branch: three **cun** below the knee, it diverges to enter the **outer side of the second toe**; another branch: from the **dorsum of the foot**, it enters the **big toe’s junction**, exiting at its tip.

When this channel is disturbed, there is **shivering**, **frequent sighing**, **fear of people and fire**, **startling at wooden sounds**, **restlessness**, **seclusion in darkened rooms**, and in severe cases, **climbing heights to sing**, **discarding clothes and fleeing**, **abdominal distension**—this is **brother tetany**. It governs diseases of the **blood**: **madness**, **intermittent fever**, **excessive sweating**, **lockjaw**, **dry throat**, **lips and gums**, **neck swelling**, **hoarseness**, **abdominal water retention**, **knee pain**, **pain along the **chest, breast, umbilicus, thigh, Futu, outer leg, and dorsum of the foot**, and **second toe dysfunction**.

When **qi** is excessive, the **front of the body is hot**; if excessive in the **stomach**, there is **rapid digestion and hunger**, and **yellow urine**. When **qi** is deficient, the **front of the body is cold**; if the **stomach is cold**, there is **distension and fullness**. For these ailments: if excessive, **bleed it**; if deficient, **supplement it**; if hot, **rapidly**; if cold, **retain the needle**; if **sunken and weak**, **moxibustion**; if neither excessive nor deficient, **follow the channel’s pathway**. When excessive, the **carotid pulse** is three times larger than the **radial**; when deficient, the **carotid** is smaller than the **radial**.

**The Spleen (Foot-Taiyin) Channel** arises at the **big toe’s tip**, follows its **inner edge**, passes behind the **metatarsal bone**, ascends before the **inner ankle**, reaches **Ding** (the **medial malleolus**), follows the **tibial crest**, emerges before the **Spleen (Foot-Jueyin) Channel**, ascends the **inner thigh**, enters the **abdomen to belong to the spleen** and network with the **stomach**, ascends the diaphragm, clings to the **throat**, connects to the **root of the tongue**, and disperses beneath the **tongue**..."

Its branch diverges again from the stomach, ascends the diaphragm, and pours into the heart.

When this channel is disturbed, it causes **pain at the root of the tongue**, vomiting upon eating, **epigastric pain**, abdominal distension with frequent belching, and relief upon passing gas, though the body feels heavy. These are the diseases originating from the spleen: **pain at the root of the tongue**, inability to move the body, indigestion, restlessness of the heart, **substernal distress**, **palpitation**, **diarrhea**, **urinary retention**, **jaundice**, inability to sweat, **rigid stiffness of the thighs and knees with internal swelling**, and **paralysis of the big toe**.

For these diseases: if excessive, **bleed it**; if deficient, **tonify it**; if hot, **vent it rapidly**; if cold, **retain it**; if sunken and weak, **moxibustion**; if neither excessive nor deficient, **follow the channel’s pathway**. When excessive, the **radial pulse** is three times larger than the **carotid**; when deficient, the **radial** is smaller than the **carotid**.

**The Heart (Hand-Shaoyin) Channel** arises in the heart, emerges to belong to the **heart system**, descends the diaphragm, and networks with the small intestine. Its branch ascends from the heart system, clings to the throat, and connects to the **ocular system**. Its direct course returns from the heart system to the lungs, descends, and exits beneath the armpit, then follows the **inner side of the upper arm**, passing behind the **Heart (Hand-Jueyin) Channel**, descending to the inner elbow, along the **inner posterior side of the arm**, reaching the **hook bone** at the wrist, entering the **inner posterior side of the palm**, and emerging at the tip of the little finger.

When this channel is disturbed, it causes **chest constriction**, **thirst with a desire to drink**, known as **arm spasm**. These are the diseases originating from the heart: **yellowing of the eyes**, **hypochondriac pain**, **pain and spasm along the inner posterior side of the upper arm**, and **heat and pain in the palm**.

For these diseases: if excessive, **bleed it**; if deficient, **tonify it**; if hot, **vent it rapidly**; if cold, **retain it**; if sunken and weak, **moxibustion**; if neither excessive nor deficient, **follow the channel’s pathway**. When excessive, the **radial pulse** is twice as large as the **carotid**; when deficient, the **radial** is smaller than the **carotid**.

**The Small Intestine (Hand-Taiyang) Channel** arises at the tip of the little finger, follows the **outer side of the hand** to the wrist, exits the **ankle’s midpoint**, ascends along the **lower edge of the humerus**, exits between the two tendons at the inner elbow, ascends along the **outer posterior side of the upper arm**, exits the **shoulder joint**, encircles the **scapula**, crosses above the shoulder, enters the **suprasternal fossa**, networks with the heart, follows the throat, descends the diaphragm, and reaches the stomach to belong to the small intestine. Its branch ascends from the suprasternal fossa along the neck to the cheek, reaching the **outer canthus**, then enters the ear. Another branch diverges upward from the cheek to the nose, reaching the **inner canthus**, and obliquely networks across the face.

When disturbed, it causes **diseases of the jaw**—**lockjaw**, **chin swelling**, inability to turn the head, **shoulder sensation as if pulled**, and **upper arm sensation as if broken**.

It governs **fluids** and their disorders: **deafness, jaundiced eyes, swollen cheeks, pain in the neck, chin, shoulder, elbow, and outer posterior upper arm**.

For these ailments:
- If **excess**, **bleed** it;
- If **deficient**, **supplement** it;
- If **hot**, **rapidly** needle it;
- If **cold**, **slowly** needle it;
- If **sunken**, **moxibustion** is applied.
When neither excess nor deficient, **follow the channel’s course** for treatment.
**Excess** is when **Rényīng** is twice as large as **Cùnkǒu**; **deficiency** is when **Rényīng** is smaller than **Cùnkǒu**.

---
The **Bladder Channel of the Foot, Taiyang**, arises at the **inner canthus**, ascends the forehead to meet at the crown.
Its branch goes from the crown to the **upper ear angle**.
Its direct course enters to network the **brain**, then descends separately at the nape, follows the **inner shoulder blade**, hugs the spine to the **lumbar center**, enters to follow the **loins**, networks the **kidneys**, and belongs to the **bladder**.
Its branch descends from the lumbar center, hugs the spine through the **buttocks**, enters the **perineum**.
Its other branch diverges from the inner shoulder blade, left and right, descends to pierce the **scapula**, hugs the spine’s interior, passes the **elbow hinge**, follows the **posterior flank**, and converges at the **perineum**. Below, it pierces the **inner hamstring**, exits behind the **external ankle**, follows the **Achilles tendon**, reaching the **outer side of the little toe**.

When disturbed, it causes **frontal headache**, **eyes as if dislodged**, **nape sensation as if pulled**, **spinal pain**, **lumbar sensation as if broken**, and **inability to bend the loins**.
**Perineal constriction**, **hamstring sensation as if split**—this is **ankle tetany**.

It governs **tendon disorders**: **hemorrhoids, malaria, mania, epilepsy, convulsions, headache, nape stiffness, jaundiced eyes with excessive tearing and sighing, pain in the neck, back, lumbar, loins, buttocks, hamstrings, and feet**; **little toe dysfunction**.

For these ailments:
- If **excess**, **bleed** it;
- If **deficient**, **supplement** it;
- If **hot**, **rapidly** needle it;
- If **cold**, **slowly** needle it;
- If **sunken**, **moxibustion** is applied.
When neither excess nor deficient, **follow the channel’s course** for treatment.
**Excess** is when **Rényīng** is twice as large as **Cùnkǒu**; **deficiency** is when **Rényīng** is smaller than **Cùnkǒu**.

---
The **Kidney Channel of the Foot, Shàoyīn**, arises beneath the **little toe**, traverses the **sole**, exits below **Rángǔ**, follows behind the **inner ankle**, branches to enter the **heel**, ascends the **inner hamstring**, exits the **inner flank**, follows the **inner posterior thigh**, pierces the **spine**, and belongs to the **kidneys**, networking the **bladder**.
Its direct course ascends from the kidneys, pierces the **liver and diaphragm**, enters the **lungs**, follows the **throat**, hugging the **root of the tongue**.
Its branch emerges from the lungs to network the **heart**, descending to the **chest**.

When disturbed, it causes **hunger without desire to eat**, **face as if charred**, **hemoptysis upon coughing**, **labored breathing**, **restlessness while seated**, **blurred vision as if seeing nothing**, **heart sensation as if suspended in hunger**, **fearfulness from insufficient qi**, **palpitations as if about to be seized**—this is **bone tetany**.

It governs **kidney disorders**: **dry mouth and tongue, swollen throat with upward-rising breath, dry lockjaw and pain, vexation, heart pain, jaundice, intestinal obstruction, pain in the inner posterior spine and thigh, flaccidity, tetany, excessive sleep, and heat/pain beneath the feet**.

For these ailments:
- If **excess**, **bleed** it;
- If **deficient**, **supplement** it;
- If **hot**, **rapidly** needle it;
- If **cold**, **slowly** needle it;
- If **sunken**, **moxibustion** is applied.
When neither excess nor deficient, **follow the channel’s course** for treatment.
**Moxibustion** requires: **eating raw meat voraciously, loosening the belt, disheveling the hair, using heavy staffs and cumbersome footwear**.

**Excess** is when **Cùnkǒu** is twice as large as **Rényīng**; **deficiency** is when **Cùnkǒu** is smaller than **Rényīng**.

---
The **Heart’s Channel of the Hand, Juéyīn (Heart Envelope)**, arises in the **chest**, emerges to belong to the **heart envelope**, descends the **diaphragm**, and networks the **Sanjiao**.

Its branch follows the **chest**, exits the **ribcage**, descends three **cun** below the **armpit**, ascends to the **armpit**, descends along the **inner upper arm**, travels between the **Taiyin and Shàoyīn channels**, enters the **elbow**, descends the **arm between the two tendons**, enters the **palm**, and follows the **middle finger to its tip**.
Its other branch diverges from the **palm**, following the **second finger to its tip**.

When disturbed, it causes **palm heat**, **arm and elbow contraction**, **armpit swelling**, and severely, **ribcage and chest fullness**, **heart sensation as if pounding violently**, **flushed face and jaundiced eyes**, **incessant laughter**.

It governs **channel disorders**: **vexation, heart pain, palm heat**.

For these ailments:
- If **excess**, **bleed** it;
- If **deficient**, **supplement** it;
- If **hot**, **rapidly** needle it;
- If **cold**, **slowly** needle it;
- If **sunken**, **moxibustion** is applied.
When neither excess nor deficient, **follow the channel’s course** for treatment.
**Excess** is when **Cùnkǒu** is **once as large as Rényīng**; **deficiency** is when **Cùnkǒu** is smaller than **Rényīng**.

---
The **Sanjiao Channel of the Hand, Shàoyang**, arises at the **second finger’s tip**, ascends between the two fingers, follows the **outer wrist**, exits between the two forearm bones, ascends through the **elbow**, follows the **outer upper arm**, reaches the **shoulder**, and emerges behind the **Foot Shàoyang channel**, entering the **suprasternal fossa**, spreading across the **chest**, dispersing to the **heart envelope**, descending the **diaphragm**, and belonging to the **Sanjiao**.
Its branch ascends from the **chest**, exits the **suprasternal fossa**, ascends the **nape**, binds behind the **ear**, ascends straight to the **upper ear angle**, then curves downward to the **cheek**.
Its other branch enters the **ear from behind**, exits forward, passes **in front of the ear**, crosses the **cheek**, reaching the **outer canthus**.

When this channel is disturbed, it causes **deafness with a dull, muffled sound** (*hūn hūn dūn dūn*), **hoarseness, throat obstruction, and swollen throat**. These are diseases arising from **qi imbalance**, including **profuse sweating, pain at the outer canthus, cheek pain, and pain along the posterior ear, shoulder, upper arm, elbow, and outer forearm**, rendering the **second and third fingers useless**.

For these ailments, if the channel is **excess**, **bleed it**; if **deficient**, **tonify it**; if **hot**, **move it rapidly**; if **cold**, **retain the needle**; if **sunken**, **moxibust**. When neither excess nor deficient, **select points along the channel**. Excess is indicated when **Rén Yíng** pulses **twice as large as Cùn Kǒu**; deficiency when **Rén Yíng is smaller than Cùn Kǒu**.

The **Foot Shàoyang (Gallbladder) channel** originates at the **outer canthus**, ascends to the **temporal angle**, descends behind the ear, follows the neck **anterior to the Hand Shàoyang channel**, reaches the shoulder, then **crosses posterior to the Hand Shàoyang**, entering the **suprasternal fossa**. Its branch enters the ear from behind, exits forward, and reaches the **outer canthus**. Another branch diverges from the canthus, descends to **Dà Yíng**, converges with the **Hand Shàoyang**, reaches **Shùn**, descends to **Jiè Chē**, then follows the neck to merge below the **suprasternal fossa** in the chest, piercing the **diaphragm**, connecting to the **liver**, and belonging to the **gallbladder**. It courses along the **ribcage**, exits at **Qì Jiē**, encircles the **pubic hairline**, and horizontally enters the **□ Yàn** (missing character). Its direct path descends from the **suprasternal fossa** through the armpit, follows the chest, passes the **lower ribs**, and descends to merge in the **□ Yàn**, then follows the **□ Yáng**, exiting the **outer knee**, descending anterior to the **fibular head**, reaching the **end of Jué Gǔ**, exiting anterior to the **external malleolus**, ascending the **dorsum of the foot**, and entering between the **fourth and fifth toes**.

When disturbed, it causes **bitter taste in the mouth, frequent sighing, heart and rib pain preventing turning, and in severe cases, a dusty complexion, loss of luster, and external foot heat**—this is **Yang Syncope**. These are diseases arising from **bone imbalance**, including **headache, jaw pain, outer canthus pain, suprasternal swelling and pain, armpit swelling, flank atrophy, sweating with chills, malaria, chest and rib pain extending from the lower ribs to the outer knee, external malleolus, and all joints**, rendering the **second and third fingers useless**.

For these ailments, if the channel is **excess**, **bleed it**; if **deficient**, **tonify it**; if **hot**, **move it rapidly**; if **cold**, **retain the needle**; if **sunken**, **moxibust**. When neither excess nor deficient, **select points along the channel**. Excess is indicated when **Rén Yíng** pulses **twice as large as Cùn Kǒu**; deficiency when **Rén Yíng is smaller than Cùn Kǒu**.

The **Foot Jièyīn (Liver) channel** originates at the **hairy base of the big toe**, ascends along the **inner ankle**, eight *cùn* above the ankle, crossing posterior to the **Foot Tàiyīn**, following the **inner thigh**, entering the **pubic hair**, passing the **genitals**, reaching the **lower abdomen**, flanking the **stomach**, belonging to the **liver**, and connecting to the **gallbladder**. It ascends through the **diaphragm**, spreads across the **ribs**, follows the **posterior throat**, enters the **occiput**, connects to the **eye system**, ascends to the **forehead**, and converges with the **Governing Vessel at the crown**. Its branch descends from the **eye system** along the **inner cheek**, encircling the **inner lip**. Another branch diverges from the liver, pierces the **diaphragm**, and ascends to the **lungs**.

When disturbed, it causes **lumbar pain preventing flexion/extension, male inguinal hernia, female lower abdominal swelling, and in severe cases, mutism and dryness, with a dusty complexion and loss of color**. These are diseases arising from **liver imbalance**, including **chest fullness, vomiting, diarrhea, scrotal hernia, urinary incontinence, and retention**.

For these ailments, if the channel is **excess**, **bleed it**; if **deficient**, **tonify it**; if **hot**, **move it rapidly**; if **cold**, **retain the needle**; if **sunken**, **moxibust**. When neither excess nor deficient, **select points along the channel**. Excess is indicated when **Cùn Kǒu** pulses **twice as large as Rén Yíng**; deficiency when **Cùn Kǒu is smaller than Rén Yíng**.

If the **Hand Tàiyīn (Lung) channel’s qi is severed**, the **skin and hair wither**, for the **Tàiyīn** **warms the skin and hair with qi**. Thus, if qi fails to nourish, the **skin and hair wither**, and **fluids depart from the joints**, causing **brittle nails and hair breakage**. Hair breakage means **hair dies first**, and by **Ding (4th Heavenly Stem)**, death follows—**Fire overcomes Metal**.

If the **Hand Shàoyīn (Heart) channel’s qi is severed**, the **pulse does not flow**, and **blood does not circulate**, leading to **dull complexion**. Thus, if the face turns **black like lacquered wood**, **blood dies first**, and by **Gǔi (10th Heavenly Stem)**, death follows—**Water overcomes Fire**.

If the **Foot Tàiyīn (Spleen) channel’s qi is severed**, the **pulse fails to nourish the muscles**, for the **tongue is the root of the muscles**. If the pulse does not nourish, the **muscles soften**, causing **tongue atrophy and sublingual fullness**, leading to **inverted lips**. Inverted lips mean **muscle dies first**, and by **Yǐ (2nd Heavenly Stem)**, death follows—**Wood overcomes Earth**.

If the **Foot Shàoyīn (Kidney) channel’s qi is severed**, the **bones wither**, for the **Shàoyīn** is the **winter pulse**, **deep and moistening the marrow**. Thus, if the bones are not moistened, **flesh cannot adhere**, causing **soft, receding flesh**, **prolonged teeth with plaque**, and **dull hair**. Dull hair means **bone dies first**, and by **Jǐ (6th Heavenly Stem)**, death follows—**Earth overcomes Water**.

If the **Foot Jièyīn (Liver) channel’s qi is severed**, the **tendons are severed**, for the **Jièyīn** is the **liver pulse**, and the **liver governs the tendons**. Tendons gather at **Yīn qi**, and their vessels connect to the **root of the tongue**. Thus, if the pulse does not nourish, the **tendons contract**, drawing the **tongue and testicles upward**, causing **blue lips, curled tongue, and retracted testicles**. This means **tendons die first**, and by **Xīn (8th Heavenly Stem)**, death follows—**Metal overcomes Wood**.

If the **five Yin channels’ qi are all severed**, the **eye system rotates**, causing **eye movement**, and **mind (zhì) dies first**. If mind dies first, death follows **within half a day**.

If the **six Yang channels’ qi are severed**, the **Yin separates from the intestines**, causing **uncontrolled elimination and diarrhea**. When qi is severed, **profuse sweating occurs**, so if diagnosed in the **morning, death comes by evening**; if diagnosed in the **evening, death comes the next day**.

The **twelve regular channels** run **deep between the muscles**, unseen.

The **regular channels** that are commonly visible are those of the **Foot Taiyin** extending above the ankle, where they have no place to conceal themselves.

All **floating pulses** that are constantly visible belong to the **collateral vessels**. The **great collaterals** of the **Six Meridians**—**Hand Yangming** and **Hand Shaoyang**—arise between the fingers and ascend to join the elbow’s center.

Those who drink alcohol have their **defensive qi** first circulating through the skin, filling the **collaterals** first. When the **collaterals** are full, the **defensive qi** has already balanced, and the **nutritive qi** then fills the **regular channels**, making them greatly prominent.

When a **pulse suddenly moves**, it is due to **pathogenic qi** residing there, lingering at its origin or extremity. If it does not move, there is **heat**; if it is not firm, it will sink and become hollow, differing from the norm—thus one knows which **pulse is affected**.

**Leigong** asked: *"How do we distinguish between the **regular channels** and the **collaterals**?"*
The **Yellow Emperor** replied: *"The **regular channels** are normally unseen. Their fullness or emptiness is known through the **guqi** (wrist pulse). All visible pulses are **collaterals**."*

**Leigong** said: *"Your words are unclear. Explain further."*
The **Yellow Emperor** replied: *"All **collaterals** cannot traverse major joints. They must follow indirect paths to emerge, then re-enter and merge within the skin. Their junctions are visible externally. Thus, when puncturing **collaterals**, one must strike above their knots. If blood is profuse even without knots, quickly drain it to expel the pathogenic influence and release the blood—otherwise, it will linger and cause **bi syndrome** (obstruction).

When diagnosing **collaterals**, if the pulse is **green**, there is **cold and pain**; if **red**, there is **heat**. If the **stomach is cold**, the **collaterals of the Hand Yu** (Little Finger) will appear **green**; if the **stomach has heat**, the **collaterals of the Hand Yu** will be **red**. If they turn **violently black**, it indicates **prolonged obstruction**. If they show **red, black, and green**, it is **cold and heat qi**. If they are **short and green**, it signifies **weak qi**.

When treating **cold and heat**, the **collaterals** will be blood-rich. One must ask the patient to **inhale deeply** before puncturing, draining until the blood is exhausted, then adjusting the **fullness or emptiness**. If the **collateral is small and short**, the **qi is weak**; if drained excessively, the patient will feel **oppressed**. If asked to inhale deeply, they may faint and be unable to speak. If they feel **oppressed**, have them sit upright immediately.

The **branch of Hand Taiyin** is called **Lieque**, arising above the wrist between the tendons, running parallel to the **Taiyin channel** into the palm, then dispersing into the **Yuji** (Little Finger) region. If **full**, the hand is **sharp and hot**; if **empty**, there is **yawns and sighs**, frequent **urination**, and **enuresis**. Puncture it **half an inch from the wrist**, where it diverges toward **Yangming**.

The **branch of Hand Shaoyin** is called **Tongli**, located **one and a half inches from the wrist**, diverging upward along the channel into the **heart**, connecting to the **root of the tongue** and the **eye system**. If **full**, it causes **chest oppression**; if **empty**, speech is **impaired**. Puncture it **one inch behind the palm**, where it diverges toward **Taiyang**.

The **branch of Hand Jinxin** (Pericardium) is called **Neiguan**, located **two inches from the wrist**, emerging between the two tendons, ascending along the channel to strike the **heart wrapper**, connecting to the **heart system**. If **full**, there is **heart pain**; if **empty**, **stiff neck**. Puncture it between the two tendons.

The **branch of Hand Taiyang** is called **Zhizheng**, located **five inches above the wrist**, internally connecting to **Shaoyin**. Its divergence ascends to the **elbow**, collateraling the **shoulder joint**. If **full**, the **joints slacken and the elbow is impaired**; if **empty**, **warts** form—small ones like **finger scabs or scabies**. Puncture it at its divergence.

The **branch of Hand Yangming** is called **Pianli**, located **three inches from the wrist**, diverging into **Taiyin**. Its divergence ascends along the arm, passing the **shoulder joint**, curving to the **cheek and side teeth**; another divergence enters the **ear**, merging with the **master vessel**. If **full**, there is **tooth decay and deafness**; if **empty**, **tooth cold, obstruction, and numbness**. Puncture it at its divergence.

The **branch of Hand Shaoyang** is called **Waiguan**, located **two inches from the wrist**, wrapping externally around the arm, entering the **chest**, and merging with **Jinxin**. If **full**, the **elbow contracts**; if **empty**, it **does not flex**. Puncture it at its divergence.

The **branch of Foot Taiyang** is called **Feiyang**, located **seven inches from the ankle**, diverging toward **Shaoyin**. If **full**, there is **sighing, suffocation, and head/back pain**; if **empty**, **yawns and sighs**. Puncture it at its divergence.

The **branch of Foot Shaoyang** is called **Guangming**, located **five inches from the ankle**, diverging toward **Jueyin**, descending to collateral the **foot**. If **full**, there is **syncope**; if **empty**, **flaccidity and weakness**, making it difficult to rise while seated. Puncture it at its divergence.

The **branch of Foot Yangming** is called **Fenglong**, located **eight inches from the ankle**, diverging toward **Taiyin**. Its divergence follows the **outer edge of the shinbone**, ascending to collateral the **head and nape**, merging with the **qi of all channels**, descending to collateral the **throat**. If **qi reverses**, there is **throat obstruction and emaciation**; if **full**, **mania and epilepsy**; if **empty**, **foot paralysis and channel exhaustion**. Puncture it at its divergence.

The **branch of Foot Taiyin** is called **Gongsun**, located **one inch behind the base joint**, diverging toward **Yangming**. Its divergence enters to collateral the **intestines and stomach**. If **Jue qi reverses**, there is **acute diarrhea**; if **full**, **sharp intestinal pain**; if **empty**, **distension and bloating**. Puncture it at its divergence.

The **branch of Foot Shaoyin** is called **Dazhong**, located behind the **ankle**, wrapping around the **heel**, diverging toward **Taiyang**. Its divergence ascends along the channel to the **heart wrapper**, descending externally through the **lumbar and spine**. If **qi reverses**, there is **restlessness and oppression**; if **full**, **constipation**; if **empty**, **lumbar pain**. Puncture it at its divergence.

The **branch of Foot Jueyin** is called **Ligou**, located **five inches from the inner ankle**, diverging toward **Shaoyang**. Its divergence ascends directly along the channel to the **testicles**, knotting at the **penis**. If **qi reverses**, there is **sudden testicular swelling and hernia**; if **full**, **erection**; if **empty**, **sudden itching**. Puncture it at its divergence.

The **branch of the Conception Vessel** is called **Weiyi**, descending from the **coccyx**, dispersing into the **abdomen**. If **full**, there is **abdominal skin pain**; if **empty**, **itching and scratching**. Puncture it at its divergence.

The **branch of the Governing Vessel** is called **Changqiang**, flanking the **loins**, ascending the **nape**, dispersing over the **head**, descending to the **shoulder blades**, diverging toward **Taiyang**, and penetrating the **loins**. If **full**, the **back stiffens**; if **empty**, the **head feels heavy**. If the patient **shakes their head high**, or if there is **excessive pressure on the spine**, puncture it at its divergence.

The **Spleen’s great collateral**, named **Dàbāo**, emerges three *cùn* below the **Yuānyè armpit**, spreading across the **chest and ribs**. When **full**, the entire body aches; when **empty**, all joints relax excessively. These **collaterals**—like the **networking blood** of the Spleen—are all punctured at the **Spleen’s great collateral**.

In general, these **fifteen collaterals**: when **full**, their symptoms manifest visibly; when **empty**, they descend. If unseen, seek them **above and below**—for **primary channels** and **collateral divergences** differ in their paths.

---
**Phonetic Glosses:**
- *Piè* (瞥) – *wù* (务)
- *Shùn* (顺) – *liè* (劣切)
- *Kē* (髁) – *dān* (单)
- *Gē* (哥) – *zǎo* (早)
- *Yán* (檐) – *dàn* (淡)
- *Yé* (邪) – same as *xié* (斜)
- *Chénchén* (惇惇) – *túhún* (土浑切)
- *Guāng* (胱) – *yóu* (由)

The Yellow Emperor asked Qībó: "I have heard that when humans align with the Dao of Heaven, internally they have the five **Zàng** (internal organs), corresponding to the five tones, five colors, five seasons, five flavors, and five positions; externally they have the six **Fǔ** (external organs), corresponding to the six **Lǜ** (musical pitches). The six **Lǜ** establish the **primary channels** of **Yīn** and **Yáng**, harmonizing with the twelve months, twelve **shén** (earthly branches), twelve **jié** (solar terms), twelve **jīngshuǐ** (channel waters), and twelve times. The twelve **primary channels** are the source of life, the origin of illness, the means of healing, the cause of disease, the beginning of study, and the end of skill—easy for the coarse, difficult for the refined.

Please explain their **separation and convergence**, their **entering and exiting**?

Qībó bowed twice with his head to the ground and said: "How clear is your question! This is what the coarse overlook and the refined pause upon. Let me now complete my explanation.

The **primary channel** of the **Foot Tàiyáng** (Large Intestine) diverges and enters the **wèi** (popliteal region). One branch descends five *cùn* below the **shī** (perineum), diverges and enters the **gāng** (anus), connects with the **Pángguāng** (Bladder), and disperses through the **shèn** (kidneys). It follows the **chénchén** (lateral thoracic vessels) to the heart and disperses there. The direct path rises from the **chénchén**, exits at the **jǐng** (nape), and reconnects with the **Foot Tàiyáng**—this completes one **primary channel**.

The **primary channel** of the **Foot Shàoyīn** (Kidney) reaches the **wèi**, then diverges and converges with the **Foot Tàiyáng**. It ascends to the **shèn**, at the **shí-sì qīng** (14th vertebra), exits and connects with the **Dàimài** (Girdle Vessel). The direct path binds to the **shēnběn** (root of the tongue), then exits at the **jǐng**, converging with the **Foot Tàiyáng**—this completes one **convergence**.

All divergences of the **Yīn** channels are their **primary channels**.

The **primary channel** of the **Foot Shàoyáng** (Gallbladder) ascends to the **yán** (axillary hairline), enters between the **jìxià** (flanks), follows the chest, connects with the **dǎn** (Gallbladder), and disperses through the **shànggān** (upper liver) to the heart. It then ascends, clasping the **yàn** (pharynx), exits at the **yíèrìngzhōng** (mid-cheek), disperses across the face, and binds to the **mùjìng** (eyelid tendons), converging with the **Foot Shàoyáng** at the **wàipì** (external armpit).

The **primary channel** of the **Foot Juéyīn** (Liver) diverges at the **kē** (upper thigh), ascends to the **máojì** (hairline), and converges with the **Foot Shàoyáng**, traveling alongside its divergence—this completes the second **convergence**.

The **primary channel** of the **Foot Yángmíng** (Stomach) ascends to the **yán**, enters the **fùlǐ** (abdominal cavity), and connects with the **wèi** (Stomach). It disperses through the **pí** (Spleen), ascends to the heart, follows the **yàn** to the mouth, and then proceeds upward along the **shùn** (esophagus), returning to bind the **mùjìng**, converging with the **Foot Yángmíng**.

The **primary channel** of the **Foot Tàiyīn** (Spleen) ascends to the **yán**, converging with the **Foot Yángmíng**, and travels alongside its divergence, ascending to bind at the **yàn**, piercing through the **shějīngzhōng** (tongue)—this completes the third **convergence**.

The **primary channel** of the **Hand Tàiyáng** (Small Intestine) points downward to the earth, diverges at the **jiāngè** (shoulder joint), enters the **yè** (axilla), and follows the heart, striking the **xiǎochángjiǎng** (Small Intestine).

The **primary channel** of the **Hand Shàoyīn** (Heart) diverges and enters between the **yuān yè** (two axillary tendons), connecting with the heart, ascending to the **hóulǎng** (throat), and exiting at the face, converging with the **mùnèipì** (inner eyelid)—this completes the fourth **convergence**.

The **primary channel** of the **Hand Shàoyáng** (San Jiao/Triple Burner) points upward to the sky, diverges at the **diān** (vertex), enters the **quēpén** (suprasternal fossa), descends to follow the **Sānjiāo** (Triple Burner), and disperses through the chest.

The **primary channel** of the **Hand Xīnmài** (Pericardium) diverges and descends three *cùn* below the **yuān yè**, entering the chest, diverging to connect with the **Sānjiāo**, exiting along the **hóulǎng**, exiting behind the ear, and converging with the **Hand Shàoyáng** below the **wángǔ** (outer canthus)—this completes the fifth **convergence**.

The **primary channel** of the **Hand Yángmíng** (Large Intestine) follows the hand upward along the **yīngrǔ** (chest), diverges at the **jiānguā** (shoulder), enters below the **zhùgǔ** (vertebral column), follows the **dàcháng** (Large Intestine), and connects with the **fèi** (Lung). It ascends along the **hóulǎng**, exits at the **quēpén**, and converges with the **Hand Yángmíng**.

The **primary channel** of the **Hand Tàiyīn** (Lung) diverges and enters before the **yuān yè** of the **Hand Shàoyīn**, follows the **fèi** (Lung), and disperses through the **Hand Tàiyáng** above, exiting at the **quēpén**, ascending along the **hóulǎng**, and reconverging with the **Hand Yángmíng**—this completes the sixth **convergence**.

**Chapter Twelve: The Twelve Channels and Their Waters**

The Yellow Emperor asked Qìbó, saying:
*"The twelve channels, externally corresponding to the twelve channel-waters and internally connecting to the five viscera and six bowels—though their size, depth, breadth, and distance differ, and though the five viscera and six bowels vary in height, size, and capacity for receiving nourishment, how do they harmonize in correspondence?"*

Now, the channel-waters receive water and distribute it; the five viscera store the vital *qi*, spirit, soul, and will; the six bowels receive nourishment and transmit it, receiving *qi* to elevate it; and the channels receive blood to nourish it. How then should they be regulated in unison? What are the depths for acupuncture and the number of moxibustion applications?

Qìbó replied:
*"Excellent question! Heaven is supremely high, beyond measure; Earth is supremely vast, beyond reckoning. This is what is meant. Moreover, man is born between Heaven and Earth, within the six extremes—this is Heaven’s height and Earth’s breadth, beyond mortal capacity to fathom or attain.*

*Now, take a man of eight *chǐ* in stature: his flesh and skin are tangible, measurable by touch and sight. Upon death, his viscera can be dissected to observe their firmness or fragility, the size of his bowels, the quantity of nourishment received, the length of his channels, the clarity of his blood, the abundance of his *qi*—whether the twelve channels have more blood and less *qi*, or less blood and more *qi*, or whether they are uniformly abundant or uniformly deficient—all follow a fixed principle. When treating with needles and moxa, one must adjust the channel-*qi* accordingly. Is this not the constant harmony?"*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"I have heard this with delight, yet my understanding remains unclear. I wish to hear it fully."*

Qìbó answered:
*"This is how man aligns with Heaven and Earth, responding to yin and yang—it must be carefully examined.*

*The Foot Tàiyáng externally corresponds to **Qīngshuǐ** (Clear Water) and internally connects to the **pángguāng** (Bladder), through which the waterways flow. The Foot Shàoyáng corresponds to **Wèishuǐ** (Wei Water) and connects to the **dǎn** (Gallbladder). The Foot Yángmíng corresponds to **Hǎishuǐ** (Sea Water) and connects to the **wèi** (Stomach). The Foot Tàiyīn corresponds to **Húshuǐ** (Lake Water) and connects to the **pí** (Spleen). The Foot Shàoyīn corresponds to **Rǔshuǐ** (Ru Water) and connects to the **shèn** (Kidney). The Foot Juéyīn corresponds to **Miánshuǐ** (Mian Water) and connects to the **gān** (Liver).*

*The Hand Tàiyáng corresponds to **Huáishuǐ** (Huai Water) and connects to the **xiǎocháng** (Small Intestine), from which the waterways emerge. The Hand Shàoyáng corresponds to **Luòshuǐ** (Luo Water) and connects to the **sānjiāo** (Triple Burner). The Hand Yángmíng corresponds to **Jiāngshuǐ** (River Water) and connects to the **dàcháng** (Large Intestine). The Hand Tàiyīn corresponds to **Héshuǐ** (He Water) and connects to the **fèi** (Lung). The Hand Shàoyīn corresponds to **Jìshuǐ** (Ji Water) and connects to the **xīn** (Heart). The Hand Jīnmǎo corresponds to **Zhāngshuǐ** (Zhang Water) and connects to the **xīnbāo** (Pericardium).*

*Thus, these five viscera and six bowels, with their twelve channel-waters, have external sources and internal origins, all internally and externally interconnected like an unbroken ring. The same applies to the channels themselves.*

*Heaven is yang, Earth is yin; above the waist is Heaven, below the waist is Earth. Therefore, north of the Sea is yin; north of the Lake is yin within yin. South of the Zhang is yang; north of the He to the Zhang is yin within yang. South of the Luo to the River is the extreme yang. This is but one corner of yin and yang—how man aligns with Heaven and Earth."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"The channel-waters correspond to the channels in terms of distance, depth, and the abundance of water and blood—how should they be treated with acupuncture?"*

Qìbó replied:
*"The Foot Yángmíng is the sea of the five viscera and six bowels. Its channel is large, its blood abundant, its *qi* vigorous and hot. To treat it, the needle must penetrate deeply to disperse stagnation, yet must not linger to deplete it. The depth for Foot Yángmíng is six *fēn*, with retention for ten breaths. For Foot Tàiyáng, five *fēn*, seven breaths. For Foot Shàoyáng, four *fēn*, five breaths. For Foot Tàiyīn, three *fēn*, four breaths. For Foot Shàoyīn, two *fēn*, three breaths. For Foot Juéyīn, one *fēn*, two breaths.*

*For the Hand channels—both yin and yang—they receive *qi* swiftly and their *qi* arrives quickly. The needle depth must not exceed two *fēn*, and retention must not exceed one breath. Adjustments for variations in length, size, fatness, or thinness should follow the principle of *fǎ tiān zhī cháng* (conforming to Heaven’s constant). The same applies to moxibustion. Exceeding these measures invites the *èhuǒ* (malevolent fire), causing bones to wither and channels to stiffen; over-penetration with needles risks *tuōqì* (depleting *qi*)."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"Can the size of the channels, the abundance of blood, the thickness of the skin, the firmness of the flesh, and the size of the viscera be measured?"*

Qìbó replied:
*"What can be measured should be taken from the average. Do not excessively strip flesh while ensuring blood and *qi* remain unweakened. But for those who are emaciated, withering, and lacking flesh—how can one measure and puncture them? Instead, carefully palpate, press, and probe, observing their coldness, warmth, excess, or deficiency, and adjust accordingly. This is the true method of *yīnshì ér wéizhēn* (adapting to circumstances)."*

**Yellow Emperor’s Inner Classic – *Língshū* (Spiritual Pivot) – *Jīngjīn* (Muscle Tendons)**

**Volume VII: Muscle Tendons of the Foot**

**Foot Taiyang (Greater Yang) Muscle Tendon**
Rising from the upper side of the little toe, it converges at the ankle. Its branch ascends to the knee, where it binds. Below, it follows the outer side of the foot to the heel, then ascends along the calf, binding at the popliteal fossa.
Its divergence binds at the outer side of the heel, then ascends through the middle of the calf’s inner edge, binding with the popliteal fossa and ascending to the buttocks, then hugging the spine upward to the nape.
Its branch enters and binds at the root of the tongue.
Its direct path binds at the occipital bone, then spreads over the head and descends to the face, binding at the nose.
Its branch forms the upper eyelid’s network, descending to bind at the *shùn* (mandibular angle).
Its other branch, from behind the armpit’s outer edge, binds at the shoulder’s *kūlú* (acromion).
Its further branch enters beneath the armpit, ascends from the *quēpén* (supraclavicular fossa), and binds at the *wángǔ* (clavicle).
Its final branch emerges from the *quēpén*, ascending to bind at the *shùn* (mandibular angle).

**Its Diseases:**
- Spasm of the little toe,
- Swelling and pain at the heel,
- Popliteal cramps (*lǔlái*),
- Spinal rigidity,
- Stiffness of the nape tendons,
- Inability to raise the shoulder,
- Armpit spasms,
- Pain at the *quēpén*’s knot, rendering lateral movement impossible.

**Treatment:**
Burning-needle cauterization and pricking, counting by *zhī* (acupuncture points), targeting pain as the *shū* (output). Named *Zhòngchūn bì* (Mid-Spring Paralysis).

---

**Foot Shaoyang (Lesser Yang) Muscle Tendon**
Rising from the second toe, it binds at the outer ankle, ascending along the outer edge of the leg to bind at the outer edge of the knee.
Its branch diverges from the outer *fǔgǔ* (ankle bone), ascending to the *wángǔ* (clavicle), with the anterior binding above the *fútù* (patella) and the posterior at the buttocks.
Its direct path ascends to the *shā* (lateral ribs), then along the front of the armpit’s edge, connecting to the chest’s *yīngrǔ* (breast), binding at the *quēpén*.
Straightforward, it ascends from the armpit, piercing the *quēpén*, emerging before the Taiyang path, following behind the ear, ascending to the temple, crossing the crown, descending to the jaw, and binding at the *shùn* (mandibular angle).
Its branch binds at the outer corner of the eye as the *wéiwéi* (outer canthus).

**Its Diseases:**
- Spasm of the second toe,
- Knee rotation spasm, rendering flexion/extension impossible,
- Tightness of the *bāng* (viscera) tendons, pulling the knee’s anterior and posterior,
- Ascending pain at the *shā* (lateral ribs),
- Drawing the *quēpén*, chest, and neck taut,
- Tightness of the *wéiwéi* (canthus) tendons, shifting from left to right,
- Right eye unable to open,
- Ascending to the right temple, merging with the *jiāo* (temporal) pulse, linking left to right—thus injuring the left temple, rendering the right foot unusable. Named *Wéijīn xiāngjiāo* (Intertwined Restraint Tendons).

**Treatment:**
Burning-needle cauterization and pricking, counting by *zhī*, targeting pain as the *shū*. Named *Mèngchūn bì* (Early-Spring Paralysis).

---

**Foot Yangming (Bright Yang) Muscle Tendon**
Rising from the three central toes, it binds above the *fú* (ankle), then externally ascends to bind at the outer edge of the knee, straightening upward to bind at the *kēshū* (knee joint), ascending along the ribs to attach to the spine.
Its direct path ascends along the *gē* (rib), binding at the knee.
Its branch binds at the outer *fǔgǔ*, merging with Shaoyang.
Its direct path ascends along the *fútù*, binding at the *hái* (pelvis), converging at the genitals, spreading across the abdomen to the *quēpén*, ascending the neck, hugging the mouth, merging at the *shùn* (mandibular angle), descending to bind at the nose, then uniting with Taiyang as the upper eyelid’s network and Yangming as the lower eyelid’s network.
Its branch binds from the cheek to the front of the ear.

**Its Diseases:**
- Spasm of the middle toe,
- Foot stiffness and rigidity,
- *Fútù* spasm,
- Anterior swelling, hernia (*chuāngshàn*),
- Abdominal tendon tightness, drawing the *quēpén* and cheek,
- Sudden jaw deviation (*piè*),
- Severe cases: inability to close the eyes; heat causes tendon laxity, preventing eye opening.
- Cold in the cheek tendons draws the cheek and deviates the mouth;
- Heat relaxes the tendons, preventing contraction, causing deviation (*piè*).

**Treatment:**
Apply *mǎgāo* (horse grease) to the taut tendons. Mix white wine with *guì* (cinnamon) to rub the lax tendons. Use *sānggōu* (mulberry fishhook) to hook them, placing live mulberry ash in a pit, adjusting height to sit upon, and applying the grease to warm the taut cheek while drinking fine wine and eating fine roasted meat. Those who abstain from wine must force themselves—three sessions suffice.
Additionally, burning-needle cauterization and pricking, counting by *zhī*, targeting pain as the *shū*. Named *Jìchūn bì* (Late-Spring Paralysis).

---

**Foot Taiyin (Greater Yin) Muscle Tendon**
Rising from the inner side of the big toe’s tip, it binds at the inner ankle.
Its direct path connects to the inner *fǔgǔ* of the knee, ascending along the inner thigh to bind at the *yīn* (genital region), converging at the genitals, ascending the abdomen to bind at the navel, following the abdominal interior to bind at the ribs, dispersing in the chest, and attaching to the spine.

**Its Diseases:**
- Spasm of the big toe,
- Inner ankle pain,
- Knee rotation spasm,
- Inner *fǔgǔ* pain,
- Tightness drawing the *yīn* (genital region) and navel, radiating to both ribs and the chest’s midline, spinal pain.

**Treatment:**
Burning-needle cauterization and pricking, counting by *zhī*, targeting pain as the *shū*. Named *Mèngqiū bì* (Early-Autumn Paralysis).

---
**Foot Shaoyin (Lesser Yin) Muscle Tendon**
