Rising beneath the little toe, it follows the Taiyin path downward beneath the inner ankle, binding at the heel, merging with Taiyang to bind beneath the inner *fǔgǔ*, ascending with Taiyin along the inner thigh to bind at the genitals, following the spine’s inner edge upward to the nape, binding at the occipital bone, merging with the Foot Taiyang tendon.

**Its Diseases:**
- Foot inversion spasm,
- Pain and spasm at all binding points along its path.

「Its Diseases:**
When the disorder lies here, it governs emaciation (瘠) that recovers (愈) and spasticity (痉).
When the disorder lies externally, the patient cannot bend forward (俯).
When the disorder lies internally, the patient cannot bend backward (仰).
Thus, in *yang* (陽) diseases, the waist is bent backward (折), and bending forward (俯) is impossible.
In *yin* (陰) diseases, bending backward (仰) is impossible.

**Treatment:**
Burning-needle cauterization and pricking, counting by *zhī* (知), targeting pain as the *shū* (输).
For internal disorders, apply moxibustion (熨) and guide the *qi* (氣) with medication (引饮药).

This tendon, when knotted and twisted (折纽), with severe *zhī* (知) symptoms, is incurable and fatal.
Named *Zhòngqiū bì* (Mid-Autumn Paralysis).

---
**Foot Jueyin (Lesser Yin of the Foot) Tendon**
Rising above the great toe (大指), it ascends to bind at the anterior inner ankle (内踝之前), then follows the tibia upward.
It binds beneath the inner *fǔgǔ* (腳), ascends along the inner thigh (陰股), and binds at the genitals (陰器).
It intertwines with all tendons (络诸筋).

**Its Diseases:**
- Great toe (大指) spasm,
- Anterior inner ankle pain (内踝之前痛),
- Inner *fǔgǔ* (腳) pain,
- Inner thigh spasm and pain (陰股痛转筋),
- Genital dysfunction (陰器不用).
When injured internally, it fails to rise (不起).
When injured by cold, the *yin* (陰) contracts inward (阴缩入).
When injured by heat, it relaxes and cannot contract (纵挺不收).

**Treatment:**
Promote *yin* (陰) *qi* (氣) circulation with water-based therapies (行水清阴气).
For spasm (转筋), apply burning-needle cauterization and pricking, counting by *zhī* (知), targeting pain as the *shū* (输).
Named *Jìqiū bì* (Late-Autumn Paralysis).

---
**Hand Taiyang (Greater Yang of the Hand) Tendon**
Rising above the little finger (小指), it binds at the wrist (腕).
It ascends along the inner edge of the arm (臂内廉), binding behind the sharp bone of the inner elbow (肘内锐骨之后).
When struck (弹), it resonates above the little finger; it enters and binds beneath the armpit (腋下).
Its branch (支) travels posteriorly along the armpit’s outer edge (腋后廉), ascends to encircle the shoulder blade (肩胛), follows the neck, and emerges at the anterior *Taiyang* (太陽) region.
It binds at the mastoid bone behind the ear (完骨).
Its branch (支) enters the ear (耳中).
The direct path (直者) descends from above the ear, binds at the jaw (颔), and ascends to the outer canthus of the eye (目外).

**Its Diseases:**
- Little finger spasm (小指支),
- Pain behind the sharp inner elbow bone (肘内锐骨后廉),
- Pain radiating along the arm’s inner edge into the armpit (腋下),
- Pain at the posterior armpit edge (腋后廉),
- Pain encircling the shoulder blade and extending to the neck (引颈而痛),
- Resonating pain within the ear (应耳中呜痛),
- Jaw and eye dysfunction (引颔目瞑), where vision returns only after a long time (良久乃得视).
When the neck tendon is tense (颈筋急), it results in **tendon paralysis** (筋痪) and neck swelling (颈肿).

**Treatment for Cold/Heat in the Neck:**
Burning-needle cauterization and pricking, counting by *zhī* (知), targeting pain as the *shū* (输).
For swelling (肿), apply repeated cauterization (复而锐之).
Its branch (支) ascends to the inner canthus (曲牙), follows the front of the ear, and reaches the outer canthus of the eye (目外).
It ascends to the jaw (颔) and binds at the corner (角).
When pain occurs along its path, it branches and spasms (支转筋).

**Treatment:**
Burning-needle cauterization and pricking, counting by *zhī* (知), targeting pain as the *shū* (输).
Named *Zhòngxià bì* (Mid-Summer Paralysis).

---
**Hand Shaoyang (Lesser Yang of the Hand) Tendon**
Rising from the tip of the ring finger (小指次指之端), it binds at the wrist (腕).
It ascends along the arm (臂), binding at the elbow (肘).
It encircles the outer edge of the shoulder (燸外廉), ascends to the shoulder, and travels along the neck, merging with the Hand *Taiyang* (太陽) tendon.
Its branch (支) curves at the cheek and enters to bind the root of the tongue (舌本).
Its branch (支) ascends to the inner canthus (曲牙), follows the front of the ear, and reaches the outer canthus of the eye (目外).
It ascends to the jaw (颔) and binds at the corner (角).

**Its Diseases:**
When pain occurs along its path, it branches and spasms (支转筋), or the tongue rolls inward (舌卷).

**Treatment:**
Burning-needle cauterization and pricking, counting by *zhī* (知), targeting pain as the *shū* (输).
Named *Jìxià bì* (Late-Summer Paralysis).

---
**Hand Yangming (Greater Brightness of the Hand) Tendon**
Rising from the tip of the index finger (大指次指之端), it binds at the wrist (腕).
It ascends along the arm (臂), binding externally at the elbow (肘外).
It ascends to the shoulder’s acromion (孺), binding at the acromion (髑).
Its branch (支) encircles the shoulder blade (肩胛), clinging to the spine (挟脊).
The direct path (直者) follows the acromion upward along the neck (从肩髑上颈).
Its branch (支) ascends to the cheek, binding at the outer canthus (目外).
The direct path (直者) ascends to the anterior *Taiyang* (太陽) region, reaches the left corner (左角), intertwines with the head (络头), and descends to the right jaw (右颔).

**Its Diseases:**
When pain occurs along its path, it branches and spasms (支转筋), or the shoulder cannot be raised (肩不举).
The neck cannot turn left or right (颈不可左右视).

**Treatment:**
Burning-needle cauterization and pricking, counting by *zhī* (知), targeting pain as the *shū* (输).
Named *Mèngxià bì* (Early-Summer Paralysis).

---
**Hand Taiyin (Greater Yin of the Hand) Tendon**
Rising above the index finger (大指), it follows the finger upward, binding behind the fish bone (鱼后).
It travels along the lateral side of the radial artery (寸口外侧), ascends along the arm (臂), binding at the elbow’s center (肘中).
It ascends along the inner edge of the upper arm (孺内廉), enters the armpit (腋), and exits at the supraclavicular fossa (缺盆).
It binds at the anterior shoulder (肩前), the acromion (髑), and the supraclavicular fossa (缺盆).
It descends to bind at the chest’s lateral region (胸裹), disperses through the *guān* (贯) and merges below the *bēn* (贲), reaching the lateral ribs (季胁).

**Its Diseases:**
When pain occurs along its path, it branches and spasms (支转), causing **central tendon pain** (中筋痛).
Severe cases lead to **breathing paralysis** (息贲) and rib tension with hemoptysis (胁急吐血).

**Treatment:**
Burning-needle cauterization and pricking, counting by *zhī* (知), targeting pain as the *shū* (输).
Named *Zhòngdōng bì* (Mid-Winter Paralysis).

---
**Hand Jinxu (Heart’s Master) Tendon**
Rising from the middle finger (中指), it runs parallel with the *Taiyin* (太陰) tendon.
It binds at the inner edge of the elbow (肘内廉), ascends along the arm’s inner side (臂阴), and binds beneath the armpit (腋下).
It descends, dispersing anteriorly and posteriorly, clinging to the ribs (挟胁).
Its branch (支) enters the armpit, disperses through the chest, and binds at the arm (臂).

**Its Diseases:**
When pain occurs along its path, it branches and spasms (支转筋), causing pain in the anterior chest and **breathing paralysis** (息贲).

**Treatment:**
Burning-needle cauterization and pricking, counting by *zhī* (知), targeting pain as the *shū* (输).
Named *Mèngdōng bì* (Early-Winter Paralysis).

---
**Hand Shaoyin (Lesser Yin of the Hand) Tendon**
Rising from the inner side of the little finger (小指之内侧), it binds at the sharp bone (锐骨).
It ascends to bind at the inner edge of the elbow (肘内廉), enters the armpit (腋), crosses the *Taiyin* (太陰) tendon, clings to the breast’s lateral region (乳裹), and binds at the chest’s center (胸中).
It follows the arm (臂) downward, striking the navel (脐).

**Its Diseases:**
When injured internally, it causes **internal tension** (内急).
The heart presses against the **sternal depression** (心承伏梁), and the lower disorder results in **elbow net** (肘网).
When pain occurs along its path, it branches and spasms (支转筋), causing **tendon pain** (筋痛).
Severe cases with **sternal depression** (伏梁), blood-streaked sputum (唾血), and pus (脓) are incurable and fatal.

**Treatment:**
Apply **needle inversion** (翻针) cauterization and pricking, counting by *zhī* (知), targeting pain as the *shū* (输).
For heat-induced relaxation (筋纵不收), do not use burning-needle cauterization.
Named *Jìdōng bì* (Late-Winter Paralysis).

---
**General Principles on Tendon Disorders:**
When tendons are tense due to cold (筋急), they are bent backward (反折) and rigid (筋急).
When tendons are relaxed due to heat (筋弛), they cannot contract (纵不收).
When *yin* (陰) tendons are injured, they become flaccid and dysfunctional (阴痿不用).
When *yang* (陽) tendons are tense, they are bent backward (反折).
When *yin* (陰) tendons are tense, the patient cannot extend forward (俯不伸).

**Needle Inversion (刺) Treatment:**
*刺* (刺) refers to treating cold-induced rigidity (寒急).
For heat-induced relaxation (热则筋纵不收), do not use burning-needle cauterization.
Named *Jìdōng bì* (Late-Winter Paralysis).

---
**Foot Yangming (Greater Brightness of the Foot) and Hand Taiyang (Greater Yang of the Hand) Tendons:**
When their tendons are tense (筋急), the mouth and eyes become **oblique** (僻), and **eye paralysis** (瞑) prevents sudden vision (卒视).
All treatments follow the methods described above.

---
**[Phonetic Release (音释)]:**
頄 *qiú* (求)

**Chapter 14: Bone Measurements (骨度第十四)**

The Yellow Emperor asked **Bó Gāo** (伯高):
*"When measuring the length of the meridians based on pulse locations, how is this determined?"*

**Bó Gāo** replied:
*"First, measure the size, breadth, length, and shortness of the bones and joints, and then the meridian measurements will be established."*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"I wish to hear the standard measurements for an average person. For someone seven *chi* and five *cùn* tall, what are the dimensions of their bones and joints?"*

**Bó Gāo** replied:
*"The circumference of the head’s largest bone is two *chi* and six *cùn*.
The chest circumference is four *chi* and five *cùn*.
The waist circumference is four *chi* and two *cùn*.

From the hairline to the nape of the neck is one *chi* and two *cùn*.
From the hairline to the chin is one *chi*.
A noble man’s hairline reaches the crown.

From the larynx to the suprasternal notch is four *cùn*.
From the suprasternal notch to the [diaphragmatic] region is nine *cùn*—if this exceeds, the lungs are enlarged; if insufficient, the lungs are small.

From the [diaphragmatic] region to the navel is eight *cùn*—if this exceeds, the stomach is enlarged; if insufficient, the stomach is small.

From the navel to the pubic bone is six *cùn* and five *fēn*.
The pubic bone is six *cùn* and five *fēn* in length.
From the upper edge of the pubic bone to the upper edge of the inner ankle is one *chi* and eight *cùn*.
From the upper edge of the inner ankle to its lower edge is three *cùn* and five *fēn*.
From the lower edge of the inner ankle to the ground is three *cùn*.
From the back of the knee to the popliteal fossa is one *chi* and six *cùn*.
From the popliteal fossa to the ground is three *cùn*.
Thus, if the bone circumference is large, it is excessive; if small, it is insufficient.

From the occipital bone to the spine is one *chi*.
The length from the armpit to where the arm is hidden is four *cùn*.
From the armpit to the lower ribs is one *chi* and two *cùn*.
From the lower ribs to the [lumbar] region is six *cùn*.
From the [lumbar] region to the knee is one *chi* and nine *cùn*.
From the knee to the outer ankle is one *chi* and six *cùn*.
From the outer ankle to the *jīnggǔ* bone is three *cùn*.
From the *jīnggǔ* bone to the ground is one *cùn*.

Behind the ear, at the *wán* bone, the breadth is nine *cùn*.
In front of the ear, at the ear gate, the breadth is one *chi* and three *cùn*.
The distance between the two cheeks is seven *cùn*.
The breadth between the two nipples is nine *cùn* and five *fēn*.
The breadth between the two [armpits] is six *cùn* and five *fēn*.

The foot is one *chi* and two *cùn* long, four *cùn* and five *fēn* wide.
From the shoulder to the elbow is one *chi* and seven *cùn*.
From the elbow to the wrist is one *chi* and two *cùn* and five *fēn*.
From the wrist to the base of the middle finger is four *cùn*.
From the base of the finger to its tip is four *cùn* and five *fēn*.

From the hairline to the spine is two *cùn* and five *fēn*.
From the lumbar vertebrae to the coccyx, spanning twenty-one vertebrae, is three *chi*.
The uppermost vertebra is one *cùn* and four *fēn* and one *lì* in length, with the odd division below.
Thus, from the seventh vertebra to the lumbar region is nine *cùn* and seven *fēn* and seven *lì*.
These are the standard bone measurements of an average person, by which the lengths of the meridians are established.

Therefore, when observing the meridians on the body:
If they appear **floating and firm**, or **bright and large**, they indicate **abundant blood**.
If they are **fine and deep**, they indicate **abundant qi**.

---
**[Phonetic Release (音释)]**
髑舫 *dúfáng* (上 *xǔhē*切，又 *xǔfá*切)；
下 *yún* (云 *wànjū*切)；
骨牌 *gǔpái* (步 *bùmǐ*切)；
股 *gǔ* (also *gǔ*).

---
**Chapter 15: The Fifty Circulations (五十营第十五)**

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"I wish to hear about the fifty circulations—how are they arranged?"*

Qi Bao replied:
*"The heavens encompass the Twenty-Eight Lunar Mansions, each divided into thirty-six portions. Human qi completes one full circuit in eighteen hundredths of a portion. The sun traverses the Twenty-Eight Lunar Mansions, while the human meridians—twenty-eight in number—ascend, descend, branch left and right, and extend front and back. They encircle the body over sixteen *zhàng* and two *chǐ*, corresponding to the Twenty-Eight Lunar Mansions. The water clock measures a hundred *kè*, dividing day and night.

Thus, with each inhalation, the pulse moves twice, and qi travels three *cùn*. With each exhalation, the pulse moves twice again, and qi travels another three *cùn*. When breath settles, qi travels six *cùn*. In ten breaths, qi travels six *chǐ*; in a day, it travels two *fēn*. In two hundred seventy breaths, qi travels sixteen *zhàng* and two *chǐ*, completing one full circuit through the body. The water clock descends two *kè*, and the day progresses twenty-five *fēn*.

In five hundred forty breaths, qi completes two full circuits, the water clock descends four *kè*, and the day advances forty *fēn*. In two thousand seven hundred breaths, qi circulates ten times, the water clock descends twenty *kè*, and the day reaches five *xiū* and twenty *fēn*. In thirteen thousand five hundred breaths, qi completes fifty circulations, the water clock descends a hundred *kè*, and the day spans the Twenty-Eight Lunar Mansions, exhausting the water clock’s flow. The pulse then terminates.

What is called *jiāotōng* is the simultaneous movement of all meridians in unison. Thus, the fifty circulations fulfill the lifespan granted by Heaven and Earth—a total of eight hundred ten *zhàng* traversed.

**Chapter 16: The Circulation of Vital Essence (营气第十六)**

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"The Dao of vital essence—how does it treasure the grains within?*
Grains enter the stomach, then pass to the lungs, overflowing internally and dispersing externally. When concentrated, they flow through the meridian channels, perpetually circulating without end, endlessly renewing—this is the rhythm of Heaven and Earth.

Thus, qi emanates from *Tài Yīn*, flows into *Shǒu Yáng Míng*, ascends upward, then enters *Zú Yáng Míng* and descends to the *fū* above the ankle, converging at the base of the great toe to reunite with *Tài Yīn*, ascending to the [missing character].

From the spleen, it enters the heart’s center, follows *Shǒu Shǎo Yīn* down the arm, exiting beneath the armpit, and converges at the little finger, merging with *Shǒu Tài Yáng*. It ascends, passing through the armpit and entering the inner elbow, reaching the inner canthus of the eye. Descending from the crown to the nape, it merges with *Zú Tài Yáng*, follows the spine down to the buttocks, and descends to the tip of the little toe. It then travels along the sole, entering *Zú Shǎo Yīn*, ascending to the kidneys.

From the kidneys, it enters the heart, dispersing externally through the chest, following the *heart’s governing vessel* down the arm, passing between the two tendons, entering the palm, and exiting at the middle finger’s tip. It returns to the second finger’s tip, merging with *Shǒu Shǎo Yáng*, ascending to *dànzhōng*, and dispersing through the *Sān Jiāo*. From *Sān Jiāo*, it flows into the gallbladder, exits the ribs, and enters *Zú Shǎo Yáng*, descending to the *fū* above the ankle. It then returns from the ankle to the base of the great toe, merging with *Zú Jué Yīn*, ascending to the liver. From the liver, it ascends to the lungs, follows the throat, enters the occipital aperture, and reaches the *chùmén*.

Its divergent branches ascend the forehead, follow the crown down the nape, and enter the [missing character]—this is the *Dū Mai*.

The *Lùo* vessel of the yin organs ascends past the pubic hair, enters the navel, follows the abdominal cavity upward, enters the *quēpén*, and descends into the lungs, then exits through *Tài Yīn*.

This is the path of the circulating vital essence, its constant ebb and flow.

**Chapter 17: The Pulse’s Measure (脉度第十七)**"

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear of the Pulse’s Measure."*

Qì Bó replied: *"The six yang channels of the hand, from hand to head, measure five *zhàng*—five times six, three *zhàng*. The six yin channels of the hand, from hand to the chest, measure three *zhàng* five *cùn*—three times six, one *zhàng* eight *chǐ*; five times six, three *zhàng*—together, two *zhàng* one *chǐ*.

The six yang channels of the foot, from foot to head, measure eight *zhàng*—six times eight, four *zhàng* eight *chǐ*. The six yin channels of the foot, from foot to the chest, measure six *zhàng* five *cùn*—six times six, three *zhàng* six *chǐ*; five times six, three *zhàng*—together, three *zhàng* nine *chǐ*.

The *Lù Mai* vessel, from foot to eye, measures seven *zhàng* five *cùn*—one times seven, one *zhàng* four *chǐ*; two times five, one *chǐ*—together, one *zhàng* five *chǐ*. The *Dū Mai* and *Rèn Mai* each measure four *zhàng* five *cùn*—two times four, eight *chǐ*; two times five, one *chǐ*—together, nine *chǐ*.

In total, all measure sixteen *zhàng* two *chǐ*—this is the great conduit of the vital *qi*.

The *jīng* channels are the interior; their branches that spread horizontally are the *luò* vessels. The further branches of the *luò* are the *sūn* vessels. When the *luò* vessels are overfilled with blood, they must be swiftly drained; when they are full, they must be reduced; when they are empty, they must be supplemented with medicine.

The five *zàng* organs constantly circulate within the seven orifices above, thus:
- The *lung*’s *qi* communicates with the nose; when the *lung* is harmonious, the nose can discern fragrant and foul odors.
- The *heart*’s *qi* communicates with the tongue; when the *heart* is harmonious, the tongue can discern the five flavors.
- The *liver*’s *qi* communicates with the eyes; when the *liver* is harmonious, the eyes can distinguish the five colors.
- The *spleen*’s *qi* communicates with the mouth; when the *spleen* is harmonious, the mouth can discern the five grains.
- The *kidney*’s *qi* communicates with the ears; when the *kidney* is harmonious, the ears can hear the five tones.

When the five *zàng* are disharmonious, the seven orifices do not function. When the six *fǔ* are disharmonious, *zhì* (stagnation) ensues.

Thus, when *xie* (pathogenic *qi*) lodges in the *fǔ*, the yang channels become disharmonious. When the yang channels are disharmonious, *qi* stagnates, and when *qi* stagnates, the yang *qi* becomes excessive. When the yang *qi* is too excessive, the yin does not flow freely; when the yin channels are obstructed, blood stagnates, and when blood stagnates, the yin *qi* becomes excessive. When the yin *qi* is too excessive, the yang *qi* cannot nourish—this is called *guān* (closure). When the yang *qi* is too excessive, the yin *qi* cannot nourish—this is called *gé* (blockage). When both yin and yang are excessive and cannot nourish each other, this is called *guān gé* (closure-blockage). *Guān gé* means death before the allotted time.

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Where does the *Lù Mai* originate and terminate? What *qi* nourishes the eyes?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"The *Lù Mai* is a divergent branch of the *Shào Yīn* (Kidney). It arises behind the *rán gǔ* (medial malleolus), ascends above the inner ankle, and follows the inner thigh into the groin. It ascends along the chest’s interior, enters the *quēpén* (sternal notch), and emerges before the *rèn yíng* (laryngeal prominence). It then enters and belongs to the inner canthus of the eye, merging with the *Tài Yáng* (Small Intestine) and *Yáng Jì* (Bladder) channels as it ascends. When its *qi* flows harmoniously back and forth, the eyes are moist; when its *qi* does not nourish, the eyes do not close."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Why does the *qi* circulate only through the five *zàng* and not nourish the six *fǔ*?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"The *qi* cannot cease its movement—like water flowing, like the moon’s unending course. Thus, the yin channels nourish the *zàng*, and the yang channels nourish the *fǔ*, like an endless ring, with no discernible beginning or end, returning again and again. Its overflowing *qi* irrigates the *zàng* and *fǔ* internally and moistens the skin and pores externally."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"The *Lù Mai* has yin and yang—what channel corresponds to its count?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"For men, count the yang; for women, count the yin. The correct count is the *jīng* (channel); the incorrect count is the *luò* (vessel)."*

The Yellow Emperor asked Qì Bó: *"From where does a person receive qi? Where do yin and yang converge? Which qi is called *yíng*? Which is called *wèi*? From where does *yíng* originate? Where does *wèi* assemble? The old and the vigorous differ in qi, and yin and yang occupy different positions—may I hear where they converge?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"A person receives qi from food. Food enters the stomach, then passes to the lungs, and the five *zàng* and six *fǔ* all receive qi from it. The refined portion becomes *yíng*, and the turbid portion becomes *wèi*. *Yíng* circulates within the vessels, while *wèi* circulates outside them. *Yíng* flows ceaselessly, completing a full cycle every fifty degrees. Yin and yang interconnect like an endless ring. *Wèi* qi travels twenty-five degrees through yin and twenty-five degrees through yang, dividing into day and night. Thus, when qi reaches yang, one awakens, and when it reaches yin, one rests. Hence it is said: At noon, the *yáng lǒng* reaches its peak as *zhòng yáng* (heavy yang), and at midnight, the *yīn lǒng* reaches its peak as *zhòng yīn* (heavy yin). Therefore, *Tài Yīn* governs the interior, and *Tài Yáng* governs the exterior, each traveling twenty-five degrees, dividing into day and night. At midnight, it is the *yīn lǒng*; after midnight, yin begins to wane. At dawn, yin is exhausted, and yang receives qi. At noon, the *yáng lǒng* peaks; in the west, yang begins to wane. At sunset, yang is exhausted, and yin receives qi. At midnight, they converge, and all people sleep—this is called *hé yīn* (uniting yin). At dawn, when yin is exhausted, yang receives qi again, and so it continues without end, aligning with the cycles of Heaven and Earth."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Why do the elderly not sleep at night, and why do the young and vigorous not sleep during the day?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"In the vigorous, *qi* and blood are abundant, their flesh is supple, and their *qi* pathways are unobstructed. The circulation of *yíng* and *wèi* follows its regular course, so they are alert by day and sleep by night. In the elderly, *qi* and blood are diminished, their flesh is withered, and their *qi* pathways are sluggish. The *zàng* qi clashes, *yíng* qi weakens, and *wèi* qi turns inward aggressively. Thus, they are not alert by day nor do they sleep at night."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear the pathways by which *yíng* and *wèi* circulate."*

Qì Bó replied: *"*Yíng* originates from the *zhōng jiāo* (middle burner), and *wèi* originates from the *xià jiāo* (lower burner)."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear where the three burners originate."*

Qì Bó replied: *"The *shàng jiāo* (upper burner) originates from the upper orifice of the stomach, extending upward through the throat, passing through the diaphragm, and spreading across the chest. It travels to the armpits, following the *Tài Yīn* pathway, then returns to *Yáng Míng*, ascending to the tongue and descending to the *Yáng Míng* of the foot. It always travels with *yíng* through twenty-five degrees of yang and twenty-five degrees of yin in one cycle. Thus, it completes fifty degrees before reassembling at *Shǒu Tài Yīn*."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"When a person has heat, food and drink enter the stomach, but the qi is unsettled, and sweat emerges—sometimes on the face, sometimes on the back, or halfway down the body—why does it not follow the *wèi* qi’s usual path?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"This is due to external injury by wind, which opens the pores. The sweat evaporates through the pores, and *wèi* qi rushes outward, unable to follow its proper course. This qi is fierce, swift, and slippery; when it encounters an opening, it escapes. Thus, it is called *lòu xiè* (leakage)."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"I wish to hear where the *zhōng jiāo* originates."*

Qì Bó replied: *"The *zhōng jiāo* also lies alongside the stomach, emerging after the *shàng jiāo*. This is where qi is received, where it separates dregs from essence, steams the body fluids, and refines them into subtle substances. These ascend into the lung vessels, transforming into blood to nourish life. Nothing is more precious than this, so it alone circulates through the *jīng* pathways and is called *yíng* qi."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Blood and qi share the same essence but have different names—how so?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"*Yíng* and *wèi* are refined *qì*; blood is vital *qì*. Thus, blood and qi share the same essence but differ in name. Therefore, depleting blood results in no sweat, and depleting sweat results in no blood. Hence, a person has two deaths but no second life."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"I wish to hear where the *xià jiāo* originates."*

Qì Bó replied: *"The *xià jiāo* branches from the intestines and drains into the bladder, permeating it. Thus, water and grain always reside in the stomach, forming dregs that descend into the large intestine, becoming the *xià jiāo*. They permeate and descend together, separating and filtering fluids, flowing through the *xià jiāo* into the bladder."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"When a person drinks alcohol, it also enters the stomach, yet urine is excreted before the food is fully digested—why?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"Alcohol is the refined essence of cooked grains, its qi fierce and clear. Thus, it enters after food but its liquid is excreted first."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Excellent. I have heard that the *shàng jiāo* is like mist, the *zhōng jiāo* like vomiting, and the *xià jiāo* like filtration—this is what you mean."*

---
**Chapter Nineteen: The Qi of the Four Seasons**

The Yellow Emperor asked Qì Bó: *"The qi of the four seasons takes different forms, and the origins of a hundred diseases vary accordingly. What are the fixed principles for *jiǔ* (moxibustion) and *cí* (acupuncture)?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"The qi of the four seasons occupies distinct regions, and the principles of *jiǔ* and *cí* are determined by locating the correct *qì* points. In spring, target the *jīng* (channels), blood, pulses, and the spaces between muscles. For severe cases, use deep needling; for mild cases, use shallow needling. In summer, target the *shèng jīng* (flourishing channels), *sūn luò* (branch vessels), and the spaces between skin and muscle. For autumn, target the *jīng* and *liǎn* (collateral vessels). If the pathogen is in the *fǔ*, treat at the *hé* (convergence) points. In winter, target the *jǐng* (wells) and *róng* (springs), ensuring deep insertion and retention. For *wēn nüè* (intermittent fever with no sweat), it is the *wǔshíjiǔ qí* (59th disease). For *fēng chuáng* (wind-induced bedriddenness) and *pú zhàng* (skin distension), it is the *wǔshíqī qí* (57th disease). When treating by drawing blood from the skin, exhaust it completely. For *shí xiè* (chronic diarrhea), supplement the upper three *yīn* channels and *Yīn Líng Quán*, retaining the needle for a long time until heat subsides. For *zhuǎn jīn* (spasms), if it occurs in yang, treat the yang; if in yin, treat the yin—always using rapid needling. For *tú chuáng* (paralysis), first target a point three *cùn* below *Huán Gǔ*, using a *bì* needle. After needling, insert a tube, then withdraw the needle while pushing the tube inward. Repeat until the paralysis is exhausted. If the response is firm and slow, the patient will feel troubled; if rapid and urgent, the patient will feel at ease. Needle once, then stop when the paralysis is relieved."*

「When administering *yǐnbì* (drink-closing) medication, if the patient is unable to stand during needling, they should drink while seated. When drinking, they should not eat; when eating, they should not drink. They must not consume other foods. The treatment lasts for 135 days.

If *zhuó bì* (fixed numbness) persists and prolonged coldness does not abate, needle the *Sān Lǐ* (ST-36) forcefully. If the bones become dry and the intestines are obstructed, needle *Sān Lǐ* (ST-36) with vigorous *xiě* (draining) and *xū bǔ* (void-supplementing) techniques.

For *lì fēng* (malignant wind), needle the swollen area with a blunt needle first. After needling, use a sharp *ruì zhēn* (needle) to pierce the site, pressing out the noxious *qi*. When the swelling is exhausted, cease. The patient should eat only what is prescribed and avoid other foods.

If the abdomen constantly rumbles, *qi* surges upward to the chest, and the patient gasps unable to stand for long, the evil lies in the *Dà Cháng* (large intestine). Needle *Huáng Shuǎng* (UB-47) at its *yuán* (origin), *Jù Xū* (ST-39) above *Lián* (廉), and *Sān Lǐ* (ST-36).

If the lower abdomen draws the testicles upward, strains the lumbar spine, and surges into the heart, the evil is in the *Xiǎo Cháng* (small intestine). It connects to the testicles, ascends to the spine, pierces the liver and lungs, and networks with the heart’s *xì* (system). When *qi* is excessive, it causes *jué nì* (syncope), surging upward to disrupt the intestines and stomach, darkening the liver, dispersing into *Huáng Shuǎng* (UB-47), and congealing at the *qí* (navel). Thus, needle *Huáng Shuǎng Yuán* (UB-47’s origin) to disperse it, needle *Tài Yīn* (SP-2) to supply it, needle *Jué Yīn* (PC-7) to subdue it, and needle *Jù Xū Xià Lián* (ST-39 below廉) to remove it. Press along the affected channels to regulate them.

For persistent vomiting with bitter taste, prolonged sighing, a hollow *xiānxiān* (resonant) sensation in the heart, and fear of being seized—*yǐn* (evil) in the *Dǎn* (gallbladder), *nì* (rebellion) in the *Wèi* (stomach)—when *dǎn yè* (gallbladder fluid) leaks, the mouth becomes bitter; when *wèi qì* (stomach *qi*) rebels, the patient vomits bitterly. Hence, it is called *ǒu dǎn* (vomiting of the gallbladder). To reverse the *wèi qì nì* (stomach *qi* rebellion), needle the *Shào Yáng xuè luò* (GB-38’s blood network) to close the gallbladder’s rebellion, then adjust the *xū* (void) and *shí* (full) states to expel the evil.

If food and drink do not descend, and the *gè* (diaphragm) is obstructed, the evil is in the *Wèi Yuàn* (stomach’s courtyard). If it is in the upper courtyard, needle *Yì* (ST-45) to push it downward; if in the lower courtyard, disperse and remove it.

If the lower abdomen aches and swells, and the patient cannot urinate, the evil is in the *Sān Jiāo* (triple burner) constriction. Needle the *Tài Yáng dà luò* (GB-14’s major network), observing where the network channel intersects with the *Jué Yīn xiǎo luò* (PC-7’s minor network) and blood congeals—whether at the swollen area or in the stomach’s courtyard—and needle *Sān Lǐ* (ST-36).

To discern its dispersion or recurrence, examine the patient’s eye color. To determine the fate of the illness, observe their overall form and listen to their *dòngjìng* (movement and stillness). Hold the breath and check *Rén Yíng* (REN-22) at the *gǔkǒu* (mouth of the pulse), noting if the pulse is firm, excessive, and slippery—then the illness advances daily. If the pulse is soft, the illness will soon subside. When all channels are full, the illness will resolve in three days.

*Gǔkǒu* (mouth of the pulse) diagnoses *yīn* (cold), while *Rén Yíng* (REN-22) diagnoses *yáng* (heat).」

**#2** Twenty-five: *Tai Su* Volume Twelve, *Ying Wu Zhou* ("The Fifty Circuits of the Nutritive System"), reads *"Twenty."*

**#3** *Shang xíng* ("ascending"): In *Jia Yi Jing* Volume One, Chapter Ten, the character *"xíng"* is followed by the phrase *"zhì miàn"* ("until the face").

**#4** *Hé* ("harmonize"): Originally *"lì"* ("benefit"), corrected according to the Zhao Fu Ju Jing Tang edition.

**#5** *Xià* ("below"): In *Tai Su* Volume Twenty-Four, the opening passage reads *"shàng"* ("above").

**#6** *Bì jiān* ("must be firm"): In *Tai Su* Volume Thirteen, *Za Ci* ("Miscellaneous Needling"), the character *"jiān"* is followed by *"shù zhī"* ("and bind it").

**#7** Annotation: This sentence is likely a miscopy and should read *"Bì jí cí zhī"* ("Must be needled urgently").

**#8** *Lái* ("come"): Likely a miscopy for *"cí"* ("needle"). The following *"lái"* is also a miscopy.

**#9** *Zhuó* ("press"): Originally *"zhě"* ("who/that"), corrected according to the Zhao Fu Ju Jing Tang edition.

---

**Yellow Emperor’s Inner Canon – *Lingshu* (Spiritual Pivot) – *Jizhu* (Collected Commentary) Volume Nine**
**Five Evils (Wǔ Xié) – Chapter Twenty**

When evil is in the **lung**, the patient suffers from **skin and flesh pain**, **chills and fever**, **shortness of breath**, **sweating**, **coughing**, and **shoulder and back spasms**. Needle the **external and lateral regions of the chest** (*yīng zhōng wài liǎn*), the **three joints of the back** (one variant reads *"five joints"* and another *"five joints beside the forehead"*), pressing firmly until a sharp, clear sensation is felt—then needle. Alternatively, needle the **suprasternal fossa** (*quē pén zhōng*) to **drain** it.

When evil is in the **liver**, the patient suffers from **pain in both flanks**, **internal cold**, **malignant blood stagnation**, **tendency to spasm**, **periodic leg swelling**. Needle the **channel’s junction** (*xíng wèn*) to **draw out the flanks**, **reinforce Sān Lǐ** (ST-36) to **warm the stomach**, **take blood from the vessels** to **dissipate the malignant blood**, and **needle the green pulse at the ear junction** (*ěr wèn*) to **remove the spasms**.

When evil is in the **spleen and stomach**, the patient suffers from **muscle pain**, **excessive yang qi** and **insufficient yin qi**, leading to **internal heat and excessive hunger**; or **insufficient yang qi** and **excessive yin qi**, leading to **internal cold, intestinal rumbling, and abdominal pain**. If both **yin and yang are excessive** or **both are insufficient**, the patient experiences **both cold and heat**, all of which are **regulated at Sān Lǐ** (ST-36).

When evil is in the **kidney**, the patient suffers from **bone pain, yin numbness**—where **pressing yields no sensation**—**abdominal distension, lower back pain, difficult defecation, shoulder, back, neck, and occipital pain**, and **occasional dizziness**. Needle **Yǒng Quán** (KID-1) and **Kūn Lún** (BL-60), draining all blood present.

When evil is in the **heart**, the patient suffers from **heart pain, melancholy, sadness, occasional dizziness, and fainting**. Observe whether there is **excess or deficiency** and **regulate its output** (*shū*).

---
**Phonetic Notes:**
*"Dùn"* sounds like *"quán"* (权). *"Tóng"* is homophonous with *"tǒng"* (筒). *"Zhuó"* in *"zhú bì"* (着痹) is pronounced *"zháo"* in the first tone, *"bì"* as *"bì"* (闭). *"Ruì zhēn"* (锐针) is pronounced *"rù huì"* (余惠), meaning *"sharp needle."* *"Máng"* (芒) is implied.

**Chapter 21: Diseases of Cold and Heat**

When **skin cold-heat** occurs, the patient may not tolerate lying down, with **parched hair and lips like dried wax**, unable to sweat. Treat by **draining the collateral vessels of the Three Yang** and **supplementing the Hand Taiyin**.

When **muscle cold-heat** occurs, there is **muscle pain**, **parched hair and lips like dried wax**, and inability to sweat. **Drain the Three Yang from below** to remove excess blood, then **supplement the Foot Taiyin** to induce sweating.

When **bone cold-heat** occurs, the patient finds no relief, with **persistent sweating**, teeth yet unparched—**needle the minor yin collaterals at the inner thigh**. If the teeth are already parched, **the case is untreatable and fatal**. The same applies to **bone tetany**.

For **tetanic numbness**, where **ulcerative qi rises to the abdomen**, treat by **draining the yin-yang collaterals**, adjusting based on the primary ailment—**draining excess yang, supplementing yin channels**.

If the body is injured with **profound blood loss**, or struck by **wind-cold**, or from **falls**, resulting in **limp, unresponsive extremities**, this is called **"body lassitude"**—**needle the three convergences below the navel**. The **three convergences** are:
- **Yangming** and **Taiyin** (the **lower abdomen**),
- **Guanyuan** (three *cun* below the navel).

For **ulcerative numbness**, where **ulcerative qi ascends to the abdomen**, **drain the yin-yang collaterals**, adjusting based on the primary ailment—**draining excess yang, supplementing yin channels**.

The **pulsating artery at the neck** is **Renying** (Foot Yangming), located **anterior to the hyoid tendon**. Posterior to the hyoid tendon lies **Futou** (Hand Yangming). The next artery is **Tianyou** (Foot Shaoyang), and the following is **Tianzhu** (Foot Taiyang). The **armpit artery** is **Tianfu** (Arm Taiyin).

For **headache with Renying distension and chest fullness**, unable to breathe—**needle Renying**.
For **sudden emaciation with whip-like pain**—**needle Futou and the base of the tongue to induce bleeding**.
For **sudden deafness with clouded senses, blurred vision**—**needle Tianyou**.
For **sudden tetany with itching and dizziness, unable to bear weight**—**needle Tianzhu**.
For **sudden pestilential reversal**, where **liver and lungs clash**, blood gushing from nose and mouth—**needle Tianfu**.

These are the **five Tianyou regions**.

The **Arm Yangming** has a branch encircling the teeth, called **Dawying**—**needle for lower tooth decay**, located below the jaw.
If **fear of cold**, **supplement it**; if **no fear of cold**, **drain it**.

The **Foot Taiyang** has a branch encircling the teeth, called **Jiaosun**—**needle for upper tooth decay**, located **anterior to the nose and jaw**.
When disease manifests, if the pulse is **full**, **drain it**; if **empty**, **supplement it**. **Bleed from the nose daily**.

The **Foot Yangming** has a branch **flanking the nose into the face**, called **Xuangu**—**opposing the eye’s root**, treating excess by **reducing** and deficiency by **augmenting**, reversing by **strengthening**.

The **Foot Taiyang** has a path **entering the nape into the brain**, called **Yanxi**—**for head and eye pain**, located **between the two neck tendons**, branching into **Yinjiao and Yangju**. Where **yin and yang intersect**, **yang enters yin, yin exits yang**, converging at the **eye’s sharp pupil**—**excess yang causes glare**, **excess yin causes dimness**.

For **heat tetany**, **needle Foot Taiyin and Shaoyang**, **retaining the needle**.
For **cold tetany**, **needle Foot Yangming and Shaoyin at the foot**, **retaining the needle**.

For **prolonged tongue with drooling, restlessness, and euphoria**—**needle Foot Shaoyin**.
For **shivering with alcohol exposure, chin quivering, inability to sweat, abdominal distension, and restlessness**—**needle Hand Taiyin**.

When **needling the empty**, **direct the needle outward**; when **needling the full**, **direct the needle inward**.
- **Spring**: treat **collateral vessels**,
- **Summer**: treat **muscle layers**,
- **Autumn**: treat **qi orifices**,
- **Winter**: treat **channel junctions**.
These four seasons align with their respective times:
- **Collateral vessels** govern **skin and hair**,
- **Muscle layers** govern **flesh and sinews**,
- **Qi orifices** govern **tendons and vessels**,
- **Channel junctions** govern **bones, marrow, and five viscera**.

The body has **five critical regions**:
1. **Futu** (groin),
2. **Fei** (calf, *fei* as "end"),
3. **Back**,
4. **Visceral faces** (anterior body),
5. **Neck**.
If **ulcerative carbuncles** appear in these five regions, **death is inevitable**.

If disease begins in the **arms**, first **needle Hand Yangming and Taiyin to induce sweating**.
If disease begins in the **head**, first **needle Neck Taiyang to induce sweating**.
If disease begins in the **foot channels**, first **needle Foot Yangming to induce sweating**.
**Arm Taiyin** can induce sweating, as can **Foot Yangming**.

Thus, if **needling yin channels induces excessive sweating**, **counter with yang**; if **needling yang channels induces excessive sweating**, **counter with yin**.

The harms of needling:
- If the needle **strikes but does not withdraw**, **essence is depleted**—**disease worsens and stagnates**.
- If the needle **fails to strike but withdraws**, **qi is disrupted**—**leading to ulceration and carbuncles**.

---
**Phonetic Notes:**
*"Gǎo là"* (槁腊) also pronounced *"sì xiǎo"* (思亦切).

**Epilepsy and Madness (癫狂)**

**Diseases of the Teeth and Face:**
*"Qǔ"* (齲丘禹切) – tooth decay (*齿蠹*).
*"Shùn duì"* (顺逵仇二音) – forehead and face (*面额*).
*"Yù"* (悦) – pronounced *"mèn"* (闷).
*"Fēi"* (腓) – pronounced *"féi"* (肥).

**Epilepsy (癫疾):**
When epilepsy first arises, the patient first feels displeasure, a heavy and painful head, and severe redness in the eyes upon movement. Later, they become restless at heart. If observed on the face, needle **Arm Taiyang, Yangming, and Taiyin**—when the blood shifts, the condition ceases.

When epilepsy begins with sudden weeping, gasping, and palpitations, observe **Hand Yangming and Taiyang**. If the left side is stronger, attack the right, and if the right is stronger, attack the left—when the blood shifts, the condition ceases.

When epilepsy begins with sudden stiffness, followed by back pain, observe **Foot Taiyang, Yangming, Taiyin, and Arm Taiyang**—when the blood shifts, the condition ceases.

To treat epilepsy, always reside with the patient and observe where needling is required. When the disease manifests, if the affected area shows excess, bleed it, and place the blood in a gourd vessel. At the onset of the seizure, the blood will move on its own. If it does not move, moxibustion is applied to the **Xionggu (穷骨)**—the skeletal bones—twenty times.

**Bone Epilepsy (骨癫疾):**
The face, teeth, and all facial muscles swell, while the bones remain exposed. Sweating and restlessness ensue. If there is excessive vomiting with frothy spittle and downward leakage of *qi*, treatment is futile.

**Tendon Epilepsy (筋癫疾):**
The body becomes exhausted and rigidly contracted. Needle the **Dazhu (大杼)** pulse on the nape. If there is excessive vomiting with frothy spittle and downward leakage of *qi*, treatment is futile.

**Vascular Epilepsy (脉癫疾):**
Sudden collapse occurs, with all four limbs’ pulses distended and relaxed. If the pulses are full, bleed them thoroughly. If not full, apply moxibustion to the **Taiyang** on the nape and the **Dai (带)** pulse three inches from the waist, targeting all branch and convergence points. If there is excessive vomiting with frothy spittle and downward leakage of *qi*, treatment is futile.

If epilepsy manifests violently like madness, death is inevitable.

**Madness (狂):**
When madness first arises, the patient initially feels sorrow, forgets joy, is prone to anger, and easily frightened—caused by grief and hunger. Treat by needling **Hand Taiyin and Yangming**; when the blood shifts, the condition ceases. Also needle **Foot Taiyin and Yangming**.

When madness erupts, with little sleep and no hunger, the patient boasts of wisdom, argues endlessly, and claims nobility, often insulting others day and night. Treat by needling **Hand Yangming, Taiyang, Taiyin, the tongue’s underside, and Shaoyin**. If the pulses are strong, bleed them; if not, release the tension.

If madness involves wild speech, startling, excessive laughter, love of music, and ceaseless erratic behavior—caused by extreme fear—treat by needling **Hand Yangming, Taiyang, and Taiyin**.

If madness includes hallucinations, phantom sounds, and excessive shouting—stemming from deficient *qi*—treat by needling **Hand Taiyang, Taiyin, Yangming, Foot Taiyin, the head, and both cheeks**.

If madness involves excessive eating, visions of ghosts and spirits, and laughter without external expression—caused by great joy—treat by first needling **Foot Taiyin, Taiyang, and Yangming**, then **Hand Taiyin, Taiyang, and Yangming**.

If madness is newly emergent and not yet severe, first needle the **Qūquán (曲泉)**’s lateral moving pulses. If bleeding does not cease after a time, apply the method and moxibustion to the skeletal bones twenty times.

**Wind Reversal (风逆):**
Sudden swelling of the four limbs, a trembling body, intermittent chills, restlessness when hungry, and dizziness when full. Observe **Hand Taiyin (both yin and yang aspects)**, **Foot Shaoyin and Yangming** channels. For muscular symptoms, target the **Rong (荣)** points; for skeletal symptoms, target the **Jing (井)** and **Jing (经)** points.

**Syncope (厥逆):**
Sudden stiffness of the feet, a chest as if about to burst, and intestines as if cut by a knife. Restlessness and inability to eat, with pulses—both large and small—rough. Apply warm needling to **Foot Shaoyin**; apply cleansing needling to **Foot Yangming**. If cold, supplement; if hot, bleed.

If syncope involves abdominal distension, intestinal rumbling, and chest fullness preventing breath, needle the lower chest.

If the sides ache with hand movements, press the back and face with the hands—immediate relief indicates success.

If internal obstruction prevents movement, needle **Foot Shaoyin, Taiyang, and the Xionggu (骸骨)** with long needles. If *qi* reverses, needle **Taiyin, Yangming, and Jueyin**; if severe, target the moving pulses of **Shaoyin and Yangming**.

**Deficient *Qi* (少气):**
The body trembles (*shīshī*), bones ache, and there is heaviness and lethargy. Supplement **Foot Shaoyin**.

**Shortness of Breath (短气):**
Breath is shallow and disconnected; movement exhausts *qi*. Supplement **Foot Shaoyin** and remove stagnant blood from the vessels.

---
**Fever Diseases (热病)**

**Hemiplegia (偏枯):**
One-sided paralysis with pain but no speech or mental disorder. The disease lies between the skin and muscles. Use a large needle to supplement deficiencies and reduce excesses—only then may recovery occur.

**Convulsions (痱):**
No pain, but limbs cannot contract. Mental confusion is mild, speech faintly coherent—treatable. If severe, with inability to speak, treatment is futile.

If disease first arises in the **yang** and later enters the **yin**, first needle the **yang**, then the **yin**, using superficial techniques.

If fever persists for three days and the **Cègu (气口)** pulse is calm while the **Rènyīng (人迎)** is agitated, needle all **yang** channels with fifty-nine punctures to reduce heat, induce sweating, and supplement the **yin** to address deficiencies.

If body heat is extreme and both **yin** and **yang** pulses are calm, **do not needle**. If needling is necessary, act swiftly—if no sweating follows, bleed.

*"Do not needle"* refers to cases with signs of impending death.

If fever lasts seven or eight days, with a **Cègu** pulse that is short (or taut), needle urgently—sweating will follow. Use shallow needling on the **Hand Daizhi (大指间)**.

「If fever persists for seven or eight days, with a pulse that is faint and small, the patient will convulse, and their mouth will become dry, dying within half a day; if the pulse is intermittent, they will die within one day.

If fever has already induced sweating but the pulse remains agitated and the patient is short of breath, yet heat recurs, **do not needle the skin**#5—if the shortness of breath is extreme, death follows.

If fever lasts seven or eight days, with a pulse that is neither agitated nor dispersed, and sweating appears only later within three days; if no sweating occurs after three days, death follows on the fourth day. **Do not needle those who have not yet sweated**#6.

If fever first manifests as **skin pain and nasal congestion, with a flushed face**, needle the **skin**, using the **first needle**#7, with fifty-nine punctures, **piercing the nasal cavity**#7 and **seeking the skin’s connection to the lungs**#7—if the **fire**#7 is not found, the **fire**#7 refers to the **heart**.

If fever first presents as **body stiffness**, with **heat upon pressure**#8, agitation, and delight, **dry lips and constricted throat**#8, needle the **skin**#9, using the **first needle**#7, with fifty-nine punctures—if the abdomen swells and the mouth becomes dry, with **cold sweat emerging**, **seek the pulse’s connection to the heart**#9, and if the **water**#9 is not found, the **water**#9 refers to the **kidneys**.

If fever causes **excessive thirst and frequent drinking**, with **easily startled reflexes**, and the patient cannot rise, needle the **skin and flesh**, using the **sixth needle**#7, with fifty-nine punctures—if the **eyes become greenish-blue**#7, **seek the flesh’s connection to the spleen**#7, and if the **wood**#7 is not found, the **wood**#7 refers to the **liver**.

If fever presents as **a greenish-blue face and brain pain**, with **agitated hands and feet**, needle the **tendons**#7, using the **fourth needle**#7, at the **Four Reversals**#7—if the **tendons are tense and the eyes sunken**#7, **seek the tendons’ connection to the liver**#7, and if the **metal**#7 is not found, the **metal**#7 refers to the **lungs**.

If fever causes **frequent convulsions**, with **spasms and madness**, needle the **pulse**, using the **fourth needle**#7—**bleed swiftly if excess remains**; if **epileptic seizures occur and hair falls out**, **seek the blood’s connection to the heart**#7, and if the **water**#7 is not found, the **water**#7 refers to the **kidneys**.

If fever causes **body heaviness and bone pain**, with **deafness and a preference for darkness**, needle the **bones**, using the **fourth needle**#7, with fifty-nine punctures—if the **bone disease prevents eating**, with **gnashing of teeth and blue ears**, **seek the bones’ connection to the kidneys**#7, and if the **earth**#7 is not found, the **earth**#7 refers to the **spleen**.

If fever is **painless**, with **deafness and inability to withdraw oneself**, and **dry mouth**, with **extreme yang heat** and **some yin cold**, the heat lies in the **marrow**—death is inevitable, and treatment is futile.

If fever causes **headache**, with **visible forehead veins**, **eye spasms and painful pulse**, and **tendency to bind tightly**#7, it is a case of **heatstroke with alternating cold and heat**#7—needle with the **third needle**#7, observing whether excess or deficiency remains, and treat accordingly for **cold and heat**#7.

If fever causes **body heaviness** and **intestinal heat**, needle with the **fourth needle**#7, at the **cheek and the lower spaces between the fingers**, **seeking the qi’s connection to the stomach**#10—if the **qi is obtained**#10.

If fever causes **sudden, severe pain around the navel**, with **chest and flank fullness**, needle **Yǒngquán (涌泉)** and **Yīnlíngquán (阴陵泉)**, using the **fourth needle**#7, and **needle the navel’s interior**.

If fever has already induced sweating and the pulse is **harmonious and sweating is possible**, needle **Yújì (鱼际)**, **Tàiyuān (太渊)**, **Dàdū (大都)**, and **Tàibái (太白)**—**bleeding will remove the heat**, and **supplementing will induce sweating**; if sweating is **excessively profuse**, needle the **horizontal pulse above the inner ankle** to stop it.

If fever has already induced sweating but the pulse remains **agitated and strong**, this is the **extreme of yin pulse**—death follows;
If fever has induced sweating and the pulse is **calm**, life follows.

If fever’s pulse remains **strong and agitated but sweating does not occur**, this is the **extreme of yang pulse**—death follows;
If fever’s pulse is **strong and agitated but sweating occurs and the pulse becomes calm**, life follows.

There are nine cases of fever where needling is **forbidden**:
1. If sweating does not emerge and the **face becomes deeply red**#7, with **convulsions**#7, death follows;
2. If there is **diarrhea with extreme abdominal fullness**, death follows;
3. If the **eyes are unclear** and the heat does not cease, death follows;
4. If **elderly or infants** have heat with **abdominal fullness**, death follows;
5. If sweating does not emerge and there is **vomiting with blood**, death follows;
6. If the **tongue’s root rots** and the heat does not cease, death follows;
7. If there is **persistent coughing**#7, with **sweating that does not reach the feet**, death follows;
8. If the **marrow is heated**, death follows;
9. If there is **heat with tetany**#7, with **waist collapse**, **severe spasm**#7, **gnashing of teeth**, and **inability to speak**#7, death follows.

In all these nine cases, needling is **forbidden**.

The **fifty-nine punctures** are as follows:
- On each **outer and inner side of both hands**, three punctures each, totaling twelve **acupuncture points**#7;
- On each **space between the fingers**#7, one puncture each, totaling eight **acupuncture points**#7—**the feet are treated similarly**;
- On the **head**, one inch into the hairline with a three-fen#7 offset, three punctures each, totaling six **wounds**#7;
- Further, **three inches into the hairline with a five-fen#7 offset**, totaling ten **acupuncture points**#7;
- On the **fore and aft of the ears**, and **below the mouth**, one puncture each, and one at the **nape**, totaling six **acupuncture points**#7;
- On the **vertex**#7, **crown**#7, **hairline**#7, **Lianquán (廉泉)**, **Fēngchí (风池)** (two), and **Tiānzhù (天柱)** (two).

If **qi accumulates in the chest**, causing **shortness of breath**, needle the **end of the big toe on the Foot Taiyin (太阴)** channel, **one build#11-leaf’s length from the nail**—if cold, **retain the needle**; if heat, **withdraw it swiftly**, and the shortness of breath will cease.

If **heart colic** causes **sudden, severe pain**, needle **Foot Taiyin (太阴)** and **Jueyin (厥阴)**, **exhaustively puncturing the blood vessels**.

If **throat obstruction** causes **tongue rolling**, with **dry mouth**, **agitation and heart pain**, and **pain in the inner arm’s Lian (廉)**, **avoid needling the head**, and instead needle the **base of the little and second fingers**, **one scallion-leaf’s length from the nail**.

If **eye pain is red**, beginning from the **inner canthus**, needle at **Yīnjiāo (阴娇)**.

If **wind tetany** causes **body inversion and folding**, first needle **Foot Taiyang (太阳)**, **the lateral side of the leg**, and **the blood vessels to induce bleeding**; if **cold remains internally**, needle **Sānlǐ (三里)**.

For **malaria**, needle **Yīnjiāo (阴娇)**, **above the three hairs**, and **the blood vessels to induce bleeding**.

If **men are afflicted like by a parasitic infection** and **women are afflicted like by a uterine disorder**, with **the body and waist resembling dissolution**, and **no desire for food or drink**, first needle **Yǒngquán (涌泉)** to induce bleeding; if the **pulse above the navel**#7 is **strong**, **exhaustively induce bleeding**.

**[Phonetic Notes]**#7 *Jìng* (痉) is pronounced *Jù píng jiǐng qiě*.
*Jìn* (噤) is pronounced *Jù jìn*.
*Lìng* (龄) is pronounced *jiè*.
**Heatstroke with alternating cold and heat**#7 is the **24th illness**.

---

**Headache (厥头痛):**
If the **head aches (厥)**, and the **face appears swollen and restless (烦心)**, needle **Zúyángmíng (足阳明)** and **Tàiyīn (太阴)**.

If the **headache (厥头痛)** is accompanied by **pain in the head’s pulse (头脉)**, **melancholy (心悲)**, **excessive weeping (善泣)**, and **the occipital artery (视头动脉)** is **distended (反盛)**, **exhaustively bleed it**; afterward, regulate **Zújuéyīn (足厥阴)**.

If the **headache (厥头痛)** is **persistent and heavy (贞贞)**, with **lines drawn on the head (写头上五行)**, **five lines in total (行五)**, first needle **Shǒushàoyīn (手少阴)**, then **Zúshàoyīn (足少阴)**.

If the **headache (厥头痛)** is **marked by forgetfulness (意善忘)**, and **pressure brings no relief (按之不得)**, needle **the head’s and face’s left and right arteries (头面左右动脉)**, then **Zútàiyīn (足太阴)**.

If the **headache (厥头痛)** first **afflicts the nape (项)**, and **the waist and spine respond (腰脊为应)**, first needle **Tiānzhù (天柱)**, then **Zútàiyáng (足太阳)**.

If the **headache (厥头痛)** is **severe**, with **pulsing heat in the ears’ anterior and posterior vessels (耳前后脉涌有热)**—one text says **Shíyǒu (石有)** has **dynamic arteries (动脉)**—bleed them; afterward, needle **Zúshàoyáng (足少阳)**.

**True Headache (真头痛):**
If **the entire head throbs in pain (脑尽痛)**, with **hands and feet cold up to the joints (手足寒至节)**, it is **incurable (死不治)**.

**Headache (头痛) not to be needled on the face (脸):**
If struck or fallen upon, **evil blood (恶血)** remains **internally (在於内)**; if **flesh is injured (肉伤)**, and **pain persists (痛未已)**, then **needling is permissible (可则刺)**, but **distal points are not to be used (不可远取)**.

**Headache (头痛) not to be needled:**
If **major *bì* (痹)** is **evil (为恶)**, and **the condition worsens daily (日作者)**, it may be **mitigated slightly (可令少愈)**, but **not cured (不可已)**.

If **half the head is cold and painful (头半寒痛)**, first needle **Shǒushàoyáng (手少阳)** and **Yángmíng (阳明)**, then **Zúshàoyáng (足少阳)** and **Yángmíng (阳明)**.

**Heartache (厥心痛):**
If **heartache (厥心痛)** **corresponds with the back (与背相控)**, and **heals easily (善愈)**, as though **struck from behind (如从后触其心)**, with **a hunched posture (偃楼)**, it is **kidney-heartache (肾心痛)**; first needle **Jīnggǔ (京骨)** and **Kūnlún (昆仑)**, then **Rángǔ (然谷)**.

If **heartache (厥心痛)** is **accompanied by abdominal distension (腹胀)** and **chest fullness (胸满)**, with **the heart pain being especially severe (心尤痛甚)**, it is **stomach-heartache (胃心痛)**; needle **Dàdū (大都)** and **Tàibái (太白)**.

If **heartache (厥心痛)** feels **as if pierced with a sharp needle (如以锥针刺其心)**, and **pain is extreme (心痛甚)**, it is **spleen-heartache (脾心痛)**; needle **Rángǔ (然谷)** and **Tàixī (太溪)**.

If **heartache (厥心痛)** presents **with a livid complexion (色苍苍)**, resembling **a dying state (如死状)**, and **no relief throughout the day (终日不得太息)**, it is **liver-heartache (肝心痛)**; needle **Hángwèn (行问)** and **Tàichōng (太冲)**.

If **heartache (厥心痛)** appears **as if the heart were externally exposed (外若徒居心痛问)**, and **pain worsens with movement (动作痛益甚)** without **altering the complexion (色不变)**, it is **lung-heartache (肺心痛)**; needle **Yújì (鱼际)** and **Tàyuān (太渊)**.

**True Heartache (真心痛):**
If **hands and feet are cold up to the joints (手足情至节)**, with **severe heart pain (心痛甚)**, it **begins in the morning and ends at dusk (旦发夕死)**, or **begins in the evening and ends the next morning (夕发日一死)**.

**Heartache (心痛) not to be needled:**
If **internal *qi* is excessive (中有盛聚)**, it **must not be needled on the face (不可取於脸)**.

If **worms or *jiāo* (蛟瘠)** are found within the intestines (肠中有虫痕及蛟瘠)**, **needling with small pins is forbidden (皆不可取以小针)**.

If **heart-intestinal pain (心肠痛)** **forms dense masses (浓作痛肿聚)**, **moving up and down (往来上下行)**, with **periods of remission (痛有休止)**, **abdominal heat (腹热)**, **thirst with excessive saliva (喜渴涎出)**, it is **the *jiāo* (蛟瘠)**. **Press firmly with the hand (以手聚按)** and **hold steady (而坚持之)**, ensuring **the worm does not shift (无令得移)**; use **a large needle (大针)** to pierce it, **holding for a long time (久持之)** until **the worm stirs not (虫不动)**, then **remove the needle (乃出针)**. **Pain then migrates upward (悲腹侬痛，形中上者)**.

**Deafness (耳聋) with no hearing (无闻):**
Needle **within the ear (耳中)**.

**Tinnitus (耳呜):**
Needle **the artery before the ear (耳前动脉)**.

**Ear pain (耳痛) not to be needled:**
If **pus is within the ear (耳中有脓)**, or **if there is dryness (乾)**, it is **a severe condition (取狞)**; **the ear loses hearing (耳无闻)**.

**Deafness (耳聋):**
Needle **the junction of the nail and flesh on the small and second fingers of the hand (手小指次指爪甲上与肉交者)**, first **hand (手)**, then **foot (足)**; **left for right, right for left (左取右，右取左)**.

**Tinnitus (耳呜):**
Needle **the artery of the hand (手中)**.

**Leg cramp (足霹) preventing lifting:**
Needle **laterally (侧而取之)**, at **the *shū* (枢) and **Hé (合)** junction, using **a sharp, round needle (圆利针)**; **large needles are forbidden (大针不可刺)**.

**Bloodletting (病注下血):**
Needle **Qūquán (曲泉)**.

**Wind *bì* (痹) with excessive *liàn* (滦)**:
If **the disease is incurable (不可已)**, **the foot feels like walking on ice (足如履冰)**, **as if descending into a well (时如入扬中)**, **with *liàn* (滦) in the thigh meridian (股经淫滦)**, **restlessness in the heart (烦心)**, **headache (头痛)**, **occasional vomiting (时呕)**, **occasional diarrhea (时悦)**, **dizziness (眩)** that **ends in sweating (已汗出)**, **prolonged illness leading to visual impairment (久则目眩)**, **with alternating sadness and fear (悲以喜恐)**, **shortness of breath (短气)**, **and no joy (不乐)**, it **will not exceed three years before death (不出三年，死也)**.

**[Phonetic Notes]**
**Zhènzhèn (贞贞)** is pronounced *Chàn gēng qiè*.

「侬乃老切」—*Nóng nǎi lǎo qiè* (untranslated phonetic notation).

**Phonetic Notes:**
- **Tragic sound (悲音)**—*Pēng yīn* (boiling sound).
- **Full (满)**—*Mǎn* (also).

**Procedures:**
- **Remove earwax (取咛上)**—*Qǔ ěr zhōng gòu* (extract the wax from within the ear).
- **Biàn (卞)**—*Bìàn* (top, referring to the crown of the head).

---
**From the *Huangdi Neijing Ling Shu Jizhu* (卷之十, *Disease Origins*, 第二十五):**
*(Annotations from *Taisu* and *Jiayi Jing* variants provided for textual cross-references.)*

---
**#1** *Taisu* (卷十六, *Miscellaneous Sayings on Cold and Heat*) states: **"hand" (手)** instead of *foot* (足).
**#2** *Whip (鞭)*—originally *"whip"* (鞭), corrected from *Zhao Fu Respectful Hall* edition.
**#3** *Its (其)*—*Taisu* (卷二十六, *Miscellaneous Sayings on Cold and Heat*) uses **"excessive" (甚)**.
**#4** *Then (则)*—originally *"distinguish" (别)*, corrected from *Zhao Fu Respectful Hall* edition.
**#5** *Do not puncture the skin (勿刺肤)*—*Jiayi Jing* (卷七第一) reads: **"need not puncture" (勿庸刺)**.
**#6** *Spleen (胰)*—*Jiayi Jing* (卷七第一) reads: **"meridian" (庸)**.
**#7** *Harsh (苛)*—originally *"strange" (奇)*, corrected from *Zhao Fu Respectful Hall* edition.
**#8** *Leaning (倚)*—*Jiayi Jing* (卷七第一) reads: **"distressed" (烦)**.
**#9** *Skin (皮)*—likely a miswriting; should be **"pulse" (脉)**.
**#10** *Arm (胳)*—*Jiayi Jing* (卷七第一) reads: **"network" (络)**.
**#11** *Feathergrass (蓬)*—*Jiayi Jing* (卷九第二) reads: **"chive" (韭)**.

「When illness appears first and then is followed by nausea (逆), treat its **root (本)**; when nausea appears first and then is followed by illness, treat its **root (本)**; when cold (寒) appears first and then illness arises, treat its **root (本)**; when illness appears first and then cold arises, treat its **root (本)**; when heat (热) appears first and then illness arises, treat its **root (本)**; when illness appears first and then heat arises, treat its **root (本)**#1; when diarrhea (泄) appears first and then other illnesses arise, treat its **root (本)**. Adjust (调) it **necessarily (必)**, then treat other illnesses.

When illness appears first and then abdominal distension (中满) follows, treat its **manifestation (标)**; when illness appears first#2 and then diarrhea follows, treat its **root (本)**; when abdominal distension appears first and then mental distress (烦心) follows, treat its **root (本)**.

There is **guest-qi (客气)** and there is **harmonious-qi (同气)**.

When urination and defecation are not smooth (不利), treat its **manifestation (标)**; when urination and defecation are smooth (利), treat its **root (本)**.

When illness arises with **excess (余)**, root is the **manifestation (标)**—first treat its **root (本)**, then its **manifestation (标)**; when illness arises with **deficiency (不足)**, manifestation is the **root (本)**—first treat its **manifestation (标)**, then its **root (本)**.

Examine and question carefully, **excessively (甚)**; adjust with intention (以意调之). For mild cases, treat both simultaneously (间者并行); for severe cases, treat one at a time (甚为独行).

When urination and defecation are not smooth first and then other illnesses arise, treat its **root (本)**. *Miscellaneous Illnesses* (杂病) #26.

**Fainting (厥)** with pain along the spine (脊) extending to the crown (至顶), heavy head (头沉沉然), blurred vision (目谎谎然), stiff lumbar spine (腰脊强), **extract (取) blood from the** foot **Taiyang (太阳) meridian’s** *Xin (血) network (络)*.

**Fainting (厥)** with chest fullness (胸满) and facial swelling (面肿), dry lips (唇漯漯然), sudden speech difficulty (暴言难), **excessively (甚)** even unable to speak (甚则不能言), **extract (取)** from the foot **Yangming (阳明)**.

**Fainting (厥)** with *qi* rushing to the throat (气走喉), unable to speak, accompanied by numbness in hands and feet (手足情), difficult defecation (大便不利), **extract (取)** from the foot **Shaoyin (少阴)**.

**Fainting (厥)** with abdominal distension (腹向向然), excessive cold-qi (多寒气), rumbling in the abdomen (谷谷), difficult urination (便没难), **extract (取)** from the foot **Taiyin (太阴)**.

**Hoarseness (哑) and dryness (乾)**, mouth hot like glue (口中热如胶), **extract (取)** from the foot **Shaoyin (少阴)**.

Pain in the knee (膝中痛), **extract (取)** from the *Tui Bi (犊鼻)* (calf’s nose), using a **round-tipped needle (圆利针)**. **Puncture (发)** and inquire (问之), needle as thick as a *wù* (骛) (a type of arrow), **insert (刺)** into the knee without hesitation (膝无疑).

**Throat obstruction (喉痹)**, unable to speak, **extract (取)** from the foot **Yangming (阳明)**; if able to speak, **extract (取)** from the hand **Yangming (阳明)**.

**Malaria (疟)**, without thirst (不渴), **paroxysms (作)** follow the day’s cycle (问日而作), **extract (取)** from the foot **Yangming (阳明)**; with thirst and paroxysms following the day’s cycle (渴而日作), **extract (取)** from the hand **Yangming (阳明)**.

**Toothache (齿痛)**, without aversion to cold or heat (不恶情饮), **extract (取)** from the foot **Yangming (阳明)**; with aversion to cold or heat (恶情饮), **extract (取)** from the hand **Yangming (阳明)**.

**Deafness (聋)** without pain (不痛), **extract (取)** from the foot **Shaoyang (少阳)**; deafness with pain (聋而痛), **extract (取)** from the hand **Yangming (阳明)**.

**Epistaxis (衄)** that does not cease, with blood flowing uncontrollably (恤血流), **extract (取)** from the foot **Taiyang (太阳)**; if blood flows uncontrollably (恤血), **extract (取)** from the hand **Taiyang (太阳)**. If it does not cease (不已), **puncture (刺)** below the *Wan Gu (宛骨)* (a bone in the upper arm); if it does not cease (不已), **puncture (刺)** the *Xin (血) network (络)* in the **Taiyang (太阳)** meridian to induce bleeding (出血).

**Lumbar pain (腰痛)**, when pain is accompanied by cold (痛上寒), **extract (取)** from the foot **Taiyang (太阳)** and **Yangming (阳明)**; when pain is accompanied by heat (痛上热), **extract (取)** from the foot **Jueyin (厥阴)**; when unable to bend or straighten (不可以俯仰), **extract (取)** from the foot **Shaoyang (少阳)**.

When **heat (热)** causes **shortness of breath (喘)**, **extract (取)** from the foot **Shaoyin (少阴)** and the *Xin (血) network (络)* in the **Taiyang (太阳)** meridian.

When **emotional disturbances (喜怒)** cause loss of appetite (不欲食), **puncture (刺)** the foot **Taiyin (太阴)**#3; when **anger (怒)** causes excessive speech (多言), **puncture (刺)** the foot **Shaoyang (少阳)**.

**Headache (颅痛)**, **puncture (刺)** the hand **Yangming (阳明)** and the *Sheng Mai (盛脉)* (abundant pulse) in the forehead (颜) to induce bleeding (出血).

**Neck pain (项痛)**, when unable to bend or straighten (不可俯仰), **puncture (刺)** the foot **Taiyang (太阳)**; when unable to turn the head (不可以顾), **puncture (刺)** the hand **Taiyang (太阳)**.

**Lower abdominal distension (小腹满)**, rising to the stomach (上走胃), reaching the heart (至心), with alternating cold and heat (淅淅身时寒热), difficult urination (小便不利), **extract (取)** from the foot **Jueyin (厥阴)**.

**Abdominal distension (腹满)**, difficult defecation (大便不利), large abdomen (腹大), also rising to the chest (上走胸嗑), with **labored breathing (喘息)** and harsh sounds (喝喝然), **extract (取)** from the foot **Shaoyin (少阴)**.

**Abdominal distension (腹满)**, food not digested (食不化), abdominal rumbling (腹向向然), unable to defecate (不能大便), **extract (取)** from the foot **Taiyin (太阴)**.

**Heart pain (心痛)** radiating to the lumbar spine (引腰脊), with desire to vomit (欲呕), **extract (取)** from the foot **Shaoyin (少阴)**.

**Heart pain (心痛)**, abdominal bloating (腹胀), with **harsh rumbling (啬啬然)** and difficult defecation (大便不利), **extract (取)** from the foot **Taiyin (太阴)**.

**Heart pain (心痛)** radiating to the back (引背), unable to breathe (不得息), **puncture (刺)** the foot **Shaoyin (少阴)**; if not ceased (不已), **extract (取)** from the hand **Shaoyang (少阳)**.

**Heart pain (心痛)** radiating to lower abdominal distension (引小腹满), with no fixed location (上下无常处), difficult urination (便没难), **puncture (刺)** the foot **Jueyin (厥阴)**.

**Heart pain (心痛)** with only shortness of breath (但短气), insufficient to cause distress (不足以息), **puncture (刺)** the hand **Taiyin (太阴)**.

**Heart pain (心痛)**, when at the **Nine Nodes (九节)**#4, **puncture (刺)** the **Nine Nodes (九节)**; if not ceased (按已), **puncture (刺)** the pressing point (按之), and it will cease immediately (立已); if not ceased (不已), search above and below (上下求之), and upon finding it (得之), it will cease immediately (立已).

**Forehead pain (颜痛)**, **puncture (刺)** the foot **Yangming (阳明)** *Qu Zhou (曲周)* (a specific location) **pulse (动脉)** to induce bleeding (见血), and it will cease immediately (立已); if not ceased (不已), press the *Ren Ying (人迎)* (a pulse point) along the meridian (於经), and it will cease immediately (立已).

When *qi* **rushes upward (逆上)**, **puncture (刺)** the **depressed (陷) points** in the chest (膺中) and the **pulse (动脉)** below the sternum (下胸).

**Abdominal pain (腹痛)**, **puncture (刺)** the **pulse (动脉)** on either side of the umbilicus (脐左右); if not ceased (已刺), press the punctured point (按之), and it will cease immediately (立已); if not ceased (不已), **puncture (刺)** the *Qi Jie (气街)* (a pulse point), and upon pressing the punctured point (已刺按之), it will cease immediately (立已).

**Paralysis (痿) and fainting (厥)**, when the **four extremities (四末)** are **bound and numb (束悦)**, **rapidly (乃)** **dissolve (疾解)** the **networks (络)** in two sessions per day (日二); if numbness persists (不仁), it will resolve in ten days (十日而知), without rest (无休), and the illness will cease (病已止).

**Hiccups (哕)**#5, **insert (以草刺)** a grass needle (草) into the nose (鼻), causing sneezing (嚏); upon sneezing (嚏), hiccups will cease (嚏而已).

If there is no relief (无息), **rapidly (疾)** **welcome and guide (迎引)** the *qi*, and it will cease immediately (立已); if **startled (大惊)**, it can also be relieved (亦可已).」

**Phonetic Explanation:**
*"Xiàng"* sounds like *"xiǎng"* (响).
*"Gǔ"* sounds like *"hú"* (斛).

**Chapter 27: *Zhōu Bì* (Circulating Paralysis)**
The Yellow Emperor asked Qī Bó:
*"When **circulating paralysis (周痹)** manifests in the body, it moves upward and downward, following the pulse in its ascent and descent, corresponding left and right, leaving no space unasked. I wish to hear—does this pain reside within the blood vessels, or between the muscles? How does it arise? Its shifting pain cannot be reached by a single needle, nor can its treatment be fixed while the pain persists—yet the pain ceases on its own. What principle governs this?"*

Qī Bó replied:
*"This is not **circulating paralysis (周痹)**—it is **generalized paralysis (众痹)**."*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"I wish to hear of **generalized paralysis (众痹)**."*

Qī Bó answered:
*"This occurs in fixed locations, erupting and subsiding alternately, shifting and resurfacing, with the right corresponding to the left and the left to the right. It does not circulate—it merely erupts and rests in turn."*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"Excellent. How should one puncture it?"*

Qī Bó replied:
*"When puncturing this, though the pain may cease, one must still treat its source to prevent recurrence."*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"Excellent. Now, what of **circulating paralysis (周痹)**?"*

Qī Bó answered:
*"**Circulating paralysis (周痹)** resides within the blood vessels, ascending and descending along the pulse, unable to shift laterally—each remains in its proper place."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"How should one puncture it?"*

Qī Bó replied:
*"If the pain moves from above downward, first puncture the lower point to **check (过)** it, then align the lower with the upper and puncture the upper point to **release (脱)** it. If the pain moves from below upward, first puncture the upper point to **check (过)** it, then puncture the lower point to **release (脱)** it."*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"Excellent. How does this pain arise? What causes it to be named thus?"*

Qī Bó replied:
*"When **wind, cold, and damp qi** lodge between the outer layers of flesh, pressing and congealing into **stagnation (沫)**, the stagnation, encountering cold, clumps together. As it clumps, it forces apart the flesh, causing **fissures (分裂)**—and where there are fissures, there is pain. When pain arises, the **spirit (神)** converges there, and where the spirit converges, **heat (热)** follows. Heat then leads to **ache and lassitude (痛懈)**, and when the ache subsides, **syncope (厥)** occurs. Syncope then gives rise to other **paralytic disorders (他痹)**, and this is how the cycle unfolds."*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"Excellent. I have grasped its meaning. This disorder does not originate within the **zàng (藏)**, nor does it manifest outwardly upon the **skin (皮)**—it resides solely between the layers of flesh, where the **true qi (真气)** cannot circulate freely, hence its name, **circulating paralysis (周痹)**.*

Thus, when puncturing **paralytic disorders (痹)**, one must first **examine (切循)** the six **meridians (经)** below, assessing their **emptiness and fullness (虚实)**. Then, one must locate the **blood stagnation (血结)** within the major **collaterals (大络)** that fails to flow, as well as the **hollow points where the pulse sinks (虚而脉陷空者)** and **regulate (调)** them. Apply **moxibustion (熨)** to promote circulation, and if the **stagnation (痣)** is firm, **redirect (转引)** the needle to disperse it.*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"Excellent. I have grasped its meaning, and also its methods. This is the **ninth principle (九者)** for **mastering the meridians (经巽)** and **harmonizing the diseases of the twelve meridians (十二经脉阴阳之病)**."*

【Sound Annotation】 *Chéng xū liù qiè.*

**Chapter Twenty-Eight: Questions on the Mouth**

The Yellow Emperor, in leisurely repose, dismissed his attendants and questioned Qi Bo, saying: *"I have already heard the Nine-Needle Canon, discussing the inversions and regularities of yin and yang, and the six meridians have been explained. Now I wish to learn the oral teachings."*

Qi Bo rose from his seat and bowed twice, replying: *"Noble inquiry indeed! This is what the former masters transmitted orally."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear the oral teachings."*

Qi Bo answered: *"All diseases begin with the influences of wind, rain, cold, and heat; the fluctuations of yin and yang from joy and anger; and the effects of diet, dwelling, sudden fright, and terror. When these occur, blood and qi separate, yin and yang disperse, the meridians and collaterals are obstructed, the pulse pathways are blocked, yin and yang counter each other, the defensive qi stagnates, the meridians and vessels become hollow and empty, and blood and qi flow irregularly. Thus, their natural order is lost.

Now, regarding disorders not found in the meridians, I shall explain their treatments.

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What qi causes the act of yawning?"*
Qi Bo replied: *"The defensive qi moves through the yang during the day and returns to the yin at night. The yin governs the night, and the night is external; the yang governs above, the yin governs below. Thus, yin-qi accumulates beneath, while yang-qi has not yet fully exhausted. The yang is drawn upward, and the yin is drawn downward; yin and yang pull against one another, causing repeated yawns. When yang-qi is spent and yin-qi prevails, the eyes close; when yin-qi is spent and yang-qi prevails, one awakens. Treat by supplementing the Foot Shaoyin and draining the Foot Taiyang."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What qi causes the act of belching?"*
Qi Bo replied: *"When food enters the stomach, the stomach’s qi ascends to the lungs. If lingering cold-qi and fresh food-qi both return to the stomach, the old and new clash, the true and the pathogenic attack each other, and the qi flows counter to its natural course, then it is expelled from the stomach—thus belching occurs. Treat by supplementing the Hand Taiyin and draining the Foot Shaoyin."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What qi causes the act of sighing (xī)?"*
Qi Bo replied: *"This is when yin-qi is excessive and yang-qi is deficient. The yin-qi moves swiftly while the yang-qi is sluggish; if yin-qi is excessive and yang-qi is utterly spent, then sighing (xī) occurs. Treat by supplementing the Foot Taiyang and draining the Foot Shaoyin."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What qi causes shivering and cold sensations?"*
Qi Bo replied: *"When cold-qi invades the skin, yin-qi is excessive and yang-qi is deficient—thus shivering and cold tremors arise. Treat by supplementing all the yang meridians."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What qi causes the act of groaning (yè)?"*
Qi Bo replied: *"When cold-qi invades the stomach, it flows upward in reverse, dispersing from below, and is expelled from the stomach—thus groaning (yè) occurs. Treat by supplementing the Foot Taiyin and Foot Yangming. This is also called the ‘eyebrow root’ treatment."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What qi causes sneezing?"*
Qi Bo replied: *"When yang-qi is harmonious and flows freely, it fills the heart and is expelled through the nose—thus sneezing occurs. Treat by supplementing the Foot Taiyang’s *Rong* and the eyebrow root. This is also called the ‘above-eyebrow’ treatment."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What qi causes the act of gasping (hán)?"*
