Qi Bo replied: *"If the stomach is not full, all the vessels become empty. When the vessels are empty, tendons and sinews slacken. If tendons and sinews slacken, movement in yin regions requires effort, and the breath cannot return—thus gasping (hán) occurs. Treat by supplementing the *flesh-between* points where the disorder manifests."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What qi causes weeping and tears when one is sorrowful?"*
Qi Bo replied: *"The heart is sovereign over the five zàng and six fǔ. The eyes are where the宗脉 converge, the pathway of the上液. The mouth and nostrils are the gates of the breath. Thus, when sorrow, grief, or melancholy stir the heart, the heart moves, and all the five zàng and six fǔ tremble. The宗脉 are affected, and the液 pathway opens—thus tears flow. The液 nourishes the essence and moistens the hollow orifices; when the上液 pathway opens, weeping occurs. If weeping does not cease, the液 is exhausted, and the essence is not nourished. If the essence is not nourished, the eyes cannot see—thus it is called ‘essence depletion.’ Treat by supplementing the *Tianzhu* meridian (挟颈#6)."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What qi causes deep sighing (tàixī)?"*
Qi Bo replied: *"When worry and thought tighten the heart’s cords, the breath pathway constricts, and the breath does not flow freely—thus one sighs deeply to expel it. Treat by supplementing the Hand Shaoyin, *Heart Master* (心主), and retaining the Foot Shaoyin’s *Rong* and Foot Shaoyin’s *Ling*."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What qi causes drooling?"*
Qi Bo replied: *"All food and drink enter the stomach. If there is heat in the stomach, the虫 stir, and the stomach relaxes. When the stomach relaxes, the *Lianquan* (廉泉) well opens—thus drooling occurs. Treat by supplementing the Foot Shaoyin."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What qi causes a buzzing sound in the ears?"*
Qi Bo replied: *"The ears are where the宗脉 converge. If the stomach is empty, the宗脉 become hollow and sink downward, and the collaterals are depleted—thus the ears buzz. Treat by supplementing the *Guest Master* (客主人) and the points on the tip of the Hand Yangming’s *Daizhi* (大指) nail where it meets the flesh."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What qi causes one to bite one’s own tongue?"*
Qi Bo replied: *"This is when逆流 ascends, and the pulse’s qi reaches its peak. When Foot Shaoyin’s qi reaches its peak, one bites the tongue; when Foot Shaoyin’s *Rong* qi reaches its peak, one bites the cheek; when Foot Yangming’s qi reaches its peak, one bites the lips. Observe which meridian governs the disorder and treat accordingly.

In total, these twelve pathological signs are all instances of奇邪 invading the hollow orifices. Thus, where the邪 resides, the natural qi is always insufficient. If the上气 is insufficient, the brain feels unfulfilled, the ears suffer buzzing, the head feels heavy, and the eyes grow dizzy; if the中气 is insufficient, the intestines and bowels change, and the intestines suffer rumbling; if the下气 is insufficient, then痿厥 and心悦 arise. Treat by supplementing the points below the Foot Taiyang’s external malleolus (溜#7)."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How should these disorders be treated?"*
Qi Bo replied: *"For yawning, the kidney governs—supplement the Foot Shaoyin; for belching, the lung governs—supplement the Hand Taiyin and Foot Shaoyin; for sighing (xī), yin and yang are utterly severed—supplement the Foot Taiyang and drain the Foot Shaoyin."*

Shivering with cold, supplement the *zhūyáng* channels;
Sighing (*yī*), supplement Foot Taiyin and Yangming;
Sneezing (*tì*), supplement Foot Taiyang and *méiběn*;
Coughing (*hàn*), supplement the *fēnròu* points where the disorder resides;
Tears flowing, supplement *Tiānzhù* (GB-10) and *Xiájiǎng* (GB-11)—*Xiájiǎng* being the midline of the head;
Deep sighing (*tàixī*), supplement Hand Shaoyin and *Xīnzhǔ* (PC-6), and Foot Shaoyang’s *liú* points;
Drooling (*xiánxià*), supplement Foot Shaoyin;
Ear buzzing (*ěrwū*), supplement *Kèzhǔrén* (GB-16) and the junction of flesh and nail on the thumb;
Spontaneous tongue protrusion (*zìpēnshé*), observe the governing disorder and supplement accordingly;
Dizziness and head tilt (*mùxuān tóuqīng*), supplement the *liú* points below the external malleolus;
*Wéijué xīnyuè*, prick the *liú* points two *cùn* above the junction of the big toe or below the external malleolus.

---
*The Yellow Emperor’s Inner Canon: Miraculous Pivot (Língshū) – Collected Commentary, Volume 10, concluded.*

---
*#1* The original text omitted these ten characters; supplemented from *Jiǎ-Yǐ Jīng*, Volume 6, Section 2.
*#2* "Disease" likely omits a following *"while"* (*ér*).
*#3* "Small" (*xiǎo*) appears as *"minor"* (*shǎo*) in *Tàisù*, Volume 30, *"Joy and Anger."*
*#4* "Prick" (*cì*) corrected from *"sequence"* (*cì*) per the Zhao Fǔ Respectful Hall edition.
*#5* "Belching" (*hàn*) corrected from *"year"* (*suì*) per *Jiǎ-Yǐ Jīng*, Volume 12, Section 1.
*#6* "Neck" (*jǐng*) appears as *"nape"* (*yǐng*) in *Tàisù*, Volume 27, *"Twelve Evils."*
*#7* "Drain" (*liú*) appears as *"retain"* (*liú*) in the Zhao Fǔ Respectful Hall edition.

---
*The Yellow Emperor’s Inner Canon: Miraculous Pivot – Collected Commentary, Volume 11, Master Transmission, Section 29.*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have heard#1 that my former teachers possessed certain principles stored in their hearts, which they did not commit to writing in any fixed form. I wish to hear and preserve them, then follow them in practice—applying them above to govern the people and below to cultivate my own body—so that the common folk may suffer no illness, and thus the ruler and people may live in harmony, with virtue and benevolence flowing downward, ensuring that descendants have no worries, and these teachings may be passed on to future generations without end. May I hear them?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"Your question reaches far indeed! Governing the people and governing oneself, treating others and treating oneself, addressing minor and major ailments, managing the state and the family—none can be treated successfully by opposing their nature. Only by following their nature can they be treated. Following their nature does not mean merely observing the proper sequence of yin and yang pulses and the flow of qi. Rather, it means aligning with the will of the people and common folk."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one follow their nature?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"When entering a country, inquire about its customs; when entering a home, ask about its taboos; when ascending the hall, question its rituals; and when attending to the sick, ask what would ease their condition."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one ease the sick?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"If there is internal heat and dampness is drained, the patient will prefer cold. For those suffering from cold, heat is preferred. Heat in the stomach consumes the grains, causing the patient to feel anxious and perpetually hungry, while the skin above the navel is hot. Heat in the intestines produces yellowish, gruel-like discharges, and the skin below the navel grows cold. Cold in the stomach causes abdominal distension; cold in the intestines causes intestinal rumbling, with frequent diarrhea. If the stomach is cold and the intestines are hot, there will be distension and diarrhea. If the stomach is hot and the intestines are cold, the patient will suffer from voracious hunger and pain in the lower abdomen."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"The stomach prefers cold and hunger#2, while the intestines prefer warmth and drink. When these two desires conflict, how should one proceed? Moreover, for the kings and great lords—those who feast on blood and grain—who indulge their desires, scorn others, and lack the ability to restrain themselves, if one restrains them, they will resist; if one follows their desires, their illnesses will worsen. How should one treat them, and what should take precedence?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"All people, without exception, dread death and seek life. If one informs them of their ruin, speaks of their blessings, guides them toward what would ease their condition, and opens their minds to their suffering, even those without virtue would find it difficult not to listen."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How should one treat them?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"In spring and summer, first address the superficial symptoms, then treat the root cause; in autumn and winter, first address the root cause, then treat the superficial symptoms."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one ease those whose conditions conflict?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"For those whose conditions conflict, their diet, clothing, and shelter should be adjusted to suit their need for cold or warmth, ensuring they are not exposed to lingering cold or excessive heat. In their food and drink, hot should not be overly scorching, and cold should not be overly piercing; if the balance of cold and warmth is appropriate, the vital qi will hold firm, and no harmful influences will take root."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"The fundamental teachings concern the body’s form, limbs, and flesh, as well as observing the relative sizes of the five viscera#3 and six palaces. Now, when addressing a ruler who ascends the throne and inquires of me, who can I press and trace to provide an answer?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"The body’s form and limbs are the coverings of the viscera and palaces, not the windows through which their conditions are observed."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I already know how the five viscera#3 manifest in the face. How can one observe their conditions through the limbs?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"The five viscera and six palaces, along with the four auxiliary organs, have the shoulders as their covering, and the hollow of the collarbone as their pathway, allowing one to observe their external manifestations."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"One more question."*

Qì Bó replied: *"The heart, which governs the five viscera and six palaces, has the sternal notch as its pathway. The [missing] bone#4, if it protrudes, indicates the condition of the diaphragm, and the [missing] bone#4, if it is excessive, reveals the state of the [missing] organ#5."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Good."*

Qì Bó replied: *"The liver, as the commander, has its condition observed externally. To determine its strength and stability, examine the size of the eyes. The [missing] organ#6, which governs the defense and calculation, has its condition observed externally. To determine its function in receiving nourishment, examine the lips and tongue for their quality and defects, and thus know the signs of fortune or misfortune."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Good."*

Qì Bó replied: *"The kidney, which governs the external aspects of the body, has its condition observed through the ability to distinguish distant sounds. To determine its nature, examine the ears for their preferences and aversions."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Good. I wish to hear how the six palaces are observed."*

Qì Bó replied: *"The stomach is like the sea, with broad shoulders, a large nape, and an expanded chest, allowing it to contain the five grains. The nostrils, if long, indicate the condition of the large intestine. Thick lips and a long philtrum indicate the condition of the small intestine. If the area below the eyes is full and rounded, the gallbladder is likely horizontal. If the nostrils are externally placed, the bladder may leak. If the nasal septum rises centrally, the sanjiao#7 is constricted. These are the ways to observe the six palaces. Above and below, in three categories, the viscera are stable and well."*

---
*#1* The original text omitted these ten characters; supplemented from *Jiǎ-Yǐ Jīng*, Volume 12, Section 1.
*#2* "Hunger" (*cì*) corrected from *"prick"* (*cì*) per the previous section.
*#3* "Visceras" (*zàng*) refers to the five viscera (心, 肝, 脾, 肺, 肾).
*#4* "Bone" (*gǔ*) likely refers to the *"xiphoid process"* (劍骨) or *"sternum"* (胸骨).
*#5* "Organ" (*zàng*) likely refers to the *"diaphragm"* (膈) or *"sanjiao"* (三焦).
*#6* "Organ" (*zàng*) corrected from *"palace"* (*fǔ*) per the previous section.
*#7* "Sanjiao" (三焦) – the three warmers, a concept in TCM referring to the upper, middle, and lower regions of the body's metabolic processes.*

**Decision on Qi (Chapter 30)**
The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have heard that humans possess **jing** (精), **qi** (气), **jin** (津), **ye** (液), **xue** (血), and **mai** (脉). I thought these were all just one **qi**, but now they are distinguished as six. I do not understand why."*

Qìbó replied: *"The two **shen** (神) struggle and contend, then merge to form shape—this is **jing**. It is born before the body and is called **jing**."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What is **qi**?"*
Qìbó said: *"The upper **jiao** (焦) opens and spreads, disseminating the flavors of the five grains. It nourishes the skin, fills the body, and moistens the hair like mist and dew—this is **qi**."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What is **jin**?"*
Qìbó said: *"When the pores open and sweat pours out—this is **jin**."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What is **ye**?"*
Qìbó said: *"When grain enters and **qi** is full, it descends into the bones like a marsh, lubricating joints and supplementing the brain and marrow, making the skin supple—this is **ye**."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What is **xue**?"*
Qìbó said: *"The middle **jiao** receives **qi** and extracts its essence, transforming it into **xue**—this is **xue**."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What is **mai**?"*
Qìbó said: *"When **ying** (营) **qi** is obstructed and has nowhere to retreat—this is **mai**."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"If these six **qi** are in excess or deficiency, how can one know the abundance or scarcity of **qi**, the fullness or emptiness of the brain and marrow, and the clarity or turbidity of the blood and vessels?"*

Qìbó replied: *"If **jing** is depleted, hearing is impaired; if **qi** is depleted, vision is blurred; if **jin** is depleted, the pores open and sweat pours out; if **ye** is depleted, joints stiffen, the complexion dims, the brain wastes away, the spine aches, and ringing sounds are heard in the ears; if **xue** is depleted, the complexion pales and loses luster; if **mai** is depleted#3, the pulse becomes hollow and empty. These are their signs."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Among these six **qi**, which are most noble?"*
Qìbó replied: *"Each of the six **qi** has its own domain. Though their nobility and inferiority may vary, the five grains and the stomach are the greatest benefactors."*

---
**The Intestines and Stomach (Chapter 31)**
The Yellow Emperor asked Bó Gāo: *"I wish to hear of the intestines and stomach—how they transmit grain, their size, length, and capacity for receiving food."*

Bó Gāo replied: *"Let me explain fully. The measurements from where grain enters to where it exits are as follows: From the lips to the teeth, nine **fen**; the mouth is two **cun** wide; from the teeth to the epiglottis, three **cun** deep, with a capacity of five **ge**; the tongue weighs ten **liang**, is seven **cun** long, and two **cun** wide; the throat weighs ten **liang**, is two **cun**#4 wide, and extends sixteen **cun** to the stomach; the stomach, when stretched, is two **zhang** six **cun** long, one **zhang** five **cun** wide, and five **cun** in diameter, with a capacity of two **dou** five **sheng**; the small intestine clings to the spine, looping leftward in overlapping coils. Where it joins the **huí cháng** (回肠), it attaches above the navel, winding sixteen times in a circuit, two **cun**#5 wide, eight **fen** in diameter (minus half a **fen**), and three **zhang** three **cun**#6 long; the **huí cháng** circles the navel, looping leftward in sixteen reverse coils, four **cun** wide, one **cun** in diameter (minus half a **fen**), and two **zhang** one **cun** long; the large intestine clings to the spine, receiving the **huí cháng**, looping leftward above and below the spine, eight **cun** wide, two **cun** in diameter (plus half a **fen**), and two **zhang** eight **cun** long. From where grain enters to where it exits, the total length is six **zhang** four **cun** four **fen**, winding thirty-two times in reverse loops."*

---
**The Fasted Commoner (Chapter 32)**
The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to know why a person dies after seven days without food."*

Bó Gāo replied: *"Let me explain. The stomach is one **zhang** five **cun** long, five **cun** in diameter, and two **zhang** six **cun** wide, capable of holding three **dou** five **sheng** of water and grain. Normally, two **dou** of grain and one **dou** five **sheng** of water remain within it. The upper **jiao** releases refined **qi**, swift and forceful, while the lower **jiao** irrigates the intestines below. The small intestine is two **cun**#5 wide, eight **fen** in diameter (minus half a **fen**), and three **zhang** two **cun** long, holding two **dou** four **sheng** of grain and six **sheng** three **ge** (plus half a **ge**) of water. The **huí cháng** is four **cun** wide, one **cun** in diameter (minus half a **fen**), and two **zhang** one **cun** long, holding one **dou** of grain and seven **sheng** half of water. The large intestine is eight **cun** wide, two **cun** in diameter (plus half a **fen**), and two **zhang** eight **cun** long, holding nine **sheng** three **ge** eight **fen** (plus one **fen**) of grain. The total length of the intestines and stomach is five **zhang** eight **cun** four **cun**, holding nine **dou** two **sheng** one **ge** (plus half a **ge**) of water and grain. In a healthy person, the stomach fills while the intestines empty, and vice versa, allowing **qi** to circulate freely. Thus, the five viscera remain stable, the blood and vessels harmonious, and the **shen** (神) resides. The **shen** is the refined **qi** of water and grain. Therefore, the intestines and stomach should retain two **dou** of grain and one **dou** five **sheng** of water. A healthy person defecates twice daily, expelling two **sheng** half each time—five **sheng** per day. Over seven days, this totals three **dou** five **sheng**, exhausting all retained water and grain. Thus, a healthy person dies after seven days without food because the refined **qi**, **jin**, and **ye** are all depleted."*

---
**The Sea of Discourse (Chapter 33)**
The Yellow Emperor asked Qìbó: *"I have heard of the **ci** (刺) method from Master [Zhong]. His teachings never stray from **ying** and **wei**, **xue**, and **qi**."*

The Yellow Emperor asked Qìbó: *"The twelve primary channels—do they not internally connect to the **fǔzàng** (府藏, *Storage Palaces*), and externally network with the limbs and joints? Yet you speak of harmonizing them with the four seas. How so?"*

Qìbó replied: *"Humans also possess four seas and twelve channel waters. The channel waters all converge into the seas. The seas gather the west, south, north, and east, and are thus called the four seas."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How do humans correspond to this?"*

Qìbó answered: *"Humans have the **suíhǎi** (髓海, *Bone Marrow Sea*), the **xuèhǎi** (血海, *Blood Sea*), the **qìhǎi** (气海, *Qi Sea*), and the **shuǐgǔzhīhǎi** (水谷之海, *Sea of Water and Grain*). These four correspond to the four seas."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Your teachings harmonize human and Heaven, Earth, and the four seas—how profound! I wish to hear how this correspondence is achieved."*

Qìbó replied: *"One must first clearly understand the locations of **yīn-yáng** (阴阳, *Yin-Yang*), **biǎolǐ** (表里, *Exterior-Interior*), **róng** (荣, *Nourishing*), and **shū** (输, *Converging*). Only then can the four seas be defined."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How are they defined?"*

Qìbó answered: *"The stomach is the **Sea of Water and Grain** (水谷之海), its converging point above at **qìjiē** (气街, *Qi Street*), below at **sānlǐ** (三里, *Three Li*).
The **chōngmài** (冲脉, *Penetrating Vessel*) is the **Sea of the Twelve Channels** (十二经之海), its converging point above at **dàzhù** (大杼, *Great Zhu*), below emerging at **jùxū** (巨虚, *Great Emptiness*) above and below at **lián** (廉, *Flanks).
**Tánzhōng** (膻中, *Mid-Chest*) is the **Sea of Qi** (气之海), its converging point above at the **zhùgǔ** (柱骨, *Pillar Bone*) above and below, and forward at **rényíng** (人迎, *Welcome of Man).
The brain is the **Sea of Bone Marrow** (髓之海), its converging point above at its **cover**, below at **fēngfǔ** (风府, *Wind Palace)."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What benefits or harms arise from these four seas? What causes life or decay?"*

Qìbó replied: *"When they are in harmony, life ensues; when they are contrary, decay follows. When one knows how to regulate them, benefit arises; when one does not, harm ensues."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How do the four seas become contrary or harmonious?"*

Qìbó replied: *"If the **Qi Sea** (气海) is in excess, the **qi** swells in the chest, causing **yùxī** (悦息, *Contented Breathing*) and flushed face.
If the **Qi Sea** is deficient, the **qi** is scarce, insufficient even for speech.
If the **Blood Sea** (血海) is in excess, one constantly feels their body is large, agitated yet unaware of their illness.
If the **Blood Sea** is deficient, one also constantly feels their body is small, constricted yet unaware of their illness.
If the **Sea of Water and Grain** (水谷之海) is in excess, the abdomen is full.
If the **Sea of Water and Grain** is deficient, hunger persists, yet one cannot accept nourishment.
If the **Bone Marrow Sea** (髓海) is in excess, one feels light and nimble, with abundant strength, surpassing their natural limits.
If the **Bone Marrow Sea** is deficient, the brain spins, ears ring, channels ache with vertigo and dizziness, vision fails, and one lies sluggishly, content in repose."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"I have already heard of contrary and harmonious states. How does one regulate them?"*

Qìbó replied: *"Carefully observe their converging points, and regulate their excesses and deficiencies. Avoid violating their principles—when in harmony, they restore balance; when contrary, they lead to decay."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Well spoken."*

**Chapter 34: The Five Disorders**
The Yellow Emperor asked: *"The twelve channels, categorized under the five elements and divided into the four seasons—what causes their disorder? What brings their healing?"*

Qìbó replied: *"The five elements have their sequence, the four seasons their divisions. When they align, harmony prevails; when they conflict, disorder arises."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What do you mean by alignment?"*

Qìbó replied: *"The twelve channels correspond to the twelve months. The twelve months are divided into the four seasons—spring, autumn, winter, and summer—each with its distinct **qi**. The **yíngwèi** (营卫, *Nourishing-Defensive*) follow their cycles, **yīn-yáng** (阴阳) are in balance, and the **qīngzhuó** (清浊, *Pure-Turbid*) do not interfere. Thus, alignment leads to harmony."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What do you mean by conflict and disorder?"*

Qìbó replied: *"When pure **qi** resides in **yīn** (阴) and turbid **qi** in **yáng** (阳), the **yíng** (营, *Nourishing Qi*) flows along the channels, while the **wèi** (卫, *Defensive Qi*) moves contrary. When pure and turbid **qi** interfere, disorder arises in the chest—this is called **dàyù** (大悦, *Great Delight*).
Thus, when **qi** is disordered in the heart, one suffers **fánxīn mìhē** (烦心密嘿, *Anxious Heart and Silent Speech*), bows their head, and lies still.
When **qi** is disordered in the lungs, one pants and gasps, raising their hands while inhaling.
When **qi** is disordered in the intestines and stomach, it causes **huòluàn** (霍乱, *Sudden Turmoil*).
When **qi** is disordered in the arm channels, it leads to **sìjué** (四厥, *Four Reversals*).
When **qi** is disordered in the head, it results in **jué nì** (厥逆, *Reversed Collapse*), heaviness of the head, vertigo, and falling."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Are there methods to treat the Five Disorders through **ci** (刺, *acupuncture)?"*

Qìbó replied: *"There are methods to approach and to withdraw—when one discerns their proper application, they hold the key to preserving life."*

When **qi** resides in the heart, treat it at the **Shǎo Yīn** (*Hand Lesser Yin*) channel’s **Shǔ** (*Output*) point of the **Heart Governor**.
When **qi** resides in the lungs, treat it at the **Tài Yīn** (*Hand Greater Yin*) channel’s **Róng** (*Nourishing*) point and the **Shǎo Yīn** (*Foot Lesser Yin*) channel’s **Shǔ** (*Output*) point.
When **qi** resides in the intestines and stomach, treat it at the **Tài Yīn** (*Foot Greater Yin*) and **Yáng Míng** (*Yang Brightness*) channels; if the disorder persists below, treat it at **Sān Lǐ** (*Three Miles*).
When **qi** resides in the head, treat it at **Tiān Zhù** (*Heavenly Pillar*) and **Dà Zhù** (*Great Pillar*); if the disorder persists, treat it at the **Tài Yáng** (*Foot Greater Yang*) channel’s **Róng** (*Nourishing*) and **Shǔ** (*Output*) points.
When **qi** resides in the arms and legs, first clear the blood vessels, then treat the **Yáng Míng** (*Yang Brightness*) and **Shǎo Yáng** (*Lesser Yang*) channels’ **Róng** (*Nourishing*) and **Shǔ** (*Output*) points.

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one apply reinforcement and reduction?"*

Qìbó replied: *"Slow insertion and slow withdrawal—this is called **dǎo qì** (导气, *Guiding Qi*). Reinforcement and reduction without form—this is called **tóng jīng** (同精, *Harmonizing Essence*). It is not about excess or deficiency, but about the disordered **qi** clashing in opposition."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"This is a profound Dao, a clear theory. May I record it on jade tablets and name it *Treatise on Regulating Disorders*?"

**Chapter Thirty-Five: Treatise on Distension**

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does the pulse at the **cùnkǒu** (*wrist*) manifest in distension?"*

Qìbó replied: *"When the pulse is large, firm, and rough, this is distension."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one recognize distension in the **zàngfǔ** (*zang-fu organs*)?"*

Qìbó replied: *"The **yīn** (*yin*) pertains to the **zàng** (*zang*), the **yáng** (*yang*) to the **fǔ** (*fu*)."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Does the **qi** that causes distension reside within the blood vessels, or within the **zàngfǔ** (*zang-fu organs*)?"*

Qìbó replied: *"Both exist, yet neither is the dwelling place of distension."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Then where does distension dwell?"*

Qìbó replied: *"Distension resides outside the **zàngfǔ** (*zang-fu organs*), pressing against them like a wall around the chest and ribs, swelling the skin. Hence, it is called *distension*."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"..."*

*"The **zàngfǔ** (*zang-fu organs*) lie within the chest, ribs, and abdomen like treasures locked in a chest—each has its proper place, though their names differ while their locations coincide. Within a single domain, their **qi** varies. I wish to hear the reason."*

The Yellow Emperor asked again: *"Explain its meaning."*

Qìbó replied: *"The chest and abdomen are the outer enclosure of the **zàngfǔ** (*zang-fu organs*).
**Shàn Zhōng** (*Mid-Chest*) is the palace of the **Heart Governor**.
The **stomach** is the **Tài Cāng** (*Great Granary*).
The **throat and small intestine** serve as conduits for transmission.
The **five apertures of the stomach** are the inner gates and doors.
**Lián Quán** (*Adam’s Apple*) and **Yù Yīng** (*Jade Flower*) are the pathways of **jīn yè** (*body fluids*).
Thus, the **wǔ zàng liù fǔ** (*Five Zang and Six Fu*) each have their boundaries, and their diseases each have distinct forms.

When **yíng qì** (*Nourishing Qi*) flows through the vessels and **wèi qì** (*Defensive Qi*) moves counter to the pulse, it causes **pulse distension**. When **wèi qì** merges with the pulse and follows the **fēn** (*muscle layers*), it causes **skin distension**.
For reduction, three treatments suffice: near disorders require one treatment, distant disorders require three. Whether **xū** (*deficient*) or **shí** (*excess*), the skill lies in swift reduction."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"I wish to hear the forms of distension."*

Qìbó replied: *"When the **heart** is distended, one suffers **fánxīn** (*anxious heart*), shortness of breath, and restless sleep.
When the **lungs** are distended, there is a hollow fullness with wheezing and coughing.
When the **liver** is distended, the ribs ache with pain radiating to the lower abdomen.
When the **spleen** is distended, one yawns frequently, the limbs feel restless and weary, the body feels heavy and unable to wear clothing, and one cannot stand still.
When the **kidneys** are distended, the abdomen is full with pain radiating to the lower back, and the lumbar region aches.

Distension of the **six fu**:
When the **stomach** is distended, the abdomen is full, the **stomach’s courtyard** aches, the nose detects a burnt odor, eating is hindered, and defecation is difficult.
When the **large intestine** is distended, the intestines rumble with a painful, splashing sensation; in winter, excessive cold exposure leads to **fěn xiè** (*undigested food in stool*).
When the **small intestine** is distended, the lower abdomen is taut and swollen, with pain radiating to the waist.
When the **bladder** is distended, the lower abdomen is full with **qì yì** (*urinary urgency*).
When the **triple burner** is distended, **qi** fills the skin, causing a light, floating sensation without firmness.
When the **gallbladder** is distended, the ribs ache with bitterness in the mouth and a tendency to sigh deeply.

All these forms of distension share a single principle: clarity in discerning **nì shùn** (*reversed and顺 proper flow*), and precision in needle count.
Reduce the **xū** (*deficient*), reinforce the **shí** (*excess*), guide the **shén** (*spirit*) from its chamber—when **xié** (*evil*) displaces **zhèng** (*right*), the **zhēn** (*true*) cannot be fixed. Such recklessness shortens life.
Reinforce the **xū** (*deficient*), reduce the **shí** (*excess*), restore the **shén** (*spirit*) to its chamber, and seal the void for long endurance—this is the mark of a master physician."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Where does distension originate? What causes it?"*

Qìbó replied: *"The **wèi qì** (*Defensive Qi*) in the body normally flows alongside the pulse and through the muscle layers, moving with proper **shùn** (*sequence*) and **nì** (*reversal*), following **yīn-yáng** (*yin-yang*) harmony. Thus, the **wǔ zàng** (*Five Zang*) renew in cycles, the **sì jì** (*Four Seasons*) follow order, and the **wǔ gǔ** (*Five Grains*) transform. Only then does **jué qì** (*Reversed Qi*) settle below, **yíng-wèi** (*Nourishing-Defensive Qi*) stagnate, **hán qì** (*Cold Qi*) rise counterflow, and **zhēn-xié** (*True-Evil Qi*) clash. When the two **qi** (*qi forces*) contend, distension forms."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Excellent. How does one resolve confusion?"*

Qìbó replied: *"When aligned with the True, the three alignments are achieved."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Excellent."*

The Yellow Emperor asked Qìbó: *"The treatise on distension states: 'Regardless of excess or deficiency, the practitioner must swiftly drain. For nearby cases, one treatment suffices; for distant cases, three are needed. Yet if one applies three treatments and the distension does not subside, what is the error?'*

Qìbó responded: *"This refers to cases where the needle penetrates only the flesh and misses the central *qì* orifice. If the central *qì* orifice is not struck, the *qì* becomes internally obstructed. If the needle does not pierce the *huāng* (deep muscle layer), the *qì* cannot flow freely. If it passes only through the superficial flesh, the Defensive *qì* becomes disordered, and yin-yáng forces clash. In such cases, though drainage is required, it is not performed, so the *qì* does not descend. When three treatments fail to relieve the distension, one must alter the approach—only then will the *qì* descend. If it still does not descend, begin anew, and all will be secure. How could there be any danger?*

*In treating distension, one must carefully examine the pulse. If drainage is called for, drain; if supplementation is needed, supplement. Like the drum responding to the *gēng* (a percussion instrument), how could the distension fail to resolve?"*

---
*(Annotations preserved in footnotes as per original text.)*

---

**Chapter 36: *Wǔ Xiàn Lǜ Yè* – Distinguishing the Five Fluids**

The Yellow Emperor asked Qìbó:
*"When water and grain enter the mouth, they are transported to the intestines and stomach. Their fluids separate into five forms. In cold weather, with thin clothing, they become urine and gas; in hot weather, with thick clothing, they become sweat. When grief and sorrow converge, they become tears. When internal heat slackens the stomach, they become saliva. When perverse *qì* ascends in reverse, it obstructs the flow, and if the flow is blocked, it becomes water distension. I understand this, but I do not know how these conditions arise. I wish to hear the Way."*

Qìbó replied:
*"All water and grain enter the mouth, and their five flavors each flow to their respective reservoirs. Their fluids then follow their designated paths. Thus, the Three Burners exhale *qì* to warm the muscles and flesh, filling the skin—this is their *tīn* (moisture). What flows but does not circulate becomes *yè* (fluid).

When the weather is hot and clothing is thick, the pores open, and sweat emerges. If cold seeps between the muscles, phlegm accumulates and causes pain.

When the weather is cold, the pores close, and *qì* stagnates. Water descends into the bladder, becoming urine and gas.

The Five *Zàng* and Six *Fǔ* have the heart as their sovereign, the ears as their sentinels, the eyes as their marshals, the lungs as their ministers, the liver as their generals, the spleen as their guardians, and the kidneys as their external rulers.

Thus, the fluids of the Five *Zàng* and Six *Fǔ* all ascend to moisten the eyes. When the heart grieves and *qì* converges, the heart’s cords tighten. When the heart’s cords tighten, the lungs rise, and when the lungs rise, fluid overflows. The heart’s cords and lungs cannot constantly rise—sometimes ascending, sometimes descending—thus, coughing and weeping occur.

When internal heat consumes grain in the stomach, worms move erratically, filling the intestines and stomach, causing the stomach to slacken. When the stomach slackens, *qì* reverses, and saliva emerges.

The fluids of the Five Grains, harmonized and refined, seep into the bone cavities, nourishing the marrow and descending to the groin. If *yin* and *yáng* are disharmonious, fluid overflows and descends to the groin, draining the marrow. If this descends excessively, it causes emptiness, leading to lower back pain and cramping of the sinews.

When the *yin-yáng* pathways are blocked, the Four Seas are sealed, the Three Burners are not drained, and fluids are not metabolized, water and grain move together through the intestines and stomach. Separating at the *huí cháng* (ileum), they lodge in the lower burner, unable to seep into the bladder. Thus, the lower burner distends, and fluid overflow causes water distension. This is the order and disorder of the five separated fluids."*

---

**Chapter 37: *Wǔ Yuè Wǔ Shǐ* – The Five Orifices and Their Functions**

The Yellow Emperor asked Qìbó:
*"I have heard that acupuncture has five orifices and five gates to observe the five *qì*. These five *qì* are the functions of the Five *Zàng* and the counterparts of the five seasons. I wish to hear where their functions manifest."*

Qìbó replied:
*"The five orifices are the gates of the Five *Zàng*."*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"I wish to hear where they emerge, so that their patterns may be established."*

Qìbó replied:
*"The pulse emerges at the *qì kǒu* (collarbone region), and color appears in the *míng táng* (forehead). The five colors alternate in appearance, corresponding to the five seasons, each according to its regularity. When the *jīng qì* enters the *Zàng*, it must be treated within the interior."*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"Excellent. Are the five colors solely determined by the *míng táng*?"*

Qìbó replied:
*"When the five orifices are distinct, the *quē tíng* (nasal cavity) expands, and the *míng táng* is established. The *míng táng* is broad and vast, its shadows visible from afar. Its walls are high and its foundation deep, with drooping corners extending outward. When the five colors are properly aligned, the forehead is level, broad, and vast—such a person will live to a hundred. Those who see this should be treated with acupuncture, for such individuals have abundant *xuè-qì*, firm and dense flesh, and can endure strong needle techniques."*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"I wish to hear of the five orifices."*

Qìbó replied:
*"The nose is the orifice of the lungs; the eyes, of the liver; the lips, of the spleen; the tongue, of the heart; and the ears, of the kidneys."*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"How do we diagnose through these orifices?"*

Qìbó replied:
*"By observing the Five *Zàng*. Thus, when the lungs are diseased, breathing is labored and the nostrils flare; when the liver is diseased, the sclera turns yellowish-green; when the spleen is diseased, the lips turn yellow; when the heart is diseased, the tongue curls and shortens, its edges reddening; and when the kidneys are diseased, the face and cheeks darken."*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"Where do the five pulses emerge, and where do the five colors appear? What are the constant and perilous signs?"*

Qìbó replied:
*"When the five orifices are indistinct, the *quē tíng* does not expand, the *míng táng* is small, its shadows are not visible, and its walls are low and unstable. The drooping corners retreat inward. Such a person, though appearing ordinary, is in grave danger—death will come swiftly."*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"When the five colors appear in the *míng táng*, do they reveal the *qì* of the Five *Zàng* in terms of left-right and high-low positions, each with its own form?"*

Qìbó replied:
*"The *Zàng* and *Fǔ* within the body each occupy their proper stations, with left, right, high, and low all following their due measures."*

---

**Chapter 38: *Nì Shùn Féi Shòu* – Diagnosing Order, Disorder, Fatness, and Emaciation**

The Yellow Emperor asked Qìbó:
*"I have learned the Dao of acupuncture from you, and it is now fully mastered. Your teachings seem effortless yet are unshakably firm. Was your study thorough, or did you intuitively perceive patterns through observation?"*

Qìbó replied:
*"The Sage’s Way aligns above with Heaven, below with Earth, and in the middle with human affairs. It must have clear laws to establish measures and standards. Only then can it be verified and transmitted.

Thus, a carpenter cannot abandon the ruler and measure lengths arbitrarily, nor discard the plumb line and level water by eye. A craftsman cannot abandon the compass to draw circles or the square to make right angles.

Those who understand this use natural principles—easy teachings that govern the constants of order and disorder."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear of the natural principles in practice."*

Qìbó replied: *"When probing the *Rú* water#2, one need not exert effort—yet the water can be exhausted. Following the *Décuì*#3 and *Cìchōng*#4 channels, the *jīng*#5 may be made to flow. This refers to the smoothness and roughness of *qi*, the clarity and turbidity of blood, and the ebb and flow of their movement."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear of the variations in humans—white or black, fat or lean, small or tall—each having its own measure?"*

Qìbó replied: *"For those of robust constitution and strong vitality, whose flesh and skin are firm, if *xié*#6 has been added, one must needle deeply and dwell. These are the fat individuals. For those with broad shoulders and shallow armpits, thin necks, thick black skin, and full lips—whose blood is dark and turbid, whose *qi* is rough and sluggish, and who are greedy in giving and taking—one must needle deeply and dwell, increasing the number of points."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one needle the lean?"*

Qìbó replied: *"The lean have thin skin, little color, sparse flesh, and light speech. Their blood is clear and their *qi* smooth, easily detached from the *qi* and easily depleted in blood. One must needle shallowly and swiftly."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one needle the average?"*

Qìbó replied: *"Observe their white and black hues, and adjust accordingly. Those whose features are balanced and harmonious have *qi* and blood in accord. One must not deviate from the standard measures when needling these."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one needle a vigorous warrior with true bones?"*

Qìbó replied: *"When needling a vigorous warrior with true bones—whose flesh is firm and joints relaxed—if he is heavy, his *qi* is rough and his blood turbid. One must needle deeply and dwell, increasing the number of points. If he is agile, his *qi* is smooth and his blood clear. One must needle shallowly and swiftly."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one needle an infant?"*

Qìbó replied: *"Infants have tender flesh, little blood, and weak *qi*. One must use a fine needle, needle shallowly, and withdraw swiftly. Twice daily suffices."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"What of probing the *Rú* water?"*

Qìbó replied: *"If the blood is clear and the *qi* turbid, and one drains swiftly, the *qi* will be exhausted."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"What of following the *Décuì* and *Cìchōng* channels?"*

Qìbó replied: *"If the blood is turbid and the *qi* rough, and one drains swiftly, the *jīng*#5 may be made to flow."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"What of the pulse’s inverse or顺#7 movement?"*

Qìbó replied: *"The three *Yīn* pulses of the hand flow from the *zàng*#8 to the hand; the three *Yáng* pulses of the hand flow from the hand to the head; the three *Yáng* pulses of the foot flow from the head to the foot; the three *Yīn* pulses of the foot flow from the foot to the abdomen. The Yellow Emperor said: ‘Why does the *Shàoyīn* pulse alone descend?’ Qìbó replied: ‘Not so! The *Chōng* pulse is the sea of the five *zàng*#8 and six *fǔ*#9. All five *zàng* and six *fǔ* draw from it. Its upward flow emerges at the *tóu*#10, permeating all *Yáng* channels, nourishing all the *jīng*#11. Its downward flow enters the great network of the *Shàoyīn*, emerging at the *qìjiē*#12, descending along the inner *lián*#13 of the *yín*#14 stock, penetrating the *bīn*#15, and running concealed within the *jiàn*#16 bone, descending to the posterior of the inner *lì*#17, then diverging. Its downward flow also merges with the *Shàoyīn* channel, permeating the three *Yīn* pulses. Its anterior flow runs concealed, emerging at the *fǔ*#18 network, descending along the *fǔ*#18, and entering the space between the great toe. It permeates all networks and warms the flesh and muscles. Thus, if the divergent network is obstructed, the *fǔ*#18 network above does not move. If it does not move, there is *jué*#19, and if *jué*#19 occurs, there is cold."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How can this be verified?"*

Qìbó replied: *"By guiding the speech and testing the pulse. If the pulse does not move when pressed, then one can clarify the inverse flow of the *jīng*#11."*

The Yellow Emperor exclaimed: *"How profound! The Sage’s Way is clear as the sun and moon, subtle as the finest hairs. Were it not for Master, who else could teach it?"*

**Blood Network Treatise, Thirty-Ninth**

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear of those strange *xié*#6 that do not lie along the channels."*

Qìbó replied: *"The blood networks are those."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"If one needles the blood networks and the patient collapses, why is that? If blood spurts out upon needling, why is that? If blood is scant, dark, and turbid, why is that? If blood emerges clear and half turns to fluid, why is that? If withdrawal of the needle causes swelling, why is that? If blood emerges in varying amounts while the face turns pale, why is that? If withdrawal of the needle leaves the face unchanged but the patient feels agitated and pleased, why is that? If much blood is drawn yet the pulse remains unmoved, why is that? I wish to hear the reasons."*

**Blood Network Treatise, Thirty-Ninth (continued)**

Qìbó replied:
*"When the pulse-qi is robust yet the blood is deficient, needling will cause the patient to lose their vital breath—losing breath causes collapse. When both blood and qi are abundant but yin-qi predominates, the blood becomes slippery, and needling causes it to spurt forth. When yang-qi stagnates and lingers without release, the blood grows dark and turbid, so it cannot spurt. When one has recently drunk, the liquid seeps into the networks before blending with the blood, so the blood emerges while the fluid separates. Those who have not recently drunk but harbor internal dampness will develop swelling over time. When yin-qi accumulates in the yang, its force courses through the networks, so needling causes the qi to move before the blood emerges, resulting in swelling. When yin and yang newly converge but have not yet harmonized, and one then drains them, both yin and yang are depleted, separating exterior and interior—thus the face pales and takes on a wan hue. When much blood is drawn, the complexion remains unchanged, yet the patient feels agitated and pleased—this is because needling the networks depletes the channels. The channels’ yin aspects, when depleted, cause agitation and vexation. When yin and yang converge to form stagnation, this is internal overflow into the channels and external pooling in the networks. In such cases, both yin and yang are in excess, so even if much blood is drawn, it cannot be fully depleted."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one diagnose this?"*

Qìbó replied:
*"The blood vessels are robust, firm and horizontal with a crimson hue, wandering without fixed location—small as a needle, large as a sinew. Needling them with precision ensures complete efficacy. Thus, one must never miscount. If miscounted and corrected, each must return to its proper measure."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"When the needle enters and adheres to the flesh, why is that?"*

Qìbó replied:
*"When heated qi adheres to the needle, the needle grows warm, and warmth causes the flesh to cling to it, making it firm."*

---
**Clear and Turbid Qi, Fortieth**

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have heard that the twelve channels correspond to the twelve tributary waters, yet their five colors differ, and their clarity and turbidity vary. If a person’s blood and qi are uniform, how do they correspond?"*

Qìbó replied:
*"If a person’s blood and qi could truly be uniform, then all under heaven would be one—how could there be disorder?"*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I ask of one person, not of the multitude of the world."*

Qìbó replied:
*"Even one person may have disordered qi, just as the multitude may contain disordered individuals—they are but one in essence."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear of human qi’s clarity and turbidity."*

Qìbó replied:
*"That which receives grain is turbid; that which receives breath is clear. The clear ascends to yin, the turbid descends to yang. When turbid yet clear, it rises to the throat; when clear yet turbid, it flows downward. When clarity and turbidity intermingle, it is called disordered qi."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"If yin is clear and yang is turbid, yet turbid contains clarity and clear contains turbidity, how does one distinguish them?"*

Qìbó replied:
*"The great distinction of qi is this: the clear ascends to the lungs, the turbid descends to the stomach. The stomach’s clear qi rises through the mouth; the lungs’ turbid qi descends into the channels, accumulating in the sea."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"All yang channels are turbid—why is the hand’s Greater Yang most turbid?"*

Qìbó replied:
*"The hand’s Greater Yang alone receives the turbidity of yang; the hand’s Lesser Yin alone receives the clarity of yin. Its clarity ascends to the hollow cavities, its turbidity descends through the channels. All yin channels are clear, yet the foot’s Lesser Yin and Second Lesser Yin uniquely receive their turbidity."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one treat this?"*

Qìbó replied:
*"The clear qi is slippery, the turbid qi is rough—this is the nature of qi. Thus, when needling yin, one must go deep and dwell; when needling yang, one must go shallow and swiftly. When clarity and turbidity intermingle alone, one regulates them with the proper count."*

**[Phonetic Note]** *"悦"* sounds like *"闷"* (agitation/vexation).

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have heard that Heaven is yang, Earth is yin, the Sun is yang, and the Moon is yin. How do these correspond in the human body?"*

Qìbó replied: *"Above the waist is Heaven, below the waist is Earth. Thus, Heaven is yang, Earth is yin. The twelve meridians of the feet correspond to the twelve months—since the Moon is born from water, those below are yin. The ten fingers of the hands correspond to the ten days—since the Sun governs fire, those above are yang."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How do these correspond to the meridians?"*

Qìbó replied:
*"Yin (寅), the birth of yang in the first month, governs the left foot’s Lesser Yang.
*Wèi* (未), the sixth month, governs the right foot’s Lesser Yang.
*Mǎo* (卯), the second month, governs the left foot’s Greater Yang.
*Wǔ* (午), the fifth month, governs the right foot’s Greater Yang.
*Chén* (辰), the third month, governs the left foot’s Bright Yang.
*Sì* (巳), the fourth month, governs the right foot’s Bright Yang—these two yangs converge in the front, hence *Bright Yang*.
*Shēn* (申), the birth of yin in the seventh month, governs the right foot’s Lesser Yin.
*Chǒu* (丑), the twelfth month, governs the left foot’s Lesser Yin.
*Yǒu* (酉), the eighth month, governs the right foot’s Greater Yin.
*Zǐ* (子), the eleventh month, governs the left foot’s Greater Yin.
*Xū* (戌), the ninth month, governs the right foot’s Obstructed Yin.
*Hài* (亥), the tenth month, governs the left foot’s Obstructed Yin—these two yins exhaust each other, hence *Obstructed Yin*.

*Jiǎ* governs the left hand’s Lesser Yang, *jǐ* the right hand’s Lesser Yang.
*Yǐ* governs the left hand’s Greater Yang, *wù* the right hand’s Greater Yang.
*Bǐng* governs the left hand’s Bright Yang, *dīng* the right hand’s Bright Yang—these two fires unite, hence *Bright Yang*.
*Gēng* governs the right hand’s Lesser Yin, *guǐ* the left hand’s Lesser Yin.
*Xīn* governs the right hand’s Greater Yin, *rén* the left hand’s Greater Yin.

Thus, the foot’s yang is the Lesser Yang within yin;
the foot’s yin is the Greater Yin within yin.
The hand’s yang is the Greater Yang within yang;
the hand’s yin is the Lesser Yin within yang.

Above the waist is yang, below the waist is yin.
As for the five viscera:
The heart is the Greater Yang within yang,
the lung is the Lesser Yin within yang,
the liver is the Lesser Yang within yin,
the spleen is the Ultimate Yin within yin,
the kidney is the Greater Yin within yin.

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one treat this?"*

Qìbó replied:
*"In the first, second, and third months, vital qi resides on the left—do not needle the left foot’s yang meridians.
In the fourth, fifth, and sixth months, vital qi resides on the right—do not needle the right foot’s yang meridians.
In the seventh, eighth, and ninth months, vital qi resides on the right—do not needle the right foot’s yin meridians.
In the tenth, eleventh, and twelfth months, vital qi resides on the left—do not needle the left foot’s yin meridians."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"The Five Phases assign *jiǎ* and *yǐ* (wood) as rulers of spring, spring being the azure color, governing the liver—the foot’s Obstructed Yin. Yet you say *jiǎ* governs the left hand’s Lesser Yang—how does this accord with the sequence?"*

Qìbó replied: *"This is the yin-yang of Heaven and Earth, not the sequential progression of the Four Seasons and Five Phases. Moreover, yin and yang are nameless yet formless—count them as ten, divide them as a hundred, scatter them as a thousand, extend them as ten thousand. This is what is meant."*

---
**Chapter 42: Transmission of Disease**

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have received the Nine Needles from my teacher and privately studied various methods—such as guiding and leading *qi*, massage, moxibustion, warming, needling, cauterization, and medicinal drinks. May one practice only one method, or must all be employed?"*

Qìbó replied: *"These are the methods of the common people, not the exclusive practice of one individual."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"This is what is meant by ‘holding fast to one without loss, all things accomplished.’ Now that I have heard the essentials of yin and yang, the principles of emptiness and fullness, the excesses of imbalance, and the categories of treatable diseases, I wish to hear of diseases that vary, spread excessively, or are severed and ruined beyond treatment. May I hear of these?"*

Qìbó replied: *"Your question is profound! The Dao shines like awakening at dawn, yet is constrained like night’s darkness. If one wears it and serves it, the spirit is complete; if one exhausts it, the spirit finds itself. The principles of life’s spirit can be inscribed on bamboo and silk, yet cannot be transmitted to descendants."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"What is meant by ‘awakening at dawn’?"*

Qìbó replied: *"Clarity in yin and yang, like the resolution of confusion, like sobering from drunkenness."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"What is meant by ‘night’s darkness’?"*

Qìbó replied: *"Silent as a wound without sound, formless as mist—disrupting hair and pores, vital *qi* tilting wildly, perverse pathogens floating and spreading, blood and vessels transmitting errantly, great *qi* entering the viscera, abdominal pain descending into excess—this can lead to death, not life."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does great *qi* entering the viscera manifest?"*

Qìbó replied: *"If disease first arises in the heart, it spreads to the lung in one day, to the liver in three days, to the spleen in five days. If it persists beyond three days, death follows. At winter’s midnight, or summer’s noon.

If disease first arises in the lung, it spreads to the liver in three days, to the spleen in one day, to the stomach in five days. If it persists beyond ten days, death follows."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"At winter’s solstice, it enters; at summer’s noon, it exits."*

If disease first arises in the **liver** (*gān*), it spreads to the **spleen** (*pí*) in three days, to the **stomach** (*wèi*) in five days, to the **kidneys** (*shèn*) in three days. If it persists beyond three days, death follows.

At winter’s solstice, it enters; at summer’s dawn, one should eat lightly.

If disease first arises in the **spleen** (*pí*), it spreads to the **stomach** (*wèi*) in one day, to the **kidneys** (*shèn*) in two days, to the **bladder** (*pángguāng*) in three days. If it persists beyond ten days, death follows.

At winter’s night’s end, at summer’s late meal.

If disease first arises in the **stomach** (*wèi*), it spreads to the **kidneys** (*shèn*) in five days, to the **bladder** (*pángguāng*) in three days, then ascends to the **heart** (*xīn*) in five days. If it persists beyond two days, death follows.

At winter’s midnight, at summer’s noonday.

If disease first arises in the **kidneys** (*shèn*), it spreads to the **bladder** (*pángguāng*) in three days, then ascends to the **heart** (*xīn*) in three days, to the **small intestine** (*xiǎocháng*) in three days. If it persists beyond three days, death follows.

At winter’s great dawn, at summer’s early morning.

All diseases transmit in sequence to the **viscera** (*zàng*). Such cases all have a fixed death period and may not be pricked. If the disease affects one **viscus** (*zàng*), or two, three, four, then pricking may be applied.

**Chapter 43: Excessive Pathogenic Factors and Dreaming**

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear how excessive pathogenic factors (*yínxié*) manifest as dreams."*

Qibo replied: *"When pathogenic factors invade from the outside and have not yet settled in a fixed location, they wander excessively among the **viscera** (*zàng*), unable to remain in one place. They move with the **honorific vessels** (*róng*) and **defensive vessels** (*wèi*), and with the **soul** (*hún*) and **spirit** (*pò*), causing the person to feel restless externally and experience vivid dreams.

When **qi** is excessive in the **bowels** (*fǔ*), there is surplus externally and deficiency internally. When **qi** is excessive in the **viscera** (*zàng*), there is surplus internally and deficiency externally."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does this surplus and deficiency manifest?"*

Qibo replied:
*"When **yin qi** is excessive, one dreams of crossing vast waters in fear.
When **yang qi** is excessive, one dreams of great fires and blazing light.
When both **yin** and **yang** are excessive, one dreams of mutual destruction.

If the upper body is excessive, one dreams of flying.
If the lower body is excessive, one dreams of falling.
Extreme hunger causes dreams of taking.
Extreme satiety causes dreams of giving.
Excessive **liver qi** causes dreams of anger.
Excessive **lung qi** causes dreams of terror, weeping, or wild flight.
Excessive **heart qi** causes dreams of laughter, fear, and sudden fright.
Excessive **spleen qi** causes dreams of singing and dancing, with a heavy body that cannot be lifted.
Excessive **kidney qi** causes dreams of the lumbar and sacral regions dissolving and disconnecting.

For these twelve cases of excess, when they arise, they may be **drained** (*xiě*), and the condition will immediately resolve.

When **ulterior qi** (*juéqi*) lodges in the **heart** (*xīn*), one dreams of hills, mountains, and fire.
When it lodges in the **lungs** (*fèi*), one dreams of flight and sees strange metallic objects.
When it lodges in the **liver** (*gān*), one dreams of mountains, forests, and trees.
When it lodges in the **spleen** (*pí*), one dreams of hills, marshes, collapsing houses, and wind and rain.
When it lodges in the **kidneys** (*shèn*), one dreams of standing at the edge of a precipice or drowning in water.
When it lodges in the **bladder** (*pángguāng*), one dreams of wandering.
When it lodges in the **stomach** (*wèi*), one dreams of eating and drinking.
When it lodges in the **large intestine** (*dàcháng*), one dreams of fields and wildlands.
When it lodges in the **small intestine** (*xiǎocháng*), one dreams of crowded marketplaces and crossroads.
When it lodges in the **gallbladder** (*dǎn*), one dreams of lawsuits or self-mutilation.
When it lodges in the **external genitalia** (*yīnqì*), one dreams of sexual intercourse.
When it lodges in the **neck** (*jǐng*), one dreams of decapitation.
When it lodges in the **channels** (*jīng*), one dreams of walking but unable to proceed, or being trapped in deep valleys or graveyards.
When it lodges in the **thighs and arms** (*gǔgāng*), one dreams of rituals, bowing, and rising.
When it lodges in the **uterus** (*bāozhí*), the **vital essence** (*shēngqì*) is divided, and one dreams of drowning or incontinence.

For these cases of deficiency with fixed patterns (*shù*), when they arise, they may be **supplemented** (*bǔ*), and the condition will immediately resolve."*

---

**Chapter 44: The Four Seasons and Disease Progression**

The Yellow Emperor said: *"The origin of all diseases must arise from dryness, dampness, cold, heat, wind, and rain, as well as from the emotions of joy and anger, and from diet, residence, and the convergence of **qi** into form. When they settle in the **viscera**, they take on names. I understand this. But why do most diseases begin with morning clarity, daytime ease, evening worsening, and nighttime severity?"*

Qibo replied: *"This is due to the influence of the four seasonal **qi**."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear how the four seasonal **qi** operate."*

Qibo replied: *"Spring generates, summer grows, autumn harvests, and winter stores—this is the constant pattern of **qi**. Humans follow it accordingly. Dividing a single day into the four seasons: morning corresponds to spring, noon to summer, evening to autumn, and midnight to winter.

In the morning, human **qi** begins to generate, while pathogenic **qi** weakens—hence morning clarity.
At noon, human **qi** flourishes and overcomes pathogenic factors—hence ease.
In the evening, human **qi** begins to decline, while pathogenic **qi** emerges—hence worsening.
At midnight, human **qi** retreats into the **viscera**, while pathogenic **qi** dominates the body—hence severity."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"But sometimes the pattern reverses—why is this?"*

Qibo replied: *"This occurs when the body does not conform to the seasonal **qi**, and a single **viscus** dominates the disease. Such cases worsen when the **viscus** is weak against the season and improve when it is strong."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How should one treat this?"*

Qibo replied: *"Align with the natural seasons, and the disease’s progression can be predicted. Those who follow this principle are skilled; those who oppose it are crude."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Excellent."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have heard that acupuncture has five variations to govern the five *shu*-points. I wish to hear their principles."*

Qibo replied: *"Humans have five viscera, and the five viscera have five variations. These five variations govern five *shu*-points, thus making twenty-five *shu*-points to correspond with the five seasons."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear of the five variations."*

Qibo replied: *"The liver is a *male* viscus, its color green, its season spring, its tone *jue*, its flavor sour, and its days *jia* and *yi*;
the heart is a *male* viscus, its color red, its season summer, its days *bing* and *ding*, its tone *zheng*, its flavor bitter;
the spleen is a *female* viscus, its color yellow, its season late summer, its days *wu* and *ji*, its tone *gong*, its flavor sweet;
the lung is a *female* viscus, its color white, its tone *shang*, its season autumn, its days *geng* and *xin*, its flavor pungent;
the kidney is a *female* viscus, its color black, its season winter, its days *ren* and *gui*, its tone *yu*, its flavor salty.
These are the five variations."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How do these govern the five *shu*-points?"*

Qibo replied: *"The viscera govern winter, and in winter, one needles the *jing*-point;
the colors govern spring, and in spring, one needles the *ying*-point;
the seasons govern summer, and in summer, one needles the *shu*-point;
the tones govern late summer, and in late summer, one needles the *jing*-point of the channel;
the flavors govern autumn, and in autumn, one needles the *he*-point.
This is how the five variations govern the five *shu*-points."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How do the *yuan*-points combine to result in six *shu*-points?"*

Qibo replied: *"The *yuan*-point alone does not correspond to the five seasons, so it is combined with the *jing*-point to fulfill their numbers, thus making thirty-six *shu*-points."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"What is meant by ‘the viscera govern winter,’ ‘the seasons govern summer,’ ‘the tones govern late summer,’ ‘the flavors govern autumn,’ and ‘the colors govern spring’? I wish to hear their reasoning."*

Qibo replied: *"When disease is in the viscera, one takes the *jing*-point;
when disease manifests in the colors, one takes the *ying*-point;
when disease aligns with the season and is severe, one takes the *shu*-point;
when disease manifests in the tones, one takes the *jing*-point of the channel;
when the channel is full and the blood is disturbed, or when disease arises from improper diet, one takes the *he*-point. Thus, it is called ‘the flavors govern the *he*-point.’
This is what is meant by the five variations."*

*"The external examination is the forty-fifth [section]."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have heard of the Nine Needles in nine chapters, and I have personally received their principles, gaining some understanding of their intent. The Nine Needles, beginning at one and ending at nine, yet I have not yet grasped their essential Dao. The Nine Needles—when small, they reach no interior; when great, they extend to no exterior. They cannot be measured as deep or shallow, nor confined as high or low. They are boundless and elusive, overflowing without end. I know they harmonize with the Dao of Heaven, human affairs, and the transformations of the Four Seasons—but I wish to integrate them into a single, unified whole, like the finest hairs bound together. Is this possible?"*

Qibo replied: *"Your question is profound indeed! It is not merely about the Dao of the needles, for governing a state follows the same principle. The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish only to hear of the Dao of the needles, not matters of state."* Qibo said: *"To govern a state is to embody the Dao itself. Without the Dao, how could one unify the small and great, the shallow and deep, into a single whole?"*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear it fully."*

Qibo replied: *"The sun and moon, water and mirror, drum and echo. The brilliance of the sun and moon never loses their shadows; the clarity of water and mirror never fails to reflect form. The drum’s response never lags behind its sound—when moved, it resonates in harmony, capturing all its essence."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How wondrous! Their radiance cannot be obscured. Their unobscured nature lies in their adherence to yin and yang. When combined and examined, when pressed and verified, when observed and understood, it is like clear water and a bright mirror never failing to reveal form. If the Five Tones are not resonant and the Five Colors are not distinct, if the Five Viscera are agitated, then the exterior and interior will follow in succession—like the drum’s response to its strike, the echo’s return to sound, the shadow’s likeness to form. Thus, the distant one examines the interior through the exterior, the near one examines the exterior through the interior. This is called the ultimate of yin and yang, the canopy of Heaven and Earth. Let this be preserved in the sacred halls of Linglan, lest it be revealed to the unworthy."*

*The Yellow Emperor’s Inner Canon, Spiritual Pivot, Volume 14*
*Five Variations, Section 46*

The Yellow Emperor asked Shàoyú, saying: *"I have heard that the onset of a hundred ailments must arise from wind, rain, cold, and heat. They enter through the hair follicles and pores, sometimes returning, sometimes lingering, sometimes causing wind edema and sweating, sometimes leading to consumption and dampness, sometimes to cold and heat, sometimes to lingering numbness, sometimes to accumulations. These perverse pathogens overflow in myriad ways, beyond counting. I wish to know their cause. When people fall ill at the same time, some suffer here, others there—could it be that Heaven generates wind for humankind? Why do the afflictions differ so?"*

Shàoyú replied: *"Heaven’s generation of wind is not to single out the people; its movement is just and impartial. Those who transgress are struck, those who avoid it are spared. It does not seek out people—people themselves invite it by their actions."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"At the same time, people encounter wind and fall ill, yet their diseases differ. I wish to hear the reason."*

Shàoyú said: *"Excellent question! Let me compare it to a carpenter. When a carpenter sharpens his axes and chisels, hones his knives and planes, and hews wood, the wood’s yin and yang—its hardness and brittleness—vary. The hard resists the blade, the brittle splits at the skin. When it reaches the joints, the axe and chisel falter. Within a single piece of wood, hardness and brittleness differ: the hard is rigid, the brittle easily injured. Yet the wood’s thickness, the depth of its grain, the abundance of its sap—all vary. And so, when spring frost or violent winds strike, the early-blooming, late-leafed trees lose their flowers and wither. In prolonged drought, the brittle, thin-skinned trees with little sap wither and their branches dry. In lingering dampness and excessive rain, the thin-skinned, sap-rich trees rot and decay. In sudden gales, the rigid and brittle trees snap and crack. In autumn frost and fierce winds, their roots loosen and leaves fall. These five injuries—each has its target. Now apply this to humankind!"*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one compare humans to wood?"*

Shàoyú answered: *"The injuries to wood strike its branches—its rigidity and brittleness, its firmness—before harm occurs. In humans, chronic illnesses arise where bones, joints, skin, and pores are weak and unguarded, for there the pathogens lodge, making disease constant."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one foretell who will suffer wind, fainting, or sweating?"*

Shàoyú replied: *"If the flesh is not firm and the pores are lax, one will easily fall ill with wind."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one know if the flesh is not firm?"*

Shàoyú replied: *"If the flesh lacks firmness and lacks clear grain, and the skin is not taut—if the pores are lax—this is the mark of a diffuse, undifferentiated constitution."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one foretell who will suffer consumption and dampness?"*

Shàoyú replied: *"Those whose Five Viscera are all soft and weak will easily fall ill with consumption and dampness."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one know if the Five Viscera are soft and weak?"*

Shàoyú replied: *"The weak must contrast with the strong. The strong are prone to anger, and the weak are easily injured."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one discern the weak from the strong?"*

Shàoyú replied: *"Observe those with thin skin, deep-set and firm eyes, a long, straight, and upright stature—these are the rigid in heart. The rigid are prone to anger, and anger drives *qi* upward in rebellion. The chest accumulates stagnation, blood and *qi* reverse and linger, the diaphragm and skin swell, the blood vessels stagnate, and heat ensues. Heat consumes the flesh, leading to consumption. This describes those whose constitution is violently rigid yet whose muscles are weak."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one foretell who will suffer cold and heat?"*

Shàoyú replied: *"Those with small bones and weak flesh will easily suffer cold and heat."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one know the size of the bones, the firmness of the flesh, and the uniformity of their color?"*

Shàoyú replied: *"The facial bones are the root of all bones. If the face is large, the bones are large; if the face is small, the bones are small. If the skin is thin and the flesh lacks firmness, if the arms hang limp and the complexion is sallow—if their natural hue is obscured and they stand out uncleanly—this is the sign. Then, if the arms are thin, their marrow is insufficient, and they will easily suffer cold and heat."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one foretell who will suffer numbness?"*

Shàoyú replied: *"Those with coarse pores and unfirm flesh will easily suffer numbness."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Does numbness have a high or low predilection?"*

Shàoyú replied: *"To know its high or low placement, observe the affected region."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one foretell who will suffer intestinal accumulations?"*

Shàoyú replied: *"If the skin is thin and lacks luster, if the flesh is not firm yet appears moist—then the intestines and stomach are weak. Weakness allows pathogens to linger, leading to accumulations. Between the spleen and stomach, if cold and heat are disordered, even slight pathogenic intrusion will cause stagnation and accumulation, eventually forming masses."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have now understood the forms of disease! I wish to hear of their timing."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one foretell the timing of disease?"*

Shàoyú replied: *"First establish the person’s age, then one may know the timing. If the season is high (spring/summer), disease will arise; if the season is low (autumn/winter), it will decline. Though it may not descend to the lower regions, if there is a year when the *sùn* (collateral channels) and *jīng* (main channels) are in flux, the disease will manifest. This is called ‘diseases arising from bodily constitution,’ a record of the five seasonal transformations."*

**Phonetic Gloss:**
- *sùn* (穑) pronounced *yuàn* (as in "wide")
- *jīng* (通) pronounced *táo* (as in "peach")
- *wù* (恶) pronounced *nuò* (as in "weak")
- *shèng* (胜) pronounced *sà* (as in "deer")

---

The Yellow Emperor questioned Qíbó, saying: *"The vital *blood*, *qi*, and *spirit* are what sustain life and circulate through the essence of existence. The *jīngmài* (meridian channels) are what convey *qi* and *blood*, nourishing the *yin* and *yang*, moistening the sinews and bones, and lubricating the joints. The *wèiqì* (defensive *qi*) is what warms the flesh, fills the skin, and renders the *pores* firm, governing the opening and closing of the body. The *zhìyì* (will and intent) are what command the *spirit*, gather the *húnpò* (ethereal soul and corporeal soul), moderate cold and heat, and harmonize joy and anger. Therefore, when *blood* is harmonized, the *jīngmài* flow freely, nourishing *yin* and *yang*, strengthening the sinews and bones, and making the joints supple. When the *wèiqì* is harmonized, the flesh is well-nourished and flexible, the skin smooth and tender, and the *pores* dense and sealed. When the *zhìyì* is harmonized, the *spirit* remains focused and upright, the *húnpò* do not scatter, regret and anger do not arise, and the five *zàng* (viscera) are impervious to pathogens. When cold and heat are moderated, the six *fǔ* (entrances) transform grains, and wind-*bì* (numbness) does not occur, the *jīngmài* flow freely, and the limbs find peace. This is the state of human equilibrium.*

*The five *zàng* are what store the *spirit*, *qi*, *blood*, and *húnpò*. The six *fǔ* are what transform water and grains, circulating the *jīnè* (body fluids). This is how humans fully receive the gifts of *Heaven*—without distinction of wisdom or folly, virtue or vice, they are all equally endowed. Yet some exhaust their natural lifespan without suffering from perverse or deviant diseases, remaining unweakened even at a hundred years. Though exposed to wind and rain, extreme cold or scorching heat, they are unharmed. Others never leave their chambers, fearing neither rest nor vigilance, yet still fall ill. Why is this? I wish to hear the reason."*

Qíbó replied: *"How profound your question is!"*

The five *zàng* are what harmonize with Heaven and Earth, correspond to yin and yang, and connect with the four seasons, transforming the five *jié* (phases). The five *zàng* inherently possess variations in size—great or small, elevation—high or low, texture—firm or brittle, shape—upright or tilted—and alignment—balanced or skewed. The six *fǔ* likewise vary in size—long or short, thickness—thick or thin, structure—compact or straight, and tension—relaxed or taut. These twenty-five distinctions, each differing in kind, may be either auspicious or inauspicious, fortunate or calamitous. Let me explain their effects.

When the **heart** is small, it remains tranquil, and perverse influences cannot harm it, but it is easily injured by worry.
When the **heart** is large, worry and anxiety cannot harm it, but it is easily injured by perverse influences.
When the **heart** is high, it presses upon the lungs, causing joy and forgetfulness, but it is difficult to open through speech.
When the **heart** is low, it is exposed externally, making it susceptible to cold and easily startled by speech.
When the **heart** is firm, its essence is securely stored.
When the **heart** is brittle, it is prone to diseases of consumption, dampness, and internal heat.
When the **heart** is upright, it harmonizes and is difficult to injure.
When the **heart** is skewed, its governance is inconsistent, lacking stability.

When the **lungs** are small, they require little drink, and one does not suffer from wheezing or coughing.
When the **lungs** are large, they require much drink and are prone to chest constriction (*xiōngbì*), throat constriction (*hóubì*), and reversed *qi*.
When the **lungs** are high, they cause upward *qi*, shoulder breathing, and coughing.
When the **lungs** are low, they press upon the *zǎi* (diaphragm), causing pain beneath the ribs.
When the **lungs** are firm, one does not suffer from coughing or upward *qi*.
When the **lungs** are brittle, one is prone to diseases of consumption, dampness, and internal heat, and is easily injured.
When the **lungs** are upright, they harmonize and are difficult to injure.
When the **lungs** are skewed, one suffers from lateral chest pain.

When the **liver** is small, its essence is securely stored, and there is no pain beneath the ribs.
When the **liver** is large, it presses upon the stomach and throat, constricting the throat and causing pain beneath the ribs.
When the **liver** is high, it presses upward upon the *zǎi*, causing rib pain and a sensation of fullness in the *sháo* (rib region).
When the **liver** is low, it presses upon the stomach, leaving the ribs hollow, making them susceptible to perverse influences.
When the **liver** is firm, its essence is securely stored and difficult to injure.
When the **liver** is brittle, one is prone to diseases of consumption, dampness, and internal heat, and is easily injured.
When the **liver** is upright, it harmonizes and is difficult to injure.
When the **liver** is skewed, one suffers from pain beneath the ribs.

When the **spleen** is small, its essence is securely stored and difficult to injure by perverse influences.
When the **spleen** is large, one suffers from *chénshā* (a chronic, dull pain) and cannot walk swiftly.
When the **spleen** is high, the pain draws toward the lateral ribs.
When the **spleen** is low, it descends upon the large intestine, making its essence susceptible to perverse influences.
When the **spleen** is firm, its essence is securely stored and difficult to injure.
When the **spleen** is brittle, one is prone to diseases of consumption, dampness, and internal heat, and is easily injured.
When the **spleen** is upright, it harmonizes and is difficult to injure.
When the **spleen** is skewed, one is prone to fullness and distension.

When the **kidneys** are small, their essence is securely stored and difficult to injure.
When the **kidneys** are large, one is prone to waist pain, unable to bend or bow, and easily injured by perverse influences.
When the **kidneys** are high, one suffers from pain in the back and loins, unable to bend or bow.
When the **kidneys** are low, one suffers from *yāoshī* (lumbar hernia) pain, unable to bend or bow, manifesting as *húshàn* (hernia).
When the **kidneys** are firm, one does not suffer from waist or back pain.
When the **kidneys** are brittle, one is prone to diseases of consumption, dampness, and internal heat, and is easily injured.
When the **kidneys** are upright, they harmonize and are difficult to injure.
When the **kidneys** are skewed, one suffers from *yāoshī* pain.

These twenty-five variations are the common afflictions of humanity.
