The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How do you know this to be so?"*

Qíbó replied:
*"When the complexion is red and finely textured, the **heart** is small.
When it is coarse, the **heart** is large.
When the *hóuqì* (supraorbital ridge) is absent, the **heart** is high.
When the *hóuqì* is small, short, and raised, the **heart** is low.
When the *hóuqì* is long, the **heart** is firm; when it is weak, small, and thin, the **heart** is brittle.
When the *hóuqì* descends straight and does not raise, the **heart** is upright.
When the *hóuqì* leans to one side, the **heart** is skewed.

When the complexion is white and finely textured, the **lungs** are small.
When it is coarse, the **lungs** are large.
When the shoulders are broad and the back is concave at the throat, the **lungs** are high.
When the armpits are wide and the ribs are spread, the **lungs** are low.
When the shoulders and back are thick, the **lungs** are firm.
When the shoulders and back are thin, the **lungs** are brittle.
When the back and chest are thick, the **lungs** are upright.
When the ribs are unevenly spaced, the **lungs** are skewed.

When the complexion is green and finely textured, the **liver** is small.
When it is coarse, the **liver** is large.
When the chest is broad and the spine is concave, the **liver** is high.
When the ribs are drawn inward and the spine is concave, the **liver** is low.
When the chest and ribs are robust, the **liver** is firm.
When the ribs are weak, the **liver** is brittle.
When the chest and abdomen are harmoniously proportioned, the **liver** is upright.
When the ribs are unevenly raised, the **liver** is skewed.

When the complexion is yellow and finely textured, the **spleen** is small.
When it is coarse, the **spleen** is large.
When the lips are upturned, the **spleen** is high.
When the lips hang downward, the **spleen** is low.
When the lips are firm, the **spleen** is firm.
When the lips are large but not firm, the **spleen** is brittle.
When the lips are evenly aligned, the **spleen** is upright.
When the lips are unevenly raised, the **spleen** is skewed.

When the complexion is black and finely textured, the **kidneys** are small.
When it is coarse, the **kidneys** are large.
When the ears are high, the **kidneys** are high.
When the area behind the ears is concave, the **kidneys** are low.
When the ears are firm, the **kidneys** are firm.
When the ears are thin and not firm, the **kidneys** are brittle.
When the ears are well-formed and positioned before the *yáchē* (ear canal), the **kidneys** are upright.
When the ears are unevenly raised, the **kidneys** are skewed.

These variations, if maintained in balance, bring stability; if diminished, they bring disease."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Excellent."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"This is not what I asked. I wish to hear of those who are incapable of falling ill—who live out their full natural lifespan despite profound grief and fear, their vigilant resolve never faltering; who are unharmed by extreme cold or heat; and yet, even those who remain indoors, shielded from all dread, still fall ill. Why is this? I wish to know the reason."*

Qìbó replied: *"The five **viscera** and six **bowels** are the dwelling places of pathogenic influences. Let me explain their nature.

When the five **viscera** are all small, one suffers few illnesses but endures constant melancholy and great sorrow. When the five **viscera** are all large, one is sluggish in action and difficult to provoke with worry. When the five **viscera** are all high, one favors elevated actions. When the five **viscera** are all low, one tends to be inferior to others. When the five **viscera** are all firm, one is free of disease. When the five **viscera** are all brittle, one is never free of illness. When the five **viscera** are all upright, one harmonizes and gains the trust of others. When the five **viscera** are all skewed, one harbors deceitful intentions, is prone to theft, and cannot be trusted—speaking in contradictions.

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear of the six **bowels**’ correspondences."*

Qìbó answered: *"The **lung** corresponds to the **large intestine**; the **large intestine** reflects in the **skin**. The **heart** corresponds to the **small intestine**; the **small intestine** reflects in the **pulse**. The **liver** corresponds to the **gallbladder**; the **gallbladder** reflects in the **sinews**. The **spleen** corresponds to the **stomach**; the **stomach** reflects in the **flesh**. The **kidneys** correspond to the **triple burner** and **bladder**; the **triple burner** and **bladder** reflect in the **fine pores** and **downy hair**."

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How do these correspondences manifest?"*

Qìbó replied: *"The **lung** reflects in the **skin**: thick skin indicates a thick **large intestine**; thin skin indicates a thin **large intestine**. Relaxed skin with a spacious abdomen indicates a large and long **large intestine**; taut skin indicates a short and constricted **large intestine**. Smooth skin indicates a straight **large intestine**; skin and flesh inseparable indicates a knotted **large intestine**.

The **heart** reflects in the **pulse**: thick skin indicates thick pulses, and thick pulses indicate a thick **small intestine**; thin skin indicates thin pulses, and thin pulses indicate a thin **small intestine**. Relaxed skin indicates relaxed pulses, and relaxed pulses indicate a large and long **small intestine**; thin skin with a faint pulse indicates a small and short **small intestine**. When all the **yang** meridians are excessively winding, the **small intestine** becomes knotted.

The **spleen** reflects in the **flesh**: firm, ample flesh indicates a thick **stomach**; sparse flesh indicates a thin **stomach**; small and sparse flesh indicates a weak **stomach**. Flesh unproportionate to the body indicates a descending **stomach**, which causes the lower passage to constrict and function poorly. Flaccid flesh indicates a relaxed **stomach**; flesh without fine creases indicates a constricted **stomach**; flesh with many creases indicates a knotted **stomach**, which causes the upper passage to constrict and function poorly.

The **liver** reflects in the **nails**: thick, yellow nails indicate a thick **gallbladder**; thin, red nails indicate a thin **gallbladder**; firm, green nails indicate a constricted **gallbladder**; soft, red nails indicate a relaxed **gallbladder**; straight, white nails without constriction indicate a straight **gallbladder**; malformed, black nails with many ridges indicate a knotted **gallbladder**.

The **kidneys** reflect in the **bones**: dense texture with thick skin indicates thick **triple burner** and **bladder**; coarse texture with thin skin indicates thin **triple burner** and **bladder**; sparse fine pores indicate a relaxed **triple burner** and **bladder**; taut skin without downy hair indicates a constricted **triple burner** and **bladder**; coarse, abundant downy hair indicates a straight **triple burner** and **bladder**; sparse downy hair indicates a knotted **triple burner** and **bladder**."

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Thickness, thinness, beauty, and deformity all have visible forms. I wish to know the diseases they portend."*

Qìbó answered: *"Observe their external correspondences to discern the internal **viscera**, and thus you will know the source of the illness."*

「**Phonetic Explanation:** *Embū* (high-cutting, *chì*), *qiāo* (knocking). □*jié* (to bind). □*yú* (at). *Huángdì Nèijīng Língshū Jízhù* Volume 14, *Jìng* #1: *"Heart"*—the original text had an extra *"below"* character, deleted according to *Jiǎyǐ Jīng* Volume 1, Chapter 5. #2: *"Li"*—as in *"li"* (inside). In *Jiǎyǐ Jīng* Volume 1, Chapter 5, it reads *"li"* (interior). #3: *"Dà"*—as *"li"* (interior). In *Jiǎyǐ Jīng* Volume 1, Chapter 5, it reads *"huǎn"* (relaxed). *Huángdì Nèijīng Língshū Jízhù*, Volume 15, *Jìn Fú* (Prohibitions and Services), Chapter 48.

**外揣** (*Wàichuǎi*)—speaking of the entire body as a unified whole, yet still unclear about its meaning.

The Yellow Emperor said: *"The Dao/Way of acupuncture begins with the **jingmai** (channels and vessels), tracing their pathways and knowing their **dùliàng** (extent and measure). Internally, it targets the **wùzàng** (five viscera), externally, the **liùfǔ** (six palaces). Adjusting the **wèiqì** (defensive *qi*) and **yíngqì** (nutritive *qi*)—this is the root of all hundred diseases. Regulating their **xūshí** (excesses and deficiencies), the excesses and deficiencies will cease. Pricking their **xuèluò** (blood networks), when the blood is exhausted, the illness will not linger.

Léigōng (Thunder Official) said: *"These are all the principles I have already mastered, but I still do not know their **jié** (binding/constraints)."* The Yellow Emperor replied: *"To constrain a method is like binding a bag—if the bag is full yet not bound, it will leak. If a method is perfected yet not constrained, its **shén** (spiritual essence) will not be retained. Léigōng said: *"I wish to learn how to constrain even before the bag is full, so that I may become a skilled practitioner, though not necessarily a teacher for all under heaven."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"To become a skilled practitioner, one must measure the **rènmài** (pulse of the **rèn** channel—**cúnkǒu** [inch-opening]) as the **zhōng** (central), and the **rènmài** (pulse of the **rèn** channel—**rènyíng** [human-welcoming]) as the **wài** (external). When both pulses correspond and move in tandem—like drawing a taut thread of equal size—if the **rènyíng** pulse is slightly larger in spring and summer, and the **cúnkǒu** pulse is slightly larger in autumn and winter, this is called a **píngrén** (balanced person).

If the **rènyíng** pulse is onefold larger than the **cúnkǒu**, the illness lies in the **foot Shàoyáng** (Lesser Yang) channel; if it is onefold larger and **zào** (turbulent), it lies in the **hand Shàoyáng** channel.
If the **rènyíng** pulse is twofold larger, the illness lies in the **foot Tàiyáng** (Greater Yang) channel; if it is twofold larger and **zào**, the illness lies in the **hand Tàiyáng** channel.
If the **rènyíng** pulse is threefold larger, the illness lies in the **foot Yángmíng** (Bright Yang) channel; if it is threefold larger and **zào**, the illness lies in the **hand Yángmíng** channel.
When **shèng** (excessive), it manifests as **heat**; when **xū** (deficient), it manifests as **cold**. If **jǐn** (tight), it causes **painful numbness**; if **dài** (alternating), it fluctuates between severe and mild.

When excessive, **prick** (*xiě*) it; when deficient, **reinforce** (*bǔ*) it. If tight and painful, take the **fēnròu** (dividing flesh) method. If alternating, take the **xuèluò** (blood networks) and administer **yào** (medicine) orally. If the pulse is **xiànxià** (sunken), use **jiǔ** (moxibustion). If neither excessive nor deficient, follow the **jingmai** (channels) and **prick** (*cì*) accordingly—this is called **jingcì** (channel pricking).

If the **rènyíng** pulse is fourfold larger, it is both **large** and **frequent**, called **yìyáng** (overflowing Yang). Overflowing Yang leads to **wàigé** (external blockage)—once this occurs, the illness is incurable and death is imminent.
One must carefully examine the pulse from **běn** (origin) to **mò** (end), observing its **cold** and **hot** qualities to verify the illnesses of the **zàngfǔ** (viscera and palaces).

If the **cúnkǒu** pulse is onefold larger than the **rènyíng**, the illness lies in the **foot Juéyīn** (Concealed Yin) channel; if it is onefold larger and **zào**, it lies in the **hand Xīnmài** (Heart Master) channel.
If the **cúnkǒu** pulse is twofold larger, the illness lies in the **foot Shàoyīn** (Lesser Yin) channel; if it is twofold larger and **zào**, it lies in the **hand Shàoyīn** channel.
If the **cúnkǒu** pulse is threefold larger, the illness lies in the **foot Tàiyīn** (Greater Yin) channel; if it is threefold larger and **zào**, it lies in the **hand Tàiyīn** channel.

When excessive, it causes **distension and fullness**; when deficient, it causes **internal heat**, **discharge of undigested food** (*chū méi*), **shortness of breath**, and **urine color changes**.
When tight, it causes **painful numbness**; when alternating, it fluctuates between **pain and remission**.」

When excessive, **drain it**; when deficient, **tonify it**. When tight, **first prick with needles and then moxibustion**; when alternating, **first take the blood vessels and then regulate it**. When sunken and weak, **apply moxibustion alone**.

The sunken and weak pulse is where **pulse and blood congeal within**, with **stagnant blood** inside. Since the blood is cold, **moxibustion is appropriate**.

If neither excessive nor deficient, **treat according to the channel**.

If the **cúnkǒu** pulse is fourfold larger, it is called **Inner Pass (Nèiguān)**. The **Inner Pass**—when both large and rapid—is **untreatable and fatal**.

One must carefully examine its **root and branch**, its **cold and warmth**, to verify the illness of the **zàng-fǔ**.

To **unblock the nourishing and transporting networks**, only then can it be transmitted to the **greater numbers**.

The **greater numbers** say:
When excessive, **drain it alone**; when deficient, **tonify it alone**. When tight, **moxibustion, needling, and medication**. When sunken and weak, **moxibustion alone**. If neither excessive nor deficient, **treat according to the channel**.

What is called **channel treatment**—medication, **moxibustion, and needling**—are all included.

If the pulse is **urgent**, it **draws taut**; if large yet weak, it **desires rest**, and **avoids exertion**.

**[Phonetic Annotation]** *Dìchǔxiá* (enemy-chu-xia) rhyme.

**The Five Colors (Chapter Forty-Nine)**

**Leigong asked the Yellow Emperor:**

*"The Five Colors—do they solely pertain to the Bright Hall? I do not yet understand their meaning."*

The Yellow Emperor replied:

*"The Bright Hall is the nose. The Obscure (阙) is the eyebrows, the Courtyard (庭) is the face, the Abundant (蕃) are the sides of the cheeks, and the Concealed (蔽) are the ear gates. Between them, the space should be square and broad—ten steps away, all visible from the outside. If such is the case, one’s lifespan shall surely reach a hundred years."*

Leigong asked: *"How does one distinguish the Five Officials?"*

The Yellow Emperor said:

*"The Bright Hall’s bones rise high and stand straight, its five zàng aligned at the center, the six fǔ flanking its sides. The head and face ascend to the Obscure and Courtyard, the Royal Palace lies at the lower extreme. The five zàng rest in the chest, their true colors manifesting while diseased hues vanish. When the Bright Hall is moist and clear, how could the Five Officials fail to be distinguished?"*

Leigong asked: *"What of those not distinguished? May I inquire?"*

The Yellow Emperor replied:

*"When the Five Colors appear, each emerges from its designated region. If the bone of that region is sunken, disease shall surely follow. If the color spreads beyond its region, though the illness be severe, death shall not come."*

Leigong asked: *"How do the Five Officials manifest in disease?"*

The Yellow Emperor said:

*"Green and black signify pain; yellow and red signify heat; white signifies cold. These are the Five Officials."*

Leigong asked: *"When illness worsens and its pattern declines, how does one discern it?"*

The Yellow Emperor replied:

*"Both internal and external signs are present. If the pulse at the *cù-kǒu* is slippery, small, tight, and deep, the illness worsens within. If the *rén-yíng* pulse is large, tight, and floating, the illness worsens without. When the *cù-kǒu* pulse is floating and slippery, the illness advances daily. When the *rén-yíng* pulse is deep yet slippery, the illness recedes daily. If the *cù-kǒu* pulse is slippery and deep, the illness advances within. If the *rén-yíng* pulse is slippery, strong, and floating, the illness advances without. When the *cù-kǒu* and *rén-yíng* pulses differ in floating/depth and size, the illness is hard to cure.

If the illness lies in the zàng, a deep and large pulse is easy to cure, while a small pulse is ominous. If the illness lies in the fǔ, a floating and large pulse is easy to cure.

When the *rén-yíng* pulse is strong and firm, it signifies injury by cold. When the *qì-kǒu* pulse is strong and firm, it signifies injury by food."*

Leigong asked: *"How does one judge the severity of illness by color?"*

The Yellow Emperor replied:

*"When the color is coarse and bright, and the complexion is sunken and wan, the illness is severe. If the color ascends, the illness worsens. If the color descends like dispersing clouds, the illness is abating. The Five Colors each have an internal region, an external region, and a central region. If color moves from the external to the internal region, the illness shifts from without to within. If color moves from the internal to the external, the illness shifts from within to without.

When illness originates within, first treat the yin, then the yang—reversing this order worsens the condition. When illness originates in the yang, first treat the exterior, then the interior—reversing this order worsens the condition.

If the pulse is slippery, large, and irregular with a long rhythm, the illness comes from without. The patient may see strange sights or harbor strange aversions—this is the convergence of yang qi, and it may yet be transformed."*

Leigong asked: *"I have heard that wind is the beginning of a hundred illnesses, and that reversal (*jué*) arises from cold and dampness. How does one distinguish them?"*

The Yellow Emperor replied:

*"Observe the Obscure (between the eyebrows) constantly: thin moisture signifies wind; turbid surging signifies *bì* (numbness/ataxy); when it settles to the earth, it is reversal (*jué*). This is their constant pattern—each color declares its illness."*

Leigong asked: *"How does one know when a person dies suddenly without prior illness?"*

The Yellow Emperor replied:

*"When great qi enters the zàng-fǔ without prior illness, sudden death shall follow."*

Leigong asked: *"How does one know when a person dies suddenly after a minor recovery?"*

The Yellow Emperor replied:

*"If red appears on both cheeks, as large as a mother’s thumb, though the illness seems to abate, sudden death shall follow. If black appears at the Courtyard (face), as large as a mother’s thumb, the person shall die without prior illness."*

Leigong bowed twice and said: *"Excellent! Does death have a fixed time?"*

The Yellow Emperor replied: *"Observe the colors to know the hour."*

Leigong said: *"Excellent! I wish to hear further."*

The Yellow Emperor replied:

*"The Courtyard is the head and face; above the Obscure is the throat; the center of the Obscure is the lung; the lower extreme is the heart; directly below is the liver; to the left of the liver is the gallbladder; below is the spleen; above the square is the stomach; the center is the large intestine; flanking the large intestine is the kidney; opposite the kidney is the navel; above the Royal Palace (*miàn-wáng*) is the small intestine; below the Royal Palace is the bladder. The *pō* (nape) is the shoulder; behind the *pō* is the arm; below the arm is the hand. Above the inner corner of the eye is the chest and breast; ascending from the *shéng* (a landmark below the collarbone) is the back. Following the *yá-chē* (a landmark near the hip) downward is the thigh; the center is the knee; below the knee is the *jīng* (lower leg); below the *jīng* is the foot. The *jù-fèn* (inner thigh) is the *gǔ-lǐ* (inner groin); the *jù-lǚ* (bent knee) is the *xī-bì* (knee joint).

These are the regions of the five zàng and six fǔ, their branches and joints—each with its designated area. Knowing these areas, one harmonizes yin and yang, or yang and yin. Mastering these regions ensures infallible diagnosis; distinguishing left and right is the Great Way. Since male and female occupy different positions, they are called yin and yang. Examining moisture and waness determines the skill of the practitioner.

When the complexion is sunken and turbid, the illness is internal. When it is floating and moist, the illness is external. Yellow and red signify wind; green and black signify pain; white signifies cold. Yellow with a greasy sheen signifies pus; excessive red signifies blood; severe pain signifies spasm; extreme cold signifies loss of sensation in the skin.

Observe where each color appears in its region, note its floating or sinking to judge depth, examine its moisture or waness to foresee success or failure, and observe its dispersion or concentration to know proximity or distance. Watch whether the color ascends or descends to locate the illness. Gather your spirit within and, with focused mind, know past and present."*

If the **diagnostic qi** is not subtle, one cannot distinguish truth from falsehood. Fix your intent and do not withdraw—only then will you know the new and the old.

If the color is clear yet not coarse, its sinking or waness is severe; if unclear and without luster, the illness is not grave.

When the color is dispersed, flickering like a colt’s movement without yet gathering, the illness is scattered and the **qi** is painful, for the gathering has not yet formed.

When the **kidney** dominates the **heart**, the **heart** is first afflicted, and the **kidney** responds. All colors manifest thus.

In men, if the color appears in the **face sovereign** (*miàn wáng*), it signifies lower abdominal pain, below it the **testicles**; if round and straight, it signifies **penile pain**; high indicates the **root**, low the **head**—belonging to the category of *hú liè* (hernia) and *yīn* (scrotal) ailments. In women, if the color appears in the **face sovereign**, it signifies **bladder** and **uterine** diseases. Dispersed, it signifies pain; gathered, it signifies concretion. Square, round, left, or right—each follows its color and form.

If it descends randomly to the **calloused areas** (*zhī*), with a moist sheen like grease, it signifies gluttony and uncleanliness.

Left indicates left, right indicates right. If the color is perverse, its gathering and dispersal are irregular—this is what the **face sovereign** points to.

Colors—**blue-black, red, white, and yellow**—are all distinct and full, each with its own region.

When **red** deviates from its region, its color spreads large as a **pouch or gourd**, appearing in the **face sovereign** as **nocturnal emissions**.

If the color is sharp above, with the head hollow and upward-facing, sharp below and downward-facing, it appears on the left and right as prescribed.

The **Five Colors** correspond to the **Five Viscera**: **blue** to the **liver**, **red** to the **heart**, **white** to the **lung**, **yellow** to the **spleen**, and **black** to the **kidney**.

The **liver** governs **sinews**, the **heart** governs **vessels**, the **lung** governs **skin**, the **spleen** governs **flesh**, and the **kidney** governs **bones**.

---
**Discussion on Courage (Section 50)**
The Yellow Emperor asked **Shaoyu**:
*"Here is a man—walking or standing side by side with another, equal in age, with clothing of equal thickness. Suddenly, they encounter a violent wind and rain. One falls ill, another does not; sometimes both fall ill, sometimes neither does. Why is this?"*

Shaoyu replied: *"Your question is urgent, Sire. What do you wish to know?"*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear it all."*

Shaoyu said:
*"Spring’s **green wind**, summer’s **yang wind**, autumn’s **cool wind**, and winter’s **cold wind**—each of these seasonal winds manifests illness in different forms."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How do these seasonal winds affect the sick?"*

Shaoyu replied:
*"Those with **yellow** skin, thin **skin**, and weak **flesh** cannot endure spring’s **empty wind**; those with **white** skin, thin **skin**, and weak **flesh** cannot endure summer’s **empty wind**; those with **blue** skin, thin **skin**, and weak **flesh** cannot endure autumn’s **empty wind**; those with **red** skin, thin **skin**, and weak **flesh** cannot endure winter’s **empty wind**."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Do those with **black** skin not fall ill?"*

Shaoyu replied: *"If **black** in color, with thick **skin** and firm **flesh**, they are indeed unharmed by the four seasonal winds. But if their **skin** is thin and their **flesh** not firm, or if their color is uneven, and they encounter **empty wind** in **long summer**, they will fall ill. If their **skin** is thick and their **flesh** firm, even if they encounter **empty wind** in **long summer**, they will not fall ill. Only if their **skin** is thick and their **flesh** firm, yet they are **severely** exposed to cold—both externally and internally—will they fall ill."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Excellent."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"As for people who endure pain and those who do not, it is not a matter of courage or cowardice. The brave man who cannot endure pain will advance when facing danger but halt when facing pain; the coward who endures pain will fear danger upon hearing it and remain unmoved when encountering pain.

The brave man who endures pain will not fear danger and will remain unmoved when encountering pain. The coward who cannot endure pain, when facing danger and pain, will avert his gaze, his face turning pale, unable to speak, losing his breath in shock, his complexion shifting—now dead, now alive. I have observed this, but I do not know its cause. I wish to hear its explanation."*

Shaoyu replied: *"Enduring pain or not is determined by the thickness of the **skin**, the firmness or fragility of the **flesh**, and the tension or slackness of the **muscles**—not by courage or cowardice."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear how courage and cowardice arise."*

Shaoyu replied: *"The brave man has deep-set, steady eyes, a long, straight brow, and **San Jiao** pathways aligned horizontally. His **heart** is upright, his **liver** large and firm, his **gallbladder** full and extended. When angry, his **qi** surges, his chest expands, his **liver** rises, and his **gallbladder** spreads horizontally. His pupils dilate, his gaze sharpens, his hair stands on end, and his face turns ashen—this is how the brave man manifests."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear how cowardice arises."*

Shaoyu replied: *"The coward has large, unsteady eyes, with **yin** and **yang** misaligned. His **San Jiao** pathways are vertical, his **vertebrae** short and small, his **liver** suspension slack, his **gallbladder** neither full nor extended, his **intestines** and **stomach** protruding, and his flanks hollow. Though he rages fiercely, his **qi** cannot fill his chest. Though his **liver** and **lungs** rise, his **qi** weakens and sinks again, so he cannot sustain his anger—this is how cowardice manifests."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"When a coward drinks alcohol, his anger does not yield to that of the brave—what **viscus** causes this?"*

Shaoyu replied: *"Alcohol is the refined essence of **water** and **grains**, the fluid of ripened grains. Its **qi** is fierce and violent. When it enters the **stomach**, the **stomach** distends, and the **qi** reverses upward, filling the chest. The **liver** floats, and the **gallbladder** spreads horizontally. At this moment, he may rival the brave, but when his **qi** weakens, he regrets it. Though he behaves like the brave, he does not know to avoid it—this is called **alcohol’s perversity**."*

---
**Facial Expressions on the Back (Section 51)**
The Yellow Emperor asked **Qibo**:
*"I wish to hear of the **visceral expressions** that appear on the back."*

Qibo replied:
*"The **great facial expression** of the **chest** is at the end of the **zhù bone**; the **lung’s facial expression** is in the space of the **San Jiao**; the **heart’s facial expression** is in the space of the **Wu Jiao**; the **diaphragm’s facial expression** is in the space of the **Qi Jiao**; the **liver’s facial expression** is in the space of the **Jiu Jiao**; the **spleen’s facial expression** is in the space of the **Shi Yi Jiao**; the **kidney’s facial expression** is in the space of the **Shi Si Jiao**—all adjacent to the **spine**, three *cùn* apart. Press these points: if there is a response in the center and the pain subsides, then it is indeed the **facial expression**."*

The **Qibo** replied:
*"**Moxibustion** may be applied, but **acupuncture** must not. When the **qi** is abundant, **drain** it; when it is deficient, **tonify** it. For **tonification with fire**, do not fan the flame—it must extinguish itself. For **drainage with fire**, fan the flame swiftly, passing the **moxa** until the fire dies.*

**Qibo** said:
*"The **Five Visceral Organs** store the **spirit** (*shén*), **mind** (*jīng*), **soul** (*hún*), and **ethereal soul** (*pò*). The **Six Palaces** receive **water and grain** (*shuǐ gǔ*) and govern the **transformation of substances** (*huà wù*). Their **qi** internally nourishes the **Five Visceral Organs** and externally interconnects with the **limbs and joints** (*zhī jié*).

The **superficial defensive-qi** (*wèi qì*), which does not follow the **channels** (*jīng*), is called **defensive-qi**; the **essential-qi** (*jīng qì*), which courses through the **channels**, is called **nutritive-qi** (*yíng qì*). **Yin** and **yang** flow in tandem, their external and internal pathways interlinked like an endless ring. The **Five Visceral Organs** and **Six Palaces** harmonize in unceasing rhythm—who can fully comprehend this?

Yet, though their distinctions between **yin** and **yang** are profound, each has its **root** and **branch**, its **deficiency** and **excess**, and its **separation** from the **superficial** and **deep** layers. One who can discern the **yin-yang** of the **Twelve Channels** (*shí'èr jīng*) knows where diseases originate. One who can gauge the **deficiency** and **excess** of the **Six Palaces** (*liù fǔ*) knows the **height** and **depth** of illnesses. One who understands the **street-like pathways** (*jiē*) of the **Six Palaces’ qi** can discern the **unbinding** and **binding** of **disease at the gates** (*ménhù*).

One who knows the **firmness** and **softness** of **deficient-qi** (*xū qì*) and **stagnant-qi** (*shí qì*) can determine where **tonification** or **drainage** is needed. One who knows the **roots** and **branches** of the **Six Channels** (*liù jīng*) can be free from doubt in all things under heaven."

**Qibo** replied:
*"How profound are the **Sacred Emperor’s** words! Let me speak fully and exhaustively on this matter."*

The **roots** of the **Foot Taiyang Channel** lie five *cù* above the heel, with its **mark** at the two **network vessels** of the **Gate of Life**—the **Gate of Life** being the eyes.

The **roots** of the **Foot Shaoyang Channel** lie at the **Yin Orifice**, with its **mark** before the **Window Curtain**—the **Window Curtain** being the ears.

The **roots** of the **Foot Shaoyin Channel** lie three *cù* above the inner ankle, with its **mark** at the **back of the face** and the two pulses beneath the tongue.

The **roots** of the **Foot Jueyin Channel** lie five *cù* above the **Walking Orifice**, with its **mark** at the **back of the face**.

The **roots** of the **Foot Yangming Channel** lie at the **Lì Ditch**, with its **mark** at the **Human Welcome** pulse beside the cheek and jaw joint.

The **roots** of the **Foot Taiyin Channel** lie four *cù* above the **Middle Seal**, with its **mark** at the **back of the face** and the root of the tongue.

The **roots** of the **Hand Taiyang Channel** lie behind the outer ankle, with its **mark** one *cù* above the **Gate of Life**.

The **roots** of the **Hand Shaoyang Channel** lie two *cù* above the junction of the little and ring fingers, with its **mark** at the lower outer corner of the ear’s upper angle.

The **roots** of the **Hand Yangming Channel** lie at the elbow’s bone, extending upward to **Separate Yang**, with its **mark** at the **chin’s convergence** beneath the jaw.

The **roots** of the **Hand Taiyin Channel** lie at the **Cùn Mouth**, with its **mark** at the **armpit’s movement**.

The **roots** of the **Hand Shaoyin Channel** lie at the tip of the **Rùi Bone**, with its **mark** at the **back of the face**.

The **roots** of the **Hand Jīn Master Channel** lie two *cù* between the two tendons behind the palm, with its **mark** three *cù* below the armpit.

In examining these, if the **lower** is **deficient**, there is **sinking** (*jué*); if the **lower** is **excessive**, there is **heat**. If the **upper** is **deficient**, there is **dizziness**; if the **upper** is **excessive**, there is **heat and pain**.

Thus, when **stagnant-qi** is **blocked**, it must be **stopped**; when **deficient-qi** is **weak**, it must be **tonified**.

Now, let me speak of the **street-like pathways** of **qi**:
There are **street-like pathways** of **qi** in the **chest**, in the **abdomen**, in the **head**, and in the **Channels**.

Thus, when **qi** is in the **head**, it is **stopped** at the **brain**.
When **qi** is in the **chest**, it is **stopped** at the **chest** and the **back of the face**.
When **qi** is in the **abdomen**, it is **stopped** at the **back of the face** and the **Rushing Pulse** at the **navel’s** left and right **moving pulses**.
When **qi** is in the **Channels**, it is **stopped** at the **street-like pathways** and **Bearing Mountain**, below the **ankle**.

When taking these points, use a **hair-thin needle**, first pressing and holding for a long time until a response is felt in the hand, then inserting and withdrawing.

The conditions treated are:
**Headache**, **dizziness**, and **falling unconscious**;
**abdominal pain**, **midsection fullness**, and **sudden distension**;
as well as **new accumulations**.

If the **pain can be moved**, it is **easily cured**;
if the **accumulation does not pain**, it is **difficult to cure**.

**Phonetic Explanation:**
*"Pin"* sounds like *"qián"* [钤].

---
**End of *Ling Shu* (Spiritual Pivot) Volume 15, Section #1**
The *Tai Su* (Great Essence), Volume 14, *Rén Yíng Mài Kǒu Zhěn* (Pulse Diagnosis at the *Rén Yíng* Point), reads **"bì"** [必] instead.

**Annotations:**
#1 *"Mí"* [糜] was originally *"kāng"* [康], corrected according to *Jiǎ-Yǐ Jīng* (Classic of *Jiǎ* and *Yǐ*), Volume 4, Section 1.
#2 *"Pí"* [皮] was originally *"zhōu pí"* [州脾], corrected based on the *Zhào Fǔ Jù Jìng Táng* edition.
#3 *"Jiāo"* [焦] is written as *"zhuī"* [椎] in *Tai Su*, Volume 11, *Qì Xué* (Qi Cavities). The same pattern applies to **"Qī Jiāo"** [七焦], **"Jiǔ Jiāo"** [九焦], **"Shíyī Jiāo"** [十一焦], and **"Shísì Jiāo"** [十四焦].

#4 *"Wèn"* [问] is rendered as *"bàng"* [傍] in Wang Bing’s annotation to *Su Wèn* (Plain Questions), *Xuè Qì Xíng Zhì Piān* (Blood, Qi, Form, and Will).
#5 *"Shí"* [石] is written as *"shí"* [实] in *Jiǎ-Yǐ Jīng*, Volume 2, Section 4.
#6 *"Shí"* [石] is written as *"shí"* [实] in *Jiǎ-Yǐ Jīng*, Volume 2, Section 4.

---
**Ling Shu (Spiritual Pivot) Volume 16, Section 53: Discussion of Pain**

**Ling Shu (Spiritual Pivot) Volume 16, Section 53: Discussion of Pain**

The Yellow Emperor asked Shào Yú: *"The strength and weakness of tendons and bones, the firmness and brittleness of muscles and flesh, the thickness and thinness of skin, and the density of the defensive* **Qi** *(*Virtue/Power*)—each varies. How do they differ in their tolerance to the pain of acupuncture, moxibustion, and cauterization? The thickness and thinness, firmness and brittleness of the intestines and stomach also differ. How do they vary in their tolerance to poisonous medicines? I wish to hear all about it."* Shào Yú replied: *"Those whose bones are strong, tendons weak, flesh slack, and skin thick endure pain well. Such persons tolerate the pain of acupuncture and cauterization, and also that of moxibustion."* The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How do you know who can endure the pain of moxibustion?"* Shào Yú answered: *"Those with black complexion and beautiful bones can endure the pain of moxibustion."* The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How do you know who cannot endure the pain of acupuncture and cauterization?"* Shào Yú replied: *"Those with firm flesh and thin skin cannot endure the pain of acupuncture and cauterization, nor that of moxibustion."* The Yellow Emperor asked: *"In cases of illness, some recover simultaneously while others do not, some find relief easily while others struggle. What is the reason for this?"* Shào Yú replied: *"When illness affects a person simultaneously, those with more* **Qi** *of heat recover easily, while those with more* **Qi** *of cold find it difficult."* The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How do you know who can overcome poison?"* Shào Yú replied: *"Those with thick stomachs, black complexions, large bones, and robust bodies can all overcome poison. Conversely, those who are lean with thin stomachs cannot overcome poison."*

**Section 54: On Life and Death**

The Yellow Emperor asked Qí Bó: *"I wish to hear about the origins of a person’s life. What* **Qi** *forms the foundation? What establishes the* **Dao** *(*Way*)? What causes death, and what brings life? How can I understand this?"* Qí Bó replied: *"The mother’s essence serves as the foundation, and the father’s essence as the framework. Those who lose their* **shén** *(*Spirit*) die, and those who retain it live."* The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What constitutes* **shén** *(*Spirit*)?"* Qí Bó replied: *"When the blood and* **Qi** *are harmonized, the* **Róng** *(*Nourishing*) and* **Wèi** *(*Defensive*) circulations are established, the five* **zàng** *(*Viscera*) are formed, and* **shén** **-Qi** *(*Spiritual* **Qi**) resides in the heart, while the* **hún** *(*Ethereal Soul*) and* **pò** *(*Corporeal Soul*) are fully possessed—then one becomes a person."* The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Why do people have varying lifespans? Some die young, some in old age, some suffer long illnesses. I wish to understand the* **Dao** *(*Way*) of this."* Qí Bó replied: *"When the five* **zàng** *(*Viscera*) are firm and unyielding, the blood and vessels are harmonized, the muscles and sinews are supple and agile, the skin is dense, the* **Yíng** *(*Nourishing*) and* **Wèi** *(*Defensive*) circulations proceed without deviation, breathing is gentle and measured, the* **Qi** *flows according to its proper limits, the six* **fǔ** *(*Organs*) transform grain into* **gu** *(*Essence*), and* **jīn-yè** *(*fluids*) are evenly distributed—each according to its proper function—then one can live long."* The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one reach a lifespan of a hundred years before dying?"* Qí Bó replied: *"By nurturing the* **Dao** *(*Way*) to make it long-lasting, by establishing a strong foundation, by harmonizing the* **Yíng** *(*Nourishing*) and* **Wèi** *(*Defensive*) circulations, by cultivating the three* **bù** *(*sections*) and three* **lǐ** *(*distances*) of the body, by attaining tall bones and full flesh, one may reach a hundred years."* The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Can you explain the waxing and waning of* **Qi** *until death occurs?"* Qí Bó replied: *"At ten years of age, the five* **zàng** *(*Viscera*) have just begun to stabilize, and the blood and* **Qi** *are now flowing. The* **Qi** *resides in the lower body, so the person enjoys running. At twenty years, the blood and* **Qi** *are at their peak, and the muscles and flesh are fully grown, so the person enjoys rushing forward. At thirty years, the five* **zàng** *(*Viscera*) are fully established, the muscles and flesh are firm, and the blood vessels are full and robust, so the person enjoys walking. At forty years, the five* **zàng** *(*Viscera*), six* **fǔ** *(*Organs*), and twelve* **jīng-mài** *(*Meridians*) are all at their fullest and balanced. The defensive* **Qi** *begins to thin, and the* **Róng** *(*Nourishing*) and* **Wèi** *(*Defensive*) circulations decline, with hair turning gray and white. The* **Qi** *remains balanced without disturbance, so the person enjoys sitting. At fifty years, the* **Qi** *of the liver begins to decline, the liver lobes grow thin, bile production diminishes, and vision becomes unclear. At sixty years, the* **Qi** *of the heart begins to decline, and the person is prone to sorrow, worry, and melancholy. The blood and* **Qi** *become sluggish, so the person prefers to stay outdoors. At seventy years, the* **Qi** *of the spleen weakens, and the skin dries out. At eighty years, the* **Qi** *of the lungs declines, and the* **pò** *(*Corporeal Soul*) departs, so speech often becomes confused. At ninety years, the* **Qi** *of the kidneys becomes exhausted, and the five* **zàng** *(*Viscera*) and meridians grow empty. At a hundred years, the five* **zàng** *(*Viscera*) are all depleted, the* **shén** **-Qi** *(*Spiritual* **Qi*) has departed, and only the physical form remains before death."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What about those who cannot reach old age and die prematurely? How does that come about?"* Qí Bó replied: *"Their five* **zàng** *(*Viscera*) are not firm. The* **Dao** *(*Way*) is not cultivated to its full length. The body is hollow and distended, with rapid breathing and sudden illnesses. Their foundation is low and weak, their vessels thin and blood scarce, their flesh not firm. They often suffer from exposure to wind and cold, leading to depletion of blood and* **Qi** *and blocked vessels. When true* **Qi** *and pathogenic* **Qi** *assail each other, they become disordered and mutually entangled, thus failing to reach old age and dying early."*

**Section 55: On Contrary and Concordant Qi**

The Yellow Emperor asked Bó Gāo: *"I have heard that* **Qi** *has contrary and concordant movements, and that the vessels have waxing and waning. There are general principles for acupuncture—can I hear about these?"* Bó Gāo replied: *"The contrary and concordant movements of* **Qi** *are in accordance with the* **Dao** *(*Way*) of Heaven and Earth, Yin and Yang, the four seasons, and the five phases. The waxing and waning of the vessels are used to gauge the depletion or excess of blood and* **Qi***. The general principles of acupuncture require one to clearly understand whether a disease is amenable to acupuncture, whether it is not yet amenable, or whether it is already beyond acupuncture’s reach."* The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one diagnose this?"* Bó Gāo replied: *"The* **Bīngfǎ** *(*Military Strategy*) states: ‘Do not confront the enemy’s advancing* **Qi***, nor attack their strong formations.’ The* **Cìfǎ** *(*Needle Method*) states: ‘Do not puncture when* **Qi** *is turbulent and the body is hot, do not puncture when sweat is profuse, do not puncture when the vessels are chaotic, nor when the disease and* **Qi** *of the vessels are contrary to each other.’"* The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How does one diagnose when acupuncture is appropriate?"* Bó Gāo replied: *"The highest skill is to puncture before the disease fully arises. The next level is to puncture before the* **Qi** *becomes excessive. The following level is to puncture when the* **Qi** *has already waned. The lowest skill is to puncture when the disease has already taken hold, or when the* **Qi** *of the body is at its peak, or when the disease and* **Qi** *of the vessels are contrary to each other. Thus it is said: ‘When the* **Qi** *is at its peak, do not dare to harm or injure. Puncture when the* **Qi** *has already waned, and success will be certain.’ It is also said: ‘The highest skill treats what has not yet become a disease; the lowest skill treats what is already a disease.’ This is what is meant."*

---

**Five Flavors (Chapter 56)**

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear how the five flavors of grains enter the five viscera and how they are distributed. What is their distinction?"*

Bó Gāo replied: *"The stomach is the sea of the five viscera and six bowels. All water and grains enter the stomach, and the five viscera and six bowels all draw their qi from the stomach.

The five flavors each follow their preferred path: sour grains first enter the liver, bitter grains first enter the heart, sweet grains first enter the spleen, spicy grains first enter the lungs, and salty grains first enter the kidneys.

Once the grain’s essence and fluids have circulated, the *Yíng* and *Wèi* flow freely, and then the dregs are transformed and passed downward in sequence."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How do the *Yíng* and *Wèi* circulate?"*

Bó Gāo replied: *"When grains first enter the stomach, their refined essence emerges from the two *jiāo* (flanks) of the stomach to irrigate the five viscera. It then branches into two pathways—the *Yíng* and *Wèi* channels.

The coarse qi that does not circulate accumulates in the chest, called the *Qìhǎi* (Sea of Qi). It emerges from the lungs, travels along the throat and pharynx, so that exhalation expels it and inhalation draws it in.

The vital essence of Heaven and Earth, in its great measure, always emerges three times and enters once. Thus, if no grains are consumed, the qi weakens after half a day and diminishes after a full day."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"May I hear of the five flavors of grains?"*

Bó Gāo replied: *"Let me speak exhaustively.

The five grains: *Háng* rice is sweet, hemp is sour, large beans are salty, barley is bitter, and yellow millet is spicy.

The five fruits: dates are sweet, plums are sour, chestnuts are salty, *yǎo* (a fruit) is bitter, and peaches are spicy.

The five livestock: cattle are sweet, dogs are sour, pigs are salty, sheep are bitter, and geese are spicy.

The five vegetables: *kuí* (a gourd) is sweet, garlic chives are sour, *lěi* (a plant) is salty, lotus is bitter, and onions are spicy.

The five colors: yellow favors sweetness, green favors sourness, black favors saltiness, red favors bitterness, and white favors spiciness.

These five categories each have their own suitability.

The five *yí* (affinities) I speak of: for spleen ailments, one should eat *Háng* rice, cow meat, dates, and *kuí* (gourd). For heart ailments, one should eat barley, sheep meat, *yǎo* (fruit), and *yuè* (a plant). For kidney ailments, one should eat large beans, yellow rolls, pig meat, and chestnuts. For liver ailments, one should eat hemp, dog meat, plums, and garlic chives. For lung ailments, one should eat yellow millet, goose meat, peaches, and onions.

The five prohibitions: for liver ailments, spicy is forbidden; for heart ailments, salty is forbidden; for spleen ailments, sour is forbidden; for kidney ailments, sweet is forbidden; for lung ailments, bitter is forbidden.

The liver’s color is green, favoring sweetness—*Háng* rice, cow meat, dates, and *kuí* (gourd) are all sweet. The heart’s color is red, favoring sourness—dog meat, hemp, plums, and garlic chives are all sour. The spleen’s color is yellow, favoring saltiness—large beans, pig meat, chestnuts, and *lěi* (plant) are all salty. The lung’s color is white, favoring bitterness—barley, sheep meat, and *xìng* (a fruit) are all bitter. The kidney’s color is black, favoring spiciness—yellow millet, goose meat, peaches, and onions are all spicy."*

**Water Edema (Chapter 57)**

The Yellow Emperor asked Qī Bó: *"How do we distinguish water edema, skin edema, drum edema, intestinal *táng* (a mass), stone *hán* (a condition), and stone water?"*

Qī Bó replied: *"When water edema first arises, the eye sockets swell slightly, like a newly formed mound. The neck pulse throbs, there is occasional coughing, the groin feels cold, the legs swell, and the abdomen becomes distended—this is when the water has fully formed.

Pressing the abdomen, the swelling rises with the hand, like water inside—this is its sign."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How do we diagnose skin edema?"*

Qī Bó replied: *"Skin edema occurs when cold qi lodges between the skin and flesh. The abdomen is distended but not firm, the body swells entirely, the skin thickens, and pressing the abdomen leaves no impression. The abdominal color does not change—this is its sign."*

*"What of drum edema?"*

Qī Bó replied: *"The abdomen swells uniformly, making the body equally distended, like skin edema. The color turns grayish-yellow, and abdominal muscles protrude—this is its sign."*

*"What of intestinal *táng*?"*

Qī Bó replied: *"When cold qi lodges outside the intestines and clashes with the *Wèi* qi, the qi cannot nourish, and if it becomes entangled, it congeals inward, generating malignant qi and festering flesh.

At first, it forms like a goose egg, gradually growing larger, until it resembles a fetus in the womb. If it persists for years, pressing it feels firm, and pushing it moves it. Menstruation still occurs on time—this is its sign."*

*"What of stone *hán*?"*

Qī Bó replied: *"Stone *hán* forms in the uterus. When cold qi blocks the uterine passage, the qi cannot flow, and stagnant blood, which should be expelled, instead lingers and grows daily, forming a mass like a fetus. Menstruation does not occur on time. This condition arises only in women and can be guided downward."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Can skin edema and drum edema be treated with acupuncture?"*

Qī Bó replied: *"First, drain the blood vessels causing the edema, then regulate the channels, and finally remove the blood vessels."*

**Thieving Wind (Chapter 58)**

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Master, you speak of how thieving wind and evil qi harm people, causing illness. Yet there are cases where individuals remain indoors, never exposed to wind or evil qi, yet suddenly fall ill. Why is this?"*

Qī Bó replied: *"These are cases where the person was previously injured by dampness, which lingers in the blood vessels and between the muscles, remaining for a long time without leaving. Or, if they experienced a fall or trauma, stagnant blood remains inside without being expelled.

If emotions become unchecked—excessive joy or anger—or if diet and climate are mismanaged, the natural flow is obstructed. When this blockage opens and encounters wind or cold, the blood and qi congeal, combining with old stagnation to form cold *bì* (numbness). If heat arises, sweating occurs, and sweating invites wind. Even without encountering thieving wind or evil qi, illness will inevitably develop from these causes."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"The cases you describe are ones where the patient knows their own condition. But what of those who have not encountered evil qi, nor experienced fear or vigilance, yet suddenly fall ill? What is the cause of this?"*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Could it be due to matters involving ghosts and spirits?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"This too has its cause—where evil *qi* lingers and has yet to manifest, and thus the mind harbors aversion toward something or yearns for another, disrupting the blood and *qi* within. The two *qi* forces then clash. The origin of this disturbance is subtle, unseen in sight and unheard in listening, hence it appears as though it were caused by ghosts and spirits."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"What of those who merely recite incantations (*zhù*)—what is their cause?"*

Qì Bó replied: *"Before the shamans (*wū*) act, they discern the prevailing *qi* behind a hundred illnesses, and they know from where the illness arises. Thus, it can be addressed through incantations."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"When the defensive *qi* lingers in the abdomen, causing spasms#1 and stagnation, and the vital organs cannot maintain their proper functions, leading to distension of the flanks, stomach fullness, and labored breathing with inverted exhalation—how should this be treated?"*

Bó Gāo replied: *"If the *qi* accumulates in the chest, treat it from above; if it accumulates in the abdomen, treat it from below; if both above and below are full, treat it from the sides."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How should one treat it?"*

Bó Gāo replied: *"If it accumulates above, needle the *rèn yíng*, *tiān tú*, and throat center; if it accumulates below, needle the *sān lǐ* and *qì jiē*; if both above and below are full, treat both above and below, and also needle one *cùn* below the lower ribs#2. For severe cases, needle the *yīng zú*."*

*"If the pulse is large and taut, or if it is absent, or if the abdominal skin is extremely tense, needling is contraindicated."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Excellent."*

The Yellow Emperor asked Bó Gāo: *"How does one determine diseases of the skin, flesh, *qi*, blood, sinews, and bones?"*

Bó Gāo replied: *"If a sheen arises between the eyebrows, the disease is in the skin. If the lips are green, yellow, red, white, or black, the disease is in the muscles. If the complexion appears damp and wan, the disease is in the blood and *qi*. If the eyes are green, yellow, red, white, or black, the disease is in the sinews. If the ears become dry, withered, and accumulate dirt, the disease is in the bones."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"What are the clinical manifestations? How should they be treated?"*

Bó Gāo said: *"The myriad diseases and their transformations are countless. Yet the skin has its pathways, the flesh its supporting structures, the blood and *qi* their conduits, and the bones their dependencies."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear the reasoning behind this."*

Bó Gāo replied: *"The pathways of the skin converge at the extremities. The supporting structures of the flesh lie within the arm’s *yang* channels between the muscles and the foot’s *Shaoyin* division. The conduits of blood and *qi* distribute to the collateral vessels, where stagnation gives rise to excess and manifestation. The sinews have no *yin* or *yang*, no left or right—one observes where the disease resides. The dependencies of the bones are the hollows that receive nourishment to benefit the brain and marrow."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How should they be treated?"*

Bó Gāo said: *"Diseases vary in their transformations—shallow or deep, nonexhaustive in their forms. Superficial cases are treated superficially; severe cases, deeply. Mild cases require few points; severe cases, many. One must adjust *qi* according to the variation—thus the master physician."*

The Yellow Emperor asked Bó Gāo: *"How does one distinguish between a person’s fatness, leanness, size, and temperature, as well as their age—old, strong, young, or small?"*

Bó Gāo replied: *"Those fifty years and above are old; those twenty years and above are strong; those eighteen years and above are young; those six years and above are small."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one measure their fatness or leanness?"*

Bó Gāo replied: *"There are those who are fat, those who are oily, and those who are merely fleshy."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one distinguish them?"*

Bó Gāo replied: *"If the subcutaneous fat is firm and abundant, and the skin is full, they are fat. If the subcutaneous fat is not firm and the skin is slack, they are oily. If the skin and flesh do not separate, they are merely fleshy."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one determine their bodily temperature?"*

Bó Gāo replied: *"The oily have soft flesh and coarse pores—they are cold-bodied. Those with fine pores are hot-bodied. The fleshy have firm flesh—those with fine pores are hot, those with coarse pores are cold."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one determine their fatness, leanness, and size?"*

Bó Gāo replied: *"The oily have abundant *qi* and slack skin, thus their bellies hang and their flanks droop. The fleshy have large, capacious bodies. The lean have small, contracted frames."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How do the *qi* and blood differ in these three types?"*

Bó Gāo replied: *"The oily have abundant *qi*; abundant *qi* brings heat, and heat endures cold. The fleshy have abundant blood, filling the form and balancing it. The lean have clear blood, smooth but scant *qi*, thus they cannot grow large. These are the distinctions among the many."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"And the common people?"*

Bó Gāo replied: *"The common people cannot combine the traits of fat, oily, or fleshy. Their blood and *qi* cannot be excessive, so their forms are neither small nor large—they simply conform to their own bodies, and are called the common people."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Excellent. How should they be treated?"*

Bó Gāo replied: *"One must first distinguish their three forms, the abundance of blood, and the clarity of *qi*, then adjust accordingly—never deviating from the standard channels. Thus, the oily have drooping bellies and sagging flanks; the fleshy have large, capacious bodies above and below; the lean, though fleshy, cannot grow large."*

From the *Jade Tablet*, the Sixty-First Section.

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I once considered the small needle a trivial thing, but you speak of aligning it with Heaven above, Earth below, and humanity within. I thought you exceeded the purpose of acupuncture. I wish to hear the reasoning."*

Qíbù replied: *"What is greater than Heaven? Only the Five Weapons are greater than the needle. The Five Weapons are instruments of death, not life. Moreover, humanity is the pivot of Heaven and Earth—how could it be neglected? Those who govern the people also rely on the needle. Compared to the Five Weapons, which is the lesser?"*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"When diseases arise, there are unpredictable emotions, irregular diet, deficient *yin* *qi*, excess *yang* *qi*, and stagnant *ying* *qi*—these manifest as paralysis and abscesses. When *yin* and *yang* fail to circulate and opposing heats clash, they transform into pus. Can the small needle treat this?"*

Qíbù replied: *"Even the sage cannot reverse transformations. Evil *qi* cannot be made to linger."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"When two armies face each other, their banners visible and blades drawn upon the battlefield, this is not a matter of a single day’s planning. To make the people obey with strict discipline and keep soldiers from harm’s way—this is not the work of a single day’s instruction, but the fruit of long practice. Yet even to suffer paralysis and abscesses, to accumulate pus and blood, is this not to stray far from the Dao/Way?

The formation of paralysis and abscesses, the congealing of pus and blood—these do not come from Heaven above nor from Earth below, but arise from the accumulation of subtle imbalances.

Thus, the sage intervenes before the disease takes form, while the fool encounters it only when it is already manifest."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"If the disease has already taken form, and you do not meet it there, if the pus has already formed, and you do not see it then—how shall you proceed?"*

Qíbù replied: *"When pus has formed, nine out of ten will die; hence, the sage does not allow it to reach that state. Instead, he clarifies the proper prescriptions, records them upon bamboo and silk, so that skilled practitioners may follow and transmit them to future generations without end—this is to prevent the disease from ever forming. Hence, the sage does not face it when it is already manifest."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"If the pus and blood have already gathered, and you then confront it—should you not guide it with the small needle to treat it?"*

Qíbù replied: *"To treat small ills with small means yields little effect; to treat great ills with great means brings many harms. Thus, when pus and blood have already formed, only the lancet and sharp-edged stone can remove them."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"If great harm is inevitable, then is it not impossible to ensure complete success?"*

Qíbù replied: *"It depends on whether one acts in accordance with or against the natural order."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear of this order of accordance and opposition."*

Qíbù replied: *"When treating wounds, if the eyes turn white with dark rings and shrink—this is the first sign of opposition. If internal medicine is administered but the patient vomits—this is the second sign of opposition. If there is abdominal pain and extreme thirst—this is the third sign of opposition. If the shoulders and nape stiffen and refuse to move—this is the fourth sign of opposition. If the voice becomes hoarse and the body wastes away—this is the fifth sign of opposition. Apart from these five, all else is in accordance with the natural order.

As for diseases in general: if the abdomen swells, the body burns with heat, and the pulse is large—this is the first sign of opposition. If the abdomen rumbles and distends while the limbs grow cold, with diarrhea and a large pulse—this is the second sign of opposition. If maggots infest and do not cease, with a large pulse—this is the third sign of opposition. If coughing leads to blood loss and emaciation, with a small, taut pulse—this is the fourth sign of opposition. If coughing causes emaciation, the body burns with heat, and the pulse is small and rapid—this is called the fifth sign of opposition. Such cases will not survive beyond fifteen days.

If the abdomen is greatly distended, the limbs grow cold, the body wastes away, and diarrhea is severe—this is the first sign of opposition. If the abdomen swells with bloody bowel movements and the pulse is large but intermittent—this is the second sign of opposition. If coughing leads to blood loss, the flesh and form waste away, and the pulse is faint—this is the third sign of opposition. If blood is vomited, the chest feels full and tightens toward the back, with a small and rapid pulse—this is the fourth sign of opposition. If coughing, vomiting, and abdominal distension occur alongside severe diarrhea, and the pulse is intermittent—this is the fifth sign of opposition. Such cases will not last even to the next hour before death.

One who does not discern these signs and yet treats them is called ‘treating against the natural order.’"*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Your words on the needle are swift and profound, harmonizing with Heaven and Earth. You ascend to measure celestial omens and descend to gauge terrestrial rhythms. Internally, you distinguish the Five *Zàng* Organs; externally, you order the Six *Fǔ* Palaces. You trace the twenty-eight junctions of the channels, and exhaust all their cycles. You can kill or save a man, yet cannot restore the dead—can you reverse this?"*

Qíbù replied: *"One can kill or save a man, yet cannot restore the dead."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have heard this and consider it unkind, yet I still wish to hear the reasoning behind it, so that I may not apply it to others."*

Qíbù replied: *"This is the clarity of the Dao/Way—it is inevitable. Just as blades and swords can kill, and wine can intoxicate, though no diagnosis is made, the outcome is still known."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear this fully."*

Qíbù replied: *"The essence a man receives is from the grains. Where the grains settle is the stomach. The stomach is the sea of grains, *qi*, blood, and essence. Where the sea sends forth its mists and vapors is the body. Where the stomach emits *qi* and blood are the channels and collaterals. The channels and collaterals are the great networks of the Five *Zàng* Organs and Six *Fǔ* Palaces—one must only meet them head-on and take what is due. The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Are there prescribed limits above and below?"*

Qíbù replied: *"Meet them at five *lǐ* intervals, and halt at the midpoint—five arrivals suffice. Five departures exhaust the organ’s *qi*, hence twenty-five punctures deplete its outlet. This is called ‘taking their Heavenly *qi*—not severing their life nor draining their years.’"*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear this fully."*

Qíbù replied: *"When treating at the doorstep, the patient dies within the home; when treating at the threshold, the patient dies in the hall. This is the art of the needle’s prohibitions."*

**Chapter Sixty-One: The Five Prohibitions**

The Yellow Emperor asked Qíbù: *"I have heard that there are Five Prohibitions in acupuncture—what are these Five Prohibitions?"*

Qíbù replied: *"These are the times when one must not puncture."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have also heard that there are Five Seizures in acupuncture."*

Qíbù replied: *"Do not neglect what cannot be seized."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have heard that there are Five Excesses in acupuncture."*

Qíbù replied: *"Supplement what is deficient without overstepping the measure."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have heard that there are Five Oppositions in acupuncture."*

Qíbù replied: *"When the disease and pulse oppose one another, it is called the Five Oppositions."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have heard that there are Nine Appropriatenesses in acupuncture."*

Qíbù replied: *"This refers to the mastery of the Nine Needles’ principles—this is the Nine Appropriatenesses."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What are the Five Prohibitions? I wish to hear the times when one must not puncture."*

Qíbù replied: *"On days *jiǎ* and *yǐ* (甲乙), when the same *tiān* *gān* (天干) repeats, do not puncture the head nor open the ear’s membrane. On days *bǐng* and *dīng* (丙丁), when the same *tiān* *gān* repeats, do not shake dust from the shoulders, throat, or *lián* *quán* (廉泉) point. On days *wù* and *jǐ* (戊己), when the same *tiān* *gān* repeats in all four seasons, do not puncture the abdomen nor the areas near the nails when water *qi* is present. On days *gēng* and *xīn* (庚辛), when the same *tiān* *gān* repeats, do not puncture the joints of the thigh or knee."*

「On days *rén* and *guǐ* (壬癸), when the same *tiān* *gān* repeats, do not puncture the soles of the feet nor the *fú* *qiāng* (足腔).

This is called the Five Prohibitions.

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What are the Five Depletions? I wish to hear the times when one must not puncture."*

Qíbù replied: *"When the flesh and form are already depleted, this is the first depletion; when blood is excessively depleted afterward, this is the second depletion; when great sweating occurs afterward, this is the third depletion; when great diarrhea follows, this is the fourth depletion; and after childbirth or excessive blood loss, this is the fifth depletion. These are all times when puncture is forbidden."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What are the Five Reversals?"*

Qíbù replied: *"When a feverish illness presents with a tranquil pulse, yet sweating has already occurred, and the pulse becomes strong and agitated—this is the first reversal; when illness causes diarrhea and the pulse is surging and large—this is the second reversal; when a fixed *zháo* (痹, stagnation) does not shift, the flesh is damaged, the body is hot, and the pulse is uneven or absent—this is the third reversal; when excessive indulgence depletes the flesh, the body is hot, the complexion is wan and white, and later heavy bleeding occurs, with the bleeding being severe and persistent—this is called the fourth reversal; when cold and heat deplete the flesh and the pulse is firm and tense—this is the fifth reversal.*

**Chapter 62: Movement and Rest**

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"The twelve channels are said to move without ceasing in the hands’ *Tài Yīn* (太阴), and the feet’s *Shǎo Yīn* (少阴) and *Yáng Míng* (阳明). Why is this?"*

Qíbù replied: *"This illustrates the *stomach channel* (胃脉). The stomach is the sea of the five *zàng* (藏, viscera) and six *fǔ* (府, bowels), its clear *qi* ascending to the lungs. The lung *qi* flows through the *Tài Yīn* channel, its movement corresponding to inhalation and exhalation. Thus, with one breath, the pulse moves twice, and with one inhalation, the pulse also moves twice. Since breathing never ceases, the pulse moves without stopping."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"When the *qi* passes the *cùn kǒu* (寸口, radial pulse point), does it rest after ten movements? Or does it lie dormant after eight movements?"*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"By what Dao does the qi return? I do not know its extremity."*

Qíbù replied: *"When the qi departs from the viscera, it suddenly surges forth like the release of a bow or crossbow, like water rushing down a steep bank. It ascends to the lungs, then returns in decline, while the remaining qi disperses upward in reverse, thus its movement becomes subtle."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"By what cause does the *Yáng Míng* channel of the feet move?"*

Qíbù replied: *"The stomach’s *qi* ascends to the lungs, and its fierce *qi* surges upward to the head, following the throat, ascending to the orbital cavity, tracing the eye’s tendons, entering the brain’s network, emerging at the forehead, descending to the *kè zhǔ rén* (客主人, host-guest points), tracing the *yá chē* (牙车, molar region), converging with the *Yáng Míng* channel, and descending to *rén yíng* (人迎). This is how the stomach’s *qi* diverges to the *Yáng Míng* channel. Thus, the interplay of yin and yang above and below moves as one. Hence, when a yang illness presents with a small yang pulse, it is perverse; when a yin illness presents with a large yin pulse, it is perverse. Therefore, when yin and yang are both still or both moving like a taut thread, imbalance brings disease."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"By what cause does the *Shǎo Yīn* channel of the feet move?"*

Qíbù replied: *"The *Chōng* channel is the sea of the twelve channels, and its great network, along with the *Shǎo Yīn*’s major collaterals, arises below the kidney, exits at the *qì jiē* (气街), traces the inner aspect of the thigh, enters the *bīn zhōng* (胭中), follows the inner aspect of the spine’s channel, and descends alongside the *Shǎo Yīn* channel to enter behind the inner ankle, then beneath the foot. Its divergent branch enters the ankle, ascends to the *fǔ shàng* (驸上), enters between the big toe’s bones, and registers in the collaterals to warm the sole—this is the pulse’s constant movement."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"The *yíng* and *wèi* circulate above and below, interpenetrating like an endless loop. Yet if one suddenly encounters perverse *qi* or encounters great cold, causing the hands and feet to slacken, how does the pulse’s yin-yang Dao, at their intersecting points, lose its course? By what means does the *qi* return?"*

Qíbù replied: *"The four extremities are where yin and yang converge—these are the great networks of *qi*. The four *jiē* (四街) are the *qi*’s thoroughfares. Thus, when a network is severed, the thoroughfare remains open; when the extremities relax, the *qi* follows its convergence, circulating like an endless loop."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Excellent. This is what is meant by ‘like an endless loop, its beginning unknown, ending only to begin anew.’"*

The Yellow Emperor asked Qíbù, saying: *"The five flavors enter the mouth, each having its own pathway and each causing its own ailment. Sour affects the sinews—excessive consumption leads to contraction; salty affects the blood—excessive consumption leads to thirst; pungent affects the *qi*—excessive consumption leads to a hollowed heart; bitter affects the bones—excessive consumption leads to alternating vomiting; sweet affects the flesh—excessive consumption leads to a contented heart. I know these effects, but I do not know their underlying mechanisms. I wish to hear their origins."*

Qíbù replied: *"When sour enters the stomach, its *qi* is astringent and contracts, failing to circulate freely between the two *jiāo* (焦). Unable to ascend or descend, it lingers in the stomach. When the stomach’s warmth harmonizes, the *qi* descends into the bladder, whose thin membrane is weak. Sour *qi* causes it to shrink and constrict, blocking its passage, so that the waterways cannot flow—hence the contraction. The sinews are where yin *qi* terminates, so when sour enters, it travels to the sinews."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Salty affects the blood, and excessive consumption leads to thirst—why is this?"*

Qíbù replied: *"When salty enters the stomach, its *qi* ascends to the middle *jiāo* and flows into the pulse vessels, carrying blood *qi* with it. Blood and salty *qi* combine, causing coagulation. The stomach’s juices then descend upon it, depleting the stomach’s moisture. When the stomach is exhausted, the throat passage becomes parched—hence the dry tongue and persistent thirst. The blood vessels are the pathway of the middle *jiāo*, so when salty enters, it travels to the blood."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Pungent affects the *qi*, and excessive consumption leads to a hollowed heart—why is this?"*

Qíbù replied: *"When pungent enters the stomach, its *qi* ascends to the upper *jiāo*, which receives *qi* to nourish all the yang pathways. The *qi* of ginger and scallions permeates it, so that the *yíngwèi* (营卫) *qi* is not received at its proper times. Prolonged stagnation in the heart’s region causes a hollowed sensation. Pungent *qi* travels alongside the *qi*, so when pungent enters, it also emerges with sweat."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Bitter affects the bones, and excessive consumption leads to alternating vomiting—why is this?"*

Qíbù replied: *"When bitter enters the stomach, the *qi* of the five grains cannot overcome it. Bitter *qi* descends to the lower *yùn* (院), where the pathways of the three *jiāo* are all obstructed and fail to circulate—hence the alternating vomiting. The teeth are where the stomach’s *qi* terminates, so when bitter enters, it travels to the bones. Its ascent and descent reveal its pathway to the bones."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Sweet affects the flesh, and excessive consumption leads to a contented heart—why is this?"*

Qíbù replied: *"When sweet enters the stomach, its *qi* is weak and subtle, unable to ascend to the upper *jiāo*. Instead, it lingers with the grains in the stomach, softening and moistening them. When the stomach is relaxed, worms stir, and their movement causes a contented sensation in the heart. Its *qi* externally connects with the flesh, so sweet travels to the flesh."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have heard that the ‘twenty-five persons of yin-yang’ (阴阳二十五人) are as you describe—how are they?"*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Master Bǎogāo has spoken—within the space between Heaven and Earth and the four cardinal directions, the quintessence never departs from the Five [Elements]. Humans likewise respond to this. Why then do the ‘twenty-five persons of yin-yang’ not align with the Five [Elements]’s governance, and how do they differ from the common multitude?"*

Qíbù replied: *"The Five [Elements]—Metal, Wood, Water, Fire, and Earth—each manifest distinct forms and colors. When their five variations are fully distinguished, the twenty-five persons are complete. Their appearances diverge in five ways from the crowd, and I have already understood them. But I wish to hear of the twenty-five persons’ physical traits, how their blood and *qi* arise, and how they may be discerned externally to reveal their internal nature. How does one perceive them from without to know their within?"*

Qíbù said: *"Your inquiry is thorough! This is the secret of the ancient masters. Even I, though learned, cannot fully illuminate it."*

The Yellow Emperor rose from his seat, bowed in reverence, and stepped back, saying: *"I have heard that if one finds such a person but does not teach them, it is called a double loss. If one finds and then reveals this knowledge, Heaven will withhold it. I wish to learn it, to keep it hidden in a golden chest, and never to proclaim it."*

Qíbù said: *"Very well. Let me speak carefully.

The Wood-formed person resembles the upper *jiǎo* [corner], akin to the Cyan Sovereign [苍帝]. Such a person has a cyan hue, a small head, long face, broad shoulders and back, a straight body, small hands and feet, a talent for cleverness, a restless mind, weak strength, and often worries over affairs. They thrive in spring and summer but falter in autumn and winter; when affected by external influences, illness arises, and their feet exhibit *juéyīn* [Liver] *qi* in a languid, *tuōtuō* [flaccid] manner.

The Great *Jiǎo* person aligns with the left foot’s *shǎoyáng* [Gallbladder], where *shǎoyáng* *qi* lingers above in a *yíyí* [stunted] state. The Left *Jiǎo* person aligns with the right foot’s *shǎoyáng*, where *shǎoyáng* *qi* descends in a *suísuī* [sluggish] manner. The Minor *Jiǎo* or Reverent *Jiǎo* person aligns with the right foot’s *shǎoyáng*, where *shǎoyáng* *qi* ascends in a *tuītuī* [pushing] manner—this is called the Right *Jiǎo*. The Divided *Jiǎo* person aligns with the left foot’s *shǎoyáng*, where *shǎoyáng* *qi* descends in a *kuòkū* [withered] state.

The Fire-formed person resembles the upper *zhēng* [surging], akin to the Red Sovereign [赤帝]. Such a person has a ruddy complexion, a wide forehead, a sunken face with a small head, a preference for bony shoulders and a concave abdomen, small hands and feet, steady movement on the ground, a restless mind, unbalanced gait, shoulders and back filled with flesh, light *qi* but stingy with wealth, little trust but much deliberation, clear-sighted in affairs, fond of beauty, impulsive in thought, and short-lived, often dying suddenly. They thrive in spring and summer but falter in autumn and winter; when affected by autumn-winter influences, illness arises, and their hands exhibit *shǎoyīn* [Kidney] *qi* in a *héhé* [tight] manner.

The Strong *Zhēng* person aligns with the left hand’s *tàiyáng* [Small Intestine], where *tàiyáng* *qi* surges above in a *jījī* [muscular] manner—this is also called the Strong person, or the Great *Zhēng*. The Minor *Zhēng* person aligns with the right hand’s *tàiyáng*, where *tàiyáng* *qi* descends in a *nǎoqià* [irritated] state. The Right *Zhēng* person aligns with the right hand’s *tàiyáng*, where *tàiyáng* *qi* ascends in a *jiāojiāo* [glowing] manner—this is also called the Bear *Zhēng*. The Divided *Zhēng* person aligns with the left hand’s *tàiyáng*, where *tàiyáng* *qi* descends in a *zhīzhī yíyí* [supporting] manner—this is also called the Strong *Zhēng*.

The Earth-formed person resembles the upper *gōng* [palace], akin to the ancient Yellow Sovereign [黄帝]. Such a person has a yellow hue, a round face, large head, fine shoulders and back, a large abdomen, smooth thighs, small hands and feet, abundant flesh, a balanced upper and lower body, steady movement on the ground, and when lifting their feet, they appear light and floating. Their mind is calm, they favor benefiting others, dislike authority and power, and are skilled at aligning with others. They thrive in autumn and winter but falter in spring and summer; when affected by spring-summer influences, illness arises, and their feet exhibit *tàiyīn* [Spleen] *qi* in a *dūndūn* [dense] manner.

The Great *Gōng* person aligns with the left foot’s *yángmíng* [Stomach], where *yángmíng* *qi* ascends in a *wǎnwǎn* [gentle] manner. The Added *Gōng* person aligns with the left foot’s *yángmíng*, where *yángmíng* *qi* descends in a *kǎnkǎn* [hollow] manner—this is also called the Common person. The Minor *Gōng* person aligns with the right foot’s *yángmíng*, where *yángmíng* *qi* ascends in a *shūshū* [rigid] manner. The Left *Gōng* person aligns with the right foot’s *yángmíng*, where *yángmíng* *qi* descends in a *wùwù* [stiff] manner—this is also called the Common person, or the *Yángmíng* above.

The Metal-formed person resembles the upper *shāng* [commerce], akin to the White Sovereign [白帝].
