Such a person has a square face, white complexion, small head, small shoulders and back, small abdomen, small hands and feet, and their bones extend outward like protruding heels. Their bones are light, their body clean and upright, their mind quick but prone to suddenness, and they are adept at governance. They thrive in autumn and winter but falter in spring and summer; when affected by spring-summer influences, illness arises, and their hands exhibit *tàiyīn* [Spleen] *qi* in a *dūndūn* [dense] manner.

The Reverent *Shāng* person aligns with the left hand’s *yángmíng* [Stomach], where *yángmíng* *qi* ascends in a *liánlián* [pure] manner. The Right *Shāng* person aligns with the left hand’s *yángmíng*, where *yángmíng* *qi* descends in a *tuōtuō* [flaccid] manner. The Great *Shāng* person aligns with the right hand’s *yángmíng*, where *yángmíng* *qi* ascends in a *jiānjiān* [vigorous] manner. The Small *Shāng* person aligns with the right hand’s *yángmíng*, where *yángmíng* *qi* descends in a *yán yán* [strict] manner.

The Water-formed person resembles the upper *yǔ* [feather], akin to the Black Sovereign [黑帝]. Such a person is black in hue, with an uneven face, large head, sharp chin, small shoulders, large abdomen, and restless hands and feet. Their movements are unsteady, their body sways when walking, their lower limbs are long, their back appears stretched and *yǎn yǎn* [prolonged], and they lack reverence, often deceiving and exploiting others, and dying violently. They thrive in autumn and winter but falter in spring and summer; when affected by spring-summer influences, illness arises, and their feet exhibit *shǎoyīn* [Kidney] *qi* in a *hànhàn* [perspiring] manner.

The Great *Yǔ* person aligns with the right foot’s *tàiyáng* [Small Intestine], where *tàiyáng* *qi* ascends in a *jiájiá* [cheeky] manner. The Small *Yǔ* person aligns with the left foot’s *tàiyáng*, where *tàiyáng* *qi* descends in a *yūyū* [twisted] manner. The Common person aligns with the right foot’s *tàiyáng*, where *tàiyáng* *qi* descends in a *jiéjié* [clean] manner—this is also called the Added person. The Extreme person aligns with the left foot’s *tàiyáng*, where *tàiyáng* *qi* ascends in an *ānjiān* [gentle] manner.

Thus, the five forms of the twenty-five variations are the reasons why the common multitude deceives one another.

The Yellow Emperor said: *"If one discerns their form but not their color, how does it manifest?"*

Qíbù replied: *"Form overcomes color, and color overcomes form. When one’s nature prevails, their years increase; when affected by external influences, illness follows its course. When their nature is lost, sorrow ensues."*

「When form and color mutually reinforce one another, wealth, honor, and great joy ensue.

The Yellow Emperor said: *"When their form and color mutually overcome each other, can one know the increase in years?"*

Qíbù replied: *"Generally, those whose years are marked by the **yīxià** [upward/downward] imbalance are greatly afflicted. Their lifespan is commonly reduced by seven years at each of the following ages: sixteen, twenty-five, thirty-four, forty-three, fifty-two, and sixty-one—these are the great **niè** [taboos] of human life, and one must not fail to cultivate self-peace. If affected by external influences, illness follows its course; if their nature is lost, sorrow ensues. At these critical times, one should avoid engaging in deceitful or harmful acts—this is what is called **niè** [taboo years].

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Master, your words speak of the **pulse**’s ascent and descent, the **qi** and **blood**’s cycles—how does one discern the **Way** of **form** and **qi**?"*

Qíbù replied: *"Above the **足阳明** [Foot Yangming] meridian, if **blood** and **qi** are abundant, the **上颊** [upper lip] hair is luxuriant and long. If **blood** is scant and **qi** is excessive, the upper lip hair is short. Thus, if **qi** is scant and **blood** is abundant, the upper lip hair is sparse. If both **blood** and **qi** are scant, there is no upper lip hair, and the **两吻** [lips] appear thin and wrinkled.

Below the **足阳明** [Foot Yangming] meridian, if **blood** and **qi** are abundant, the **下毛** [lower body hair] is luxuriant and long, extending to the chest. If **blood** is abundant and **qi** is scant, the lower body hair is short, reaching only to the navel, and when walking, one tends to lift the feet high, with thin flesh on the toes and a propensity for coldness in the feet. If **blood** is scant and **qi** is abundant, the flesh becomes fleshy yet prone to flaccidity. If both **blood** and **qi** are scant, there is no hair, or if present, it is sparse, withered, and dull, leading to frequent **痿厥** [flaccid paralysis] and **足痹** [foot numbness].

Above the **足少阳** [Foot Shaoyang] meridian, if **qi** and **blood** are abundant, the **通髯** [sideburns] are luxuriant and long. If **blood** is abundant and **qi** is scant, the sideburns are short. If **blood** is scant and **qi** is abundant, the sideburns are few. If both **blood** and **qi** are scant, there is no **须** [beard], and exposure to cold and dampness causes frequent **痹** [numbness], **骨痛** [bone pain], and **爪枯** [withered nails].

Below the **足少阳** [Foot Shaoyang] meridian, if **qi** and **blood** are abundant, the **腔毛** [hair in the calf] is luxuriant and long, and the **外踝** [outer ankle] is fleshy. If **blood** is abundant and **qi** is scant, the **经毛** [hair along the meridian] is short, and the outer ankle skin is firm and thick. If **blood** is scant and **qi** is abundant, the **□毛** [hair] is sparse, and the outer ankle skin is thin and **歃** [dry]. If both **blood** and **qi** are scant, there is no hair, and the outer ankle is emaciated with no flesh.

Above the **足太阳** [Foot Taiyang] meridian, if **blood** and **qi** are abundant, the **眉** [eyebrows] are beautiful, with fine **毫毛** [downy hair]. If **blood** and **qi** are scant, the eyebrows are coarse, and the face has few **理** [pores]. If **blood** is scant and **qi** is abundant, the face becomes fleshy. If **blood** and **qi** are harmonious, the complexion is beautiful.

Below the **足太阴** [Foot Taiyin] meridian, if **blood** and **qi** are abundant, the **跟肉** [heel flesh] is full, and the **踵** [heel] is firm. If **qi** is scant and **blood** is abundant, the heel is thin, with empty flesh. If both **blood** and **qi** are scant, one is prone to **转筋** [muscle cramps], and **踵下痛** [heel pain] occurs.

Above the **手阳明** [Hand Yangming] meridian, if **blood** and **qi** are abundant, the **髭** [mustache] is beautiful. If **blood** is scant and **qi** is abundant, the mustache is coarse. If both **blood** and **qi** are scant, there is no mustache.

Below the **手阳明** [Hand Yangming] meridian, if **blood** and **qi** are abundant, the **腋下毛** [armpit hair] is beautiful, and the **手鱼肉** [palm flesh] is warm. If both **qi** and **blood** are scant, the hands are thin and cold.

Above the **手少阳** [Hand Shaoyang] meridian, if **blood** and **qi** are abundant, the **眉** [eyebrows] are beautiful and long, and the **耳色** [ear color] is fine. If both **blood** and **qi** are scant, the ears become **焦** [dry], and their color is poor.

Below the **手少阳** [Hand Shaoyang] meridian, if **blood** and **qi** are abundant, the **手卷** [hand’s lateral flesh] is fleshy and warm. If both **blood** and **qi** are scant, the hands are cold and emaciated. If **qi** is scant and **blood** is abundant, the hands are thin but with many **脉** [veins].

Above the **手太阳** [Hand Taiyang] meridian, if **blood** and **qi** are abundant, there is much **须** [beard], and the face is fleshy yet flat. If both **blood** and **qi** are scant, the face is emaciated and of poor color.

Below the **手太阳** [Hand Taiyang] meridian, if **blood** and **qi** are abundant, the **掌肉** [palm flesh] is full. If both **blood** and **qi** are scant, the palm is thin and cold.

The Yellow Emperor said: *"For the twenty-five types of people, are there specific guidelines for acupuncture?"*

Qíbù replied: *"Those with beautiful eyebrows have abundant **blood** and **qi** in the **Foot Taiyang** meridian. Those with coarse eyebrows have scant **blood** and **qi**. If the skin is fleshy and lustrous, **blood** and **qi** are in excess. If the skin is fleshy but not lustrous, **qi** is in excess but **blood** is insufficient. If the skin is thin and lacking luster, both **qi** and **blood** are insufficient. By carefully examining these signs of **form** and **qi**—whether in excess or deficiency—one can regulate them and thus discern the **inverse** and **顺** [proper] courses of illness.

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How should one apply acupuncture to these various **yin-yang** meridians?"*

Qíbù replied: *"One should press the **寸口** [radial pulse] and **人迎** [carotid pulse] to regulate **yin** and **yang**. When examining the **经络** [meridians and collaterals], if they are **凝涩** [congealed and stiff], with a **结络** [blocked network] that does not flow freely, this indicates **痛痹** [pain and numbness] throughout the body, and in severe cases, the inability to walk. Hence, one must address **凝涩** [congealed stiffness].

For **凝涩** [congealed stiffness], one should direct **qi** to warm it, and when **blood** harmonizes, the condition will cease. For **结络** [blocked networks], the **pulse** is knotted and **blood** is not harmonious; one must **庾** [unblock] it, and then it will flow. Therefore, it is said: If **qi** is in excess above, one should guide it downward; if **qi** is deficient above, one should push it upward and allow it to rest. If **qi** lingers and does not reach its destination, one should follow and welcome it, ensuring clarity of the **经隧** [meridian pathways] before proceeding. When cold and heat contend, one should guide it to resolve the conflict. If **qi** is **宛陈** [wandering] and **blood** does not clot, one should **则而予之** [restrain and then release it].

One must first clearly understand the twenty-five types of people, then the locations of **blood** and **qi**—whether above, below, left, or right—will be known, and the acupuncture guidelines will be complete.

【Phonetic Notes】 *Di* as in *dì* [地, earth]. *Niè* as in *niè* [捏, pinch].」

「**Jiao** [1] as in *jiāo* [交, union]. **Fu** [2] as in *háng* [腑, zang-fu organ, specifically the abdominal viscera]. □ *Zhǐ yù qiè* [3]. #1 *The Yellow Emperor’s Inner Canon: Miraculous Pivot, Annotated Collection, Volume Eighteen* concludes.

#1 *Dui* [4] as *duì* [兑, Receptive (one of the Five Phases)]. #2 *Fu* [5] originally as "right" (*yòu*), corrected according to the *Zhao Fu Ju Jing Tang* edition to "abdominal viscera" (*fǔ*). #3 *Fu* [6] as *shǎo* [少, lesser]. #5 *Fu* [7] as *shǎo* [少, lesser]. #6 *Yin* [8] likely should be "yang" (*yáng*).

*The Yellow Emperor’s Inner Canon: Miraculous Pivot, Annotated Collection, Volume Nineteen*
**The Five Sounds and Five Flavors** (Chapter Sixty-Five)

Right **Zheng** [9] and lesser **Zheng**, adjust the **yang** [10] **meridian** of the right hand.
Left **Shang** [11] and left **Zheng**, adjust the **yangming** [12] **meridian** of the left hand.
Lesser **Zheng** and greater **Gong** [13], adjust the **yangming** **meridian** of the left hand.
Right **Jiao** [14] and greater **Jiao**, adjust the **shao-yang** [15] **meridian** of the right foot.
Greater **Zheng** and lesser **Zheng**, adjust the **yang** **meridian** of the left hand.
**Zhongyu** [16] and lesser **yu** [17], adjust the **yang** **meridian** of the right foot.
Lesser **Shang** and right **Shang**, adjust the **yang** **meridian** of the right hand.
**Shuan** [18] **yu** and **zhongyu**, adjust the **yang** **meridian** of the right foot.
Lesser **Gong** and greater **Gong**, adjust the **yangming** **meridian** of the right foot.
**Pan** [19] **jiao** and lesser **jiao**, adjust the **shao-yang** **meridian** of the right foot.
**Tie** [20] **Shang** and upper **Shang**, adjust the **yangming** **meridian** of the right foot.
**Tie** **Shang** and upper **jiao**, adjust the **yang** **meridian** of the left foot.
Upper **Zheng** and right **Zheng** are the same; **grain** [21] barley, **livestock** [22] sheep, **fruit** [23] apricot, **hand meridian** [24] **shao-yang**; **stores** [25] the heart, **color** [26] red, **flavor** [27] bitter, **season** [28] summer.
Upper **yu** and greater **yu** are the same; **grain** soybeans, **livestock** [29] **pigs**, **fruit** chestnuts, **foot meridian** [30] **shao-yin** [31], **stores** the kidneys, **color** black, **flavor** salty, **season** winter.
Upper **Gong** and greater **Gong** are the same; **grain** millet, **livestock** cattle, **fruit** dates, **foot meridian** **tai-yin** [32], **stores** the spleen, **color** yellow, **flavor** sweet, **season** late summer.
Upper **Shang** and right **Shang** are the same; **grain** sorghum, **livestock** parrots [33], **fruit** peaches, **hand meridian** **tai-yin**, **stores** the lungs, **color** white, **flavor** spicy, **season** autumn.
Upper **jiao** and greater **jiao** are the same; **grain** hemp, **livestock** dogs, **fruit** plums, **foot meridian** **jue-yin** [34], **stores** the liver, **color** green, **flavor** sour, **season** spring.
Greater **Gong** and upper **jiao** are the same; adjust the **yangming** **meridian** of the right foot.

Left **jiao** and greater **jiao** are the same; adjust the **yangming** **meridian** of the left foot.
Lesser **yu** and greater **yu** are the same; adjust the **yang** **meridian** of the right foot.
Left **Shang** and right **Shang** are the same; adjust the **yangming** **meridian** of the left hand.
**Jia** [35] **Gong** and greater **Guan** [36] are the same; adjust the **shao-yang** **meridian** of the left foot.
**Zhi** [37] **Pan** and greater **Gong** are the same; adjust the **yang** **meridian** of the left hand.
**Pan** **jiao** and greater **jiao** are the same; adjust the **shao-yang** **meridian** of the left foot.
Greater **yu** and greater **jiao** are the same; adjust the **yang** **meridian** of the right foot.
Greater **jiao** and greater **Gong** are the same; adjust the **shao-yang** **meridian** of the right foot.

Right **Zheng**, lesser **Zheng**, **zhi Zheng** [38], upper **Zheng**, and **Pan Zheng**.
Right **jiao**, **tie jiao** [39], upper **jiao**, greater **jiao**, and **Pan jiao**.
Right **Shang**, lesser **Shang**, **tie Shang**, upper **Shang**, and left **Shang**.
Lesser **Gong**, upper **Gong**, greater **Gong**, **jia Gong**, and left **jiao**/**Gong**.
**Zhongyu**, **shuan yu**, upper **yu**, greater **yu**, and lesser **yu**.

The Yellow Emperor asked: If women have no beard, does that mean they lack **qi** and **blood**?

Qibo replied: The **chong** [40] and **ren** [41] **meridians** both originate from the **bai** [42] (uterus), ascending along the spine as the **sea of the meridians** [43]. Their pathways that float outward traverse the right side of the abdomen, converge at the throat, and branch to connect with the lips and mouth. When **qi** and **blood** are abundant, they nourish the skin and muscles, producing warmth. If **blood** alone is abundant, it **dànmián** [44] (permeates and moistens) the skin, generating fine hair. Now, in women, **qi** is in excess, while **blood** is deficient—due to their frequent menstrual blood loss—so the **chong** and **ren** **meridians** do not nourish the lips and mouth, and thus no beard grows.

The Yellow Emperor asked: If a scholar suffers injury to his **yin** [45], causing his **yin qi** [46] to be severed and unable to rise again, and his **yin** is not utilized, yet his beard does not fall out, what is the reason for this? Why does an **eunuch** [47] alone lose his beard? I wish to hear the explanation.

Qibo replied: An **eunuch** has his **zongjin** [48] (main tendons) removed, injuring the **chong meridian**. His **blood** then **xiè** [49] (disperses) and does not return, while the skin and flesh internally solidify. The lips and mouth are not nourished, so his beard does not grow.

The Yellow Emperor asked: And if there are those born as **eunuchs** (*tianyan* [50]), who have never been injured, do not suffer blood loss, yet still lack a beard—what is the reason for this?

Qibo replied: This is where Heaven is deficient. Their **ren** and **chong** **meridians** are not abundant, their **zongjin** tendons do not fully form, and though they possess **qi**, they lack **blood**—so the lips and mouth are not nourished, and thus no beard grows.

The Yellow Emperor said: Excellent! The **sage**’s understanding of all things is like the radiance of the sun and moon, their resonant tones echoing like the **sound of drums** [51]. Hearing their voice reveals their form. No one but you could clarify the essence of all things.

Thus, the **sage** observes their complexion: those with **yellow-red** tones have abundant **yang qi** [52], those with **green-white** tones have lesser **yang qi**, and those with **black** tones have abundant **blood** and lesser **qi**.

Those with **beautiful eyebrows** have the **yang** **meridian** of the **tai-yang** [53] hand abundant in **blood**, those with **beards extending to the chin** have the **shao-yang** hand abundant in **blood**, and those with **beautiful beards** have the **yangming** hand abundant in **blood**—this is their natural disposition.

In humans, the **constant numbers** of the **meridians** are:
The **tai-yang** [54] hand **meridian** is always abundant in **blood** and deficient in **qi**,
The **shao-yang** hand **meridian** is always abundant in **qi** and deficient in **blood**,
The **yangming** hand **meridian** is always abundant in both **qi** and **blood**,
The **jue-yin** [55] foot **meridian** is always abundant in **qi** and deficient in **blood**,
The **shao-yin** foot **meridian** is always abundant in **blood** and deficient in **qi**,
The **tai-yin** foot **meridian** is always abundant in **blood** and deficient in **qi**—
this is Heaven’s **constant numbers**.

**The Origin of All Diseases** (Chapter Sixty-Six)

The Yellow Emperor asked Qibo: The origin of all a hundred diseases, how do they arise? They all stem from **wind**, **rain**, **cold**, **heat**, **dampness**, **joy**, and **anger**.

Excessive **joy** and **anger** without moderation harm the **zang** [56] (solid organs),
**Wind** and **rain** harm the upper body,
**Dampness** harms the lower body.
The three divisions of **qi** [57] are affected differently. I wish to hear their convergence.

Qibo replied: The three divisions of **qi** each differ. Some originate from **yin**, some from **yang**. Let me explain their patterns.

**The Origin of All Diseases** (Chapter Sixty-Six)

**Excessive Joy and Anger** (Chapter Sixty-Seven)

When **joy** and **anger** are unmoderated, they harm the **zang** (solid organs),
and when the **zang** are injured, diseases arise from **yin**.

When **dampness** and **cold** invade the **void** [58], diseases arise in the lower body;
when **wind** and **rain** invade the **void**, diseases arise in the upper body.
This is called the **three divisions**.

As for their **profligate excess** [59], it is countless.

The Yellow Emperor said: I have long been unable to enumerate them,
so I question the **Master** [60] and wish to fully hear the **Dao**.

Qibo replied: **Wind**, **rain**, **cold**, and **heat**,
unless they meet the **void**, cannot harm alone.
If one suddenly encounters a violent **wind** and **rain** yet does not fall ill,
it is because there is no **void**—
thus, the **evil** cannot harm alone.

This must be due to the **void-evil wind** [61] meeting the body’s form,
where **two voids** converge and the **evil** lodges in the form;
or when **two reals** meet, ordinary people’s flesh is firm.
Within the body, when the **void-evil** meets the **void** of the form,
combined with the **real** and **void** of the **three divisions**,
great diseases form.

The **three divisions of qi** have fixed lodgings,
and diseases take their names according to where they settle—
upper, lower, internal, and external—
divided into **three categories**.

Thus, when **void-evil** invades a person,
it begins at the **skin**.
If the **skin** is relaxed, the **pores** open,
and when the **pores** open, **evil** enters through the **hair follicles**.
Once it enters, it probes deep—
if it reaches the **hair follicles**, they stand erect,
and when the **hair follicles** stand erect, there is a **shivering sensation** [62].
Hence, the **skin** aches.

If it lingers and does not leave,
it then lodges in the **network vessels** [63].
When it settles in the **network vessels**, the pain is in the **muscles**,
and when the pain is in the **muscles**, it subsides [64].
Then the **major meridians** take their turn.

If it lingers and does not leave,
it lodges in the **meridians**.
When it settles in the **meridians**, there is a **shivering sensation** [65] and **fear of wind** [66].

If it lingers and does not leave,
it lodges in the **junctions** [67].
When it settles in the **junctions**, the **six major meridians** are blocked,
and the **four limbs** and **joints** become **stiff and contracted** [68],
**back pain** and **muscle rigidity** ensue.

If it lingers and does not leave,
it lodges in the **hidden and rushing pulses** [69].
When it settles in the **hidden and rushing pulses**, the **body feels heavy** and **aches**.

If it lingers and does not leave,
it lodges in the **intestines and stomach**.
When it settles in the **intestines and stomach**, there is **abdominal distension** [70].
If there is excess **cold**, the **intestines rumble**, there is **diarrhea with undigested food** [71], and **food does not digest**.
If there is excess **heat**, **perspiration** and **pus-like discharge** [72] occur.

If it lingers and does not leave,
it lodges between the **intestines and stomach** and their **confluences and sources** [73],
settling in the **pulses**.
When it lingers and does not leave,
the pain subsides but **accumulations** [74] form.

Sometimes it settles in the **branch vessels** [75],
sometimes in the **network vessels**,
sometimes in the **meridians**,
sometimes in the **junctions**,
sometimes in the **hidden and rushing pulses**,
sometimes in the **muscles and tendons**,
or in the **confluences and sources** of the **intestines and stomach**.
It may also connect externally to the **relaxed sinews** [76].
The **evil qi** is **profligate and excessive** [77],
and its variations are endless.

The Yellow Emperor said: I wish to hear fully how this occurs.

Qibo replied: When **evil** settles in the **branch and network vessels** and forms **accumulations**,
the **accumulations** move **up and down**,
lodging in the **branch and network vessels** of the **arms and hands**.
They are **floating and relaxed**,
and since they cannot **fix the accumulation** and stop it,
they **wander and shift**.

Between the **intestines and stomach**, **watery fluids** [78] **seep and trickle**,
with a **splashing sound** [79].
If there is **cold**, the **abdominal region feels taut and full**, with **rumbling and distension** [80].
Hence, the pain is **intermittent**.

When it settles in the **yangming** **meridian**,
it lodges beside the **umbilicus**,
growing larger with **fullness** and smaller with **hunger**.

When it settles in the **relaxed sinews** [81],
it resembles the **accumulation** of the **yangming** **meridian**—
**fullness** brings pain, while **hunger** brings ease.

When it settles in the **confluences and sources** of the **intestines and stomach**,
the pain connects externally to the **relaxed sinews**,
and **fullness** brings ease, while **hunger** brings pain.

When it settles in the **hidden and rushing pulses**,
pressing it yields a **response in the hand** [82].
When it erupts, **heat qi** descends to the **two thighs**,
like the sensation of **boiling water being poured**.

When it settles in the **muscles and tendons** behind the **intestines**,
**accumulations** appear only when **hungry**,
and disappear when **full**—
pressing them yields no sensation.

When it settles in the **junction pulses**,
they are **blocked and uncommunicating**,
and **percussion does not descend** [83].
The **pores and orifices** become **dry and congested** [84].
This is when **evil qi** enters from the **exterior** and moves **internally**, **up and down**.

The Yellow Emperor said: How do **accumulations** begin,
and how do they develop into their full form?

Qibo replied: **Accumulations** begin when **cold** is encountered,
and **ulceration** [85] leads to their formation.

The Yellow Emperor said: How do they form **accumulations**?

Qibo replied: **Ulceration qi** arises in the **foot sole** [86],
and the **foot sole** gives rise to **meridian cold** [87].
When the **meridian cold** sets in, the **blood vessels congeal and stiffen** [88],
and when the **blood vessels congeal and stiffen**, **cold qi** ascends and enters the **intestines and stomach**.
Once it enters the **intestines and stomach**, **abdominal distension** occurs,
and when the **abdominal distension** persists, the **fluids outside the intestines** are **pressed together** and cannot disperse,
day by day forming **accumulations**.

If one suddenly overeats or overdrinks,
the **intestines become full**.
If one’s **daily routines** are unmoderated,
or if **exertion** is excessive—
as when one **engages in sexual activity**, **sweats profusely**, or **bathes**—
then the **network vessels** are injured.
If the **yang network vessels** are injured, **blood pools externally** [89],
and when **blood pools externally**, **fecal blood** [90] appears.
If the **yin network vessels** are injured, **blood overflows internally** [91],
and when **blood overflows internally**, **rectal bleeding** [92] occurs.
When the **network vessels** of the **intestines and stomach** are injured,
**blood pools externally in the intestines**.
If there is **cold** outside the intestines,
the **fluids** and **blood** clash,
and when they clash, they **congeal and gather**,
unable to disperse—
thus, **accumulations** form.

If one suddenly contracts **external cold**,
or if **internal injury** comes from **grief and anger**,
then **qi ascends in reverse** [93].
When **qi ascends in reverse**, the **six junctions** are blocked,
**warm qi** does not flow,
**congealed blood** [94] **lodges in the orifices** and does not disperse,
and the **body fluids** become **sticky and seep slowly** [95].
When they settle and do not leave,
**accumulations** form.

The Yellow Emperor said: How do diseases arise from **yin**?

Qibo replied: **Grief and worry** injure the **heart**;
**excessive cold** injures the **lungs**;
**rage and anger** injure the **liver**;
**drunken intercourse** [96], or **sweating in the wind**, injures the **spleen**;
**excessive exertion**—
as when one **engages in sexual activity**, **sweats profusely**, or **bathes**—
injures the **kidneys**.

These are the diseases generated by the **internal, external, and three divisions**.
The Yellow Emperor said: **Well taught.**

How should one treat it? Qibo replied: Observe where it hurts to discern its **resonance** [97]. If there is **excess or deficiency**, supplement when necessary, or **drain** when necessary, and do not act contrary to the **natural rhythms of yin-yang** [98]. This is called **optimal treatment**.

---

The Yellow Emperor asked Qibo: *"I have heard of the **Nine Needles** from Master [99], and I have applied them among the common people. Yet the **blood and qi** of the populace vary in constitution—some have their **spirit** move first and their **qi** follow the needle; some have their **qi** meet the needle; some have the needle release their **qi** alone; some require repeated pricks before they know; some have their **qi** reverse upon needle insertion; and some grow worse with repeated pricks. All these six cases differ in form. I wish to hear their methods."*

Qibo said: *"Those with **excessive yang** are easily **spirit-moved** [100], and their **qi** readily disperses."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What do you mean by **excessive yang**?"*

Qibo replied: *"Those with **excessive yang** are tall and slender, their speech quick, and they move their feet with ease. Their **heart and lung** stores have **surplus qi**, and their **yang qi** is **slippery and abundant**, rising upward. Thus, their **spirit moves first**, and their **qi follows**."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"If one with **excessive yang** does not have their **spirit move first**, what is the reason?"*

Qibo replied: *"This person has **some yin** within them."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"How do you know they have **some yin**?"*

Qibo replied: *"Those with much **yang** tend to **excessive joy**; those with much **yin** tend to **excessive anger**. Those who often **anger** find it easier to disperse, so we say **some yin**. Their **yin-yang separation and union** [101] are difficult, hence their **spirit cannot move first**."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What about when the **qi meets the needle**?"*

Qibo replied: *"When **yin and yang are harmonized**, and the **blood and qi are pure, moist, slippery, and smooth**, the needle enters while the **qi exits**, swiftly meeting it."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"When the needle has already exited and the **qi moves alone**, what **qi** causes this?"*

Qibo replied: *"Their **yin qi is abundant** while their **yang qi is scant**; their **yin qi sinks** while their **yang qi floats**. The **sinking** [102] **qi** is **internally stored**, so when the needle has already exited, the **qi follows afterward**, hence it moves alone."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"When repeated pricks are needed before one knows, what **qi** causes this?"*

Qibo replied: *"This person has **much yin and little yang**; their **qi sinks** and disperses with difficulty, so repeated pricks are needed before they know."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"When the **qi reverses upon needle insertion** or when repeated pricks worsen the disease, what **qi** causes this?"*

Qibo replied: *"These cases are not due to **yin-yang qi** or the **nature of floating and sinking** [103]. They are all failures of **coarse technique** and **mistakes of the practitioner**. Their **constitution and qi** are beyond remedy."*

---

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What **qi** causes **upper diaphragm obstruction** [104]? I already know that **food and drink enter but return out**. As for **parasites causing lower diaphragm obstruction** [105], when they appear during eating, I have not yet grasped their meaning. I wish to hear it fully."*

Qibo replied: *"When **joy and anger are improper**, **diet is unregulated**, and **cold and warmth are untimed**, then **cold dampness** flows into the intestines. When cold dampness flows into the intestines, **parasites grow cold**; cold parasites **cluster and accumulate**, lodging in the **lower diaphragm**. Then the **intestines and stomach become distended**, the **defensive qi** [106] fails to circulate, and **pathogenic qi** takes residence. When one eats, the parasites rise to consume food, so the **lower diaphragm becomes empty**. An empty lower diaphragm allows **pathogenic qi** to prevail, causing the **accumulations to linger**. Lingering accumulations form **lower diaphragm obstruction**, which tightens the **lower diaphragm**. If the obstruction is **internal**, it **hurts immediately upon probing**; if it is **external**, then the obstruction is **outside** while the pain **floats**. The skin above the obstruction grows **hot**."*

---

**Yellow Emperor said:** *"How should one acupuncture this condition?"*
**Gebi replied:** *"Gently press upon the obstruction, observe where the qi flows. First, shallowly prick its sides, gradually penetrating deeper inward, then withdraw to prick again—do not exceed three pricks. Examine its sinking or floating to determine depth. After pricking, apply moxibustion, ensuring heat penetrates inward. Daily, let heat enter deeply, weakening pathogenic qi until the obstruction ruptures.

Use *wu* [107] and *shen* [108] to restrain, purging the interior. Calm and non-action will then allow qi to circulate. Later, apply bitter and salty flavors to transform and descend the grains."*

---

**Yellow Emperor asked the Minor Teacher:** *"When a person suddenly falls into grief and distress yet speaks without sound, what blocks the Way? What qi fails to flow [10], causing the voice to falter? I wish to hear the method."*

**Minor Teacher replied:** *"The throat is the passage for water and grains. The pharynx is where qi ascends and descends. The epiglottis is the door of sound. The lips are the bellows of sound. The tongue is the mechanism of sound. The uvula is the gate of sound. The soft palate is where divided qi disperses. The hyoid bone is where spirit-qi commands, governing tongue extension.

Thus, when a person’s nasal passages leak mucus uncontrollably, the soft palate fails to open, and divided qi is lost. If the epiglottis is small and thin, the released qi is swift, its opening and closing agile, and breath flows easily. But if the epiglottis is large and thick, opening and closing are difficult, breath flows slowly, and speech becomes labored.

When a person suddenly loses voice, cold qi invades the epiglottis, preventing its release. Though it attempts to descend, the opening and closing fail to align—thus, no sound."*

**Yellow Emperor said:** *"How should one acupuncture this?"*
**Gebi replied:** *"The Foot Minor Yin [109] channel ascends to the tongue, connects with the hyoid bone, and terminates at the epiglottis. Prick its collateral vessels bilaterally—this dispels turbid qi. The epiglottis’ channel ascends, connecting with the Conception Vessel; pricking at *Tiantu* [110] will make the epiglottis release."*

---
[107] *Wu* (伍): Likely refers to *wuwei* (non-action) or a specific medicinal herb/technique (context unclear).
[108] *Shen* (参): Possibly *shen* (ginseng) or a restraint method.
[109] Foot Minor Yin: *Kidney channel* (腎經).
[110] *Tiantu* (天突): "Celestial Protrusion," a key acupuncture point (CV22).

**Yellow Emperor asked Gebi:** *"When cold, heat, plague, and intermittent fevers lodge in the neck and armpits, what qi causes them?"*
**Gebi replied:** *"These are all the noxious qi of *shǔ* (鼠痪)—the cold and heat poisons that linger in the vessels and refuse to disperse."*

**Yellow Emperor said:** *"How may they be removed?"*
**Gebi replied:** *"The root of *shǔ* resides in the viscera, while its branches surface between the neck and armpits. If it floats within the vessels but has not yet penetrated deep into the muscles—only manifesting externally as pus and blood—it is easily expelled."*

**Yellow Emperor said:** *"How should it be expelled?"*
**Gebi replied:** *"Follow its root to draw out its branches; this will weaken and sever its cold and heat. Probe its path carefully and administer treatment gradually—advance and retreat slowly to remove it. If the lesion is as small as a grain of wheat, one prick suffices; three pricks will suffice."*

**Yellow Emperor said:** *"How does one determine life or death?"*
**Gebi replied:** *"Observe the eyes’ reflection: if red vessels run vertically through the pupil—
- one vessel means death in one year;
- one and a half vessels means death in a year and a half;
- two vessels means death in two years;
- two and a half vessels means death in two and a half years;
- three vessels means death in three years.
If the red vessels do not extend fully through the pupil, treatment is possible."*

---
**Yellow Emperor asked Bohao:** *"When pathogenic qi invades the body, sometimes causing the eyes to remain open without blinking or closing, what qi causes this?"*
**Bohao replied:** *"When the five grains enter the stomach, their dregs, fluids, and *zōngqì* (宗气) divide into three pathways. Thus, *zōngqì* accumulates in the chest, ascends through the throat, and courses through the heart’s vessels, governing respiration. The *yíng* (营) qi refines the fluids, infusing them into the vessels to transform into blood, which nourishes the extremities and irrigates the five viscera and six bowels, aligning with the body’s rhythmic cycles. The *wèi* (卫) qi, however, expels its fierce, brisk essence, first circulating through the extremities, between muscles and skin, without ceasing. By day, it travels through the yang; by night, through the yin, constantly passing through the Foot Minor Yin’s division, traversing the five viscera and six bowels. Now, if *jué* (厥) qi invades the five viscera and six bowels, the *wèi* qi alone defends the exterior, circulating through the yang without entering the yin. Its dominance in the yang intensifies yang’s vigor, causing yang’s *jiāo* (娇) to sink and fail to enter the yin, leaving voids. Thus, the eyes cannot close."*

**Yellow Emperor said:** *"Excellent. How should it be treated?"*

Bo Gao replied: *"Supplement what is deficient, drain what is excessive, harmonize the void and repletion, to unblock the Dao and expel the pathogenic forces. Administer a single dose of *Half-Summer Decoction* (半夏汤), and once yin and yang are restored, the symptoms will immediately abate."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Excellent. This is what is meant by resolving obstructions and stagnations, achieving great harmony in the channels and pathways, and balancing yin and yang. I wish to hear the prescription."*

Bo Gao said: *"For the decoction, take eight *sheng* of water drawn from a source beyond a thousand *li*, shake it ten thousand times, and take five *sheng* of its clear portion to boil. Use reed wood as fuel, and when the water boils, add one *sheng* of *lin* rice and five *ge* of prepared *half-summer* (半夏). Simmer slowly until it reduces to one and a half *sheng*, then strain out the dregs. Take one small cup of the liquid three times daily, gradually increasing until the condition improves. Thus, for newly arising cases, covering the cup will induce sweating from the exterior, and the illness will subside. For chronic cases, three doses will suffice."*

The Yellow Emperor asked Bo Gao: *"I wish to hear how the human limbs correspond to the heavens and earth."*

Bo Gao answered: *"The heavens are round, the earth is square, and thus the human head is round while the feet are square in correspondence. The heavens have the sun and moon, and the human has two eyes. The earth has nine regions, and the human has nine orifices. The heavens have wind and rain, and the human has joy and anger. The heavens have thunder and lightning, and the human has sound and voice. The heavens have four seasons, and the human has four limbs. The heavens have five tones, and the human has five viscera. The heavens have six pitches, and the human has six bowels. The heavens have winter and summer, and the human has cold and heat. The heavens have ten suns, and the human has ten fingers on each hand. The celestial stems are twelve, and the human has ten toes, a penis, and scrotum in correspondence—women lack these two nodes to embrace the human form. The heavens have yin and yang, and the human has husband and wife. A year has three hundred sixty-five days, and the human has three hundred sixty joints. The earth has high mountains, and the human has shoulders and knees. The earth has deep valleys, and the human has armpits and groin. The earth has twelve waterways, and the human has twelve primary channels. The earth has springs and veins, and the human has *wei* qi. The earth has grass and moss, and the human has fine hairs. The heavens have day and night, and the human has sleep and awakening. The heavens have constellations, and the human has teeth. The earth has small hills, and the human has small joints. The earth has mountains and rocks, and the human has high bones. The earth has forests and trees, and the human has sinews. The earth has clustered settlements, and the human has flesh. The year has twelve months, and the human has twelve major joints. The earth has four seasons where no grass grows, and the human has barrenness. This is how the human corresponds to the heavens and earth."*

The Yellow Emperor asked Qi Bo: *"I wish to hear the principles of needle insertion—how to grasp the needle, the logic of internal insertion, the intent behind extending or withdrawing, and the technique of pricking the skin to release stagnation. How does one determine where the pulse bends and folds, where it enters and exits, where it arrives and departs, where it moves slowly or swiftly, and where it penetrates? I also wish to fully understand the pathways by which the six bowels distribute themselves through the body—their sequential order, their points of separation, how they diverge to enter yin or yang. I wish to hear all of this in full."*

Qi Bo replied: *"Your Majesty’s questions encompass the entirety of the Dao of acupuncture."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear this fully."* Qi Bo replied:

*"The pulse of the Hand Taiyin [Lung] Meridian emerges at the tip of the thumb, curves inward, follows the junction of the white flesh, reaching *Taiyuan* [Great Abyss] behind the first joint. It pauses and diffuses, then curves outward, ascending below the joint before curving inward again to converge with the collateral vessels of the Yin hand at *Yuji* [Fish Border]. Multiple pulses flow together, their qi smooth and fluid, running concealed beneath the bones. Curving outward, it emerges at *Cunkou* [Inch Opening] and travels upward to the inner side of the elbow, entering beneath the great tendon. It curves inward, ascending along *Xieyin* [Armpit Shadow], enters the armpit, then curves inward to course through the lung. This is the sequential order of its forward and reverse bends.*

*The pulse of the Heart Governor [Pericardium] Meridian emerges at the tip of the middle finger, curves inward, ascending along the inner side of the middle finger to pause in the palm. It runs concealed between the two bones, then curves outward, emerging between the two tendons at the junction of bone and flesh. Its qi is smooth and fluid, ascending two inches outward before curving to run between the two tendons, reaching the inner side of the elbow. It enters beneath the small tendon, pausing at the convergence of the two bones, then ascends into the chest, internally connecting with the Heart’s vessels."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"The Hand Shaoyin [Heart] Meridian alone lacks a pulse—why is this?"*

Qi Bo replied: *"Shaoyin is the Heart’s meridian. The Heart is the sovereign of the five zang and six fu, the dwelling of spirit and mind. Its vessel is firm and unyielding—evil cannot penetrate it. If it did, the Heart would be injured, the spirit would depart, and death would follow. Thus, all evils that afflict the Heart lodge in its *baoguo* [envelope]. The *baoguo* is the pulse of the Heart Governor, hence its absence of a superficial pulse."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"If the Shaoyin lacks a pulse, does it not fall ill?"*

Qi Bo replied: *"Its external pathways may fall ill while the zang remains unharmed. Thus, one treats only its meridian at the sharp bone’s tip on the posterior wrist. The remaining pulses—their entry, exit, bends, and varying speeds—all follow the pattern of the Hand Shaoyin [Heart] Meridian. Therefore, the original pulses are treated according to the ebb and flow of their qi—whether deficient or replete, swift or slow—this is called *following the surge to drain* and *following decline to supplement*. Thus, evil qi departs, and true qi is fortified. This is called *conforming to Heaven’s order*."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one grasp the needle and adjust its insertion?"*

Qi Bo replied: *"One must first clearly know the origins and terminations of the twelve meridians, the cold and heat of the skin, and the ebb and flow of the pulses. If the pulse is smooth and robust, the illness advances daily. If it is empty and fine, it may endure for some time. If it is large yet rough, it signifies pain and numbness. If yin and yang pulses are equal, the illness is hard to treat. If the origin and termination remain warm, the illness persists. If the warmth declines, the illness departs.*

*Measure the cubit, examining the flesh’s firmness or fragility, its size, smoothness or roughness, cold or warmth, dryness or moisture. Observe the five colors of the eyes to discern the five zang and determine life or death. Examine the blood vessels, noting their hue, to know of cold, heat, pain, or numbness."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I still do not grasp the method of needle insertion and withdrawal."*

Qi Bo replied: *"The Dao of holding the needle requires steadiness and uprightness, calmness and stillness. One must first discern deficiency and repletion, then adjust speed accordingly. The left hand grasps the bone, while the right follows the meridian, ensuring no contact with the fruit of the bone. When draining, maintain uprightness and precision; when supplementing, close the skin and guide the qi with the needle. Thus, evil qi is expelled, and true qi takes residence."*

The Yellow Emperor asked Qi Bo: *"How does one prick the skin to release stagnation?"*

Qi Bo replied: *"Follow the divisions of the flesh. Separate the skin to the left, gently insert the needle, and adjust it slowly. The spirit remains undisturbed, and evil qi departs."*

The Yellow Emperor asked Qi Bo: *"There are eight hollows in the body—how does one diagnose them?"*

Qi Bo answered: *"By observing the five zang."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"How does one diagnose them?"*

Qi Bo replied: *"If the Lung or Heart harbors evil, its qi lingers at both elbows. If the Liver is afflicted, its qi flows to both armpits. If the Spleen is afflicted, its qi lingers at both sides of the waist. If the Kidneys are afflicted, its qi lingers at both cheeks. These eight hollows are the chambers of the joints, through which true qi passes and blood vessels wander. Evil qi and corrupt blood cannot dwell there—if they do, they injure tendons, vessels, bones, and joints, preventing the joints from bending and extending. Hence, the illness becomes *luan* [contracted]."*

The Yellow Emperor asked the Master of Fewer:

*"I have heard that there are yin and yang in people—what is meant by a yin-person? What is meant by a yang-person?"*

The Master of Fewer replied:
*"Between Heaven and Earth, within the six directions, nothing departs from the Five Phases. Humans correspond to them, not merely as one yin and one yang alone, but broadly speaking—words cannot exhaust their full meaning. The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear their essence briefly. Are there wise and virtuous people who can embody this fully?"*

The Master of Fewer answered:
*"There are those of Great Yin, Lesser Yin, Great Yang, Lesser Yang, and those of Harmonized Yin-Yang. These five types differ in demeanor, and their tendons, bones, qi, and blood are all unequal."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"How do they differ? May I hear?"*

The Master of Fewer replied:
*"The Great Yin-person is greedy yet unkind, his thoughts sink like murky water, he loves concealment but despises openness, his heart is harmonious yet unexpressed, he neglects the times, and acts only after others. This is the Great Yin-person.

The Lesser Yin-person is slightly greedy and deceitful, seeing others lose as if he gains, delighting in harm, and when others prosper, he grows resentful. His heart is cruel and without mercy—this is the Lesser Yin-person.

The Great Yang-person dwells in pomp, loves speaking grandly without substance, his ambitions spread far, his actions disregard right and wrong, and though his undertakings fail, he never regrets. This is the Great Yang-person.

The Lesser Yang-person holds himself in vain esteem, clings to petty rank, and though he seeks external favor, he lacks inner loyalty—this is the Lesser Yang-person.

The Harmonized Yin-Yang-person dwells quietly, without fear or excessive joy, yielding to circumstances, neither competing nor resisting, adapting with the times. Though honored, he remains modest, speaking without arrogance—this is the utmost balance.

Ancient masters of acupuncture and moxibustion observed these five dispositions before treating them: draining the excess, supplementing the deficient."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"How does one treat these five types?"*

The Master of Fewer replied:
*"The Great Yin-person has much yin and little yang; his yin-blood is turbid, his defensive qi sluggish, yin and yang unharmonized, his tendons slack and his skin thick. If not drained promptly, his condition cannot be shifted.

The Lesser Yin-person has much yin and little yang, a small stomach and large intestines, his six bowels unregulated. His Yangming pulse is small, while his Taoyang pulse is large—adjustment must be precise, for his blood is prone to collapse and his qi to decay.

The Great Yang-person has much yang and little yin—adjustment must be cautious, lest his yin be drained and his yang over-drained. If yang is excessively drained, he may fall into madness; if both yin and yang are drained, he dies suddenly without recognition.

The Lesser Yang-person has much yang and little yin, his channels small but his collaterals large, his blood concentrated internally while his qi disperses outward. Though his yin is solid, his yang is hollow—if only his collaterals are drained, he grows strong, but his qi collapses swiftly, his central qi insufficient, and the illness worsens.

The Harmonized Yin-Yang-person has balanced yin and yang, his blood and vessels regulated. Carefully diagnose his yin and yang, observe the presence or absence of evil, assess his demeanor and posture, determine excess or deficiency, drain the excess, supplement the deficient, and if neither is extreme, follow the channels. This is how one harmonizes yin and yang and distinguishes the five types."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"If these five types meet unexpectedly, how can one distinguish them?"*

The Master of Fewer answered:
*"Common people do not resemble these five types, so among twenty-five people, none may match them. The five types are especially distinct from the crowd."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"How does one distinguish the five types?"*

The Master of Fewer replied:
*"The Great Yin-person appears dark and murky, his thoughts sink heavily, his stature is tall and imposing, his cheeks sunken—this is the Great Yin-person.

The Lesser Yin-person appears sly and furtive, his yin-nature deceitful. He stands with agitation, walks hunched—this is the Lesser Yin-person.

The Great Yang-person appears haughty and arrogant, his body twisted, his cheeks sunken—this is the Great Yang-person.

The Lesser Yang-person stands with his head tilted back, walks swaying, his arms and elbows often jutting from his back—this is the Lesser Yang-person.

The Harmonized Yin-Yang-person appears composed and natural, clear and serene, cheerful and gentle, dignified and modest—common people call him a true gentleman. This is the Harmonized Yin-Yang-person."*

The pulse of the **Lesser Yin** is dark and straight—*ànzhí-rěn*[^1].
The **virtue/power** of the **Lesser Yin** is **sly and deceptive**—*sǒucí-lǜ*[^2].

---
**The *Huangdi Neijing* (Lingshu) – *Jizhu* (Collected Commentaries) – Volume 20: Pulse**
*(Annotations from variant texts:)*

1. **Lung** [*Jia-Yi Jing* Vol. 12, 3rd section substitutes "肺" for "脉"]
2. **Sinking** [*Jia-Yi Jing* Vol. 12, 3rd section substitutes "满" for "陷"]
3. **Spring Pulse** [*Original text lacks these two characters; supplemented from Zhao Fu Respectful Hall edition. The *Taisu* Vol. 5 renders this as "云气"]
4. **Its** [*Taisu* Vol. 9, *Pulse Circulation and Differences* substitutes "其" for "少"]
5. **Two inches above** [*Taisu* Vol. 9, *Pulse Circulation and Differences* substitutes "上行三寸"]
6. **Lesser Yin** [*Taisu* Vol. 9, *Pulse Circulation and Differences* substitutes "太阴"]
7. **Already** [*Jia-Yi Jing* Vol. 5, 7th section substitutes "已" for "以"]
8. **Inner fruit** [*Jia-Yi Jing* Vol. 5, 7th section substitutes "肉里" for "内果"]
9. **Disease** [*Zhao Fu Respectful Hall edition reads "疽" (carbuncle); phonetic annotation confirms "拘" (rigidity) is correct]
10. **Harmonize** [*Jia-Yi Jing* Vol. 1, 16th section substitutes "抑" for "和"]
11. **Appropriate** [*Jia-Yi Jing* Vol. 1, 16th section substitutes "宣" for "宜"]
12. **Qi** [*Jia-Yi Jing* Vol. 1, 16th section appends "在" after "气"]
13. **Comportment and demeanor** [*Jia-Yi Jing* Vol. 1, 16th section renders as "安其容仪"]
