**Huangdi Neijing, Ling Shu (Spiritual Pivot) – Collected Commentaries, Volume 23**
**Discourse on Aging and Exposure (Sui Lu, 79th Treatise)**

The Yellow Emperor asked Qibo:
*"The classics say that in summer, one is injured by heat, and in autumn, one falls ill with malaria. Malaria flares at fixed times—why is this so?"*

Qibo replied:
*"When pathogenic factors lodge in the Fengfu (Wind Palace), the disease descends along the scapular region. The defensive *qi* gathers there once daily, night and day. The next day, it descends one segment, so the illness manifests later. This is because the pathogen first lodges in the back, so when the defensive *qi* reaches Fengfu, the pores open, allowing the pathogen to enter. Once the pathogen enters, the illness flares—this is why it manifests later in the day.

The defensive *qi* descends one segment daily at Fengfu. After twenty-one days, it reaches the coccyx. On the twenty-second day, it enters the spinal canal, flowing into the *Fuchong* (Hidden Surge) pulse. It takes nine days to emerge at the *Quepen* (Notched Basin). As its *qi* ascends, the illness worsens. Internally, it assails the five *zàng* (solid organs), connecting horizontally to the *mu* (front-mu) and *yuan* (back-shamen) points. Its path is long, its *qi* weak, and its movement slow—it cannot flare daily. Thus, the illness accumulates and erupts the following day."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"When the defensive *qi* reaches Fengfu, the pores open, allowing the pathogen to enter. But if the defensive *qi* descends one segment daily, it should no longer align with Fengfu—how does the illness persist?"*

Qibo replied:
*"Fengfu is not fixed; wherever the defensive *qi* lodges, the pores open there. The segment where the *qi* resides becomes its palace."*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"Excellent."*

*"Wind and malaria share the same category, yet wind persists while malaria remits at set times—why?"*

Qibo replied:
*"Wind-*qi* lingers in its place, while malaria-*qi* follows the meridians, descending internally to assail the organs. Thus, the illness flares when the defensive *qi* arrives."*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"Excellent."*

The Yellow Emperor asked Laoshi:
*"I have heard that the four seasons and eight winds affect the body, causing cold or heat. Cold tightens the skin and closes the pores, while heat relaxes the skin and opens the pores—thus, pathogenic winds and *xiéqi* (harmful *qi*) can enter. Or must the eight principal winds and vacuous pathogens alone harm the body?"*

Laoshi replied:
*"Not so. Pathogenic winds and *xiéqi* can enter without waiting for set times, but they must exploit when the pores are open. If they enter deeply, the illness is extreme and sudden. If they enter when the pores are closed, their penetration is shallow, lingering slowly, and the illness progresses gradually."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"Even when the body is balanced with neither cold nor heat, the pores neither open nor close—yet sudden illness can still occur. Why?"*

Laoshi replied:
*"Does Your Majesty not understand how pathogens enter? Even in repose, the pores open and close, relax and tighten, at varying times."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"May I hear more?"*

Laoshi said:
*"Humans align with Heaven and Earth, resonating with the sun and moon. When the moon is full, the Mei (Plum) waters in the west flourish, and human blood and *qi* accumulate. The flesh and muscles are plump, the skin firm, the hair strong, the pores closed, and filth adheres. At such times, even if pathogenic winds strike, they penetrate shallowly.

When the moon wanes, the eastern seas surge, and human *qi* and blood are depleted. The defensive *qi* recedes, leaving the form exposed. The flesh and muscles atrophy, the skin slackens, the pores open, the hair withers, the *lù* (interstitial spaces) thin, and filth falls away. At such times, if pathogenic winds strike, they penetrate deeply, and the illness erupts violently."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"Why do some suddenly die or fall violently ill?"*

Laoshi replied:
*"Those with three vacuities perish swiftly; those with three repletions are unharmed by pathogens."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"What are the three vacuities?"*

Laoshi said:
*"To ride the decline of one’s age, encounter the moon’s waning, and lose the harmony of the seasons—then be struck by pathogenic winds—this is called the three vacuities. Thus, those who fail to grasp the three vacuities are crude physicians."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"What are the three repletions?"*

Laoshi said:
*"To meet the peak of one’s age, encounter the moon’s fullness, and secure the harmony of the seasons—even if pathogenic winds and *xiéqi* strike, they cannot harm."*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"Your discourse is profound! Your understanding of the Way is clear!"*

The Yellow Emperor said:
*"Let this be stored in the Golden Case, naming it *The Three Repletions*.
Yet this is but the view of one man."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"I wish to hear why all years share common diseases—what causes this?"*

Laoshi replied:
*"This is the observation of the Eight Trigrams."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"How does one observe them?"*

Laoshi said:
*"Observe this: On the Winter Solstice, the Grand One (*Taiyi*) stands in the *Yūdǐ* mansion. When it arrives, the heavens must respond with wind and rain.
If the wind and rain come from the south, it is the *xū* wind—a pathogenic harm to people.
If it arrives at midnight, the common folk stay indoors and avoid it, so that year, the people suffer only minor illnesses.
If it arrives by day, the people grow slack and all are struck by the *xū* wind, so the people fall ill in great numbers.
When the *xū* evil enters and lodges in the bones without manifesting externally, by the time of *Lìchūn* (Establishing Spring), the yang *qi* surges forth, the pores open, and on that very day, if the wind comes from the west, the people are again struck by the *xū* wind. This is when the two evils clash, binding and obstructing the channel *qi*.
Thus, those who encounter both the wind and the rain are said to have met the *suìlù*—the seasonal exposure.
If the year’s harmony prevails and the pathogenic winds are few, the people suffer little illness and few deaths.
But if the year has many pathogenic winds and *xiéqi*, with cold and warmth unbalanced, then the people suffer greatly in illness and death."*

The Yellow Emperor asked:
*"How does the *xū* evil wind affect nobles and commoners differently? How does one observe it?"*

Laoshi replied:
*"On the first day of the first month, if the Grand One resides in the *Tiānliú* mansion and the wind comes from the west-north, with no rain, many will die.
On the first day of the first month, if at dawn the wind comes from the north, in spring, many will die.
On the first day of the first month, if at dawn the wind blows from the north, many will fall ill—thirteen out of ten.
On the first day of the first month, if at noon the wind comes from the north, in summer, many will die.
On the first day of the first month, if at dusk the wind comes from the north, in autumn, many will die.
If the north wind blows all day, sixteen out of ten will fall gravely ill and die.
On the first day of the first month, if the wind comes from the south, it is called *zǎoxiàng*—an early omen; if from the west, it is called *báigǔ*—white bones, foretelling calamity for the state and many deaths.
On the first day of the first month, if the wind comes from the east, it lifts roofs and scatters sand and stones—great disaster for the state.
On the first day of the first month, if the wind comes from the southeast, there will be deaths in spring.
If, on the first day of the first month, the sky is warm without wind, the common folk are spared, and none fall ill; if cold with wind, the nobles suffer, and the people fall ill in great numbers.
This is called observing the year’s wind—how it harms people.

In the second month, if the *chǒu* day has no wind, many suffer heart and abdomen ailments;
In the third month, if the *xū* day is not warm, many suffer cold and fever;
In the fourth month, if the *sì* day is not hot, many suffer pestilential diseases;
In the tenth month, if the *shēn* day is not cold, many die suddenly.

All these winds—when they blow—lift roofs, snap trees, scatter sand and stones, raise hairs, and disrupt the skin’s pores."*

The Yellow Emperor asked Qībó: *"I once ascended the Qinglìng Terrace, stood on the middle step and looked back, then crawled forward and gazed ahead, and was bewildered. I privately found this strange, inwardly marveled at it, and in solitude, with my mind at ease and my qi settled, I still could not dispel it. I would then turn#3 and turn#3, my hair disheveled and my knees drawn long, bowing to gaze upon it—yet even after prolonged observation, it did not cease. Suddenly, it stopped on its own: what kind of qi caused this?*

Qībó replied: *"The vital qi of the five zàng and six fǔ all ascends to the eyes, becoming their essence. The essence of the zàng forms the eye, the essence of bone forms the pupil, the essence of sinew forms the black sclera, the essence of blood forms the conjunctival vessels, the essence of the zàng’s vital qi forms the white sclera, and the essence of muscle and tendon binds and restrains them. The inner layer, gathering the essence of sinew, bone, blood, and vital qi, connects with the vessels and ascends to the brain, emerging from the nape. Thus, when evil qi strikes the nape, it encounters the body’s void, and its penetration is deep, it follows the eye’s system into the brain. Once it enters the brain, the brain spins, and the brain’s spinning draws the eye’s system taut, causing the eyes to spin and turn.*

If the evil qi is refined, the essence it strikes does not harmonize, and the essence scatters. When the essence scatters, vision diverges, and one sees two objects. The eyes are the essence of the five zàng and six fǔ, the place where the yíng and wèi, as well as the soul and spirit, constantly circulate, and where the divine qi is generated. Thus, when the divine spirit is exhausted, the soul and spirit scatter, and the will and intent become disordered. Therefore, the pupil and black sclera follow the patterns of yin, while the white sclera and red vessels follow the patterns of yang. Hence, when yin and yang harmoniously transmit#5, clarity of vision arises.*

The eyes are governed by the heart; the heart is the dwelling of the spirit. Therefore, when the divine spirit is disordered and does not turn, one suddenly encounters an extraordinary sight, and the divine spirit, soul, and spirit scatter without finding harmony—this is called 'bewilderment' (惑). The Yellow Emperor said: *"I doubt this is the case. I have always been bewildered in the Shù Yuàn, yet it never fails to return. When I leave, it ceases again. Do I alone exhaust my spirit in the East Yuàn? Why is this so peculiar?"*

Qībó said: *"No, it is not so."*

The heart has its joys, the spirit its revulsions; when they suddenly clash#6, the vital *qi* becomes disordered, and vision falters—this is *bewilderment* (惑). Once the spirit shifts, clarity returns. Hence, mild cases are called "confusion" (迷), severe ones "bewilderment" (惑).

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Why do some people often forget things? What *qi* causes this?"*

Qībó replied: *"The upper *qi* is insufficient, while the lower *qi* is excessive. The intestines and stomach are filled, yet the heart and lungs are empty. When empty, the *campo* and *wèiyì*#7 stagnate below; if they linger too long without ascending in due time, forgetfulness follows."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Why do some people feel constant hunger yet not crave food? What *qi* causes this?"*

Qībó replied: *"The vital *qi* converges in the spleen, and the heat *qi* lingers in the stomach. When the stomach is hot, it consumes grain; thus, hunger persists. If the stomach *qi* rises counterflow, the stomach chamber grows cold, and so the appetite for food is lost."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Why do some patients lie awake when sick, unable to sleep? What *qi* causes this?"*

Qībó replied: *"The *wèiyì*#7 *qi* cannot enter yin, lingering perpetually in yang. When yang *qi* overflows, yang’s vitality flourishes; if it cannot descend into yin, yin’s vitality is depleted—thus, the eyes cannot close."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Why do some patients, when sick, lose their sight? What *qi* causes this?"*

Qībó replied: *"The *wèiyì*#7 *qi* stagnates in yin, unable to traverse yang. When yin *qi* overflows, yin’s vitality is excessive; if it cannot ascend into yang, yang’s vitality is depleted—thus, the eyes remain shut."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"Why do some people sleep excessively? What *qi* causes this?"*

Qībó replied: *"In such individuals, the intestines and stomach are vast, yet the skin is tight and the flesh lacks elasticity. When the intestines and stomach are large, the *wèiyì*#7 *qi* lingers for long; when the skin is tight and the flesh unyielding, its movement is sluggish. If the *wèiyì*#7 *qi* lingers too long in yin, its vitality wanes, and drowsiness ensues—thus, they sleep much. Conversely, if the intestines and stomach are small, the skin smooth and relaxed, and the flesh elastic, the *wèiyì*#7 *qi* will linger long in yang—thus, they sleep little."*

The Yellow Emperor asked: *"What if these conditions are not aligned with the regular channels? Why does one suddenly sleep excessively?"*

Qībó replied: *"When perverse *qi* lingers in the upper face, the pores of the upper facial region are obstructed, and they cannot pass. After eating, it is as if they had drunk hot soup—yet the *wèiyì*#7 *qi* lingers long in yin and does not move, and so they suddenly sleep excessively."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Good."* *"How should these various disorders be treated?"*

Qībó replied: *"First address their *zàngfǔ*#8, then punish minor deviations; finally, regulate their *qi*. For those with excess, reduce it; for those with deficiency, supplement it. One must first discern their suffering in form and will—whether from joy or sorrow—before acting accordingly."*

**Chapter 81: *Tān-Jū* (Paralytic Abscesses)**

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I have heard that the intestines and stomach receive grains, and the upper burner produces *qi*, warming the flesh and nourishing bones and joints, harmonizing the sinews and tendons. The middle burner produces *qi* like dew, ascending to the valleys of the body and permeating the minute vessels. The fluids and essences blend and transform, turning red to become blood. When the blood harmonizes, the minute vessels fill first, then overflow into the collateral vessels, and finally into the main channels. Once yin and yang are established, the *qi* moves with the breath, following its proper pathways, circling in accordance with the heavens and earth, never ceasing. If one cuts and adjusts it, from deficiency to excess—if one drains, it will be insufficient; if one hastens, the *qi* will be exhausted; if one lingers, it will cause sequential harm. If one drains from excess to deficiency—if one supplements, there will be surplus; when blood and *qi* are balanced, the form and *qi* will stabilize.*

*Though I know the balance and imbalance of blood and *qi*, I do not yet know the origin of *tān-jū*—how it arises as a single pulse, invading the liver and lungs, turning them black, and killing within ten days or so. The periods of life and death vary in distance—how can one measure them? May I hear your explanation?"*

Qībó replied: *"The channels’ *qi* flows without ceasing, in harmony with the heavens and aligned with the earth. When the celestial hosts lose their measure, eclipses occur in sun and moon; when the earth’s pathways falter, waters overflow, grasses wither, and the five grains fail to grow. Roads become impassable, people cannot travel, and villages scatter in isolation. Blood and *qi* function similarly. Let me explain their nature.*

*The blood and *wei* circulate endlessly, above corresponding to the stars and below to the channels. When cold perversity invades the channels and collaterals, the blood weeps—when the blood weeps, it does not flow; when it does not flow, the *wei* *qi* converges there and cannot return, causing paralysis and swelling. Cold *qi* transforms into heat; when heat prevails, it rots the flesh. When the flesh rots, it becomes pus; when pus is not drained, it damages the sinews; when the sinews are damaged, the bones are harmed; when the bones are harmed, the marrow decays. If the marrow decays and the bone cavity is not drained, the blood dries and empties, causing the sinews, bones, flesh, and muscles to fail to nourish one another. The channels collapse and leak, blackening the five *zàng*, and the *zàng* are injured—thus death occurs."*

The Yellow Emperor said: *"I wish to hear fully the forms of *tān-jū* and the forbidden days for treatment."*

Qībó replied: *"When *tān-jū* arises in the throat, it is called *měng-jū*. If untreated, it turns to pus; if the pus is not drained, it blocks the throat, and death occurs within half a day. If it turns to pus and is drained, apply *zhūgāo* (pig fat) and *língshí* (a medicinal herb), and it will heal in three days.*

*When it arises in the neck, it is called *yāo-jū*. If the swelling is large and black-red, and not treated urgently, the heat *qi* descends into the armpit, injuring the *rèn* vessel and causing the *yáng* *qi* to erupt violently, consuming the brain and lingering in the nape—this is called *nǎo-shuò* (brain rot). If the color is dull, the neck aches as if pierced with a needle, and there is mental agitation, death is inevitable and cannot be treated.*

*When it arises in the shoulder or armpit, it is called *cī-tān*. Its appearance is black-red; if treated urgently, it causes sweating to the soles of the feet without harming the five *zàng*.*

*If *tān-jū* appears for four or five days, it should be treated with cupping.*

*When it arises beneath the armpit as a firm red swelling, it is called *mǐ-jū*. Treat it with a *biànshí* (a surgical tool), making the incision fine and long, then scrape it and apply *zhūgāo*. It will heal in six days; do not bandage it tightly. If it remains firm and does not rupture, use a *mǎdāo* (scalpel) to lance it urgently.*

*When it arises in the chest, it is called *jǐng-jū*, resembling a large soybean. If it is not treated early, it descends into the abdomen, and if untreated, death occurs in seven days.*

*When it arises in the breast, it is called *gān-jū*, with a greenish hue like *lígēng* (a type of reed). The patient constantly suffers from cold and heat; if treated urgently to remove the cold and heat, it can be cured. If not treated urgently, death occurs at ten years of age, and pus emerges posthumously.*

*When it arises in the ribs, it is called *bài-cī*. This is a disease of women. If cauterized, it becomes a large pus-filled abscess. When treated, living flesh the size of a small red bean may be found within. Take *dàsuōqiāocǎo* and *sāqiāocǎo* (two types of herbs), each one *shēng*, and boil in sixteen *shēng* of water, reducing it to three *shēng*. The patient should drink it warmly, wear thick clothing, and sit over the pot until sweating reaches the soles of the feet.*

*When it arises along the *jīng* channel of the thigh, it is called *gǔjīng-jū*. Though its appearance does not change drastically, the abscess presses against the bone. If not treated urgently, death occurs in thirty days.*

*When it arises in the buttocks, it is called *ruì-jū*, appearing as a large, firm red swelling. If treated urgently, it can be cured. If untreated, death occurs in thirty days.*

*When it arises in the inner thigh, it is called *chìshī*. If not treated urgently, death occurs in sixty days. If it appears between the thighs, death occurs in ten days if untreated.*

*When it arises at the knee, it is called *cī-tān*. The swelling is large, the color unchanged, and the patient suffers cold and heat like a hard stone. Do not apply *shí* (a surgical tool) to it—if you do, death will follow. Wait until it softens before applying *shí*; then the patient may survive.*

*If *tān-jū* arises at the joints and corresponds symmetrically, it cannot be treated.*

*If it arises in the *yáng* regions, death occurs in a hundred days; if in the *yīn* regions, in thirty days.*

*When it arises along the channels, it is called *tùpēn*, appearing red down to the bone. If treated urgently, it will not harm the patient.*

*When it arises at the inner ankle, it is called *zǒuhuǎn*, appearing as a swelling with unchanged color. If one repeatedly applies *shí* to its convergence points and stops the cold and heat, the patient will not die.*

*When it arises above or below the foot, it is called *sìyín*, appearing as a large swelling. If not treated urgently, death occurs in a hundred days.*

*When it arises beside the foot, it is called *lì-tān*, initially resembling a small finger swelling. If treated urgently and the blackened area is removed, it will not worsen. If untreated, death occurs in a hundred days.*

*When it arises on the toes, it is called *tuō-tān*. If it appears black-red, death is inevitable if untreated. If it does not appear black-red, the patient will not die."*

The *Huangdi* said, *"Master, how do you distinguish between *zhǒuhuǎn* and *jū*?"*

The *Qíbó* replied, *"When the circulating *qi* and defensive *qi* stagnate within the channels and vessels, the blood weeps and does not flow. If it does not flow, the defensive *qi* follows and becomes obstructed, unable to move, thus generating heat. When the great heat does not cease, the heat overcomes and the flesh rots. When the flesh rots, it becomes pus—but it does not sink inward, nor does the bone marrow become scorched and withered, nor are the five *zàng*-organs injured. Thus, it is called *zhǒuhuǎn*."*

The *Huangdi* asked, *"What is meant by *jū*?"*

The *Qíbó* replied, *"When the hot *qi* is excessive and sinks into the muscles and skin, the tendons and marrow wither, and it internally connects with the five *zàng*-organs, exhausting the blood and *qi*. At the site of the swelling, all tendons, bones, and flesh are consumed, leaving nothing behind. Thus, it is called *jū*. The *jū* has its skin above turned rigid and firm, like the hide of an ox’s neck. The *zhǒuhuǎn* has its skin above thin and glossy. These are their distinguishing signs."*

「The **Huángdì** asked in the *Língshū* (Spiritual Pivot), Volume 23, *"What is meant by **xuān** (瘫)?"*—originally written as *"日"* (day), corrected according to the **Zhào Fǔ Jūjìngtáng** edition.

The **Qíbó** replied: *"When **xuān** (萱) is urgent and hunger is severe, the blood is **qì** (泣) with a **shè** (涩) sound. **Yé** (爷) and **huái** (菰) were anciently pronounced as **kuà** (括), **lóu** (楼). **Jié** (燸) is pronounced **nuò** (奴到切), or also **rú** (濡). **Bùzhě** (不则) is pronounced **shàngfǔ** (上府九切). **Yāo** (夭) is pronounced **mó** (么), meaning the skin’s color is indistinct.

The *Huángdì* asked further: *"What is meant by **jū** (疽)?"*

The **Qíbó** replied: *"When the hot **qi** (气) is excessive, it sinks into the muscles and skin, causing the tendons and marrow to wither. It internally connects with the five **zàng**-organs, exhausting the blood and **qi**. At the site of the swelling, all tendons, bones, and flesh are consumed, leaving none—thus it is called **jū** (疽). The **jū** (疽) has its upper skin rigid and firm, resembling the hide of an ox’s neck. The **xuān** (瘫) has its upper skin thin and glossy. These are their distinguishing signs.

**Annotations:**
- **Xuān** (瘫) is pronounced *xuān* (萱急), *jì* (饥切).
- **Qi** (泣) is pronounced *qì* (血泣), *shè* (涩).
- **Yé** (爷) and **huái** (菰) were once pronounced *kuà* (括) and *lóu* (楼).
- **Jié** (燸) is pronounced *nuò* (奴到切) or *rú* (濡).
- **Bùzhě** (不则) is pronounced *shàngfǔ* (上府九切).
- **Yāo** (夭) is pronounced *mó* (么), indicating unclear or dim color.
- **Wèi** (卫) is written as *qì* (气) in the *Jiāyǐ Jīng* (甲乙经), Volume 11, 9th section.
- **Hé** (合) is written as *hán* (含) in the *Tàisù* (太素), Volume 26, *Xuān-Jū* (瘫疽).
- **Lěngshí** (冷食) is written as *wùlíngshí* (毋玲食) in the *Tàisù*.
- **Jié** (灸) may be mistakenly written; it should likely be *jiǔ* (久).
- **Bù** (不) is omitted in the original but added from the *Jiāyǐ Jīng* (甲乙经), Volume 11, 9th section.
- **Shàng** (上) is written as *zhuàng* (状) in the *Jiāyǐ Jīng* (甲乙经), Volume 11, 9th section.
- **Sǎn** (散) is written as *bài* (败) in the **Zhào Fǔ Jūjìngtáng** edition.
- **Liú** (留) is written as *liú* (流) in the *Jiāyǐ Jīng* (甲乙经), Volume 11, 9th section.
- **Cóngshí** (从实) is written as *hòuxū* (后虚) in the original but corrected to *cóngshí* (从实) in the *Tàisù*, Volume 26, *Xuān-Jū* (瘫疽).
- **Zhī** (知) is written as *suǒ* (所) in the original but corrected to *zhī* (知) in the **Zhào Fǔ Jūjìngtáng** edition.
- **Bùjízhì** (不急治) is omitted in the original but added from the *Jiāyǐ Jīng* (甲乙经), Volume 11, 9th section (appears twice in the text).」
